# Events & Lifecycle

> The 9 designs (events, event-multicaster, refresh-lifecycle, lifecycle-callbacks, aware-callbacks, smart-initializing, runtime-lifecycle, startup-instrumentation, lifecycle-events, context-close, transactional-events) cover the Spring reference's event/lifecycle surface with impressive fidelity and unusually careful boundary-drawing — the publish/subscribe MODEL vs dispatch SEAM split (events vs event-multicaster), the bring-up template vs the per-bean creation driver vs the per-bean callback chains (refresh-lifecycle vs bean-instantiation vs lifecycle-callbacks), the static init/destroy axis vs the runtime start/stop axis (lifecycle-callbacks vs runtime-lifecycle), and the cancel-vs-close fork are all correctly identified and consistently cross-referenced. The async-Rust reframings (no thread-locals across .await, no async Drop, explicit container-driven teardown, link-time listener discovery vs reflective scan) are coherent and repeated consistently across features. The main GAPS are: (a) the listener-side runtime filtering SPI (SmartApplicationListener.supportsEventType/supportsSourceType) and the EventListenerFactory extension seam — the actual seam @TransactionalEventListener rides — are never surfaced as first-class concepts; (b) the LifecycleProcessor is treated as an internal engine but, unlike the multicaster, is never surfaced as a replaceable magic-named bean; (c) the event-fact ENVELOPE (source + timestamp + originating-context) has no owner; (d) concurrent phase STARTUP (6.2.6) is uncovered. The main COHERENCE tensions are all the deferred cross-area keystones (bus shape, registry/DCE substrate, async-across-dyn boxing, error/isolation default, unified ordering primitive, reactive-state primitive) which several features each re-raise without a shared resolution — correctly deferred, but they must be resolved ONCE for the whole area, not per-feature, and a few features make subtly different default assumptions (notably the error-isolation default and whether the lifecycle/run-state notification channel is or is not the event bus).

_11 feature designs. Implementation-agnostic — candidate realizations for Phase 2/3, not decisions._

## Container Refresh Lifecycle  `[refresh-lifecycle]`

Spring's `AbstractApplicationContext.refresh()` is a FIXED-ORDER TEMPLATE METHOD that builds the container in dependency layers, turning bring-up into a predictable, all-or-nothing STATE MACHINE where each step establishes an invariant the next step relies on. THIS feature is that template and its state machine — distinct from post-processor-spi (which fixes the BFPP/BPP contracts), bean-instantiation (which fixes the per-bean creation driver), and container-core (engine-vs-facade split). refresh-lifecycle is the ORCHESTRATOR: the ordered sequence, the phase transitions, the failure/abort semantics. Precisely, the intent to replicate: (1) THE FIXED ORDER (v7): prepareRefresh -> obtainFreshBeanFactory (load definitions; after this defs exist but NO bean is instantiated) -> prepareBeanFactory (install standard context infrastructure: aware-processor, resolvable deps, env beans) -> postProcessBeanFactory (subclass hook) -> invokeBeanFactoryPostProcessors (run ALL BFPPs/BDRPPs, BDRPP-registry-callbacks before all factory-callbacks, Priority>Ordered>rest) -> registerBeanPostProcessors (instantiate+register ALL BPPs; MergedBDPP pulled last) -> initMessageSource -> initApplicationEventMulticaster -> onRefresh (subclass hook, e.g. create web server) -> registerListeners (register listener beans WITHOUT instantiating; flush buffered early events) -> finishBeanFactoryInitialization (install bootstrapExecutor + ConversionService + default value resolver, FREEZE configuration, then preInstantiateSingletons — eagerly create ALL non-lazy singletons, then fire SmartInitializingSingleton.afterSingletonsInstantiated once every singleton exists) -> finishRefresh (init lifecycle processor, auto-start SmartLifecycle by ascending phase, publish ContextRefreshedEvent). (2) THE CENTRAL RULE the order enforces: no application bean is instantiated until ALL definition-rewriting (BFPP) AND ALL per-bean interceptors (BPP) are registered — so every bean uniformly receives DI, proxying, lifecycle callbacks. You extend the container by HOOKING A LAYER, not reordering it. (3) FAIL-FAST, ALL-OR-NOTHING: eager singleton instantiation front-loads wiring/validation/proxy so a misconfigured app REFUSES TO START rather than failing on first request ("a context that started is a context that works"). On a RuntimeException/Error mid-refresh: destroyBeans() + cancelRefresh() (not full doClose — no ContextClosedEvent, no lifecycleProcessor.onClose) — sets active=false, leaves a NON-ACTIVE (not "closed") context so a half-built container never escapes. (4) The DEFINITION-FREEZE BOUNDARY (markBeanAsCreated / freezeConfiguration) is the structural "no more BFPP edits" line. (5) Early-event buffering: events fired before the multicaster exists are queued and flushed by registerListeners, not lost. (6) Hidden: the v7 startup runs under a ReentrantLock-guarded startupShutdownLock (not a global singleton mutex); the SmartInitializing callback runs OUTSIDE any creation lock; background-bootstrap @Bean(BACKGROUND) futures are joined before refresh completes. leaf must replicate this INTENT — a fixed-order, dependency-layered bring-up template with explicit phase transitions, the no-bean-before-all-processors-registered invariant, the definition-freeze boundary, eager fail-fast all-or-nothing semantics with a destroy-already-created cleanup on abort, and the distinction between a failed-bring-up (cancel) and a clean shutdown (close) — WITHOUT Spring's JVM lineage: the startup/shutdown synchronized monitor, the under-lock @PostConstruct (which in async Rust blocks the executor), the assumption that refresh runs synchronously around main with GC mopping up a failed build, the subclass-template-method inheritance (Rust has no abstract-class override of refresh steps), and the assumption that all steps are sync. The lifecycle must drive an ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC processor/bean population (a WASM-module-provided BFPP/BPP/bean must be invoked/created at the same fixed points), and discovery of WHICH processors/beans participate must be SCOPED (static link-time registry PLUS an optional dynamic source), not implicitly global. It must NOT pick the registry substrate, the async-execution model, how much of the sequence runs at build/AOT time vs runtime, whether the template is a fixed function or a re-orderable phase pipeline, or the cancellation/teardown substrate — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Literal fixed-order async template: refresh() is one concrete async state-machine function over named phases, with a typed PhaseState that gates each transition
Mirror Spring's refresh() as ONE concrete async function `async fn refresh(ctx: &mut ContainerBuilder) -> Result<Container, RefreshError>` that runs the named phases in literal fixed order, each phase an `.await` point, with a typed `RefreshState` enum (`Created -> DefinitionsLoaded -> Prepared -> DefinitionsPostProcessed -> Frozen -> ProcessorsRegistered -> InfraInitialized -> SingletonsInstantiated -> Refreshed`) advanced by the engine so a phase can ASSERT its precondition state. The sequence is the Spring template retyped: (1) `prepare()` — reset state, snapshot early listeners, validate required properties (cold, sync-ish; may await env reads); (2) `load_definitions()` — the definition source fills the registry (delegates to bean-registry/bean-definition); state becomes DefinitionsLoaded; (3) `prepare_factory()` — install standard context infrastructure as the first Priority-band processors (the aware-callbacks processor, resolvable-dependency entries for Container/Environment/EventPublisher, default env beans) — this is the engine-vs-facade seam (the bare engine installs none; the context facade installs these); (4) `run_definition_processors()` — drive ALL definition-tier processors (post-processor-spi's BDRPP-registry-callbacks-to-fixpoint THEN BFPP-factory-callbacks, Priority>Ordered>unordered), the analogue of invokeBeanFactoryPostProcessors; (5) `freeze_definitions()` — the markBeanAsCreated boundary: the definition store transitions to read-only, state becomes Frozen, no more definition edits possible (enforced by the registry handle's type changing); (6) `register_instance_processors()` — eagerly construct+register ALL instance-tier processors (post-processor-spi's BPPs, MergedBDPP last) so they exist before any application bean; (7) `init_message_source()` + `init_event_multicaster()` — locate-or-install the magic-named beans (messages-i18n, event-multicaster) before listeners/singletons; (8) `on_refresh()` — the EXTENSION HOOK (a Priority-ordered list of `OnRefreshHook` async callbacks, the rust-native replacement for Spring's subclass override — e.g. an integration crate's web-server-creation hook); (9) `register_listeners()` — register listener beans by descriptor WITHOUT constructing them, then flush buffered early events; (10) `finish_factory_init()` — install bootstrapExecutor/ConversionService/value-resolver, invoke any early initializer beans, then call bean-instantiation's eager pass (all non-lazy singletons, all-or-nothing) followed by smart-initializing's single lock-free callback once all exist; (11) `finish_refresh()` — start the runtime-lifecycle phased state machine (auto-start by ascending phase), publish the RefreshedEvent (lifecycle-events). The ABORT path is the literal cancelRefresh: any phase returning Err triggers `destroy_already_created()` (reverse-order teardown of whatever singletons/processors exist) + sets state to Failed (NOT Closed) — distinct from context-close's clean shutdown. The whole thing runs on the bootstrap task under the no-global-lock discipline; the startupShutdownLock analogue is a single per-container bring-up guard (NOT a global mutex) that only serializes concurrent refresh/close on the same container.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity: the eleven phases, their order, the no-bean-before-all-processors invariant, the freeze boundary, the early-event flush, the SmartInitializing-after-all-singletons, and the cancel-vs-close distinction all map 1:1 to Spring's refresh() — maximally teachable, and `cargo expand`/a stepped trace reads exactly like the Spring sequence (DX #1, §2.10 legibility).; The typed RefreshState enum makes the state machine's invariants COMPILE/RUNTIME-checkable: a phase asserts it runs only in the right predecessor state, and the freeze boundary becomes a real type transition (the definition-edit handle ceases to exist after Frozen) — the 'no more BFPP edits' line is an API shape, not a convention (§2.9 early legible failure).; OnRefreshHook as an ordered async-callback list is the honest rust-native replacement for Spring's subclass template-method override (Rust has no abstract-class refresh() to override): integration crates contribute hooks (web-server creation) as DATA at a fixed slot, scoped via the same catalog beans use — no inheritance, origin-agnostic, and the web-server-before-SmartLifecycle ordering is preserved.; All-or-nothing abort is explicit and rust-native: cancelRefresh's destroy-already-created becomes a structured unwind that runs registered async cleanup in reverse order (no async Drop, §nightly) and leaves a typed Failed state distinct from Closed — so a half-built container provably cannot escape, and the failure carries the full phase+assembly chain (§2.9).; Async-first by construction (§2.3): every phase is an explicit `.await` point on the bootstrap task, so a definition processor reading remote config, an async singleton factory, or an async web-server bind all await without blocking — Spring's under-lock @PostConstruct trap is dissolved because the bring-up guard never wraps user init code, only phase sequencing.; The engine-vs-facade split (container-core) falls out cleanly: prepare_factory() is where the facade installs its standard Priority-band infrastructure processors; a bare engine skips it and the standard features 'silently do nothing' exactly as Spring's plain BeanFactory — the parity is a phase, not a special case.

**Cons:** A single concrete refresh() function with eleven phases is the most rigid shape: it commits early to 'the order is fixed and the steps are these', foreclosing R2's re-orderable phase-pipeline and making it harder for an integration that genuinely needs a NEW phase (not just an on_refresh hook) — a new phase means editing the core function, not contributing data.; Threading the typed RefreshState through a mutually-recursive call graph (finish_factory_init calls bean-instantiation which re-enters resolution which may trigger more creation) means the state-machine guarantee only holds at the PHASE granularity, not within a phase — the intra-phase recursion (creation re-entering creation) is governed by bean-instantiation's guard, not this state, so the 'state machine' framing is partly notional below the phase level.; The cancelRefresh destroy-already-created path must precisely mirror context-close's reverse-dependency teardown but is a SECOND teardown entry point (failed-bring-up vs clean-close) with subtly different semantics (no stop() of Lifecycle beans, no ContextClosedEvent) — two teardown paths that must stay coherent, a real correctness burden (Spring's own 'stop not guaranteed before destroy on cancelled refresh' footgun carries over).; Eleven named phases is a wide surface to specify and keep stable; the message-source/multicaster init steps and the early-event-buffer flush are Spring-specific orderings that may not map cleanly if leaf's event model (events/event-multicaster) lands differently — risk of carrying JVM-shaped steps that a rust-native event design doesn't need.; The 'install bootstrapExecutor then join background futures before refresh completes' sub-step (background-bootstrap) is woven INTO finish_factory_init as a fixed concern; if Phase 2's async-execution model makes background init structural (every independent bean is just a concurrent task), this special sub-step is redundant scaffolding.

**Mechanisms considered:** one concrete `async fn refresh(&mut ContainerBuilder) -> Result<Container, RefreshError>` running named phases in literal fixed order, each an `.await` point; typed `RefreshState` enum advanced by the engine; each phase asserts its predecessor state; the freeze boundary is a real type transition (definition-edit handle disappears after Frozen); OnRefreshHook = an ordered, Priority-banded list of async callbacks contributed as descriptors (the rust-native replacement for subclass template-method override) at the fixed on_refresh slot; cancelRefresh as a structured unwind: destroy-already-created in reverse order running registered async cleanup (no async Drop), leaving a typed Failed state distinct from Closed; single per-container bring-up guard (NOT a global mutex) serializing only concurrent refresh/close on the same container — the startupShutdownLock analogue under §2.5; prepare_factory() as the engine-vs-facade seam: the facade installs standard Priority-band infrastructure processors here; the bare engine installs none

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on HOW the participating processors/beans/listeners/on-refresh-hooks are collected — leaf-core defines refresh()'s phases, the RefreshState enum, the OnRefreshHook trait, and the RefreshError type; contributing crates depend only on leaf-core and emit role-tagged descriptors (BFPP/BPP/listener/on-refresh-hook/singleton) via the SAME catalog substrate (linkme distributed-slice / build.rs codegen / explicit, Phase 2 decides). refresh() reads those descriptor streams at the fixed phases — it never inspects live types (no runtime instanceof). The refresh sequence is the NATURAL HOME for the mandatory anti-DCE self-check (cross-crate doc §152): prepare_factory()/run_definition_processors() can assert expected-vs-found infrastructure processors by role (and finish_factory_init can assert expected eager-singleton counts) so a silently-DCE'd contributing crate becomes a LOUD boot-time RefreshError naming the missing source, rather than a quietly-incomplete graph — and refresh must expose a per-phase introspection surface (what was found, what ran) so the check and startup-instrumentation are writable. The OnRefreshHook list is the cross-crate extension slot for context-specific bring-up (web-server creation) without leaf-core depending on integration crates — origin-agnostic (a WASM-module hook runs at the same slot). The two-source duality applies: the static link-time registry contributes native descriptors; an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source contributes descriptors whose factories/hooks bridge into the module — both feed the SAME phase-driven population (CANDIDATES, not a decision); but a dynamic source must register BEFORE the freeze_definitions boundary, which constrains its timing (a phase2Question). Generics can't be eagerly instantiated/registered (no 'static symbol until concrete) → the descriptor table holds only concrete types; macro hard-errors with a register-concrete escape hatch. Because refresh is ONE function in leaf-core, the phase ABI is small and stable (the thin macro never emits lifecycle logic, only descriptors).

**T2 — execution context:** The whole refresh() runs on the bootstrap task (COLD PATH, around startup), single-threaded by default for the cold definition phases and fanning out only where bean-instantiation/background-bootstrap permit. Each phase is an explicit `.await` point: definition processors may await (remote config) but run before any instance exists; the eager-singleton phase awaits every non-lazy singleton's construction (a blocking factory must use spawn_blocking — execution-context discipline §2.3); the SmartInitializing callback runs OUTSIDE any creation guard after all singletons exist (smart-initializing). NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the only refresh-level guard is a single per-container bring-up guard serializing concurrent refresh/close on the SAME container — it never wraps user init code (dissolving Spring's under-lock @PostConstruct deadlock by execution context, not a lock). CANCELLATION: cancelling the bootstrap task (or any phase erroring) triggers the cancelRefresh unwind — already-created singletons/processors are destroyed in reverse dependency order, running their registered async cleanup explicitly (no async Drop), early-ref/in-creation state is cleared, and the container is left in a typed Failed state (NOT Closed — no RefreshedEvent, no Lifecycle.stop, mirroring Spring's cancelRefresh-vs-doClose split). ERRORS propagate as a rich RefreshError carrying the failing phase, the assembly chain at the point of failure (what was being built/processed, what it needed, candidates), and per-source provenance for DCE diagnosis (§2.9); the all-or-nothing guarantee means no partially-built container with dangling resources escapes. The finish_refresh phase starts the runtime-lifecycle phased machine (a separate hot-path concern) but refresh itself returns once SmartLifecycle auto-start completes; ongoing runtime activity is handed off to runtime-lifecycle. Background-bootstrap futures (independent slow singletons) are spawned on the bootstrapExecutor during finish_factory_init and joined before refresh returns (completion forced by the all-or-nothing boundary).

#### R2 — Re-orderable phase-DAG pipeline: refresh is a topologically-ordered set of named, dependency-declaring PhaseSteps the engine sequences, with the standard steps as default contributions
Question whether the bring-up order must be a HARD-CODED function at all. Spring fixes the order because the JVM gives it no other structuring tool and it deliberately rejected a configurable pipeline. But leaf's invariant is unification (§2.11) and its cross-crate doc says to encode ordering as DATA resolved by topo-sort, never as hard sequence. So model refresh as a PHASE-DAG: a set of named `PhaseStep { id: PhaseId, after: &[PhaseId], before: &[PhaseId], priority: Order, run: async fn(&mut RefreshCx) -> Result<(), RefreshError> }` that the engine topologically sorts (with cycle detection as a rich startup diagnostic) and runs in the resolved order. The ELEVEN standard Spring steps become DEFAULT phase contributions leaf-core ships (LoadDefinitions, PrepareFactory, RunDefinitionProcessors before-> FreezeDefinitions before-> RegisterInstanceProcessors before-> InitMessageSource/InitMulticaster before-> RegisterListeners before-> EagerInstantiate before-> SmartInitialize before-> StartLifecycle before-> PublishRefreshed) with their ordering edges declared as data. The KEY INVARIANTS that must hold (no bean before all processors; defs frozen before any instance) are encoded as hard `before`/`after` edges the topo-sort enforces — and a CONTRIBUTED phase that tried to violate them (e.g. an EagerInstantiate-before-RegisterInstanceProcessors edge) produces a cycle-or-constraint-violation error at sort time (the earliest, richest diagnostic, §2.9). An integration crate can insert a NEW phase (not just an on_refresh hook) by contributing a PhaseStep with edges (e.g. CreateWebServer after RegisterListeners, before StartLifecycle) — strictly more expressive than R1's single on_refresh slot. RefreshCx is a phase-typed capability object: it exposes the definition-edit handle ONLY to phases ordered before FreezeDefinitions, the singleton-creation driver only after, etc., so 'what a phase may do' is gated by where it sits. The abort/cancel semantics are the same as R1 (destroy-already-created, Failed-not-Closed) but driven by the executed-phases ledger (unwind exactly the phases that ran, in reverse). The whole pipeline still runs on the bootstrap task cold path.

**Pros:** Maximal unification + extensibility (§2.11): bring-up is ONE primitive (a PhaseStep DAG) and integration crates extend the container by contributing PHASES with declared edges — the exact 'extend by hooking a layer' intent Spring prizes, but generalized so a new layer is data, not a core-function edit (R1's limitation). Web-server creation, scope registration, etc. slot in without touching leaf-core.; Ordering-as-data resolved by topo-sort matches the cross-crate doc's explicit guidance (§170: do all ordering at runtime via topo-sort over descriptor data, never link/section order) — and gives the SAME machinery auto-config-ordering and post-processor ordering want, so the whole framework shares one ordering primitive (the unification dividend).; Invariant violations become a SORT-TIME diagnostic: the no-bean-before-processors and defs-frozen-before-instances rules are hard edges, so a contributed phase that breaks them fails with a full cycle/constraint path BEFORE any phase runs — the earliest possible, richest diagnostic (§2.9), strictly better than discovering the violation mid-bring-up.; The phase-typed RefreshCx capability object enforces 'what you may do here' by phase position (definition-edit handle only pre-freeze, creation driver only post-register) — the freeze boundary and the no-instantiation-in-definition-phases rule become properties of the ctx handed in, not conventions, and they hold for contributed phases too.; Most AOT-friendly: a topo-sorted phase schedule is exactly what a build-time pass could EMIT as a const ordering (the §3 compile-vs-runtime keystone) — the runtime path computes the same schedule, so the expensive ordering analysis can move to build time later without changing the executor.; Cancellation/teardown is driven by the executed-phases ledger, so the unwind is mechanically derived (reverse the phases that ran) rather than a hand-maintained second teardown path — less chance of the cancel-vs-close coherence drift R1 risks.

**Cons:** Directly contradicts Spring's deliberate design choice that the order is FIXED and NOT a configurable pipeline (the doc is explicit: 'making the order fixed rather than a configurable pipeline turns bring-up into a predictable state machine') — a re-orderable DAG, even if it resolves to the same order by default, weakens the 'you cannot accidentally reorder' guarantee and the teachable linear mental model, cutting against intent fidelity.; A contributed phase with wrong/missing edges can land in a surprising position (or create a cycle) — the failure is louder than Spring's (sort-time) but the SPACE of possible orderings is now open, so reasoning about 'what order will my container actually bring up in' requires reading the resolved DAG, not a fixed list — a real DX cost (#1) versus R1's readable sequence.; More machinery for the common case where nobody adds a phase: every refresh pays topo-sort + cycle-detection over the standard steps even though they always resolve to the same order — overhead and complexity Spring's hard sequence avoids (acceptable at boot, but real, and it pre-judges that re-orderability is wanted).; The phase-typed RefreshCx capability enum means every PhaseStep matches on the ctx variant it expects and handles wrong-phase arms — ergonomics friction and a class of must-handle-but-unreachable arms a fixed-signature design avoids (the same uniformity-vs-type-safety tradeoff that recurs in unified designs).; Pushing the bring-up order to runtime-resolved data partly pre-decides the metadata/codegen and compile-vs-runtime keystones (§3) — presenting a phase-DAG as THE shape risks biasing those Phase-2 calls; must stay a CANDIDATE.; The hard invariant edges (no-bean-before-processors, freeze-before-instances) must be authored correctly and completely on the standard phases, AND every contributed phase must declare enough edges to be safely placed — an under-constrained contributed phase is a footgun the topo-sort can't catch if it's merely under-specified rather than cyclic.

**Mechanisms considered:** refresh as a set of named `PhaseStep { id, after, before, priority, run }` contributions topologically sorted by the engine (cycle/constraint detection as a startup diagnostic); the eleven Spring steps shipped as DEFAULT phase contributions in leaf-core with their ordering encoded as before/after edges (data, not hard sequence); hard invariant edges (no-bean-before-RegisterInstanceProcessors; FreezeDefinitions before any EagerInstantiate) enforced by the topo-sort — violations fail at sort time; integration crates contribute NEW phases (e.g. CreateWebServer) by declaring edges — extension by data, not a single on_refresh slot; phase-typed RefreshCx capability object gating what each phase may do by its resolved position (definition-edit handle only pre-freeze; creation driver only post-register); abort/cancel driven by the executed-phases ledger: unwind exactly the phases that ran, in reverse — mechanically derived teardown

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strongest fit for both the ordering-as-data guidance and the cross-crate extension story: leaf-core defines PhaseStep, PhaseId, the RefreshCx capability object, and ships the standard phases as default contributions; integration crates depend only on leaf-core and contribute additional PhaseSteps via the Phase-2 catalog substrate (linkme/codegen/explicit). The topo-sort over phase edges is the SAME ordering primitive auto-config-ordering and post-processor-spi want, so the cross-crate ordering machinery is shared (unification). The sort step is the natural place to run BOTH the anti-DCE self-check AND constraint validation (cross-crate doc §152): a silently-DCE'd contributing crate shows up as a missing expected phase OR a missing expected processor count → a loud sort-time RefreshError with per-source provenance, strictly better than a count-only check because it can name which phase/source is absent and which ordering constraint is unsatisfiable. Generics still can't register a phase (no 'static symbol until concrete) → macro hard-errors. The two-source duality is natural for the static half (link-time phases) but a dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source contributing a phase must register before the sort runs — and a WASM source contributing a DEFINITION still has the pre-freeze-timing constraint (a phase2Question, flagged not resolved). Because the order is data, an AOT/build-time pass could emit the resolved schedule as a const (Architecture C), which the runtime simply consumes — the cleanest AOT-lineage fit of the three, but it WIDENS the stable surface (the phase-edge vocabulary becomes ABI). CANDIDATES not a decision throughout.

**T2 — execution context:** Topo-sort + cycle/constraint detection is SYNCHRONOUS cold-path (single-threaded, around startup, before any phase runs) — pure analysis over phase descriptors, producing a resolved schedule and the earliest diagnostics. Phase EXECUTION runs on the bootstrap task in the resolved order, each PhaseStep's run() an `.await` point under the same execution-context discipline as R1 (definition phases single-threaded cold; eager-instantiate may fan out via bean-instantiation/background-bootstrap; blocking work spawn_blocking; §2.3). The phase-typed RefreshCx gives each phase only the capabilities valid at its position — the definition-edit handle (pre-freeze phases) is held only on the single-threaded cold path, sidestepping &mut-across-await aliasing by execution context, not a lock (§2.5). NO GLOBAL LOCK — only the per-container bring-up guard (concurrent refresh/close on the same container). CANCELLATION uses the executed-phases ledger: cancelling the bootstrap task unwinds exactly the phases that ran, in reverse, running their registered async cleanup (no async Drop) and leaving a typed Failed state distinct from Closed — the unwind is mechanically derived rather than a separate hand-written cancelRefresh path. ERRORS are rich RefreshError carrying the failing phase id, the assembly chain, the resolved schedule context, and provenance (§2.9); a sort-time failure (cycle/constraint/missing-expected-phase) aborts before ANY phase runs (the earliest, richest point); a run-time phase failure aborts all-or-nothing and unwinds the ledger. As in R1, finish-of-refresh hands ongoing activity to runtime-lifecycle; background-bootstrap independent phases/beans run concurrently where the schedule permits.

#### R3 — Build-time-frozen bring-up PLAN + thin runtime executor: the lifecycle's structure (which phases, what order, which singletons, the dependency waves) is computed once and frozen; refresh just executes the frozen plan
Push the AOT lineage (the doc's 'definitions are knowable at build time; instances are not' and Spring 7's refreshForAotProcessing which runs the whole BFPP/condition/parse pipeline WITHOUT instantiating beans) hardest. Separate the lifecycle's STRUCTURE from its EXECUTION. A PLAN phase (build-time codegen via leaf-codegen, OR a runtime startup-validation pass when not AOT) runs the definition-producing steps to completion against a fixed feature set + environment: load definitions, run all definition-tier processors, evaluate conditions/profiles, freeze, compute the eager-singleton set, topo-sort the singleton dependency graph into creation WAVES, and detect fatal cycles — emitting a `RefreshPlan { phase_schedule, frozen_definitions, eager_singleton_waves, lifecycle_phase_order, smart_init_set, condition_verdicts }` as a checked-in/const artifact (sqlx model, Architecture C). The RUNTIME refresh() is then a THIN executor: it consumes the frozen RefreshPlan and does only the steps that genuinely need runtime values/instances — install runtime infrastructure (executor, conversion service), resolve env-dependent placeholder VALUES (structure was resolved at plan time), eagerly construct the singletons WAVE BY WAVE (each wave's deps already built, so waves fan out concurrently with no creation guard on the planned path), fire the SmartInitializing callback after the last wave, start the runtime-lifecycle machine in the frozen phase order, publish RefreshedEvent. The all-or-nothing fail-fast moves EARLIER: the plan VALIDATES the whole graph (every injection point resolvable, no fatal cycle, all expected infrastructure present) at plan/freeze time before constructing ANYTHING — so refresh either has a valid plan or aborts before any side effect. The abort path at runtime (a wave's construction fails) still destroys-already-created in reverse wave order. The bare-engine-vs-facade difference is WHICH plan is installed. When NOT building AOT, the plan is computed at runtime in a dedicated startup-validation pass (same code, same artifact, just not checked in) — so the executor is identical across AOT and JIT, and the plan artifact is the single place the lifecycle's structure lives.

**Pros:** Best fit for the AOT lineage and §3 compile-vs-runtime keystone: the lifecycle STRUCTURE (phase order, eager set, dependency waves, condition verdicts) is exactly what Spring 7's refreshForAotProcessing freezes and what a leaf build-time pass could emit — the most expensive bring-up analysis moves to build time, dramatically faster/leaner startup, and the runtime refresh becomes a thin, readable plan-executor.; Earliest + richest fail-fast (§2.9): the plan validates the WHOLE graph (resolvability, fatal cycles, expected-infrastructure presence, condition outcomes) at plan/freeze time before ANY instance — so a misconfigured app fails with a complete diagnostic and ZERO partial side effects, the purest realization of 'a context that started is a context that works' and all-or-nothing.; Wave-parallel eager instantiation with NO creation guard on the planned path (the strongest §2.5 answer): the plan proves each wave's deps are already built, so waves fan out concurrently and background-bootstrap is just 'the executor runs independent waves wherever' — Spring's hardest-won strict/lenient creation-lock code is unneeded for planned beans.; The frozen RefreshPlan is inspectable DATA (a generated file / a dumped artifact): 'what order will my container bring up in, which beans are eager, what are the waves' is a readable artifact, not emergent runtime behavior — excellent debuggability/reproducibility (the sqlx --check lineage) and a thin-macro story (§2.10) since the plan is hand-inspectable code, not hidden logic.; Identical executor across AOT and runtime-computed plans means ONE bring-up code path serves both the native-image/optimized build and the dev/JIT build — no bimodality, and the substrate is swappable per target (cross-crate doc's identical-runtime-API-across-substrates goal).; Condition/profile verdicts frozen in the plan make the conditional strategy (conditions, profiles, auto-configuration) deterministic given the resolved feature set + environment — the plan is the single source of truth for 'what wired up', cleanly separating 'what could contribute' (link-time) from 'what does' (plan-time).

**Cons:** Diverges most from Spring's runtime-dynamism mental model and pre-judges the §3 compile-vs-runtime AND metadata/codegen keystones the hardest — presenting a frozen-plan shape as THE realization risks strongly biasing Phase 2's signal; it must be held strictly as a CANDIDATE, and its value is only as large as the fraction of the graph that can be planned statically.; Requires the dependency EDGES and condition outcomes to be knowable at plan time, but Spring discovers many edges dynamically (by-type/qualifier resolution, ObjectProvider lookups, FactoryBean products whose type is only known after creating the factory, conditions over runtime container state) — a static plan needs a conservative over-approximation plus a runtime fallback, partially reintroducing the recursive driver and the dynamism it sought to eliminate.; Sacrifices Spring's mid-bootstrap dynamism: an init-method/registrar that registers a NEW definition mid-bring-up (Spring's defensive-copy case, programmatic-registration) has no node in an already-frozen plan — leaf must forbid it (a real divergence) or support re-planning (which erodes the frozen-plan simplicity), entangled with the bean-registry mutable-vs-frozen question.; Two computation modes (build-time codegen plan + runtime-computed plan) that must produce IDENTICAL plans and the runtime fallback for dynamic edges is essentially R1/R2's recursive driver anyway — so the plan's payoff is bounded, and keeping the two modes coherent (same verdicts, same order, same waves) is a coherence burden.; The dynamic WASM-module 'classpath' source has NO build-time presence, so it can only contribute through the runtime path — a direct tension with the freeze-early/plan-at-build-time shape (the dynamic source forces a runtime re-plan or a separate runtime-only lane), flagged as a phase2Question, not resolved.; Heaviest cold-path/build-time machinery and the largest conceptual surface (a whole plan IR: phase schedule + waves + verdicts + frozen defs) — overkill for a small app, and the plan artifact becomes a stable ABI/format commitment that must version cleanly.

**Mechanisms considered:** separate PLAN phase (build-time codegen via leaf-codegen, OR a runtime startup-validation pass) that runs definition-producing steps to completion WITHOUT instantiating beans (the refreshForAotProcessing analogue); a frozen `RefreshPlan` artifact: phase_schedule + frozen_definitions + eager_singleton_waves (topo-sorted dependency layers) + lifecycle_phase_order + condition_verdicts + smart_init_set; thin runtime executor that consumes the plan: install runtime infra, resolve env-dependent placeholder VALUES, build singletons wave-by-wave (no creation guard on the planned path), SmartInitialize, start lifecycle, publish RefreshedEvent; whole-graph validation at plan/freeze time (resolvability, fatal cycles, expected-infrastructure presence, condition outcomes) — fail-fast before any instance, zero partial side effects; conservative dependency over-approximation + a runtime recursive fallback driver for dynamically-discovered/lazy/on-demand beans the plan can't capture; identical executor across AOT-emitted and runtime-computed plans (sqlx --check lineage); the plan is the single inspectable source of the lifecycle's structure

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The realization most coupled to the cross-crate substrate decision: it leans on Architecture C (build-time codegen, sqlx model) for the AOT path — leaf-codegen walks the resolved dependency graph + a manifest convention and emits the RefreshPlan as a const/checked-in artifact, sidestepping the silent-DCE layers ENTIRELY for the planned graph (no link-time slice to drop, the doc's most-reliable path §232). The plan computation IS the anti-DCE self-check + graph validation fused (cross-crate doc §152): a silently-DCE'd contributing crate appears as a missing node / unsatisfiable edge / absent expected phase → a loud plan-time error naming WHAT is unsatisfiable and from which source, with a --check-able manifest catching stale plans in CI. leaf-core defines RefreshPlan, the phase/wave IR, and the executor; leaf-codegen owns plan emission; the binary/app-root force-links integration crates whose contributions feed the plan. The HARD tension: a static plan needs every edge/condition knowable at plan time, but by-type/qualifier/ObjectProvider/FactoryBean-product edges and runtime conditions may only be knowable at runtime — so the plan must conservatively include candidate edges (coupling to candidate-resolver) plus a runtime fallback. The two-source duality is asymmetric here: the static link-time registry feeds the plan cleanly, but the dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source has no build-time presence and can only feed the runtime fallback (or force a re-plan) — the sharpest reconciliation problem of the three, a CANDIDATE/phase2Question not a decision. Generics: the plan holds only concrete nodes; macro hard-errors on generics. When not AOT, the identical plan computation runs at startup as a validation pass — same artifact, no link magic, fully portable (the doc's wasm/embedded path).

**T2 — execution context:** PLAN computation is fully SYNCHRONOUS cold-path: at build time (leaf-codegen, no async/IO into the running app) OR at startup in a dedicated single-threaded validation pass that runs the definition-tier processors (which MAY await for remote-config-driven definition edits) to completion but creates NO instances — the refreshForAotProcessing analogue, so the &mut definition store is held only on this single-threaded cold path (no aliasing-across-await issue, §2.5). RUNTIME execution: the thin executor runs on the bootstrap task; env-dependent placeholder VALUE resolution may await (the structure was already planned); eager instantiation runs WAVE BY WAVE — each wave is a bounded concurrent fan-out of construction futures inside a structured-concurrency scope (a failure/cancel in one node cancels the wave and unwinds cleanly, §2.3), with NO creation guard on the planned path because the plan proved intra-wave independence (the strongest no-global-lock posture). A later wave reads already-built deps lock-free. Blocking factories use spawn_blocking. The SmartInitializing callback fires after the final wave, outside any guard. CANCELLATION: cancelling the bootstrap task cancels the structured scope → in-flight construction futures drop; partially-built beans with async cleanup run it explicitly on the unwind path (no async Drop); un-published waves leave nothing to leak; the container is left Failed (not Closed). ERRORS: plan-phase errors carry the FULL graph context (cycle path, unsatisfiable edge, absent infrastructure, condition verdict) at the earliest possible point with zero side effects; execute-phase errors carry the failing node's assembly chain and abort all-or-nothing, unwinding completed waves in reverse. The runtime fallback driver (for dynamic/lazy/on-demand beans the plan couldn't capture) runs on the calling task with bean-instantiation's per-bean guard for the rare contended case. finish-of-refresh starts runtime-lifecycle in the frozen phase order and hands off ongoing activity.

### Rust clashes
- No subclass template-method override for refresh() — Spring structures refresh() as an abstract-class template method whose steps (obtainFreshBeanFactory, postProcessBeanFactory, onRefresh, finishRefresh) subclasses OVERRIDE to specialize bring-up (e.g. web contexts override onRefresh to create the embedded WebServer before the SmartLifecycle that starts it). Rust has no abstract-class inheritance with overridable protected methods. So the extension points that Spring expresses as subclass overrides must become DATA: an ordered list of contributed hooks/phases (R1's OnRefreshHook list, R2's contributed PhaseSteps) resolved by the engine — never an inheritance hierarchy. This is why the realizations make on-refresh extension a descriptor-contributed callback, not a method override, and why a NEW bring-up step is data (R2) rather than a subclass (Spring).
- Refresh is synchronous-around-main with GC cleanup in Spring; in leaf it is an ASYNC bring-up with explicit teardown. Spring's refresh() runs synchronously on the calling thread, holds a synchronized/ReentrantLock startup monitor, runs @PostConstruct UNDER the singleton creation lock, and relies on GC to mop up a failed half-build (cancelRefresh just nulls references). In async leaf: (a) every phase is an `.await` point, so the bring-up guard must NEVER wrap a user init `.await` (an init future awaiting under a held guard can deadlock the executor pool — the rust-native worsening of Spring's @PostConstruct-under-lock trap), forcing heavy cross-bean work to the lock-free SmartInitializing callback; (b) the startupShutdownLock becomes at most a per-container bring-up guard, never a global mutex (§2.5); (c) a FAILED refresh cannot rely on GC — with no async Drop, already-created singletons/processors holding async-cleanup resources must be torn down EXPLICITLY in reverse order on the cancelRefresh unwind path, so 'the container destroys already-created beans on abort' is a non-syntactic discipline the lifecycle must own.
- No async Drop means the all-or-nothing abort path must run explicit async cleanup. Spring's all-or-nothing guarantee (refresh destroys already-created singletons on failure so a half-built context never escapes) relies on synchronous destroy callbacks + GC. In Rust, a phase that fails after some singletons (with async-acquired resources: DB pools, spawned tasks, open connections) were built cannot clean them via Drop — the cancelRefresh unwind must retain enough state (a creation/dependency ledger) to run each bean's registered async shutdown explicitly, in reverse dependency order, with timeouts and error aggregation. The all-or-nothing fail-fast invariant is therefore only as strong as the explicit-teardown discipline on the abort path — leaving it implicit would leak resources on the common boot-failure path.
- Spring's 'no application bean instantiated until ALL BFPPs and BPPs are registered' invariant is enforced by ordering live instances; leaf enforces it by PHASE STATE + handle types, since there is no runtime type-walk to discover processors. Spring auto-detects processors by iterating live beans and instanceof-checking them, then orders the phases. Rust has no instanceof over registered beans. So the realizations enforce the invariant structurally: processors are role-tagged descriptors run at fixed phases (post-processor-spi), the freeze boundary is a real type transition (the definition-edit handle ceases to exist after Frozen), and the eager-instantiate phase provably runs only after the register-processors phase (R1's RefreshState assertion / R2's hard before-edge). The invariant is a property of the phase machine, not of inspecting instances.
- The startupShutdownLock as a ReentrantLock collides with §2.5's no-global-lock and async cancellation. Spring v6.1+ guards startup/shutdown with a ReentrantLock (replacing the synchronized monitor) plus a stuck-thread interrupt mechanism for a user System.exit during startup. In leaf this cannot be a global lock; it is at most a per-container guard serializing concurrent refresh/close on the SAME container — and the 'stuck startup thread' problem maps to async CANCELLATION (a cancelled or hung bring-up task), which leaf handles via structured concurrency + timeouts + the explicit cancelRefresh unwind rather than interrupting a JVM thread. The reconciliation: serialize bring-up per-container by execution context, not by a process-global lock.
- cancelRefresh-vs-doClose (Failed state vs Closed state) is a two-teardown-path distinction Rust must keep coherent without GC. Spring deliberately separates a FAILED refresh (destroyBeans + cancelRefresh: active=false, no ContextClosedEvent, no Lifecycle.stop, leaving a NON-active context) from a clean CLOSE (doClose: stop Lifecycle beans first, publish ContextClosedEvent, then destroy in reverse — context-close). leaf must replicate BOTH paths and their different semantics (a cancelled bring-up skips Lifecycle.stop — Spring's documented footgun that drain logic in stop() is bypassed) while sharing the reverse-dependency teardown machinery, all without GC to paper over a partial build — two entry points into explicit async teardown that must stay coherent.
- Spring's two-pass eager loop over a 'defensive copy' assumes mid-bootstrap definition mutation leaf may forbid. preInstantiateSingletons iterates a defensive copy of beanDefinitionNames so an init-method can register a NEW definition mid-bring-up without ConcurrentModificationException. Whether leaf preserves this dynamism depends on the bean-registry mutable-vs-frozen decision (and is impossible under R3's frozen plan). If the registry freezes at refresh, the defensive-copy concern vanishes but so does mid-bring-up registration (a divergence entangled with programmatic-registration and conditions). The lifecycle's eager phase must be written to whichever registry shape Phase 2 picks — it cannot assume mutability.
- Early-event buffering and the message-source/multicaster init steps are JVM-event-model-shaped and may not map to leaf's event design. Spring fixes specific phases (initMessageSource, initApplicationEventMulticaster before listeners/singletons; registerListeners flushing earlyApplicationEvents that were buffered before the multicaster existed) because its event/i18n beans are 'magic-named' singletons the context must locate-or-default at a precise moment. If leaf's events/event-multicaster/messages-i18n realizations land differently (e.g. the multicaster is always present, or events are typed channels not a single bus), these fixed phases may be unnecessary or differently-ordered — so the lifecycle must not hard-code Spring's exact event-bring-up sub-sequence as load-bearing; it is intent (infrastructure ready before listeners/singletons), not mechanism.

### Interactions
- **post-processor-spi** — DOWNSTREAM CONSUMER — the most direct coupling. refresh-lifecycle is THE DRIVER that invokes the post-processor SPI at fixed points: the run-definition-processors phase runs ALL BFPPs/BDRPPs (registry-callbacks before factory-callbacks, Priority>Ordered>unordered), the freeze phase IS the 'no more definition edits' boundary the SPI defines, and the register-instance-processors phase eagerly constructs+registers all BPPs (MergedBDPP last) before any application bean. post-processor-spi defines the processor contracts and ordering bands; refresh-lifecycle defines WHERE in the fixed-order template they fire and the all-or-nothing failure semantics around them.
- **bean-instantiation** — DOWNSTREAM CONSUMER. refresh-lifecycle's finish-factory-init phase CALLS bean-instantiation's eager pre-instantiation pass (the preInstantiateSingletons two-loop / wave executor) at the fixed point after all processors are registered, infrastructure is initialized, and definitions are frozen. refresh-lifecycle owns the all-or-nothing bring-up state machine, the phase ordering, and the abort/cancel semantics; bean-instantiation owns the per-bean construct->expose-early->populate->init->publish sequence and the creation guard that the eager phase invokes. Eager-pass failure aborts refresh (cancelRefresh).
- **container-core** — refresh-lifecycle realizes container-core's bring-up: the engine-vs-facade split shows up as the prepare-factory phase (the facade installs its standard Priority-band infrastructure processors here; the bare engine installs none, so framework features 'silently do nothing' — the Spring plain-BeanFactory parity). container-core fixes the two-layer split and the auto-detect-post-processors convention; refresh-lifecycle is the ordered template that drives that convention into a live container.
- **bean-registry** — UPSTREAM. The load-definitions and freeze phases act on bean-registry's stores: the registry holds the definitions the lifecycle loads, and the freeze/markBeanAsCreated boundary the lifecycle enforces is a registry state transition (mutable-edit -> read-only). Whether the registry is mutable mid-bring-up (Spring's defensive-copy dynamism) or frozen-at-refresh (R3's plan) is a shared phase2Question that shapes the eager phase.
- **smart-initializing** — refresh-lifecycle's eager phase fires smart-initializing's single lock-free callback (afterSingletonsInstantiated) as the SECOND loop, AFTER every eager singleton exists and OUTSIDE any creation guard — the sanctioned place for cross-bean aggregation that would deadlock in a per-bean init. refresh-lifecycle establishes the 'all eager singletons now exist' boundary; smart-initializing defines the callback contract. Fired only for eager singletons, never lazy/prototype/scoped.
- **lazy-initialization** — The eager phase reads each definition's lazy flag to GATE construction: a lazy singleton is registered/frozen but NOT built during refresh — its potential failure is relocated to first access. lazy-initialization defines the per-bean @Lazy and global-flag-with-excludes semantics; refresh-lifecycle's eager phase honors them (and in R3 excludes lazy beans from the frozen plan, building them via the runtime fallback).
- **runtime-lifecycle** — refresh-lifecycle's finish-refresh phase STARTS runtime-lifecycle's phased state machine (auto-start SmartLifecycle beans by ascending phase) as the penultimate step, then publishes the RefreshedEvent. refresh-lifecycle owns the static bring-up (build the singleton graph); runtime-lifecycle owns the running-state machine layered on top (servers listening, schedulers ticking). The handoff is precise: refresh returns once auto-start completes; ongoing activity is runtime-lifecycle's.
- **context-close** — SIBLING / MIRROR. context-close defines the clean shutdown (stop Lifecycle beans first, then destroy singletons in reverse dependency order, publish ContextClosedEvent) — the mirror of refresh's bring-up. refresh-lifecycle's ABORT path (cancelRefresh: destroy-already-created, NO Lifecycle.stop, NO ContextClosedEvent, leave a Failed state) deliberately DIFFERS from context-close's clean path (the Spring cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction), but SHARES the reverse-dependency teardown machinery context-close owns. The two teardown entry points must stay coherent.
- **lifecycle-events** — refresh-lifecycle PUBLISHES the context lifecycle facts on the event bus at fixed phases: the RefreshedEvent (ContextRefreshedEvent) at finish-refresh, and is the producer of the started/availability milestones lifecycle-events routes. lifecycle-events defines the event payloads and the readiness/liveness state; refresh-lifecycle fires them at the precise bring-up milestones (and a failed refresh does NOT publish RefreshedEvent — the cancel-vs-close distinction).
- **event-multicaster** — The init-multicaster phase locates-or-installs the multicaster bean before listeners/singletons, and the register-listeners phase flushes any events buffered before the multicaster existed (Spring's earlyApplicationEvents). event-multicaster defines the dispatch routing; refresh-lifecycle defines the fixed phase at which the multicaster must be ready and the early-event-flush ordering — though if leaf's event model differs, this exact sub-sequence is intent (infra-ready-before-listeners), not mechanism (rustClashes).
- **messages-i18n** — The init-message-source phase locates the magic-named MessageSource bean or installs a delegating default, before listeners and singletons exist — so i18n is available to beans during their init. messages-i18n defines the hierarchy-aware MessageSource; refresh-lifecycle fixes the phase at which it must be ready (a Spring-specific ordering that is intent, not load-bearing mechanism, if leaf's i18n lands differently).
- **background-bootstrap** — refresh-lifecycle's finish-factory-init phase installs the bootstrapExecutor and, after the eager pass, joins the background-init futures before refresh completes (completion forced by the all-or-nothing boundary for non-lazy beans). background-bootstrap marks which slow independent singletons init concurrently and supplies the executor; refresh-lifecycle ensures their completion is forced inside the all-or-nothing bring-up (in R2/R3 these are just planner/schedule-proven-independent waves run concurrently).
- **startup-instrumentation** — refresh-lifecycle emits the named, timed startup steps startup-instrumentation records (the spring.context.refresh root, per-phase steps, spring.beans.instantiate / smart-initialize). startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default opt-in-recording tree; refresh-lifecycle's per-phase structure IS the stable taxonomy of steps it records — the realizations expose a per-phase introspection surface so instrumentation (and the anti-DCE self-check) is writable.
- **aware-callbacks** — The prepare-factory phase installs the aware-callbacks processor (Spring's ApplicationContextAwareProcessor) as the first Priority-band infrastructure processor and registers the resolvable-dependency entries (Container, Environment, EventPublisher), so aware injection works for container-created beans during their init. aware-callbacks defines which infrastructure is injected and the group-before-init timing; refresh-lifecycle fixes the phase at which the aware processor and resolvable deps are installed.
- **configuration-classes** — @Configuration/@Bean/@Import parsing is the marquee definition-tier (BDRPP) work the run-definition-processors phase drives FIRST (highest Priority) so derived @Bean definitions exist before placeholder resolution and before freeze. configuration-classes provides the BDRPP that registers derived definitions; refresh-lifecycle's strict registry-callbacks-before-factory-callbacks-before-freeze ordering is what makes that derivation land before the rest of definition processing.
- **auto-configuration** — Auto-configuration's deferred, ordered, conditional default beans are contributed via the definition-processing phase (after user config, backing off via conditions) — refresh-lifecycle's fixed ordering (user definitions processed, then deferred auto-config selector) is what makes 'opinionated but always overridable' work. auto-config-ordering's topological batch ordering is resolved within the run-definition-processors phase; refresh-lifecycle provides the phase, auto-configuration provides the deferred contribution.
- **conditions** — @Conditional gating is evaluated during the definition-processing phase (and frozen into R3's plan): refresh-lifecycle is where 'what could contribute' (the collected descriptors) is filtered to 'what does wire up' (the conditions verdict) before freeze and before eager instantiation. conditions defines the pure-predicate evaluation; refresh-lifecycle fixes WHEN it runs (after definitions are loaded, before freeze) so condition outcomes are settled before any instance exists.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the bring-up order a HARD-CODED fixed sequence (R1, faithful to Spring's deliberate 'fixed not a configurable pipeline' choice) or a re-orderable phase-DAG resolved by topo-sort over declared edges (R2, the cross-crate doc's ordering-as-data guidance and the unification goal §2.11)? This is a meta-architecture stance on whether the lifecycle's order is a closed, teachable, un-reorderable state machine or an open extension surface — and it cross-cuts post-processor ordering, auto-config-ordering, and whether a NEW bring-up step (web-server creation) is a core-function edit, a single on_refresh slot, or contributed data.
- How much of the lifecycle's STRUCTURE moves to BUILD/PLAN time vs runtime (§3 compile-vs-runtime + metadata/codegen keystones)? R3 freezes the phase schedule, eager set, dependency waves, and condition verdicts into a plan artifact at build time (the refreshForAotProcessing lineage — earliest diagnostic, wave-parallel no-guard instantiation, fastest startup); R1/R2 compute everything at runtime. The richest-early-diagnostic timing (§2.9), startup overhead (§2.4/§2.5), the conditional strategy, and whether mid-bring-up definition registration is even allowed all hinge on this — and deciding it here pre-judges two §3 keystones, so it must stay a CANDIDATE.
- What is the cancellation + partial-construction cleanup contract for a FAILED/cancelled refresh, given no async Drop? When a phase fails (or the bootstrap task is cancelled) after some singletons/processors with async-acquired resources were built, what mechanism guarantees explicit reverse-order async teardown of exactly the already-created beans — a structured-concurrency scope-guard, an explicit creation/dependency ledger run on the unwind path, or a drop-bomb scheduling async teardown? The all-or-nothing fail-fast invariant is only as strong as this; leaving it open risks resource leaks on the common boot-failure path. Shared with context-close and bean-instantiation.
- How is the cancelRefresh (Failed) vs context-close (Closed) distinction realized, and do they share one teardown engine? Spring keeps them deliberately different (cancelled refresh skips Lifecycle.stop and ContextClosedEvent, leaving non-active not closed) — does leaf preserve that footgun-prone difference, unify them, or make the cancelled-refresh path stop Lifecycle beans too (safer than Spring)? Cross-cuts context-close, runtime-lifecycle, and lifecycle-events (which events fire on a failed bring-up).
- Is the per-container bring-up guard (the startupShutdownLock analogue) the right concurrency model, and how does it reconcile with §2.5's no-global-lock and async cancellation of a hung/stuck bring-up? Spring uses a ReentrantLock + stuck-thread-interrupt; leaf must serialize concurrent refresh/close per container by execution context (not a process-global lock) and handle a hung bring-up via structured-concurrency timeouts/cancellation rather than thread interruption — and whether multiple containers can bring up concurrently (and share an executor) is open. Shared with the async-execution model.
- Does leaf preserve Spring's mid-bring-up definition-registration dynamism (the defensive-copy two-pass loop where an init-method/registrar can register NEW definitions during refresh) or freeze the registry at refresh? Preserving it forces the eager phase (and any creation plan) to tolerate late additions / re-planning (impossible under R3's frozen plan); freezing is simpler and AOT-aligned but a divergence. Entangled with the bean-registry mutable-vs-snapshot decision, programmatic-registration, and conditions.
- How does the dynamic two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional directory-scanned WASM-module source) reconcile with the definition-FREEZE boundary and the bring-up timing? A dynamic source contributing DEFINITIONS or PROCESSORS must register before the freeze phase / before plan computation — but R3's build-time plan has no build-time presence for a WASM source, forcing a runtime-only lane or a re-plan. How and WHEN a dynamic source feeds the fixed-order bring-up (and whether it can contribute a whole PHASE in R2) is unresolved — a CANDIDATE, not a decision. Shared with the whole discovery/SPI family.
- What is the anti-DCE self-check contract specifically for the bring-up sequence, and where does the 'expected infrastructure / expected eager-singleton-count / expected phases' manifest live? A dropped contributing crate yields missing processors/beans/phases -> a feature silently does nothing (the worst Spring 'plain BeanFactory' symptom for an unintended reason). The cross-crate doc mandates an expected-vs-found self-check at a bring-up phase; what is the canonical manifest, does it live in the facade or the generated app-root, how does it produce a rich per-source diagnostic, and does it differ across catalog substrates (linkme vs codegen vs explicit)? Cross-cuts container-core, startup-instrumentation, and the registry substrate.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated refresh section twice — the fixed-order template framing and the exact eleven-step v7 sequence (325-363: prepareRefresh through finishRefresh, the no-bean-before-all-processors central rule, the per-step semantics incl. prepareBeanFactory installing the aware-processor/resolvable-deps, the BDRPP-first/Priority-ordering in invokeBeanFactoryPostProcessors, the freeze + preInstantiateSingletons + SmartInitializing in finishBeanFactoryInitialization, and finishRefresh starting SmartLifecycle + publishing ContextRefreshedEvent), the all-or-nothing fail-fast design intent (365), the hidden concepts (367-372: SmartInitializing-outside-the-lock, the ApplicationContextAwareProcessor-is-a-BPP dogfooding, early-event buffering, and crucially the cancelRefresh-vs-doClose Failed-not-Closed distinction), the init-under-the-singleton-lock + heavy-work-belongs-after rule (374-376, 2502-2504), the startupShutdownLock provenance note (401), and the preInstantiateSingletons two-pass loop that the lifecycle drives (2466-2477, incl. the v7 background-bootstrap CompletableFuture.allOf join). I grounded T1 against the cross-crate doc's DCE layers + mandatory self-check (135-152, 345-346), the ordering-as-data/topo-sort guidance (170, 355), the four architectures A/B/C/D + identical-runtime-API-across-substrates (277-337), linkme-default + thin-descriptor (165-171), generics-cannot-register (153, 349), and the no-true-classpath/scope-discovery reality; T2 against the charter invariants (§2.3 async-first/explicit-execution-context, §2.4 reactive-hot/poll-cold, §2.5 no-global-lock/minimal-Arc, §2.9 rich-early diagnostics, §2.10 thin-macros/escape-hatch, §2.11 unification) and the stable envelope (no async Drop as a first-class constraint 273-281, AFIT/RPITIT-not-dyn-safe 160-182, trait upcasting 1.86 118-126).

CRITICAL SCOPING NOTE for Phase 2/3: this feature is the ORCHESTRATOR and overlaps post-processor-spi and bean-instantiation, which are its upstream dependencies (their designs are provided and I built on them concretely, not reinvented). I drew the boundary so they compose: post-processor-spi FIXED the processor CONTRACTS and ordering bands; bean-instantiation FIXED the per-bean creation DRIVER and creation guard. refresh-lifecycle is the FIXED-ORDER TEMPLATE and STATE MACHINE — the named phases, their order, the phase transitions, the freeze boundary placement, the no-bean-before-all-processors invariant, the eager fail-fast all-or-nothing bring-up, and the cancelRefresh (Failed) vs close (Closed) abort semantics. My three realizations therefore diverge on the SHAPE OF THE LIFECYCLE TEMPLATE ITSELF (not the processor or creation mechanics): R1 = a literal concrete async refresh() function over named phases with a typed RefreshState gating transitions (highest Spring fidelity, readable linear state machine, OnRefreshHook as the rust-native replacement for subclass override — but rigid, a new phase means editing the core fn); R2 = a re-orderable phase-DAG where the eleven steps are default contributions and ordering is data resolved by topo-sort with invariants as hard edges (maximal unification + extensibility, sort-time invariant diagnostics, shares one ordering primitive with auto-config/post-processors — but contradicts Spring's deliberate fixed-order choice and opens the order space); R3 = a build-time-frozen RefreshPlan (phase schedule + eager waves + condition verdicts, the refreshForAotProcessing lineage) executed by a thin runtime executor (earliest+richest fail-fast, wave-parallel no-guard instantiation, fastest startup, AOT-aligned — but pre-judges the §3 compile-vs-runtime keystone hardest, can't capture dynamic edges, and the WASM source has no build-time presence). R1 vs R2 differ on fixed-sequence vs re-orderable-DAG; R3 differs on runtime-computed vs build-time-frozen structure. R2's phase-DAG can host R1's order as the default resolution, and R3's plan can be computed by either; the three are genuinely divergent yet Phase-2-composable.

Three things to carry forward: (1) The single most load-bearing intent is the FIXED-ORDER, ALL-OR-NOTHING, no-bean-before-all-processors state machine — Spring's design explicitly chose fixed-over-configurable to make bring-up a predictable state machine where each step establishes the next's invariant; R2's re-orderability is the sharpest divergence from that and must be weighed against the teachability/predictability it costs. (2) The cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction (Failed state, no Lifecycle.stop, no RefreshedEvent — a half-built container never escapes) combined with NO ASYNC DROP makes the abort path a non-syntactic explicit-teardown discipline: a failed refresh must run reverse-order async cleanup of already-created beans explicitly, and this is a second teardown entry point that must stay coherent with context-close — flagged as a phase2Question. (3) The under-lock @PostConstruct trap is WORSE in async Rust (an init .await under a held bring-up/creation guard can deadlock the executor pool), so the lifecycle's guard must only sequence phases / publication, never wrap user init — heavy cross-bean work goes to the lock-free SmartInitializing callback (smart-initializing), exactly as Spring already prescribes but now load-bearing for correctness, not just performance.

I refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps the processor/bean/listener/hook population ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a WASM-module/test-double processor or on-refresh hook runs at the same fixed phase), keeps WHICH-participates as DATA (descriptors resolved at phases, never runtime instanceof — Rust has no type-walk), is compatible with cross-crate Architectures A/B/C/D, and inherits the silent-DCE self-check mandate (the bring-up sequence is the natural home for the expected-vs-found check). The two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source) is captured as multiple producers of the SAME phase-driven population, with the freeze-boundary timing reconciliation flagged as a CANDIDATE/phase2Question (sharpest under R3's build-time plan). Naming ('refresh', 'RefreshState', 'PhaseStep', 'PhaseId', 'RefreshCx', 'OnRefreshHook', 'RefreshPlan', 'RefreshError', 'freeze_definitions', 'cancelRefresh') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (325-372 the refresh fixed-order template + exact sequence + hidden concepts + cancelRefresh-vs-doClose; 374-376 init-under-the-lock; 401 startupShutdownLock provenance; 407-420 doClose/destruction ordering; 2466-2477 preInstantiateSingletons two-pass loop + background-bootstrap join; 2502-2516 init-under-lock + strict/lenient creation locking; 2682-2690 refreshForAotProcessing definition-only refresh, the R3 lineage); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. 2.3/2.5/2.9/2.10/2.11, deferred keystones §3, topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (135-152 DCE layers + self-check mandate, 165-171 linkme-default/thin-descriptor/ordering-as-data, 277-337 architectures A/B/C/D, 341-362 hard constraints incl. scope-discovery-not-global); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (118-126 trait upcasting 1.86, 160-182 RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe, 273-281 no async Drop first-class constraint).

## Bean Lifecycle Callbacks (init/destroy)  `[lifecycle-callbacks]`

Spring's lifecycle callbacks are TWO ordered, MIRRORED callback chains attached to each managed bean, plus the metadata that names them and the dedup/inference rules around them. This feature owns the CALLBACK CHAINS and their ordering — distinct from its upstream bean-instantiation (which owns the construct→expose→populate→INITIALIZE→publish SEQUENCE and the creation guard; it INVOKES this feature as the initialize phase, step (e)), distinct from aware-callbacks (the *aware group* that fires as a sub-step BEFORE init), distinct from post-processor-spi (the before-init/after-init BPP hooks that BRACKET this feature's init methods), and distinct from context-close (the teardown DRIVER that walks the registered destroyers in reverse dependency order; this feature SUPPLIES the per-bean destroyer and its internal ordering, context-close decides WHEN/IN-WHAT-ORDER across beans). The precise intent to replicate:\n\n(1) INIT ORDER, per bean, exactly: @PostConstruct → afterPropertiesSet (InitializingBean) → custom init-method/@Bean(initMethod). The annotation form is RECOMMENDED over the interface form precisely to keep beans framework-decoupled and unit-testable WITHOUT the container — a DX intent leaf must honor (a POJO/struct with a plain annotated method, not a forced trait impl).\n\n(2) DESTROY ORDER, the MIRROR: @PreDestroy → DisposableBean.destroy() → custom destroy-method/@Bean(destroyMethod), with DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE — a bean that is AutoCloseable/Closeable, or a @Bean whose type exposes a public close()/shutdown(), auto-registers that method as the destroy callback unless explicitly disabled (destroyMethod=\"\") — and the documented hazard that a close() with unrelated semantics can be silently invoked as a lifecycle hook.\n\n(3) SAME-NAME DEDUP: if one method satisfies multiple mechanisms (an init() that is both @PostConstruct and the configured init-method), it runs ONCE, not per-mechanism.\n\n(4) PROTOTYPES RUN INIT BUT ARE NEVER DESTROYED: the init chain fires on prototype creation; the destroy chain is NEVER registered or invoked — the container hands the instance off and forgets it (tracking prototypes for destruction would be the leak that contradicts hand-off-and-forget; the client owns cleanup). Init-callbacks-yes / destroy-callbacks-no is a hard contract.\n\n(5) PUBLICATION GATE: a bean is publishable to others ONLY after its init chain returns (Spring runs init UNDER the singleton creation lock and publishes after it returns). leaf must keep the after-init-then-publish discipline WITHOUT inheriting the run-init-under-a-held-guard part (in async Rust an init that .awaits under a held guard blocks/deadlocks the executor — see rustClashes), routing heavy cross-bean work to the lock-free SmartInitializing callback (smart-initializing) instead.\n\n(6) STANDALONE TEARDOWN IS EXPLICIT: destruction fires only on ctx.close()/registerShutdownHook — not on process exit. In Rust with NO async Drop this is sharper still: teardown MUST be an explicit container-driven async shutdown() (not Drop), awaited in reverse dependency order with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation (the stable-envelope recommendation). This feature defines the per-bean destroyer and its internal 3-step order; context-close owns the cross-bean reverse-order walk.\n\nleaf must replicate this INTENT — two mirrored ordered chains, the annotation-over-interface DX, same-name dedup, close/shutdown inference with its opt-out and its silent-teardown hazard, prototype init-yes-destroy-no, and the after-init publication gate — WITHOUT Spring's under-lock init (executor-blocking in async), GC-reliant forget-and-collect destruction (Rust needs an explicit async destroyer since there is no async Drop), or its assumption of reflective method discovery on a loaded class (leaf discovers callbacks via macro-emitted metadata / trait impls, origin-agnostically, so a WASM-module/test-double bean's callbacks fire identically). It must NOT pick how callbacks are DISCOVERED (annotation-metadata vs trait vs descriptor fn-pointers), the async-across-dyn boxing standard for a callback future, whether init/destroy are one fused trait or separate, the timeout/error-aggregation substrate, or whether destroy-method inference is a macro-time or runtime determination — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Two mirrored ordered callback chains as a per-bean LifecycleHooks record of erased async fn-pointers (literal Spring chain, retyped); init invoked by the creation driver, destroy registered for context-close
Mirror Spring's two chains directly as data. Each bean carries a `LifecycleHooks { init: SmallVec<[InitStep; 3]>, destroy: Option<SmallVec<[DestroyStep; 3]>> }` where each step is an ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC erased async callback: `type LifecycleFn = fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture<'_, Result<(), CallbackError>>` (or a closure capturing the typed downcast — the boxing standard is Phase 2). The chain is built once per bean definition (by the thin macro emitting the steps as a `const` array, or assembled at registry-freeze) in the FIXED order: init = [post_construct?, after_properties_set?, init_method?]; destroy = [pre_destroy?, disposable_destroy?, destroy_method_or_inferred?]. SAME-NAME DEDUP is resolved when building the chain: if two mechanisms name the same callable (same emitted fn-pointer / same method ident), the later duplicate is dropped so it runs ONCE. DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE is a chain-construction rule: if no explicit destroy-method and the bean's type implements `AutoCloseable`-analogue (a leaf `Closeable`/`Shutdownable` trait the macro can detect) OR the macro sees a public `close`/`shutdown` fn, emit that as the inferred destroy step UNLESS opt-out is set. INVOCATION: the init chain is a free async fn `run_init(bean: &dyn Any, hooks) -> Result<(), CallbackError>` that bean-instantiation calls as step (e) of creation, AFTER the aware group + before-init BPP and BEFORE the after-init BPP/publish; it awaits each step in order, fail-fast (a failed init aborts creation with a rich error). The destroy chain is NOT invoked here — instead, for SINGLETONS the driver hands context-close a registered destroyer (a closure binding the bean handle + its destroy chain) recorded in the reverse-dependency teardown ledger; context-close awaits `run_destroy` in reverse order at shutdown. For PROTOTYPES, `run_init` fires identically but NO destroyer is registered — destroy-yes/no diverges purely by whether a destroyer is recorded, so the never-destroyed contract is structural. PUBLICATION GATE: `run_init` returning Ok is the precondition the driver enforces before moving the handle into `ready`.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity: the two mirrored 3-step chains, the exact init/destroy order, same-name dedup, and close/shutdown inference all map 1:1 to Spring's documented behavior — maximally teachable and auditable, and the chain-as-data is trivially testable in isolation (build a chain, assert the step order, run it on a mock bean) without the container.; Annotation-over-interface DX (§2.1) is preserved cheaply: a step can be sourced from a plain annotated async method (macro emits a fn-pointer to it) OR a trait impl — the chain doesn't care, so a struct with `#[post_construct] async fn warm(&self)` stays a POJO unit-testable without leaf, exactly Spring's portability intent.; Prototype init-yes/destroy-no falls out as ABSENCE (register no destroyer) — the never-destroyed contract cannot be violated by a forgotten branch, and it dovetails with bean-instantiation's prototype owned-move publish.; Origin-agnostic at the only seam that matters: the callback is `fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture`, so a native bean, a WASM-module bean, and a test-double run their init/destroy through the identical chain runner — the future dynamic 'classpath' needs no special path.; Async-first by construction (§2.3): each step is an `.await`, so an init that warms a DB pool awaits instead of Spring's under-lock block; and because the chain runner holds no creation guard itself (the driver scopes the guard to publication only), the @PostConstruct-under-lock deadlock is dissolved.; Destroy as an explicit registered destroyer (not Drop) directly realizes the no-async-Drop stable alternative — reverse-order, timeout-able, observable teardown that Drop cannot give (§nightly doc), cleanly handed to context-close.
