**Cons:** The `fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture` callback boxes a future per step on a dyn boundary (alloc per callback) — cold for singletons but paid per-creation for prototypes (hot path for stateful beans), a real cost flagged for Phase 2; a monomorphized typed fast-path would need a parallel non-erased chain.; Building the chain requires KNOWING which mechanisms a bean uses (which annotations/traits) — that discovery is exactly what Phase 2's metadata/codegen boundary owns, so R1 must treat 'how the macro learns the steps' as an injected input, not solve it here (and a runtime-trait-only fallback discovers fewer steps than annotation metadata).; Destroy-method inference (does the type expose close/shutdown?) is genuinely hard to determine in a thin macro without type information — Rust macros see tokens, not resolved types, so detecting an `AutoCloseable` impl or a `close` method may require a trait-bound/blanket-impl trick or a runtime check, partially deferring the inference rule (a documented tension).; Two near-identical chain shapes (init vs destroy) that must stay mirror-aligned (same dedup logic, opposite order) is duplicated structure that can drift; and the dedup key (fn-pointer identity vs method ident vs symbol) is itself unsettled.; The destroyer closure must hold a handle to the bean across the whole container lifetime until shutdown — coupling the chain's destroy capture to the singleton-handle type (Arc<dyn> vs arena slot), which is a deferred §3 keystone this realization must keep abstract.; No async Drop means a bean whose INIT chain half-completed (step 1 acquired a resource, step 2 failed) cannot self-clean — R1 must run a compensating partial-destroy (only the destroy steps mirroring the init steps that ran) on the init-failure unwind path, a subtle mirror-bookkeeping the chain runner must own.

**Mechanisms considered:** per-bean `LifecycleHooks` record of erased async fn-pointers/closures, two SmallVec chains (init ≤3, destroy ≤3) built once in fixed order; chain-construction-time dedup (drop duplicate callable by identity) and close/shutdown inference (detect Closeable/Shutdownable trait or public close/shutdown, opt-out aware); `run_init` free async fn invoked by bean-instantiation as the initialize phase; fail-fast, publication gated on Ok; singleton: register a destroyer closure (bean handle + destroy chain) into context-close's reverse-dependency teardown ledger; prototype: register nothing; origin-agnostic `fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture` callback seam so native/WASM/test-double run identical chains; partial-destroy compensation on init-failure unwind (mirror only the init steps that ran), since no async Drop

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on HOW callbacks are discovered/collected (annotation-metadata via the thin macro, trait impls, or descriptor fn-pointers — all feed the same chain). What this feature FIXES cross-crate: the chain SHAPE (two mirrored 3-step ordered lists), the dedup rule, the inference rule, and the run_init/run_destroy runners live in leaf-core; a contributing crate's macro emits only thin `const` step descriptors (fn-pointers to the bean's own callbacks) via fully-qualified absolute paths (§cross-crate doc §99-102) into the bean's descriptor — the macro holds zero chain logic. The callback fn-pointers must be `#[used]`-reachable like any registered static, or DCE silently drops a bean whose init/destroy then never fires (cross-crate §149-152) — so the per-bean count of expected callbacks is a natural input to bean-instantiation's eager-pass anti-DCE self-check. Generics cannot register a `'static` callback symbol until concretely instantiated (§153) → the macro hard-errors on lifecycle callbacks declared on generic beans, with a per-instantiation escape hatch. The WASM-module 'classpath' contributes beans whose callback fn-pointers dispatch into the module — the chain runner can't tell them apart (CANDIDATES: a static link-time registry default PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned source both contributing the SAME chain shape — not a decision). DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE is the sharpest T1 tension: detecting close/shutdown in a thin token-only macro is hard cross-crate (no type resolution), so inference may need a runtime trait-object probe (`if let Some(c) = bean.downcast_ref::<dyn Closeable>()`) in leaf-core rather than a macro-time decision — a Phase-2 placement call. No cross-crate ABI beyond the thin step-descriptor format.

**T2 — execution context:** The INIT chain runs on bean-instantiation's COLD construction path at refresh (bootstrap task) for eager singletons, or on the get-caller's task for lazy/prototype beans; each step is an `.await`, and a step that does blocking work must use spawn_blocking (§2.3 execution-context discipline). CRITICAL: the chain runner holds NO creation guard — bean-instantiation scopes its per-bean guard to publication only, so a user init `.await` never ties up the executor under a held lock (the rust-native dissolution of Spring's @PostConstruct-under-lock trap); heavy cross-bean post-init work belongs in the lock-free SmartInitializing callback (§smart-initializing), not here. PUBLICATION: the bean is published only after run_init returns Ok (safe publication of a fully-initialized instance, §concurrency-contract). The DESTROY chain runs at shutdown on context-close's task, in REVERSE dependency order, under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean TIMEOUTS and error AGGREGATION (the no-async-Drop stable alternative) — a slow/hung destroy is bounded, not infinite. CANCELLATION: cancelling an in-flight init drops the step future; because there is no async Drop, the runner must explicitly run the compensating partial-destroy (mirror the init steps that already ran) on the unwind path so a half-initialized bean's acquired resources are released. Errors propagate as a rich CallbackError naming the bean, the failing mechanism (@PostConstruct/afterPropertiesSet/init-method or the destroy mirror), and the step index (§2.9); an init failure aborts creation/refresh, a destroy failure is aggregated (one bad destroyer must not abort teardown of the rest). PROTOTYPE: init runs on the get-caller's task, no destroyer registered → cancellation drops a half-built value with only the compensating partial-destroy to run, nothing to clean at container shutdown.

#### R2 — Lifecycle as a Phase-tagged step-graph: callbacks are typed nodes (PostConstruct/AfterPropertiesSet/InitMethod | PreDestroy/Disposable/DestroyMethod) over a single ordered enum, with destroy derived as the reverse-projection of init
Question whether init and destroy should be two independently-built chains at all. Their ORDER is a mirror and their membership is correlated (a bean with init-method X often has destroy-method Y; a Closeable bean's destroy is inferred from the same type info that found no init). Model lifecycle as ONE ordered, phase-tagged step list: `enum LifecyclePhase { PostConstruct, AfterPropertiesSet, InitMethod, ... PreDestroy, DisposableDestroy, DestroyMethod }` with a canonical total order baked into the enum's discriminant, and a bean carries `Vec<LifecycleStep { phase, callback }>`. The INIT projection is `steps.iter().filter(is_init).sorted_by(phase)`; the DESTROY projection is `steps.iter().filter(is_destroy).sorted_by(phase).rev()` — destroy order is LITERALLY the reverse-projection, so the mirror is enforced by construction, not by duplicated chain-building code. SAME-NAME DEDUP is a graph-build invariant: steps are keyed by (callback-identity), and a callable that would appear under two phases collapses to a single node tagged with the EARLIER init phase / LATER destroy phase (so dedup respects the order). DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE adds a synthetic `DestroyMethod` node when the type is Closeable/Shutdownable and none is explicit — a single graph-augmentation rule. This makes lifecycle a small, declarative DATA STRUCTURE that the macro/registry EMITS and that two thin runners (forward-init, reverse-destroy) consume. The runners are identical except for filter+direction. PROTOTYPES: the graph is built fully (init+destroy nodes) but the destroy projection is simply NEVER REQUESTED for a prototype — context-close only asks for destroy projections of registered singletons. This unifies the init/destroy machinery to one structure and one builder, and makes the whole lifecycle of a bean inspectable as a single ordered table (great for diagnostics / a 'lifecycle plan' dump).

**Pros:** Strongest unification (§2.11): ONE phase enum + ONE step list + ONE builder replaces two parallel chains; destroy order is the reverse-projection of init, so the mirror CANNOT drift — a class of bug (init/destroy order desync) is eliminated by data shape.; The whole bean lifecycle becomes a single inspectable ordered table — ideal for rich diagnostics (§2.9): a 'lifecycle plan' dump shows every callback, its phase, and its forward/reverse position, making 'why did this close() fire?' (the inference hazard) directly answerable.; Same-name dedup and close/shutdown inference are single graph-augmentation rules over one structure (collapse duplicate node; add synthetic destroy node) rather than two mirrored implementations — fewer code paths, easier to prove correct.; Phase-tagged nodes generalize cleanly: future lifecycle points (a leaf-specific 'after-aware' or scope-specific hook) are new enum variants slotting into the existing total order, without inventing a new chain — extensible without bespoke machinery.; Still origin-agnostic and async-first: each node's callback is the same erased async seam as R1; the runners await nodes in order; the structure is just a tidier carrier — so it inherits R1's WASM/test-double slot-in and no-under-lock execution.; Prototype never-destroyed is enforced by 'never request the destroy projection' — even cleaner than R1 because there is no separate destroy chain to accidentally register; the same graph serves both scopes, divergence is purely at projection time.

**Cons:** Over-structuring risk for Phase 1: a phase-enum + step-graph + projection machinery is more apparatus than the problem strictly needs (the chains are ≤3 steps each), and a single ordered enum subtly encodes ordering POLICY into a type — borders on a design commitment that a simpler two-list R1 leaves open.; Holding both init and destroy nodes in one structure for every bean (even prototypes that never destroy, even acyclic beans with one init method) is marginally more memory than R1's `destroy: Option<...>` — trivial per bean but a structure carried for the whole container lifetime.; The 'reverse-projection IS destroy order' elegance is only PER-BEAN; the cross-bean reverse-dependency destruction order is context-close's job, so the structure solves the easy half (intra-bean mirror) while the hard half (inter-bean order) lives elsewhere — risk of implying the structure does more than it does.; Dedup-with-phase-collapse (a callable that is both @PostConstruct and init-method must collapse to the earlier init phase AND, if it were also a destroy mechanism, the later destroy phase) is a fiddly invariant when one callable spans both halves — rare but the graph-build must specify it precisely.; Same boxing/dyn cost as R1 on the callback seam (the structure doesn't change the async-across-dyn standard); the typed-fast-path question is unchanged and deferred.; A single total-order enum makes the ordering authoritative in ONE place — good for coherence, but if Phase 2 later wants per-scope or pluggable ordering, the enum is a refactor point (whereas R3's strategy-per-phase absorbs it).

**Mechanisms considered:** single `LifecyclePhase` enum with canonical discriminant order; bean carries `Vec<LifecycleStep { phase, callback }>`; init = forward-filtered-sorted projection; destroy = reverse-filtered-sorted projection (mirror enforced by construction); dedup as node-collapse keyed by callback identity (earlier init phase / later destroy phase); inference as a synthetic DestroyMethod node augmentation; two thin runners (forward-init, reverse-destroy) differing only by filter+direction; lifecycle plan dump for diagnostics; prototype: build full graph, never request destroy projection (never-destroyed by omission); origin-agnostic erased async callback per node (same seam as R1)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same substrate-neutrality as R1 (annotation-metadata / trait / descriptor all feed the step list). The step-graph is an even better fit for the macro/codegen boundary: the macro can emit a `const` `&[LifecycleStep]` table per bean via absolute paths — a flat, data-only artifact (thin macro, §cross-crate §102) that a future build.rs/codegen pass could equally emit, and that is trivial to cross-check against an expected manifest for the anti-DCE self-check (§149-152: a DCE'd bean's empty step table is detectable). The single-enum total order is defined ONCE in leaf-core, so cross-crate contributors can't disagree about callback order — a coherence win across crate boundaries. Generics hard-error identically (§153). Destroy-method inference faces the SAME token-only-macro limitation as R1 (detecting Closeable cross-crate), but R2 localizes it to one graph-augmentation rule that can run at runtime over the trait object if macro-time type info is unavailable — a single clearly-placed Phase-2 call. The WASM/dynamic-source beans contribute step tables with module-dispatching callbacks, walked by the identical runners (CANDIDATES: static link-time registry + optional dynamic source, not a decision). The step-table format is the only cross-crate ABI surface — minimal and ultra-stable, as the charter wants.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical execution-context posture to R1 — init projection runs forward on the cold construction path (no creation guard held across user `.await`, heavy cross-bean work deferred to smart-initializing); destroy projection runs reversed on context-close's task under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts + error aggregation (no-async-Drop stable alternative). The phase-graph adds one execution advantage: because the forward/reverse runners are literal projections of one ordered structure, the PARTIAL-DESTROY-ON-INIT-FAILURE compensation is a natural slice — on init failure at phase N, run the destroy projection FILTERED to the mirror of phases < N, computed from the same structure (no separate bookkeeping than R1's, but derived rather than tracked). CANCELLATION drops the in-flight node future and runs the mirrored partial compensation. Errors are rich CallbackError carrying bean + phase variant + direction (§2.9); init failure aborts creation, destroy failures aggregate. Prototype init runs on the get-caller's task, destroy projection never requested → cancellation needs only the partial compensation. Safe publication after the forward init projection completes (§concurrency-contract).

#### R3 — Lifecycle as a pluggable CallbackProvider chain over origin-agnostic beans: discovery and invocation are decoupled SPIs (an ordered set of LifecycleContributors each yielding init/destroy steps for a bean), unifying annotation/trait/inferred/processor-supplied callbacks
Push unification and origin-agnosticism hardest: make WHERE callbacks come from a pluggable SPI rather than a fixed annotation+interface set, so @PostConstruct-analogue, the InitializingBean/DisposableBean trait, destroy-method inference, AND post-processor-contributed callbacks (Spring's CommonAnnotationBeanPostProcessor / DestructionAwareBeanPostProcessor are themselves BPPs that DISCOVER lifecycle methods) are all the SAME mechanism. Define `trait LifecycleContributor { fn contribute(&self, def: &MergedDef, ty: TypeView) -> LifecycleContribution; }` where a contribution yields ordered init steps and ordered destroy steps with a PRECEDENCE so the merged chain reproduces the canonical order (annotation contributor at precedence 0, interface contributor at 1, init/destroy-method contributor at 2). The container holds an ordered `Vec<Box<dyn LifecycleContributor>>` (the default set reproduces Spring's exact order; users/integrations can add one — e.g. a contributor that treats a custom `#[warmup]` annotation as an init step). MERGING the contributions produces the same two ordered chains as R1/R2, with DEDUP applied across contributors (the annotation contributor and the init-method contributor both naming `init()` → one step). DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE is just another contributor (the 'Closeable/close/shutdown' contributor) that can be enabled/disabled — making the opt-out a contributor-level toggle. INVOCATION is via two thin runners over the merged chains, identical to R1. This decouples DISCOVERY (the contributor SPI, where the metadata/codegen boundary plugs in) from INVOCATION (the runner) from STORAGE (the merged chain) — so Phase 2 can choose the discovery mechanism (annotation metadata vs trait probing) by choosing contributors, without touching the runner. It also lets post-processor-spi contribute lifecycle steps uniformly (a MergedBeanDefinitionPostProcessor-analogue is a LifecycleContributor), which is exactly how Spring wires its own @PostConstruct support — dogfooding the extension point.

**Pros:** Maximal unification + dogfooding (§2.11, §67 Spring intent): annotation callbacks, the trait callbacks, destroy-method inference, and processor-supplied callbacks are ONE contributor SPI — leaf implements its own lifecycle support as default contributors, the same way Spring's @PostConstruct is 'just a BeanPostProcessor', proving the extension point is real.; Cleanly decouples DISCOVERY from INVOCATION from STORAGE: Phase 2's deferred metadata/codegen-boundary decision becomes 'which contributors are installed' rather than a rewrite of the runner — annotation-metadata, trait-probing, or build.rs-codegen each become a contributor, A/B-able.; Most extensible DX surface (§2.1): a user/integration adds a `#[warmup]` or `@RetryInit`-style lifecycle annotation by shipping ONE contributor, without forking the core — the open/closed lifecycle vocabulary Spring's meta-annotation model gives, realized as an SPI.; Destroy-method inference and its opt-out become a contributor toggle (enable/disable the Closeable contributor) — the silent-close()-teardown hazard is mitigated by making the inference an explicit, named, disableable participant rather than a hardcoded rule.; Still origin-agnostic at invocation (same erased async callback seam) AND at discovery: a contributor for the WASM-module source yields steps that dispatch into the module — the dynamic 'classpath' plugs in as a contributor, the most natural slot-in of the three.; The merged-chain output is identical to R1/R2, so the runner, the no-under-lock execution, the reverse-order/timeout destroy, and the partial-destroy compensation are all inherited — R3 only changes how the chain is ASSEMBLED.

**Cons:** Most indirection and borders on PRE-DECIDING meta-architecture (the brief's hard guardrail): a full contributor-SPI for lifecycle discovery risks declaring 'lifecycle is an ordered pluggable contributor chain', which overlaps post-processor-spi's territory and the Phase-2 metadata/codegen-boundary decision — must be held strictly as a CANDIDATE, and its generality only earns weight if the default contributor set is invisible (at which point it resembles R1/R2, questioning the SPI tax).; The contributor SPI widens the stable cross-crate ABI (the whole workspace hard-codes paths to `LifecycleContributor`, `LifecycleContribution`, `TypeView`, precedence) — a heavier commitment than R1/R2's thin step-descriptor, a real §cross-crate cost.; `TypeView`/`MergedDef` as the contributor's input must expose enough type info for inference (does the type impl Closeable? have a close method?) — but a thin token-only macro can't resolve that, so either contributors run at runtime over the trait object (boxing/probe cost on the cold path) or the macro pre-computes a richer descriptor (pushing logic into the macro, tension with §2.10 thin macros).; Ordering across contributors (precedence) re-introduces the very ordering-coordination R2's single enum eliminated — a misordered contributor precedence silently produces wrong callback order, a subtle correctness bug behind an SPI.; Cross-contributor dedup is harder than R1/R2's single-builder dedup: two independent contributors naming the same callable must be reconciled by a shared identity key the SPI must specify — coherence Spring gets implicitly from one CommonAnnotationBeanPostProcessor is now spread across contributors.; Harder default-DX legibility (§2.10): 'find the lifecycle logic' is now 'find the installed contributors and mentally merge them', muddier than R1's readable two-list or R2's single table — hurting cargo-expand teachability.

**Mechanisms considered:** ordered `Vec<Box<dyn LifecycleContributor>>`; each contributes precedence-tagged init/destroy steps; default set reproduces Spring's canonical order; merge + cross-contributor dedup (shared callback-identity key) → the same two ordered chains as R1/R2; destroy-method inference as a disableable Closeable/close/shutdown contributor; opt-out = contributor toggle; post-processor-spi dogfooding: a MergedBeanDefinitionPostProcessor-analogue IS a LifecycleContributor (Spring's @PostConstruct-is-a-BPP pattern); two thin runners over the merged chains (forward-init / reverse-destroy), identical to R1/R2; origin-agnostic erased async callback seam at invocation; a WASM-source contributor at discovery

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Best fit for the cross-crate extension-point and two-source duality, at the cost of a wider ABI. The contributor SPI is itself a cross-crate extension surface: an integration crate ships a custom `LifecycleContributor` (e.g. a tx-aware destroy contributor) registered into the ordered set — but this WIDENS the stable-ABI commitment the workspace hard-codes paths to (`LifecycleContributor`/`LifecycleContribution`/`TypeView`/precedence), a heavier surface than R1/R2's thin step-descriptor (§cross-crate §99-102). The default contributors live in leaf-core; the macro still emits only thin per-bean metadata (which annotations were present, fn-pointers) that the annotation-contributor reads — macro logic stays thin. Anti-DCE (§149-152): a DCE'd bean contributes no metadata → no steps; the self-check counts expected lifecycle-bearing beans. Generics hard-error (§153). The two-source duality is most natural here: the static link-time registry and the optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source are TWO contributors (or two inputs to the discovery contributors) feeding the SAME merged-chain runner — NOT two code paths (CANDIDATES, not a decision). The inference contributor's need for type info is the same T1 tension as R1/R2, now localized to one contributor that can probe at runtime.

**T2 — execution context:** Contributor MERGING is synchronous cold-path work (at registry freeze / definition merge, single-threaded) — it produces the chains before any bean is built. INVOCATION is identical to R1/R2: forward init on the cold construction path with NO creation guard held across user `.await` (heavy cross-bean work to smart-initializing), reverse destroy on context-close's task under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts + error aggregation (no-async-Drop alternative). If a contributor must PROBE the trait object for inference, that probe runs at merge time (cold, sync) or lazily at first build — either way off the hot path. CANCELLATION + partial-destroy compensation inherited from R1 (run the mirror of the init steps that ran). Errors: a contributor that fails to contribute is a configuration error surfaced at freeze (rich, early — §2.9); a callback failure at invocation is a CallbackError naming bean + the contributing source + mechanism + step. Prototype: init runs on the get-caller's task, destroy chain merged but never invoked. Safe publication after forward init completes (§concurrency-contract).

### Rust clashes
- @PostConstruct/init running UNDER the singleton creation lock is an EXECUTOR-blocking hazard in async Rust. Spring runs the init chain inside the creation lock and publishes only after it returns — guaranteeing field visibility but inviting deadlock when init touches other beans. In async Rust an init future that `.await`s while a guard is held ties up an executor worker and can deadlock the whole pool. leaf must KEEP the after-init-then-publish gate (visibility) but must NOT run the init chain under any held guard: the chain runner holds no lock (bean-instantiation scopes its per-bean guard to publication only), and heavy cross-bean post-init work moves to the lock-free SmartInitializing callback (§smart-initializing). The trap is dissolved, not inherited.
- NO async Drop means @PreDestroy/destroy CANNOT be a Drop impl — destruction must be an explicit container-driven async `shutdown()`/destroy chain, awaited in reverse dependency order with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation (the stable-envelope's explicit recommendation, strictly better than Drop: deterministic order, timeouts, observability). This is the single biggest shape divergence from Spring's GC-reliant 'forget and let finalizers/close run' model. The destroy chain this feature defines is invoked by context-close, never by Drop; a bean wanting cleanup implements the leaf destroy callback, not Drop (Drop may still run for sync best-effort, but the AUTHORITATIVE async teardown is the chain).
- A half-completed INIT chain cannot self-clean on the unwind path (no async Drop). If init step 1 acquires a resource and step 2 fails or is cancelled, the partially-initialized bean's resource leaks unless the runner explicitly runs a COMPENSATING partial-destroy — the mirror of only the init steps that already ran. Spring relies on GC to collect the failed bean; leaf must run async compensation deterministically. This makes the init/destroy mirror load-bearing not just for ordering but for failure recovery, and means the chain runner must track (or derive, R2) how far init progressed.
- Destroy-method INFERENCE (auto-detect public close()/shutdown() or Closeable/AutoCloseable) is hard for a thin token-only macro that sees no resolved types. Spring inspects the loaded class reflectively; leaf macros see tokens. Detecting that a bean's type implements a `Closeable` trait or exposes a `close` method may require a runtime trait-object probe (`downcast_ref::<dyn Closeable>`) in leaf-core rather than a macro-time decision, OR a blanket-impl/marker-trait trick — and the silent-teardown hazard (a `close()` with unrelated semantics fired as a lifecycle hook) is WORSE in Rust if inference fires on any `close`-named method without the developer realizing. The opt-out (destroyMethod="") and a clear diagnostic ('leaf will call X.close() at shutdown; annotate #[no_destroy_inference] to suppress') are mandatory.
- Annotation-over-interface DX requires that a plain annotated async METHOD (not a forced trait impl) can be a lifecycle callback, keeping the bean a unit-testable POJO. Spring achieves this via reflective method invocation; Rust has no reflection. leaf must let the thin macro emit a fn-pointer/closure to the bean's own `#[post_construct] async fn` so the struct stays free of leaf traits — but this means the callback seam is `fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture` (erased + boxed) rather than a typed trait method, paying an alloc per callback. The trait form (InitializingBean/DisposableBean analogue) is the typed escape hatch; the framework must support BOTH without forcing the trait, exactly Spring's portability intent.
- Prototype init-yes / destroy-no maps BETTER to Rust than to Spring. Spring runs init, never destroy, and forgets the instance (GC reclaims it). In Rust, the prototype is an OWNED MOVE to the caller — the container registers NO destroyer, so the never-destroyed contract is structural (the container retains nothing to destroy), and the client's own Drop / explicit cleanup handles teardown. The clash is only that the SAME init chain must fire for prototype and singleton while the destroy chain is registered for one and not the other — so the divergence is at destroyer-REGISTRATION (a singleton-only step), not in the chain definition.
- Async callbacks across a dyn boundary force boxed futures (AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe per the capability envelope), so EACH lifecycle callback on the dyn path allocates a future. Cold for singleton init/destroy (once at boot/shutdown), but a prototype's init chain runs per-resolution on a potentially hot path — the per-callback box-alloc is a real cost. The async-across-dyn boxing standard (boxed-on-dyn-path with a monomorphized typed fast-path as escape, or vice versa) is a deferred Phase-2 keystone shared with bean-instantiation; this feature must keep the callback seam abstract.
- Same-name DEDUP needs a stable callback IDENTITY in Rust. Spring dedups by reflective Method identity. leaf has fn-pointers (comparable but with monomorphization/codegen-unit caveats), method idents (macro-visible but not unique across the chain sources), or symbol names — none as clean as a JVM Method. The dedup key (fn-pointer equality vs emitted ident vs an explicit step-id the macro assigns) is unsettled and must be specified so an init() that is both @PostConstruct and init-method runs exactly once.
- Cancellation of an in-flight init or destroy chain has no async-Drop safety net. A cancelled init must trigger the compensating partial-destroy; a cancelled destroy (e.g. shutdown timeout elapsed mid-chain) must be bounded and the partial state logged — Spring's JVM model has no equivalent because it doesn't cancel synchronous lifecycle methods. The structured-concurrency scope + per-bean timeout that context-close uses must define what 'a destroy that timed out' leaves behind (resource possibly not released) and surface it as a rich diagnostic rather than silently swallowing it.

### Interactions
- **bean-instantiation** — PRIMARY UPSTREAM DRIVER. bean-instantiation's per-bean creation sequence INVOKES this feature as the INITIALIZE phase (step e): after the aware group + before-init BPP, before the after-init BPP/publish. This feature OWNS the ordered init chain (@PostConstruct → afterPropertiesSet → init-method) and its dedup; bean-instantiation owns when it runs in the sequence and enforces the publication gate (publish only after run_init returns Ok). For singletons, the driver records this feature's destroyer into the teardown ledger; for prototypes it records nothing. The no-under-held-guard discipline is a shared contract: the driver must not hold its per-bean creation guard across this feature's init `.await`s.
- **context-close** — PRIMARY DOWNSTREAM CONSUMER. This feature DEFINES the per-bean destroy chain (@PreDestroy → DisposableBean.destroy → destroy-method/inferred) and its internal 3-step order; context-close OWNS the cross-bean reverse-dependency walk, the stop-Lifecycle-then-destroy sequencing, the structured-concurrency scope, per-bean timeouts, and error aggregation. Because there is no async Drop, the destroyer this feature supplies is an explicit async callback context-close awaits in reverse order — not a Drop. The partial-destroy-on-init-failure compensation also runs this feature's destroy chain (filtered to the init steps that ran).
- **scopes** — POLICY SIBLING. scopes fixes per-scope lifetime: singleton = init + destroy-at-close; prototype = init-only, NEVER destroyed; web/custom = init + destruction-callback-on-scope-end (Spring's Scope.registerDestructionCallback). This feature realizes those policies: the SAME init chain fires for all scopes, but the destroy chain is REGISTERED only where the scope destroys (singleton → context-close ledger; custom scope → the scope's registerDestructionCallback equivalent; prototype → registered nowhere). The never-destroyed contract is the prototype scope's policy enforced structurally here by registering no destroyer.
- **aware-callbacks** — ADJACENT-BEFORE. The aware group (bean name, factory, environment, context, event publisher) fires as a GROUP BEFORE this feature's init chain — Spring drives the context-level aware group via a single ApplicationContextAwareProcessor (a BPP) so it lands between before-init BPP and @PostConstruct. This feature's init chain starts AFTER the aware group completes; the two are ordered neighbours in bean-instantiation's initialize phase, not the same mechanism.
- **post-processor-spi** — BRACKETING + (in R3) SOURCE. The before-init BPP runs immediately before this feature's init chain; the after-init BPP (where AOP proxies attach) runs immediately after it — so this feature's init chain is bracketed by the two BPP hooks. In Spring, @PostConstruct/@PreDestroy support is ITSELF a BPP (CommonAnnotationBeanPostProcessor) and destroy callbacks come via DestructionAwareBeanPostProcessor; R3 makes this dogfooding explicit (a LifecycleContributor can be a processor). The eager pass runs only after all BPPs are registered (refresh ordering), so callback discovery is settled before any init fires.
- **smart-initializing** — RELIEF VALVE. smart-initializing's single lock-free callback fires AFTER every eager singleton's init chain has completed — it is the sanctioned home for heavy cross-bean post-init work that must NOT run inside this feature's @PostConstruct-analogue (which in async Rust would block the executor / touch a partially-built graph). This feature provides the per-bean init that completes before that boundary; smart-initializing provides the after-all-exist callback. The split (cheap per-bean init here, expensive cross-bean work there) is the rust-native dissolution of Spring's under-lock-init trap.
- **factory-bean** — LIFECYCLE-BEARING + the orphaned-product hazard. A FactoryBean is itself a bean with its own init/destroy chain; its PRODUCT may also have lifecycle. Spring orphans the product (does NOT auto-call product.close(); the factory must implement DisposableBean and delegate). With no async Drop and container-driven reverse-order shutdown, an orphaned product holding async-cleanup resources is a leak — so whether the product's destroy chain rides the factory's registered destroyer (more disciplined) or stays orphaned (Spring-faithful) is a shared phase2Question. This feature defines both the factory's and (if registered) the product's chains; factory-bean decides the product's teardown ownership.
- **configuration-classes** — CALLBACK SOURCE. @Bean(initMethod=...)/@Bean(destroyMethod=...) name lifecycle callbacks on factory-method-produced beans, and @Bean destroy-method INFERENCE (auto-detect close/shutdown) applies here specifically. This feature consumes those names/inference flags to build the chains for @Bean-style beans; configuration-classes supplies the metadata. The destroyMethod="" opt-out is a configuration-classes attribute this feature must honor.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — DISTINCT-BUT-ORDERED. runtime-lifecycle (start/stop/isRunning, phased) is the SEPARATE running-state machine layered on top of the static graph — NOT init/destroy. The relationship is sequencing at shutdown: context-close stops Lifecycle beans (runtime-lifecycle) FIRST to quiesce activity, THEN runs this feature's destroy chains. Spring is explicit they are different concerns (build graph → finalize → RUN → stop → destroy); this feature must not conflate its destroy chain with runtime-lifecycle's stop.
- **concurrency-contract** — PUBLICATION SAFETY. The publication gate this feature enforces (publish only after the init chain returns Ok) is the concrete realization of safe-publication-of-fully-initialized-singletons — visibility of a fully-initialized instance across threads, not runtime method thread-safety. concurrency-contract frames the doctrine; this feature is where 'fully initialized' is defined (init chain complete) and bean-instantiation is where the move-into-ready happens.
- **bean-definition** — METADATA UPSTREAM. The merged definition carries the init-method/destroy-method names, the destroy-inference opt-out flag, and (via the bean's type) the basis for trait/Closeable detection. This feature reads the merged def to BUILD the chains; bean-definition fixes the metadata record and freeze boundary. The annotation-vs-interface-vs-name discovery is where the metadata/codegen boundary (Phase 2) plugs into chain construction.
- **component-stereotypes** — INDIRECT — the bean-ness that makes a struct eligible for lifecycle callbacks comes from being a managed component; the lifecycle annotations (#[post_construct]/#[pre_destroy] analogues) attach to such beans. This feature fires for any managed bean regardless of stereotype; the stereotype family just determines membership, not callback behavior.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- How are lifecycle callbacks DISCOVERED — annotation-metadata emitted by the thin macro (a fn-pointer per #[post_construct]/#[pre_destroy] method), a forced trait impl (InitializingBean/DisposableBean analogue), runtime trait-object probing, or build.rs codegen — and is it a blend? This is the metadata/codegen-boundary keystone (§3) viewed through lifecycle: annotation-metadata gives the best DX (POJO stays leaf-trait-free, Spring's portability intent) but needs the macro to emit reachable callback statics; trait impls are typed/zero-erasure but force the bean to depend on leaf. Shared with bean-definition and the whole annotation-model.
- Is the lifecycle callback seam an erased `fn(&dyn Any) -> BoxFuture` (origin-agnostic, WASM/test-double ready, but boxes a future per callback — a cost on the prototype hot path) or a monomorphized typed trait method (zero-cost but not origin-agnostic), with the other as an escape hatch? This is the async-across-dyn boxing standard (§3 async-execution model) viewed through callbacks. Shared with bean-instantiation and scopes.
- Where does DESTROY-METHOD INFERENCE (detect public close()/shutdown() or Closeable/AutoCloseable) get DECIDED — at macro time (impossible for a thin token-only macro without type resolution), via a runtime trait-object probe in leaf-core, or via a marker-trait/blanket-impl trick — and how is the silent-teardown hazard (a close() with unrelated semantics fired as a lifecycle hook) surfaced as a loud, opt-out-able diagnostic? Cross-cuts the thin-macro invariant (§2.10) and the type-erasure substrate.
- What is the partial-construction / partial-init CLEANUP contract given no async Drop: when an init chain fails or is cancelled at step N, what mechanism guarantees the mirror destroy of steps < N runs (the compensating partial-destroy), and how does this compose with bean-instantiation's broader partial-construction unwind (factory already acquired a resource before init even started)? Shared with context-close, bean-instantiation, and the async-execution model — leaving it open risks resource leaks on the common boot-failure path.
- Is lifecycle ONE fixed mechanism (R1/R2 — a built-in set of recognized init/destroy sources) or a pluggable LifecycleContributor SPI (R3 — discovery decoupled from invocation, post-processors and integrations contribute callbacks)? The SPI maximizes unification/dogfooding and extensibility but widens the stable cross-crate ABI and borders on pre-deciding the meta-architecture. Shared with post-processor-spi and the metadata/codegen boundary.
- What is the stable IDENTITY used for same-name DEDUP across callback sources — fn-pointer equality (codegen-unit caveats), the emitted method ident, an explicit macro-assigned step-id, or a symbol — so a method serving multiple mechanisms runs exactly once? Depends on the discovery mechanism and the type-erasure substrate.
- FactoryBean product teardown ownership: does the product's destroy chain ride the factory's registered destroyer (disciplined, no orphaned-resource leak) or stay orphaned per Spring (the factory must delegate)? With no async Drop and container-driven reverse-order shutdown, orphaning is leak-prone. Shared with factory-bean and context-close.
- The structured-concurrency scope, per-bean TIMEOUT, and ERROR-AGGREGATION substrate for the destroy walk (the no-async-Drop stable alternative): what bounds a hung destroy, what does a timed-out destroy leave behind (possibly-unreleased resource), and how are aggregated destroy failures reported richly rather than swallowed? Primarily context-close's, but this feature's per-bean destroy chain is the unit it operates on.
- The two-source duality for callback discovery: a static link-time registry (default) AND an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source both contributing beans whose init/destroy chains fire identically — how do dynamically-sourced beans' callbacks register their destroyers into the (possibly already-planned) teardown order? A CANDIDATE, not a decision; shared with the discovery/SPI family and context-close.

What is UNIQUELY this feature's: the TWO MIRRORED ORDERED CHAINS and their internal 3-step order, same-name dedup, destroy-method close/shutdown inference + opt-out, the annotation-over-interface DX, the after-init publication gate's DEFINITION of 'fully initialized', and the partial-destroy-on-init-failure compensation. My three realizations diverge on the SHAPE OF THE CALLBACK MODEL (not the sequence, not the cross-bean order): R1 = two literal mirrored chains of erased async fn-pointers (highest Spring fidelity, simplest, dovetails with the driver; cons: parallel-chain drift risk, inference-in-thin-macro hard); R2 = one phase-tagged ordered step-graph with destroy as the reverse-PROJECTION of init (strongest unification — mirror can't drift, single inspectable lifecycle table for diagnostics; cons: more apparatus, encodes ordering into a type); R3 = a pluggable LifecycleContributor SPI decoupling discovery/invocation/storage (maximal unification + dogfooding + extensibility + best WASM slot-in; cons: most indirection, widens the stable ABI, borders on pre-deciding meta-architecture). R1↔R2 differ on two-chains vs one-projected-graph; R3 differs on fixed vs pluggable-discovery. All three EMIT the identical merged-chain runner, so they are Phase-2-composable (R2's graph can back R1's two views; R3's contributors can produce either).\n\nThree things to carry forward: (1) NO ASYNC DROP is the dominant clash — destroy MUST be an explicit container-driven async chain (not Drop), and a half-completed INIT chain needs a compensating partial-destroy on the unwind path; both are surfaced as phase2Questions and as the reason this feature's mirror is load-bearing for FAILURE RECOVERY, not just ordering. (2) The under-lock-init trap becomes an executor-blocking hazard — the chain runner holds NO guard across user `.await`s (bean-instantiation scopes the guard to publication only), heavy cross-bean work goes to smart-initializing; this is the rust-native dissolution, not inheritance. (3) Destroy-method INFERENCE (close/shutdown) is the sharpest thin-macro tension — a token-only macro can't resolve types, so inference likely needs a runtime trait-object probe, and the silent-teardown hazard is WORSE in Rust and demands a loud opt-out diagnostic.\n\nI refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps the callback seam abstract (erased-async vs typed-trait is a deferred keystone), keeps discovery abstract (annotation-metadata vs trait vs codegen — the metadata/codegen boundary), keeps the bean handle abstract (the §3 ownership keystone the destroyer captures), keeps destroy-inference placement open, and keeps the timeout/aggregation substrate as context-close's. The two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM source) is captured as multiple producers of beans whose callbacks fire identically — a CANDIDATE, most natural in R3. Naming ('LifecycleHooks', 'InitStep'/'DestroyStep', 'LifecyclePhase', 'LifecycleContributor', 'CallbackError', 'Closeable'/'Shutdownable', 'run_init'/'run_destroy', 'destroyer') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. ## Aware Callbacks  `[aware-callbacks]`

Spring's `Aware` family injects CONTAINER INFRASTRUCTURE into the rare beans that genuinely need to reach back into the container, fired as a GROUP at a fixed point in initialization (after property population, before `@PostConstruct`/init). Two precise tiers to replicate (intent, not mechanism): (1) the FACTORY-TIER three — `BeanNameAware` → `BeanClassLoaderAware` → `BeanFactoryAware` — knowable by ANY bare bean factory with zero context infrastructure, so `invokeAwareMethods` hardwires them; (2) the CONTEXT-TIER group of seven — `EnvironmentAware` → `EmbeddedValueResolverAware` → `ResourceLoaderAware` → `ApplicationEventPublisherAware` → `MessageSourceAware` → `ApplicationStartupAware` → `ApplicationContextAware` — which are NOT invoked one-by-one by the factory but are ALL driven by a single `BeanPostProcessor` (`ApplicationContextAwareProcessor`) whose `postProcessBeforeInitialization` invokes the seven in order, so they fire as a group and are physically a sub-step of the first before-init pass. THREE design-intent points to replicate: (a) DOGFOODING — the framework injects its own context callbacks through the SAME public BPP extension point it asks third parties to use (post-processor-spi), so the minimal bean-kernel stays decoupled from the context-services layer; the rejected alternative (branching inside `initializeBean` per context interface) would couple the two modules. (b) The CLEAN SPLIT between callback-injection and dependency-injection of the SAME objects: `prepareBeanFactory` calls `ignoreDependencyInterface(...)` for the awares (so a `setApplicationContext` setter is NOT treated as an autowire injection point) while ALSO `registerResolvableDependency(...)` making `ApplicationContext`/`BeanFactory`/`ResourceLoader`/`ApplicationEventPublisher` injectable BY TYPE — two doors to the same infrastructure. (c) PHILOSOPHY — Aware ties code to the framework API and INVERTS IoC (the bean reaches into the container instead of being given collaborators), so it is reserved for INFRASTRUCTURE beans (BPPs, registrars, selectors, auto-proxy creators) and application beans are steered toward constructor injection (or, when truly necessary, autowiring the context by type). A related nuance: `ImportSelector`/`ImportBeanDefinitionRegistrar`/`BeanRegistrar` may obtain `Environment`/`BeanFactory`/`ClassLoader`/`ResourceLoader` EITHER by implementing the matching Aware OR by declaring a single constructor with those parameter types, and the Aware callbacks fire BEFORE `registerBeanDefinitions()`. leaf must replicate this INTENT — a fixed-timing, group-fired provision of container infrastructure to infrastructure beans, dogfooded through the same instance-decoration extension point, with the callback-vs-by-type duality, the infrastructure-only steering, and origin-agnostic provision (a WASM-module/test-double bean must receive the same infrastructure handles through the identical seam) — WITHOUT Spring's setter-mutation idiom, its under-the-singleton-lock invocation, its ten-interface taxonomy as ABI, or its assumption that the bean is a mutable JVM object reached after `new`. It must NOT pick which handle TYPE is injected (the deferred scope/ownership keystone), whether provision is a setter-style post-construction mutation or a pre-construction context argument, the dyn/boxing standard for the callback surface, or the cross-crate substrate by which the infrastructure processor is discovered — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Literal trait-tier split: a factory-tier hardwired aware step + a context-tier group fired by one dogfooded BPP-analogue (Spring-faithful, Rust-retyped to async setters)
Mirror Spring's two-tier taxonomy directly. Define small marker traits — factory-tier `BeanNameAware { fn set_bean_name(&self, name: &BeanName); }`, `BeanFactoryAware { fn set_bean_factory(&self, bf: &dyn BeanFactory); }` (the ClassLoaderAware analogue is dropped — no Rust classloader; see rustClashes) — and context-tier `EnvironmentAware`, `EmbeddedValueResolverAware`, `ResourceLoaderAware`, `EventPublisherAware`, `MessageSourceAware`, `StartupAware`, `ContextAware`, each a one-method trait taking a borrowed infrastructure handle. Because Rust beans are typically already constructed (often behind a shared handle) by the time init runs, the 'setter' is `&self` + interior provision (a `OnceCell`/`OnceLock` field the macro can emit, OR an async method `async fn set_x(&self, x: &Handle)` so a bean that does async work on receipt awaits) — the exact provision shape is a Phase-2 call (see phase2Questions). The FACTORY tier is hardwired into bean-instantiation's initialize phase as a fixed sub-step `invoke_aware_factory_tier(bean, ctx)` that the bare engine can run with ZERO context infrastructure (it only knows the bean's name and the factory handle). The CONTEXT tier is a single dogfooded infrastructure bean — `ContextAwareProcessor`, a `role=Infrastructure` BPP-analogue (post-processor-spi) auto-detected by the context façade (container-core) and registered ahead of any application bean — whose before-init hook downcasts/queries the bean for each of the seven context-tier traits and invokes them IN ORDER. So on a bare engine the context awares simply never fire (the processor isn't present) — exactly Spring's 'features silently inert without auto-detection' semantic, made structural. The macro emits, per `#[bean]`, a tiny const flag-set on the descriptor saying which aware traits this concrete type implements (so the processor does a cheap flag check, not a speculative downcast per trait). The duality is honored explicitly: implementing an Aware trait does NOT make that infrastructure an autowire injection point (the resolver, autowiring-resolution, ignores aware-trait methods), while the same handles remain resolvable BY TYPE (`get::<Environment>()` works) — two doors, one infrastructure.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity: the factory-tier-on-the-bare-engine vs context-tier-via-a-dogfooded-BPP split, the fixed group-before-init timing, and the dogfooding through post-processor-spi all map 1:1 to Spring — maximally teachable, and the 'bare engine has no context awares' behaviour falls out of the processor's absence exactly as Spring intends.; Dogfooding is literal (charter §2.11 unification): the context-tier group is ONE infrastructure bean built on the same BPP-analogue extension point users get, so the bean-kernel stays decoupled from the context-services layer and adding/replacing the aware set is the ordinary processor mechanism — no bespoke branch in initialize.; Cheap dispatch: the per-descriptor flag-set lets the processor skip beans that implement no context-tier trait (the overwhelming common case) with one bitmask test — honoring low-overhead (§2.1 #2) without speculative per-trait downcasts.; Clean callback-vs-by-type split is expressible (charter-aligned): aware traits are NOT injection points (autowiring-resolution ignores them) yet `get::<Environment>()` still resolves — the two-door design is preserved as Rust trait-impl-vs-type-query rather than Spring's ignoreDependencyInterface/registerResolvableDependency pair.; Origin-agnostic: the processor queries traits / a descriptor flag-set, never how the bean was constructed, so a WASM-module or test-double bean receives the same handles through the identical seam.; Infrastructure-only steering is enforceable as a diagnostic: implementing a context-tier Aware on a non-`role=Infrastructure` bean can warn (rich-diagnostics §2.9), nudging app beans to constructor injection exactly as Spring steers.

**Cons:** The ten-interface taxonomy is a lot of surface to port as ten one-method traits; some (EmbeddedValueResolverAware, StartupAware) may be premature before value-injection/startup-instrumentation exist, risking dead trait surface (mitigated by introducing tiers incrementally, but a coherence burden).; The '&self setter that mutates an already-shared bean' is the core impedance mismatch: providing infrastructure AFTER construction into a bean that may already be `Arc`-shared forces interior mutability (a OnceCell field per aware) or an async-setter idiom — neither is Spring's plain `set` and both leak provision mechanics into the bean (fights §2.5 minimal-state and the POJO ideal).; Downcast-to-trait on a type-erased bean handle needs either trait-upcasting from a common `Bean` supertrait (requires every aware-bean's stored handle to be `dyn Bean`, constraining the registry substrate) or per-trait `Any` gymnastics — couples this feature to the deferred type-erasure substrate.; Two distinct firing sites (factory-tier inline in bean-instantiation; context-tier inside one BPP) must stay timing-coherent (both strictly before init, factory-tier strictly before context-tier) — a sequencing contract spread across two owners that can drift.; Async-setter awares run during initialize; if init runs under any per-bean creation guard, an aware `.await` under a held guard is the same executor-blocking hazard as @PostConstruct-under-lock (rustClashes) — the timing must keep awares off any held guard.

**Mechanisms considered:** small per-tier marker traits (factory-tier hardwired; context-tier queried) with borrowed-handle methods; provision as `&self`+OnceCell or `async fn set_x`; factory tier as a fixed inline sub-step in bean-instantiation's initialize (runs on the bare engine, zero context infra); context tier as ONE dogfooded `role=Infrastructure` BPP-analogue (post-processor-spi) auto-detected by the context façade (container-core); per-descriptor const flag-set (macro-emitted) marking which aware traits a concrete type implements → cheap bitmask skip; trait-upcasting from a common `Bean` supertrait (stable 1.86) to query aware-trait impls without per-trait Any downcasts; callback-vs-by-type duality: autowiring-resolution ignores aware methods as injection points; handles stay resolvable by type

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on collection — the factory tier needs no registry at all (it runs inline in the initialize sequence with data the engine already has: the bean name + factory handle). The CONTEXT tier's processor is the only cross-crate concern: it is a `role=Infrastructure` bean that the context façade must auto-detect, so it inherits the silent-DCE hazard of whatever discovery substrate Phase 2 picks (linkme distributed-slice / build.rs codegen / explicit) — the façade's expected-vs-found infrastructure self-check (cross-crate doc §152) must include the ContextAwareProcessor, so a DCE'd context layer that drops the processor becomes a LOUD boot error ('context awares will not fire') rather than a silently-inert one. The per-descriptor aware-flag-set is a thin const the macro emits via absolute paths (`::leaf_core::AwareFlags`) — zero macro logic (§2.10). Generics cannot be flagged/registered (no `'static` symbol until concretely instantiated → the descriptor names concrete types only; macro hard-errors on a generic aware-bean with a per-instantiation escape hatch). The WASM-module 'classpath' slots in as more flagged descriptors whose beans the processor queries identically. CANDIDATES (not decisions): a static link-time registry default PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned source, both contributing aware-capable beans the SAME processor walks. The ten aware traits themselves live in leaf-core (a small, stable ABI the macro and downstream infra crates hard-code paths to), so widening the aware set is a leaf-core semver event.

**T2 — execution context:** Both tiers run during the per-bean INITIALIZE phase at REFRESH on the bootstrap task (cold path), strictly AFTER property population and strictly BEFORE init/@PostConstruct-analogue — the factory tier first, then the context tier (the dogfooded processor's before-init hook). Provision handles are BORROWED for the duration of the call (the bean copies/clones what it needs into its OnceCell); the infrastructure (Environment, EventPublisher, ResourceLoader, the context itself) outlives every bean, so the borrow is sound. If awares are async (`async fn set_x`), they are `.await` points that MUST NOT run under a held per-bean creation guard (bean-instantiation keeps the guard to publication only) — an aware that does I/O on receipt must use spawn_blocking if it blocks (§2.3). CANCELLATION: cancelling refresh mid-aware drops a partially-provisioned bean; because there is no async Drop, a bean that acquired an async-cleanup resource inside an async aware must rely on the driver's explicit unwind ledger (shared with bean-instantiation/context-close), not Drop. ERRORS: an aware that fails (e.g. validates its received Environment and rejects it) propagates a rich ResolveError carrying the bean name, which aware was firing, and what it expected (§2.9); aware failure aborts that bean's creation and thus refresh (all-or-nothing). The factory tier never blocks meaningfully (it copies a name / clones a factory handle). No global lock; the awares introduce no new synchronization — they read infrastructure that is already safely published.

#### R2 — Collapse the taxonomy: ONE `ContextInfrastructure` bundle delivered as a context argument, awares become read methods on it (provision-at-the-construction-seam, not setters)
Question whether Rust needs ten Aware interfaces — or even a 'setter callback that mutates a built bean' — at all. Spring's taxonomy exists because Java had only interfaces to express 'I want THIS one piece of infrastructure' and only setter injection to deliver it into an already-`new`'d object. leaf's intent (give infrastructure beans access to container infrastructure at a fixed early point) is served by ONE bundle handed to beans that want it, at the SEAM where Rust beans are actually born: construction. Define a single `ContextInfra<'a>` (or an owned `Arc<ContextHandle>`) exposing `bean_name()`, `factory() -> &dyn BeanFactory`, `environment() -> &Environment`, `resource_loader() -> &dyn ResourceLoader`, `events() -> &dyn EventPublisher`, `messages() -> &dyn MessageSource`, `context() -> &dyn ApplicationContext`, `value_resolver()`, `startup()` — i.e. the ten awares become READ methods on one capability object (so 'EnvironmentAware' is just `infra.environment()`). Delivery is the ImportAware/single-constructor idiom GENERALIZED (the Spring nuance that selectors/registrars get infra via a constructor param): a bean factory whose recipe declares a `ContextInfra` parameter receives it at construction — provision-by-injection rather than provision-by-mutation, which is idiomatic Rust (collaborators are part of identity) and dissolves the interior-mutability problem. For beans that genuinely must receive infrastructure AFTER property population but before init (Spring's exact Aware timing, e.g. a bean that needs its OWN name which isn't known until registration), an optional `async fn on_context_ready(&self, infra: &ContextInfra)` single hook fires the bundle once, as a group, at the Aware timing slot — one method instead of seven context-tier interfaces. The factory-tier name/factory are just fields on the bundle. The infrastructure-only steering becomes structural: only beans that ASK for `ContextInfra` (a constructor param or the one hook) couple to it; everyone else stays a clean POJO with constructor-injected collaborators — Spring's 'steer app beans away from Aware' enforced by making the coupling opt-in and visible in the signature.

**Pros:** Dramatically less surface: one capability bundle + one optional hook replaces ten interfaces — far lower DX ceremony (§2.1 #1) and no premature traits (StartupAware/EmbeddedValueResolverAware) before their features exist; the bundle simply grows accessor methods as neighbours land.; Provision-at-construction is idiomatic Rust and dissolves the central impedance mismatch: a `ContextInfra` constructor param means NO interior mutability, NO setter-on-a-shared-bean, NO OnceCell — collaborators (incl. infrastructure) are part of the bean's identity, exactly the constructor-injection style Spring itself steers toward (injection-styles).; Infrastructure-only steering is enforced by the type system: coupling to the container is VISIBLE as a `ContextInfra` parameter or the `on_context_ready` impl — an app bean that takes neither provably cannot reach into the container, a stronger guarantee than Spring's convention.; Generalizes the real Spring nuance (selectors/registrars get infra via a single constructor) into the primary delivery mechanism — honoring intent-not-mechanism parity (§2.2) by realizing the WANT (early infrastructure access) the rust-native way rather than porting the setter taxonomy.; One grouped hook fires the whole bundle at the Aware timing slot, trivially preserving 'fired as a group before init' with a single, legible callback instead of an ordered seven-interface dance.; The bundle is a natural origin-agnostic capability handle (a WASM/test-double bean takes the same `ContextInfra`), and a test can hand-construct a `ContextInfra` to unit-test an infrastructure bean WITHOUT the container — strong testability.

**Cons:** Lower literal parity with Spring's taxonomy: practitioners porting mental models lose the per-interface landmarks (BeanNameAware, ResourceLoaderAware…); the intent-parity argument says that's fine, but it's a teaching/migration cost and a divergence to defend.; A wide `ContextInfra` capability object risks becoming a god-handle that beans over-reach into (grabbing the whole context when they needed only the Environment), eroding the fine-grained 'I need exactly THIS' signal Spring's narrow interfaces gave and weakening the IoC discipline.; Construction-time delivery doesn't fit beans that need infrastructure only knowable AFTER property population (notably their own registered bean-name, or a value resolver configured by other BFPPs) — those still need the post-population `on_context_ready` hook, so the 'one clean seam' splits into two delivery modes anyway.; Passing `ContextInfra<'a>` by borrow into a constructor entangles the bean's construction with the context's lifetime; if the bean must store any of it, it forces cloning `Arc` handles out of the bundle (heap churn the bundle hoped to avoid) or holding a borrow that fights the singleton-handle ownership model (deferred §3).; 'Does this bean want infra?' must be visible to the creation driver (bean-instantiation) so it knows to pass the bundle / call the hook — needs a descriptor flag anyway, so some of R1's per-descriptor metadata returns.; One bundle method exposing `factory()`/`context()` re-opens the by-type-vs-callback duality as a single object: it's now easy to call `infra.factory().get::<T>()` and smear service-location across business code — the very anti-pattern Spring's narrow awares + steering discouraged.

**Mechanisms considered:** single `ContextInfra<'a>` capability bundle (the ten awares as read accessors) delivered as a construction-seam parameter (generalizing the ImportAware single-constructor idiom); optional single grouped hook `async fn on_context_ready(&self, infra: &ContextInfra)` firing the bundle at the Aware timing slot for post-population needs; descriptor flag 'wants ContextInfra' so the creation driver knows to pass it / call the hook; owned `Arc<ContextHandle>` vs borrowed `&ContextInfra<'a>` as the bundle shape (ownership deferred to §3); infrastructure-only steering enforced structurally (coupling visible in the signature) rather than by convention + a non-infra warning

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Simplest cross-crate story of the three: there is no dedicated context-aware PROCESSOR to discover (delivery is either a construction param the recipe already declares, or one grouped hook the creation driver calls) — so the silent-DCE surface shrinks to 'is the bean's descriptor present', which is the same discovery concern every bean shares (no extra infrastructure-bean to drop). The `ContextInfra` type and its accessor surface live in leaf-core as a stable ABI; the macro emits only a 'wants-infra' descriptor flag via absolute paths (§2.10). The duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM source) is irrelevant to the aware mechanism itself here — both kinds of bean simply receive the same bundle; the WASM bridge constructs its bean and the driver hands it the identical `ContextInfra`. Generics: a generic infra-needing bean still can't register (no symbol) → macro hard-errors, concrete-instantiation escape hatch. The risk this realization SHIFTS cross-crate: because `ContextInfra` exposes `factory()`/`context()`, downstream crates can service-locate, widening the de-facto API contract leaf must keep stable (the bundle's accessor set becomes load-bearing across the workspace) — a real ABI-surface cost to weigh in Phase 2.

**T2 — execution context:** Construction-time delivery runs on the bootstrap task INSIDE the bean's construct step (bean-instantiation): the `ContextInfra` borrow is live only for the constructor call; the bean clones the specific `Arc` handles it needs (Environment/EventPublisher already published and immutable). The optional `on_context_ready` grouped hook runs at the Aware timing slot — after populate, before init — as an `.await` point that, like R1, MUST NOT run under a held creation guard (executor-blocking hazard, rustClashes); a hook doing I/O uses spawn_blocking (§2.3). Because infrastructure handles are published-before-any-bean (the context layer is up before the eager pass), every accessor on the bundle reads safely-published state with no new lock (§concurrency-contract). CANCELLATION: cancelling during construction drops the half-built bean; cancelling during `on_context_ready` drops a bean that may have acquired resources from the bundle → explicit unwind ledger (no async Drop), shared with context-close. ERRORS: a constructor or hook that rejects its infrastructure returns a rich ResolveError naming the bean and the missing/invalid capability (§2.9), aborting refresh all-or-nothing. The factory-tier name is just a bundle field, read synchronously. No global lock introduced.

#### R3 — Awares as a generic 'infrastructure-provision' protocol over an origin-agnostic Provider: one `Provide<C>` trait + a registry of capability providers, fired as a group via a unified provisioning pass
Push unification (§2.11) and origin-agnosticism hardest: treat 'inject infrastructure X into a bean' as ONE generic protocol rather than a fixed set of named awares, so bean-name, factory, environment, events, resource-loader, the context, AND any future capability (a tracing span, a metrics registry, a WASM host) are all delivered by the SAME mechanism differing only in which capability is provided. Define `trait WantsContext { fn provision<'a>(&'a self, p: &'a dyn ProvisionCtx) -> BoxFuture<'a, Result<(), ResolveError>>; }` — a SINGLE grouped callback a bean implements when it wants ANY infrastructure; inside it the bean PULLS exactly the capabilities it needs from a type-keyed `ProvisionCtx` (`p.capability::<Environment>()`, `p.capability::<dyn EventPublisher>()`, `p.bean_name()`), where `ProvisionCtx` is an origin-agnostic registry of capability providers (`trait CapabilityProvider { fn capability_id(&self) -> CapabilityId; fn get(&self) -> &dyn Any; }`). The named awares become THIN convenience traits implemented in terms of the protocol (a `#[derive(EnvironmentAware)]`-style or a blanket-impl shim emits `fn provision` that pulls `Environment` and calls a user `fn on_environment`), so Spring-familiar `EnvironmentAware` still exists as sugar (DX) over one unified primitive. The grouped firing is a single provisioning PASS the creation driver runs at the Aware timing slot: for each bean flagged `wants_context`, call `provision` once. Crucially, the CAPABILITY SET is open and registry-driven: the bare engine registers only `bean_name`+`factory` capabilities (factory tier); the context façade REGISTERS the rest (environment, events, resource-loader, messages, context, startup, value-resolver) as capability providers — so 'context awares are inert on a bare engine' becomes 'those capabilities aren't registered', and a bean that pulls an unregistered capability gets a rich error ('EventPublisher requested but no event layer is installed') instead of silence. New infrastructure (from leaf or an integration crate) is added by registering a new CapabilityProvider — no new aware trait, no kernel change. The by-type-vs-callback duality is unified too: the SAME CapabilityProvider that backs `provision`-pulls also backs `get::<Environment>()` type-resolution, so callback-injection and dependency-injection genuinely share one source.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11): one `provision` protocol + a capability registry subsumes all ten awares AND any future infrastructure (tracing, metrics, a WASM host), so neighbours add capabilities by registration instead of new aware traits — the kernel never grows a branch per infrastructure kind.; Origin-agnostic to the extreme: `ProvisionCtx` is a registry of `CapabilityProvider`s, so a native Environment, a test-double Environment, and a WASM-host-backed capability are indistinguishable to the bean pulling them — the brief's origin-agnostic mandate satisfied at the capability seam.; Best 'inert on a bare engine, rich error not silence' story: an unregistered capability yields a precise diagnostic ('X requested, no provider installed; install the context layer / leaf_events crate') — strictly better than R1's silent-absence and aligned with §2.9.; Genuinely unifies the callback-vs-by-type duality: ONE CapabilityProvider backs both `provision`-pull and `get::<T>()`-by-type, so the two doors share a single source of truth instead of Spring's parallel ignoreDependencyInterface/registerResolvableDependency pair.; Spring-familiar awares survive as thin sugar traits over the protocol (DX + intent parity), so users get `EnvironmentAware` ergonomics AND leaf gets one primitive — escape-hatch-at-every-level (§2.10): drop to `provision` for full control.; Open capability set lets an integration crate ship its own infrastructure (e.g. a `Tracer` capability) provisioned through the EXACT same grouped pass — extensibility without touching leaf-core's aware taxonomy.

**Cons:** Most indirection and the highest risk of pre-deciding meta-architecture: a generic capability-provider registry is dangerously close to declaring a unified 'everything is a capability' substrate that Phase 2 owns — must be held strictly as a CANDIDATE, and its weight only pays off if leaf ships an opinionated default capability bundle (which then resembles R2/R1, questioning the protocol tax in Phase 1).; `provision` returns a boxed future at a dyn boundary (AFIT not dyn-safe), and `p.capability::<T>()` is a type-keyed `dyn Any` downcast per pull — alloc + dispatch churn on the provisioning path (cold for singletons, but a cost, and in tension with §2.5 minimal-boxing); the typed sugar traits help only the monomorphized path.; 'Pull from a god-registry' re-opens the service-location anti-pattern worse than R2: a bean can pull ANY registered capability (including `factory`/`context`) inside `provision`, smearing container access across infrastructure code — the narrow-aware discipline Spring used to discourage exactly this is now opt-in convention only.; Harder to enforce ORDER among the awares Spring fires in a specific sequence (Environment → … → ApplicationContext): a single `provision` pull-pass has no inherent ordering, so if any capability depends on another being provisioned first, the protocol must add ordering metadata — re-adding complexity the named-tier R1 got for free.; CapabilityId/CapabilityProvider is a new SPI that WIDENS the stable ABI the whole workspace hard-codes paths to (every integration crate shipping a capability binds to it) — a real semver/stability cost against the 'small stable kernel' principle (§6).; Teachability/legibility suffers: 'where do my awares fire?' is now 'a provisioning pass over a capability registry' rather than a readable ordered sub-step — hurting the cargo-expand-legibility R1 maximizes, and the indirection makes the timing contract harder to audit.

**Mechanisms considered:** single generic `WantsContext::provision(&self, &dyn ProvisionCtx) -> BoxFuture<Result<()>>` grouped callback; bean PULLS capabilities by type; `ProvisionCtx` = origin-agnostic registry of `CapabilityProvider`s keyed by CapabilityId/TypeId; bare engine registers name+factory, context façade registers the rest; named awares (EnvironmentAware…) as thin sugar traits / derives implemented in terms of `provision` (DX + parity over one primitive); unified source for callback-injection and by-type resolution: the same CapabilityProvider backs `provision`-pull and `get::<T>()`; grouped provisioning PASS run by the creation driver at the Aware timing slot; descriptor `wants_context` flag gates it; optional ordering metadata on capabilities if inter-capability provisioning order ever matters

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Best fit for the cross-crate layered-capability model AND the two-source duality: the bare engine contributes factory-tier capability providers; the context façade contributes the context-tier providers; an integration crate (or the WASM-module 'classpath') contributes MORE capability providers — all registered into the SAME `ProvisionCtx` the grouped pass reads, so the duality (static link-time registry of native capabilities PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM source of capabilities) is two producers of one capability stream, NOT two code paths (CANDIDATES, most natural here — not a decision). leaf-core hosts `WantsContext`, `ProvisionCtx`, `CapabilityProvider`, `CapabilityId` — a powerful but WIDER stable ABI than R1/R2 (every capability-shipping crate hard-codes paths to it), the chief cross-crate cost. The macro stays thin (emits a `wants_context` flag + sugar-trait impls via absolute paths, §2.10). Self-check (§152): the provisioning pass can assert that expected capability providers (esp. the context-tier set) registered, turning a DCE'd context/integration layer into a loud 'capability X has no provider' at boot. Generics: a generic capability-wanting bean can't register → macro hard-errors; per-instantiation escape hatch. Substrate-neutral on HOW providers/descriptors are collected (linkme/codegen/explicit), by design.

**T2 — execution context:** The grouped provisioning PASS runs at the Aware timing slot during the per-bean initialize phase on the bootstrap task (cold path), after populate and before init. `provision` is an `.await` point (boxed future at the dyn boundary) that MUST NOT run under a held per-bean creation guard (executor-blocking hazard, rustClashes); a provision body doing I/O uses spawn_blocking (§2.3). Capability pulls read safely-published infrastructure (the context layer and all providers are up before the eager pass), so no new synchronization beyond the registry's read access — and the registry is read-only during provisioning (built at refresh, frozen), avoiding any lock (§2.5). CANCELLATION: cancelling during `provision` drops a partially-provisioned bean; resources acquired inside `provision` need the explicit unwind ledger (no async Drop), shared with bean-instantiation/context-close. ERRORS: pulling an unregistered capability, or a provision body rejecting a capability, yields a rich ResolveError naming the bean, the capability, and whether it was unregistered-vs-invalid (§2.9) — aborting refresh all-or-nothing. If inter-capability ordering metadata is used, the pass honors it on the cold path only. No global lock; the capability registry is the only shared state and is read-only at provisioning time.

### Rust clashes
- Setter injection of infrastructure into an already-built bean is the central impedance mismatch. Spring sets Aware infrastructure via setters AFTER `new` + property population, mutating a live mutable object. Rust beans are typically CONSTRUCTED whole and may already be shared (behind a singleton handle) by init time, so 'set the bean name now' has nowhere idiomatic to go: it forces either interior mutability (a OnceCell/OnceLock field per aware — leaking provision mechanics into the bean and adding state §2.5 wants minimized) or moving provision to the construction seam (R2) where the bean receives infrastructure as a constructor argument. The rust-native realization leans toward provision-as-injection (collaborators are part of identity) rather than provision-as-mutation — but Spring's exact timing (after property population, e.g. to receive the bean's OWN registered name which isn't known at construction) still needs a post-population hook, so the clash is genuine and forces a delivery-shape decision (Phase 2).
- Aware INVERTS IoC (the bean reaches into the container) — which fights Rust's ownership/borrow discipline harder than Spring's GC'd reach-anywhere. A bean holding `&dyn ApplicationContext` or `&Environment` ties its lifetime to the container's; storing them forces cloning `Arc` handles (heap churn) or holding a borrow that fights whatever singleton-handle ownership model Phase 2 picks. Spring sidesteps this with GC (everything is freely aliasable); leaf must decide whether awares deliver borrowed handles (sound only for the callback duration) or owned/`Arc` handles the bean may retain — and the infrastructure-only steering matters MORE in Rust because the lifetime entanglement is a real cost, not just a coupling smell.
- No classloader → BeanClassLoaderAware has no analogue and should be dropped, not ported. Spring's `invokeAwareMethods` fires `BeanClassLoaderAware` only if a classloader is set; Rust has no per-context classloader (monomorphized, statically linked). This is a case (§2.2 intent-not-mechanism) where a Spring Aware exists only to route around a JVM concept Rust lacks — leaf should prove it unnecessary rather than invent a fake classloader handle. Conversely a Rust-native capability Spring lacks (a runtime handle, a tracing span) might WANT to be an aware, so the set is not a 1:1 port.
- Async fn in traits is not dyn-compatible, and the aware callback surface wants BOTH async (a bean may do async work on receiving infrastructure) AND dyn (the processor queries type-erased beans). RPITIT/AFIT can't be dyn, so the dynamic provisioning path must box the callback future (`Pin<Box<dyn Future>>`) — alloc per aware-bean (cold for singletons, but real). The design must split a zero-cost monomorphized typed path (where the concrete bean type is known) from a boxed dyn path (the processor/provisioning pass over erased beans), exactly the async-across-dyn tradeoff deferred to §3.
- Querying a type-erased bean for 'does it implement EnvironmentAware?' has no reflection analogue. Spring does `bean instanceof EnvironmentAware`. Rust has no runtime interface check on an erased handle — leaf must either trait-upcast from a common `Bean` supertrait (stable 1.86; constrains every aware-bean's stored handle to be `dyn Bean`, coupling to the registry substrate) or carry a macro-emitted per-descriptor flag-set marking which aware traits the CONCRETE type implements (a thin const, the preferred thin-macro route) — neither is Spring's free `instanceof`, and the choice couples this feature to the deferred type-erasure substrate.
- Init-under-the-singleton-lock becomes an executor-blocking hazard for async awares.
