Spring fires the context-aware group inside before-init, under the creation lock; an async aware that `.await`s under a held per-bean creation guard ties up an executor worker and can deadlock the pool (the same trap as @PostConstruct-under-lock). leaf must keep any per-bean guard scope to publication only and run awares OFF the held guard — preserving Spring's group-before-init timing while dissolving the blocking hazard rather than inheriting it (shared discipline with bean-instantiation/lifecycle-callbacks).
- No async Drop means a bean that acquired a resource inside an async aware can't self-clean on the unwind path. If `provision`/an async setter grabs an async-cleanup resource and then the bean's later init fails or refresh is cancelled, Drop can't run async teardown — the creation driver must retain an explicit cleanup ledger to release it deterministically (shared with context-close). This makes 'awares may do async work' a non-syntactic discipline, not a free callback.
- The callback-vs-by-type duality (`ignoreDependencyInterface` + `registerResolvableDependency`) has no direct Rust analogue and must be re-derived. Spring explicitly makes the same infrastructure injectable BY TYPE while NOT treating an Aware setter as an autowire injection point. In Rust there are no setters-as-injection-points to ignore, so the 'ignore' half is partly moot — but the 'same object available two ways' intent (a callback group AND `get::<Environment>()`) must be preserved coherently, ideally from ONE source (R3 unifies it; R1/R2 keep two doors) so the two paths can't diverge.
- Generic infrastructure beans can't be flagged or registered. A generic `Adapter<T>` that implements an aware trait has no `'static` symbol/TypeId until concretely instantiated, so neither the per-descriptor flag-set nor any capability-provider registration can name it — the macro must hard-error on a generic aware-bean with a per-instantiation escape hatch (consistent with the cross-crate doc's generics-cannot-register rule), unlike Spring's by-name generics.

### Interactions
- **lifecycle-callbacks** — TIGHTEST COUPLING / OWNER OF THE TIMING SLOT. The Aware group is the FIRST sub-step of the initialize sequence lifecycle-callbacks owns: Aware (factory-tier then context-tier) → before-init processors → @PostConstruct/afterPropertiesSet/init-method → after-init. lifecycle-callbacks fixes the ordered init chain and the dedup rules; aware-callbacks defines WHAT fires in the leading Aware slot and the group-before-init guarantee. This feature must slot into lifecycle-callbacks' sequence, not redefine it.
- **bean-instantiation** — UPSTREAM DRIVER. The per-bean creation sequence's INITIALIZE phase invokes the aware group at the correct moment (after populate, before init) and — critically — must keep any per-bean creation guard OFF the aware `.await` (dissolving the @PostConstruct-under-lock hazard). bean-instantiation owns the construct→populate→initialize→publish sequence and the guard discipline; aware-callbacks is invoked as the leading initialize sub-step and shares the no-async-Drop unwind ledger for awares that acquire resources.
- **post-processor-spi** — THE DOGFOODING SEAM. Spring fires the seven context-tier awares via ONE `BeanPostProcessor` (`ApplicationContextAwareProcessor`) on the public before-init hook — the framework eating its own extension point. In R1 the context-tier group IS a `role=Infrastructure` BPP-analogue defined on post-processor-spi's contracts; in R2/R3 the dogfooding is via the creation driver's provisioning pass. post-processor-spi provides the processor contract + ordering this feature's context tier rides.
- **container-core** — DEFINES FACTORY-TIER-VS-CONTEXT-TIER. The bare engine (BeanFactory layer) can fire ONLY the factory-tier awares (bean-name, factory) — it has zero context infrastructure; the context façade auto-detects the ContextAwareProcessor / registers the context-tier capabilities, which is exactly why context awares are inert on a bare engine. container-core's two-layer split is the structural reason the aware taxonomy is two-tiered; this feature realizes that split as which awares can fire at which layer.
- **environment** — PRIMARY INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE. EnvironmentAware delivers the context's single `Environment` (profiles + property-source stack) to infrastructure beans. environment owns the read/mutate-segregated abstraction reachable everywhere; aware-callbacks is one of the two doors to it (the other being by-type `get::<Environment>()`), and the same handle backs both.
- **events** — INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE. ApplicationEventPublisherAware delivers the `EventPublisher` so an infrastructure bean can publish without holding the whole context. events owns the pub/sub publisher; aware-callbacks provides early access to it at the Aware timing slot, before the bean's init may want to publish.
- **resource-loading** — INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE. ResourceLoaderAware delivers the `ResourceLoader`/pattern resolver. resource-loading owns the classpath/file/URL abstraction the context exposes; aware-callbacks is the callback door to it for beans (e.g. scanners, config loaders) that need resource access during init.
- **messages-i18n** — INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE. MessageSourceAware delivers the hierarchy-aware `MessageSource`. messages-i18n owns the magic-named MessageSource bean; aware-callbacks provides early access for infrastructure beans that resolve messages.
- **startup-instrumentation** — INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE (conditional on existence). ApplicationStartupAware delivers the startup-step recorder. startup-instrumentation owns the no-op-by-default timed-step taxonomy; this aware is premature until that feature lands (R1 risks dead surface; R2/R3 add it as a bundle accessor / capability only when present).
- **bean-naming** — SOURCE OF THE FACTORY-TIER NAME. BeanNameAware delivers the bean's deterministic registered name — which is precisely why this aware needs POST-population timing (the name is a registration fact, not a construction input). bean-naming derives/aliases the name; aware-callbacks hands the resolved name to the bean at the factory tier.
- **autowiring-resolution** — THE CALLBACK-VS-BY-TYPE SPLIT. Spring's `ignoreDependencyInterface` makes Aware methods NOT autowire injection points, while the same infrastructure stays injectable by type. autowiring-resolution must NOT treat an aware-trait method as an injection point; aware-callbacks defines the callback door, autowiring-resolution defines the by-type door — and (esp. in R3) they should share one capability source so they can't diverge.
- **import-composition** — GENERALIZES THE ImportAware NUANCE. Spring lets `ImportSelector`/`ImportBeanDefinitionRegistrar`/`BeanRegistrar` obtain Environment/BeanFactory/ClassLoader/ResourceLoader EITHER via the matching Aware OR a single constructor, with awares firing BEFORE `registerBeanDefinitions()`. R2 elevates the single-constructor idiom into the primary delivery mechanism. import-composition owns the four-altitude composition; this feature provides the early infrastructure access selectors/registrars depend on, at the right (pre-registration) timing.
- **programmatic-registration** — CONSUMER OF EARLY INFRASTRUCTURE. A `BeanRegistrar`-analogue needs Environment/BeanFactory access (via aware or constructor) at registration time. programmatic-registration owns the imperative/conditional registration contract; aware-callbacks (or the constructor idiom) supplies the infrastructure it reads — fired before its register() runs.
- **value-injection** — INJECTED INFRASTRUCTURE. EmbeddedValueResolverAware delivers the ${...}/#{...} value resolver. value-injection owns placeholder/expression resolution; this aware gives infrastructure beans the resolver directly — premature until value-injection exists (same dead-surface caveat as startup-instrumentation).
- **concurrency-contract** — SAFE-PUBLICATION DEPENDENCY. Every injected infrastructure handle (Environment, EventPublisher, ResourceLoader, MessageSource, the context) is published BEFORE any application bean's eager creation, so awares read safely-published immutable state with no new lock. concurrency-contract frames safe publication of fully-built infrastructure; aware-callbacks relies on that ordering for its reads to be sound.
- **context-close** — UNWIND PARTNER. Because there is no async Drop, a bean that acquired an async-cleanup resource inside an async aware must have that cleanup driven explicitly by context-close's reverse-order shutdown via the creation driver's cleanup ledger. context-close owns the explicit shutdown().await; aware-callbacks must register any aware-acquired resource into the same teardown ledger.
- **auto-proxy-creator** — ARCHETYPAL AWARE CONSUMER. Spring's `AbstractAutoProxyCreator` implements `BeanFactoryAware` — the auto-proxy creator is exactly the kind of infrastructure bean that legitimately needs container access, the use case Aware exists for. auto-proxy-creator (when leaf has one) will be a primary client of BeanFactoryAware; this feature provides the seam.
- **scopes** — OWNERSHIP-OF-HANDLE TENSION. Whether an aware delivers a borrowed vs owned/Arc infrastructure handle, and whether a bean may retain it, is entangled with the scope/ownership model scopes anchors (singleton vs prototype lifetime). scopes fixes per-scope lifetime; aware-callbacks' delivered-handle ownership must be consistent with it (a prototype that retains an Arc<context> outlives nothing problematic, a borrow does) — shared §3 keystone.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Delivery shape: is infrastructure provided by a POST-CONSTRUCTION callback (Spring's setter timing — required for facts only knowable after registration like the bean's own name, but forces interior mutability into an already-shared bean) or at the CONSTRUCTION SEAM as an injected argument (idiomatic Rust, dissolves interior mutability, but can't deliver post-population facts) — or both, with a clear rule for which infrastructure goes which way? This is the central aware impedance mismatch and shapes the whole API. Shared with bean-instantiation and injection-styles.
- Injected-handle ownership & lifetime (the deferred §3 scope/ownership keystone viewed through awares): does an aware deliver a BORROWED handle (`&Environment`, sound only for the callback duration — beans must clone what they keep) or an OWNED/`Arc` handle a bean may retain (heap churn, but storable)? A bean that needs to hold the EventPublisher past init forces this. The get<T> return type and the singleton-handle type drive it. Shared with scopes, container-core, autowiring-resolution.
- Taxonomy granularity: ten named Aware traits (R1, literal parity, fine-grained 'I need exactly THIS' signal, but lots of surface and premature traits before their features land), ONE ContextInfra bundle of accessors (R2, low ceremony but god-handle/service-location risk), or a generic capability-provision protocol with named-aware sugar (R3, maximal unification but widest ABI and re-opened ordering)? A unification call needing the whole catalog — and it cross-cuts whether callback-injection and by-type resolution share one source.
- How is 'does this erased bean implement aware X?' answered: trait-upcasting from a common `Bean` supertrait (stable 1.86, constrains every aware-bean's stored handle to be `dyn Bean`, couples to the registry substrate) vs a macro-emitted per-descriptor aware-flag-set (thin const, the thin-macro route) vs per-trait `Any` downcasts (alloc/dispatch churn)? Couples directly to the deferred type-erasure & registry substrate. Shared with bean-instantiation and the whole registry family.
- Async-across-dyn boxing standard for the aware/provision callback surface: AFIT isn't dyn-safe, so the processor/provisioning pass over erased beans must box the callback future (alloc per aware-bean, cold for singletons). Is the standard boxed-future-on-the-dyn-path with a monomorphized zero-cost typed fast-path as the escape, or vice versa? The async-execution-model decision (§3) viewed through the aware path. Shared with bean-instantiation, lifecycle-callbacks, post-processor-spi.
- The callback-vs-by-type duality realization: should aware callback-injection and by-type `get::<Environment>()` resolution share ONE source of truth (R3's unified CapabilityProvider — they can't diverge, but a wider SPI) or remain two coordinated doors (R1/R2 — simpler, but a coherence burden to keep aligned)? Cross-cuts autowiring-resolution and the registry substrate, and determines whether Spring's ignoreDependencyInterface/registerResolvableDependency split has a single-sourced Rust analogue.
- Capability set openness & ABI width: is the injected-infrastructure set CLOSED (a fixed leaf-core taxonomy — small stable kernel, but every new infrastructure kind is a kernel semver event) or OPEN (R3's registry where integration crates ship their own capabilities — extensible, but widens the stable ABI the whole workspace hard-codes paths to, fighting the small-stable-kernel principle §6)? Determines whether a future tracing/metrics/WASM-host capability is an aware at all. Shared with the cross-crate composition model.
- Cross-crate discovery + anti-DCE for the context-aware mechanism: in R1/R3 a `role=Infrastructure` ContextAwareProcessor / capability providers must be discovered by the context façade and survive DCE, so the façade self-check must include them (a silently-dropped context layer must loudly fail with 'context awares will not fire' rather than go inert). Which discovery substrate (linkme / build.rs codegen / explicit) and how the self-check enumerates expected aware infrastructure is unresolved. Shared with the whole discovery/SPI family and the two-source (static + WASM) duality.
- Infrastructure-only steering: should leaf ENFORCE that awares (or a ContextInfra param) are only used by `role=Infrastructure` beans — a hard error/warn nudging app beans to constructor injection (stronger than Spring's convention, and R2 makes it structural via the visible signature) — or merely document the steering? Determines how strongly the IoC-inversion smell is policed. Cross-cuts component-stereotypes and conditions (role assignment).

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the precise feature section — `Aware callbacks` (892-900: the full ordering, the context-tier-fires-as-a-group-via-one-BPP mechanic, and the design intent that Aware inverts IoC and is for infrastructure beans only) — plus the surrounding mechanics that ARE this feature's body: the exact initialize sequence and `invokeAwareMethods`' THREE factory-tier interfaces (2481-2490), the hidden 'seven context-level awares fire DURING before-init via ApplicationContextAwareProcessor, a sub-step of the first before-init pass' with the dogfooding design intent AND the ignoreDependencyInterface/registerResolvableDependency callback-vs-by-type split (2492-2496), init-under-the-singleton-lock with the SmartInitializing escape hatch (2502-2504), and the ImportAware/single-constructor-OR-aware nuance with awares firing before registerBeanDefinitions (1038, 1052-1056). I grounded T1 against the cross-crate doc (Layer-0/A/B silent-DCE + the mandatory self-check §152, thin-macro/absolute-path ABI §96-102, generics-cannot-register §153, linkme-default §166, Architectures A-D §277-333) and T2/clashes against the charter invariants (§2.2 intent-not-mechanism — load-bearing for dropping BeanClassLoaderAware; §2.3 async-first/explicit-context; §2.5 no-global-lock/minimal-Arc; §2.9 rich-early-diagnostics; §2.10 thin-macros; §2.11 unification) and the stable envelope (RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe forcing boxed callback futures §160-181, trait upcasting 1.86 §80/118-126, const TypeId 1.91 / Arc<dyn Any> §81/17, no async Drop as a first-class constraint §86/273-280).

SCOPING NOTE for Phase 2/3: aware-callbacks is a SMALL, well-bounded feature whose entire body is 'WHAT fires in the leading sub-step of the initialize phase, and how the container hands infrastructure to a bean.' I deliberately drew boundaries so it composes rather than collides: lifecycle-callbacks OWNS the init ordering (Aware → before-init → init → after-init) and aware-callbacks only defines the leading Aware slot's contents + the group-before-init guarantee; bean-instantiation OWNS the creation sequence and the guard discipline (and must keep the guard off the aware .await); post-processor-spi OWNS the BPP contract the context tier dogfoods; container-core's two-layer split is WHY the taxonomy is two-tiered (factory tier fires on the bare engine, context tier only via the façade's auto-detected processor). My three realizations diverge on the SHAPE OF INFRASTRUCTURE PROVISION (not the init ordering, not the creation sequence): R1 = literal ten-trait two-tier taxonomy with the context tier as one dogfooded role=Infrastructure BPP-analogue (highest fidelity, cheapest dispatch via a per-descriptor flag-set, but ports the setter-mutation idiom and the broad trait surface); R2 = collapse the taxonomy to ONE ContextInfra capability bundle delivered at the CONSTRUCTION SEAM (the ImportAware single-constructor idiom generalized — lowest ceremony, dissolves interior mutability, structurally enforces infra-only steering, but god-handle/service-location risk and doesn't fit post-population-only facts like the bean's own name); R3 = a generic capability-provision protocol over an origin-agnostic provider registry with named awares as thin sugar (maximal unification + origin-agnosticism + best inert-on-bare-engine-with-rich-error story + single-sourced callback-vs-by-type duality, but most indirection, widest ABI, and borders on pre-deciding meta-architecture). R1↔R2 differ on setter-mutation vs construction-seam delivery and ten-traits vs one-bundle; R3 differs on named-taxonomy vs unified-protocol. R3's protocol can host R1's named awares (as sugar) and R2's bundle (as a fixed capability set), so the three are divergent yet Phase-2-composable.

Three things to carry forward: (1) The single sharpest clash is SETTER-INJECTION-INTO-AN-ALREADY-BUILT-BEAN — Spring mutates a live object after property population, but Rust beans are constructed whole and often already shared, so leaf must choose between interior mutability (a OnceCell per aware, leaking state §2.5 wants minimized) and construction-seam delivery (R2, idiomatic but can't carry post-population facts like the registered bean-name) — a genuine delivery-shape decision flagged as the first phase2Question. (2) BeanClassLoaderAware should be DROPPED, not ported — it exists only to route around a JVM classloader Rust lacks (§2.2 intent-not-mechanism in action), while a Rust-native capability Spring lacks (a runtime handle, a tracing span) might WANT to be an aware, so the set is not a 1:1 port. (3) The init-under-the-lock detail matters in async Rust: an async aware that .awaits under a held per-bean creation guard is the same executor-blocking trap as @PostConstruct-under-lock, so awares MUST run off any held guard — shared discipline with bean-instantiation and lifecycle-callbacks.

I refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps the injected handle type abstract (the §3 scope/ownership keystone — borrowed vs owned/Arc is a phase2Question), keeps provision origin-agnostic (a WASM/test-double bean receives the same infrastructure through the identical seam), keeps the async-across-dyn boxing standard open, and is compatible with cross-crate Architectures A/B/C/D. The two-source duality (static link-time registry of native capabilities + an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM source) is captured as multiple producers of the SAME capability/aware set (most natural in R3) — a CANDIDATE, not a decision. Naming ('ContextAwareProcessor', 'ContextInfra', 'WantsContext', 'ProvisionCtx', 'CapabilityProvider', 'AwareFlags', and the trait names) is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (892-900 the Aware section + design intent; 2481-2496 invokeAwareMethods' three factory-tier interfaces + the seven context-tier awares fired by ApplicationContextAwareProcessor as a before-init sub-step + the dogfooding intent + ignoreDependencyInterface/registerResolvableDependency split; 2502-2504 init-under-the-lock + SmartInitializing escape; 1038 ImportAware-vs-single-constructor nuance + awares-before-registerBeanDefinitions; 1052-1056 ImportAware; 355/370 prepareBeanFactory registers the ApplicationContextAwareProcessor + resolvable dependencies); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. 2.2/2.3/2.5/2.9/2.10/2.11, deferred keystones §3, topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (silent-DCE + self-check mandate §135-152, thin-macro/absolute-path ABI §90-102, generics-cannot-register §153, linkme-default §166, Architectures A-D §277-333); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe forcing boxed callback futures §160-181, trait upcasting 1.86 §80/118-126, const TypeId 1.91 + Arc<dyn Any> §81/17, no async Drop first-class constraint §86/273-280).

## SmartInitializingSingleton (all-singletons-ready hook)  `[smart-initializing]`

Spring's `SmartInitializingSingleton.afterSingletonsInstantiated()` is the SECOND loop of `preInstantiateSingletons()`: a SINGLE callback invoked ONCE, after EVERY eager (non-lazy) singleton exists and is fully initialized, and crucially OUTSIDE the singleton creation lock. THIS feature is precisely that boundary-and-hook — distinct from bean-instantiation (which OWNS the eager pass that fires it, the per-bean construct→populate→init→publish sequence, and the creation guard) and from refresh-lifecycle (which OWNS the fixed-order template whose finish-factory-init step drives the eager pass). smart-initializing contributes only: (1) the CONTRACT — a registry-wide, run-once, lock-free post-all-singletons callback that runs against a COMPLETE graph; (2) the GUARANTEE that lets a bean safely query the registry (Spring's `getBeansOfType`/collection-injection) to aggregate collaborators WITHOUT triggering accidental early instantiation of half-built peers (because by the time it runs, every eager singleton already exists); (3) the SCOPING — fired ONLY for eager singletons, NEVER for lazy-on-demand singletons or any non-singleton scope. The precise intent to replicate: Spring deliberately CONSTRAINS the per-bean init contract (@PostConstruct = validate wiring, runs under the lock against a PARTIAL graph) rather than weakening publication safety, then provides this distinct lock-free phase for the EXPENSIVE / CROSS-BEAN / I-O-heavy work (async DB prep, building cross-bean indexes, wiring up listener registries) that would DEADLOCK or see a half-built graph if done in @PostConstruct. The mental model it instills: per-bean setup → @PostConstruct; registry-wide aggregation → afterSingletonsInstantiated. It is also Spring's own DOGFOODING seam — `EventListenerMethodProcessor` IS a `SmartInitializingSingleton` (it scans every singleton for @EventListener methods only once all beans exist). v7 wraps each callback in an `ApplicationStartup` step (`spring.beans.smart-initialize`) for instrumentation, and runs it after the `@Bean(BACKGROUND)` background-init futures have joined. leaf must replicate this INTENT — a once-per-refresh, lock-free, complete-graph, eager-singletons-only hook that is the sanctioned home for cross-bean post-init work — WITHOUT Spring's lineage: the under-lock @PostConstruct it exists to escape (which in async Rust is an EXECUTOR-blocking hazard, not just a deadlock risk), the assumption that the callback runs synchronously on the bootstrap thread (in async leaf it is an async hook that may .await), GC-published instance aliasing, runtime instanceof-over-live-beans to FIND which beans implement the marker (Rust has no type-walk — participation must be DATA), and the assumption of native-only construction (a WASM-module/test-double-provided bean that wants the hook must be discovered and invoked identically). It must NOT pick the registry/handle substrate, the async-execution model, how participation is collected (link-time slice vs runtime registry vs codegen), whether the hook is a marker-trait callback or a unified lifecycle/event subscription, or the cancellation/error-aggregation substrate — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Literal marker-trait callback fired as the eager pass's second loop: an async `AfterSingletonsReady` hook the engine invokes once, lock-free, over a participation list
Mirror Spring 1:1, retyped async and lock-free. Define a marker trait `trait AfterSingletonsReady { async fn after_singletons_ready(&self, cx: &ReadyCx) -> Result<(), ReadyError>; }` (placeholder name) that a bean implements to opt into the hook. bean-instantiation's eager pass exposes a clean BOUNDARY method the engine calls AFTER pass-1 (every eager non-lazy singleton constructed+initialized+published) and AFTER background-bootstrap futures have joined: `engine.fire_after_singletons_ready().await`. This second loop iterates the ALREADY-PUBLISHED eager singletons, and for each whose descriptor is flagged `participates_in_ready` (the macro emits this flag when the bean's type implements the marker — participation is DATA on the descriptor, never a runtime instanceof), it awaits `bean.after_singletons_ready(&cx)`. The `ReadyCx` is a READ-ONLY view of the now-complete container that exposes registry queries (`cx.all_of::<T>()` / `cx.get::<T>()` — collection-injection / autowiring-resolution) which are SAFE here precisely because every eager singleton already exists, so a query can never trigger a half-built peer's creation (it may still lazily build a genuinely-`@Lazy` bean on demand, matching Spring). The hook runs OUTSIDE any creation guard (the rust-native dissolution of Spring's under-lock @PostConstruct: there is no guard held, so an `.await` here can never tie up a guard or deadlock the pool). Ordering across multiple participants is resolved as DATA (descriptor `@Order`/priority, topo-sorted — never link/registration order, per cross-crate §170), defaulting to unspecified-but-stable. Each invocation is wrapped in a named startup-instrumentation step (`leaf.beans.smart-initialize`). SCOPING is structural: the second loop walks only the eager-singleton publication ledger, so lazy-on-demand singletons and prototypes/scoped beans are NEVER in it (they have no entry in that ledger at this point). Errors abort refresh all-or-nothing (a failing ready-hook is a boot failure, same fail-fast contract as the eager pass). This is the most teachable shape: `cargo expand` shows the macro emitting only `impl AfterSingletonsReady for MyBean` plus a descriptor flag, and the engine's second loop reads exactly like Spring's Phase-2 loop.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity and teachability: the marker trait + 'second loop after every eager singleton exists, outside any lock' maps 1:1 to Spring's `SmartInitializingSingleton.afterSingletonsInstantiated()` and its Phase-2 loop — a Spring developer recognizes it instantly, and `cargo expand` shows a hand-writable `impl` + a descriptor flag with zero hidden logic (§2.10 thin macros, §2.1 DX).; Dissolves the @PostConstruct-deadlock trap by EXECUTION CONTEXT, not by a lock trick: because the hook runs after the eager pass with NO creation guard held, an `.await` (async DB prep, building a cross-bean index) can never block a guarded executor worker or deadlock — exactly the charter-aligned realization of why this hook exists (§2.3, §2.5).; The complete-graph guarantee makes registry queries safe-by-construction: `cx.all_of::<T>()` cannot trigger a half-built peer because every eager singleton is already published — directly preserving Spring's 'safely call getBeansOfType to aggregate' property and dovetailing with collection-injection / autowiring-resolution as a read-only consumer.; Participation-as-DATA (a descriptor flag the macro emits) is the honest rust-native replacement for Spring's instanceof-over-live-beans (Rust has no type-walk): the engine never inspects live types, it reads the flag — origin-agnostic, so a WASM-module/test-double bean that declares the hook is discovered and invoked identically.; Scoping falls out structurally: the second loop walks only the eager-singleton publication ledger, so lazy/prototype/scoped beans are provably never fired — the 'eager-singletons-only' contract can't be violated by a forgotten branch.; Minimal new surface: one trait + one descriptor flag + one engine boundary method, composing cleanly on top of the provided bean-instantiation eager pass without reinventing it.

**Cons:** A bespoke marker trait + bespoke second-loop is a per-feature solution where leaf's §2.11 unification goal might prefer ONE post-all-singletons subscription primitive shared with lifecycle-events' ContextRefreshedEvent (Spring itself offers `@EventListener(ContextRefreshedEvent.class)` as the equivalent) — R1 risks two near-identical 'context is ready' mechanisms that must stay coherent.; The async marker trait must be `dyn`-invocable over the erased published handle (the engine holds `dyn`-erased beans), and AFIT is not dyn-safe → the hook is a boxed-future call (alloc per participant); cold and rare (once per refresh, few participants) so cheap, but it commits the hook's signature to the boxed-future-on-dyn standard that is itself a deferred keystone.; 'Fire only for eager singletons' depends on bean-instantiation's publication ledger being the exact source of truth at the firing moment; if Phase 2's lazy/background model lets a bean transition eager↔lazy or a background future publish late, the ledger snapshot timing becomes subtle (when EXACTLY is 'every eager singleton exists' — after background join, but a background bean that itself participates must be in the loop).; Ordering across participants is unspecified-but-stable by default; if a participant's aggregation depends on another participant's ready-hook having run (a meta-dependency Spring also doesn't strongly order), the topo-sort-as-data needs a clear contract or users hit surprising ordering — a DX edge.; Error semantics (a failing ready-hook aborts refresh) couple this hook to refresh-lifecycle's all-or-nothing cancel path and the no-async-Drop partial-cleanup discipline; a ready-hook that already kicked off async resources before failing has the same explicit-unwind burden as any boot-failure path.

**Mechanisms considered:** marker trait `AfterSingletonsReady` with one async method; bean opts in by implementing it; macro emits the `impl` + a `participates_in_ready` descriptor flag (participation as DATA, not runtime instanceof); engine boundary method `fire_after_singletons_ready().await` invoked by bean-instantiation's eager pass as its second loop, after pass-1 + background-bootstrap join, OUTSIDE any creation guard; `ReadyCx` read-only complete-graph view exposing registry queries (`all_of::<T>`, `get::<T>`) that are safe because every eager singleton already exists; ordering across participants resolved as DATA (descriptor priority/@Order, topo-sorted) — never registration/link order (cross-crate §170); scoping by walking the eager-singleton publication ledger only — lazy/prototype/scoped structurally excluded; each invocation wrapped in a named startup-instrumentation step (`leaf.beans.smart-initialize`); failure aborts refresh all-or-nothing; boxed-future call at the dyn boundary (AFIT not dyn-safe) for the erased published handle — cold, once-per-refresh

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on HOW participation is collected — leaf-core defines the `AfterSingletonsReady` trait, the descriptor flag, the `ReadyCx` view, and the `fire_after_singletons_ready` boundary; contributing crates depend only on leaf-core and the thin macro emits `impl AfterSingletonsReady for ConcreteBean` + sets the descriptor flag via absolute paths (§99-102, the macro holds zero logic). The PARTICIPATION SET is just the subset of the SAME descriptor/Provider table the eager pass already walks whose `participates_in_ready` flag is set — so whatever catalog substrate Phase 2 picks (linkme distributed-slice default §166 / build.rs codegen / explicit) feeds it identically; no separate registry. CRITICAL anti-DCE interaction (§152): because a silently-DCE'd contributing crate's beans never register, its ready-hooks silently never fire — a feature that should aggregate cross-bean state quietly does nothing (the exact 'plain BeanFactory' silent-failure symptom). The fired-participant count is therefore a natural input to the mandatory expected-vs-found self-check (the eager pass / facade asserts expected ready-participant count by provenance), turning a dropped crate into a loud boot error rather than a silently-empty aggregation. Generics cannot participate (no `'static` symbol until concretely instantiated → the descriptor names only concrete types; macro hard-errors on a generic bean implementing the marker, with a per-instantiation `register_component!`-style escape hatch §153). The two-source duality applies trivially: native beans contribute participating descriptors via the static link-time registry; a dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source contributes participating descriptors whose hook dispatches into the module — both are invoked by the SAME second loop through the boxed-future seam (CANDIDATES, not a decision). The trait + flag is a minimal, stable cross-crate ABI; no wide surface.

**T2 — execution context:** Runs on the BOOTSTRAP task (COLD PATH), as the final sub-step of bean-instantiation's eager pass within refresh-lifecycle's finish-factory-init phase, AFTER every eager non-lazy singleton is published AND after background-bootstrap futures have joined, and explicitly OUTSIDE any creation guard (the structural dissolution of Spring's under-lock @PostConstruct hazard: no guard is held, so an `.await` here cannot block a guarded worker or deadlock the pool — §2.5). Each participant's `after_singletons_ready(&cx).await` is an explicit `.await` point: it MAY await (async DB prep, remote index build) but a blocking call must use spawn_blocking (execution-context discipline §2.3). The `ReadyCx` registry queries are read-only and lock-free against the complete graph; they never trigger a half-built peer (every eager singleton exists), though querying a genuinely-`@Lazy` bean may build it on demand on this same task. CANCELLATION: cancelling the bootstrap task mid-ready-phase must drop the in-flight hook future; because there is no async Drop, any async resource a hook already acquired before cancellation/failure cannot self-clean via Drop — the hook's own cleanup (or the refresh cancel-path's reverse-order teardown ledger) must run it explicitly, same discipline as the eager pass (shared with context-close). ERRORS propagate as a rich ReadyError carrying which participant failed, the complete-graph context, and per-source provenance (§2.9); a failing ready-hook aborts refresh all-or-nothing (consistent with the eager pass's fail-fast — 'a context that started is a context that works' extends through this phase). Whether multiple participants' hooks run sequentially or fan out concurrently (they touch a complete, read-only graph, so independence is plausible) is an execution-model choice deferred to Phase 2; default sequential in declared/topo order for deterministic diagnostics. Fired exactly ONCE per refresh; never re-fired; never for lazy/prototype/scoped beans.

#### R2 — A unified post-refresh lifecycle PHASE / readiness signal: 'all eager singletons ready' is one milestone on the shared lifecycle-event bus, of which the per-bean callback is just one subscriber
Push unification (§2.11): question whether a bespoke marker trait is warranted when Spring ITSELF offers `@EventListener(ContextRefreshedEvent.class)` as the documented equivalent of `SmartInitializingSingleton`, and leaf already has lifecycle-events / events / refresh-lifecycle as neighbours. Model 'every eager singleton now exists' as a single MILESTONE the engine emits once: refresh-lifecycle's finish-factory-init, after the eager pass + background join, publishes a typed `SingletonsReady` milestone (a sibling of / the same channel as lifecycle-events' ContextRefreshed). SUBSCRIBERS to that milestone are the realization of the hook: (a) a bean can subscribe declaratively — the macro turns `#[on_ready] async fn warmup(&self, cx)` (or an `AfterSingletonsReady` impl) into a registered subscriber descriptor — OR (b) any @EventListener-style handler for the milestone subscribes the same way (dogfooding: leaf's own event-listener processor subscribes to discover @EventListener methods across all beans, exactly as Spring's EventListenerMethodProcessor is itself a SmartInitializingSingleton). The engine drives ONE dispatch of the milestone, lock-free, after the complete graph exists; the dispatcher (event-multicaster) invokes each subscriber's async handler in ordered (priority/topo) sequence, against a read-only complete-graph `ReadyCx`. The marker-trait hook and the context-refreshed event listener thus become TWO SHAPES OF ONE SUBSCRIPTION — no second near-identical mechanism. Scoping (eager-singletons-only as the SUBJECTS, but ANY participant — even a lazy infra bean — as a SUBSCRIBER) is encoded by the milestone's contract: the subject set is the eager-singleton ledger; subscribers are whoever registered for the milestone. This also cleanly absorbs Spring's nuance that a lazy `SmartInitializingSingleton`-equivalent might still want the hook (Spring force-includes such beans), by letting subscription be independent of the subscriber's own eagerness.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11): one 'context milestone + subscribers' primitive serves SmartInitializingSingleton, ContextRefreshedEvent, AND the @EventListener-discovery dogfooding — eliminating R1's risk of two coherence-bound 'context is ready' mechanisms, and matching Spring's own design where these ARE alternatives and the event-listener processor IS a smart-init singleton.; Dogfoods leaf's own event/listener machinery exactly as Spring does (EventListenerMethodProcessor is a SmartInitializingSingleton): the listener-discovery pass is just a subscriber to the SingletonsReady milestone, proving the events/lifecycle-events substrate is powerful enough and keeping the core small.; Lock-free complete-graph guarantee is inherited from the milestone's emission point (after eager pass + background join, no guard held), so the @PostConstruct-deadlock dissolution is identical to R1 — but now it's a property of the lifecycle bus, reused by every milestone subscriber, not re-derived per feature.; Cleanly models Spring's lazy-but-still-fired nuance: subscription is decoupled from the subscriber's own eagerness, so an infrastructure bean that is lazy can still subscribe to the readiness milestone (Spring force-includes SmartInitializingSingleton beans from global-lazy) — a contract the milestone expresses naturally rather than as a special-case in the eager loop.; Strong DX surface for the common 'do X once everything is up' need: `#[on_ready]` reads like a natural event handler and rides the same ordering/error machinery as all other listeners — fewer concepts for the user to learn (§2.1).; Origin-agnostic and substrate-neutral: a WASM-module bean subscribes to the milestone via the same descriptor path as a native one; the dispatcher doesn't care where the subscriber came from.

**Cons:** Couples a CORE container lifecycle guarantee (the all-singletons-ready boundary, which bean-instantiation/refresh-lifecycle must provide regardless) to the EVENTS subsystem, whose own design (events / event-multicaster / lifecycle-events) is itself unsettled in Phase 1 — a layering-inversion risk if the event bus ends up depending on container bring-up that depends on the bus; the milestone emission must sit at a carefully-chosen seam to avoid a cycle.; Spring's SmartInitializingSingleton and ContextRefreshedEvent have a SUBTLE timing/semantic difference (smart-init runs strictly inside preInstantiateSingletons before finishRefresh publishes ContextRefreshedEvent; the event fires later, after lifecycle start) — collapsing them into one milestone risks erasing a distinction some beans rely on (do you fire BEFORE or AFTER SmartLifecycle auto-start?), so the unification may need TWO ordered milestones anyway, partially undoing the simplification.; More machinery and indirection than R1's direct second loop: a milestone type + a dispatcher + subscriber descriptors, where R1 needs one trait + one flag — overhead and conceptual surface for what is, at minimum, a single run-once callback (acceptable cold, but it pre-judges that the event substrate is the right home).; Error/cancellation semantics now flow through the event-multicaster's dispatch model (does a failing subscriber abort the milestone? does it abort refresh?) — the all-or-nothing fail-fast contract this hook needs may not match the multicaster's default 'listeners are independent, errors are collected' semantics, forcing a special milestone-dispatch mode.; Borders on pre-deciding the events-vs-lifecycle meta-architecture (a Phase-2/3 concern): presenting 'it's just an event subscriber' as THE shape biases how lifecycle-events, events, and event-multicaster get unified — must be held as a CANDIDATE, and its value depends on those neighbours landing as a shared bus.; Ordering between 'bean's own @PostConstruct-equivalent', 'its readiness subscriber', and 'a ContextRefreshed listener' becomes a single ordered stream the user must reason about — richer but harder to predict than R1's clearly-separate per-bean-then-all-beans phases.

**Mechanisms considered:** a typed `SingletonsReady` milestone emitted ONCE by refresh-lifecycle/finish-factory-init after the eager pass + background join, on the shared lifecycle-event channel (sibling of / same bus as ContextRefreshed); subscribers as the hook realization: `#[on_ready]`/`AfterSingletonsReady`-impl → a registered subscriber descriptor; @EventListener(SingletonsReady) handlers subscribe identically; dispatch driven once by event-multicaster, lock-free, ordered (priority/topo) over a read-only complete-graph `ReadyCx`; subscription decoupled from subscriber eagerness (models Spring's lazy-SmartInitializingSingleton-still-fired nuance); possibly TWO ordered milestones (SingletonsReady before SmartLifecycle start; ContextRefreshed after) to preserve Spring's timing distinction; dogfood: leaf's @EventListener-discovery pass is itself a SingletonsReady subscriber (mirrors Spring's EventListenerMethodProcessor)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Subscribers are descriptors in the SAME catalog table the rest of the framework uses (linkme default / codegen / explicit, Phase 2) — leaf-core defines the milestone type, the subscriber descriptor shape, the `ReadyCx`, and the dispatch boundary; the thin macro emits a subscriber descriptor (with priority + an absolute-path-referenced async handler fn) for `#[on_ready]`/@EventListener(SingletonsReady), holding zero logic (§99-102). Because subscription is data on the descriptor, the milestone's subscriber set is origin-agnostic (native, WASM-module, test-double subscribe identically). Same silent-DCE hazard as R1 but BROADER: a dropped crate loses both its beans AND its readiness subscribers (and its @EventListener-discovery contribution), so the anti-DCE self-check (§152) should assert expected subscriber/listener counts per provenance — a dropped integration's 'wire up once everything's ready' logic silently vanishing is exactly the worst-case symptom. The events-as-substrate choice WIDENS the cross-crate ABI relative to R1 (the milestone + subscriber-descriptor vocabulary becomes shared surface that downstream crates hard-code paths to) — a real cost to weigh against the unification dividend. Generics-cannot-subscribe inherited (concrete handlers only; macro hard-errors). Two-source duality: native + dynamic WASM-module subscribers feed the SAME dispatch (CANDIDATES, not a decision). The layering constraint is a T1 concern too: the milestone-emitting seam must not create a leaf-core dependency cycle between the container-bring-up crate and the events crate (dependencies point inward, no cycles — §6) — likely the milestone TYPE lives in a low kernel crate both depend on.

**T2 — execution context:** The milestone is emitted on the BOOTSTRAP task (cold path) at finish-factory-init, after the eager pass + background-bootstrap join, with NO creation guard held — so subscriber handlers run lock-free against the complete graph, and an `.await` in a handler cannot deadlock a guarded worker (§2.5, §2.3). Each subscriber's async handler is an explicit `.await` point (may await; blocking → spawn_blocking). Dispatch ORDER is data-driven (priority/topo) for deterministic diagnostics; whether subscribers run sequentially or concurrently is the event-multicaster's execution-model choice (deferred), but the complete-graph subjects are read-only so concurrent dispatch is plausible. The critical T2 tension vs R1: the multicaster's DEFAULT error model (listeners independent, errors collected/logged, dispatch continues) likely CONFLICTS with this hook's needed all-or-nothing fail-fast (a failed readiness hook should abort refresh) — so the SingletonsReady dispatch needs a fail-fast mode distinct from ordinary event dispatch, and that mode's error must surface as a rich boot-abort with the failing subscriber + complete-graph context (§2.9). CANCELLATION: cancelling bootstrap mid-dispatch drops in-flight handler futures; no async Drop means a handler's already-acquired async resources need explicit cleanup on the cancel/abort path (shared with context-close and refresh-lifecycle's cancel ledger). If leaf keeps TWO ordered milestones, the SingletonsReady one fires before runtime-lifecycle's SmartLifecycle auto-start and the ContextRefreshed one after — the execution boundary between 'graph complete' and 'components running' must be explicit. Fired exactly once per refresh; subscribers never re-invoked.

#### R3 — A first-class post-init WORK-LEDGER the engine drains lock-free after the graph completes: beans declaratively ENQUEUE cross-bean work during init; the engine runs it once, all-graph-ready
Invert the marker-trait framing. Instead of 'implement a callback the engine will call back into', let a bean DECLARE its expensive cross-bean post-init work as DEFERRED WORK ITEMS during its own (cheap, validation-only) init, which the engine collects into a per-container POST-INIT LEDGER and DRAINS exactly once, lock-free, after the complete graph exists. Concretely: during a bean's init phase (which stays Spring's 'validate wiring' contract — cheap, no cross-bean awaits), it can call `cx.defer_until_ready(move |ready_cx| async move { /* build cross-bean index, async DB prep */ })` to push a closure/future-factory onto the ledger; or the macro turns an `#[after_singletons_ready]` method into an enqueue at the right point. The engine's finish-factory-init step, after the eager pass + background join, DRAINS the ledger: each work item runs lock-free against the read-only complete-graph `ReadyCx`, ordered by data (priority/topo/declaration). This reframes the hook from 'a trait the bean IS' to 'work the bean SCHEDULES', which is arguably more rust-native (closures + an async work queue) and decouples WHO declares the work from WHEN it runs. It naturally generalizes: a single bean can enqueue MULTIPLE ready-time work items (Spring's one-method-per-bean limit lifted); infrastructure that needs cross-bean aggregation (the @EventListener-discovery pass) enqueues a single sweep item; and the ledger is a uniform place to also hang refresh-lifecycle's 'after all singletons' instrumentation. Scoping is enforced at ENQUEUE time: only an eager-singleton's init can enqueue ready-work that is guaranteed to run (a lazy bean built on demand AFTER the drain has no drain to join — its enqueue either errors or is documented as a no-op, matching 'not fired for lazy-on-demand'). The drain is the single lock-free phase; prototypes/scoped beans, having no eager-init participating in the pass, never enqueue.

**Pros:** Most rust-native framing of 'do X once everything's up': an async work-ledger drained after graph completion is plain closures + a queue, no callback-into-erased-dyn-beans dance — and it lifts Spring's one-afterSingletonsInstantiated-method-per-bean limit (a bean can enqueue several independent ready-time work items, each with its own ordering).; Decouples WHO declares the work from the bean's identity: the work item is a closure capturing what it needs, so it doesn't require the bean to be invocable via an erased dyn handle nor a marker trait on the bean type — sidestepping R1's boxed-future-over-dyn-bean call and keeping the bean a plain POJO whose ready-work is just data it scheduled (§injection-styles ethos).; Enqueue-time scoping is sharp: only an eager singleton's init runs before the drain, so only it can enqueue work that joins the drain — 'not fired for lazy/prototype/scoped' becomes a structural property of WHEN enqueue happens relative to the single drain, not a ledger-walk filter.; The ledger is a natural unification point (§2.11): readiness instrumentation, @EventListener-discovery sweeps, and user warmup work are all just items the engine drains in one lock-free phase — one mechanism, many producers, without coupling to the events subsystem (unlike R2).; Lock-free complete-graph guarantee identical to R1/R2 (drain runs after eager pass + background join, no guard held), so the @PostConstruct-deadlock dissolution holds; and because work is enqueued as owned closures, cancellation/cleanup can attach per-item (a work item can carry its own async-cleanup, easing the no-async-Drop unwind).; Flexible ordering and dependency expression: items carry priority/declaration metadata and the engine topo-sorts the drain — and a single bean's multiple items can be individually ordered, richer than Spring's per-bean single callback.

**Cons:** Diverges most from Spring's mental model: developers know `implements SmartInitializingSingleton { afterSingletonsInstantiated() }`, not 'enqueue a deferred work closure during init' — lower intent-recognizability and a teaching cost (§2.1 DX), and the macro sugar (`#[after_singletons_ready] fn`) must hide a non-obvious 'this becomes an enqueue at init time' transformation, straining the thin-macro/legible-cargo-expand invariant (§2.10) more than R1's direct `impl`.; 'Enqueue during init' reintroduces a subtle coupling to the per-bean init phase that the feature exists to ESCAPE: if a bean enqueues from inside its (under-no-guard but still init-phase) setup, the timing of enqueue vs the bean's own publication must be precise, and a closure capturing `&self` / bean state raises ownership/lifetime questions (the work runs later, lock-free, so the captured state must outlive init and be safely shareable — likely forcing Arc/clone, in tension with §2.5 minimal-Arc).; A lazy or on-demand-built bean that 'wants' the ready hook has NO clean story: it builds after the drain, so its enqueue is a no-op/error — this is faithful to Spring's 'not fired for lazy-on-demand' for the common case but loses Spring's deliberate force-include of lazy SmartInitializingSingleton beans (SPR-7014 lineage), a real parity gap R1/R2 handle better.; More moving parts than R1 for the core contract: a ledger data structure, an enqueue API on the creation context, a drain phase, and per-item ordering/cleanup metadata — heavier than a marker trait + flag for what is fundamentally a run-once callback.; Closures-as-work-items are harder to introspect/diagnose than named trait impls: a failing or slow item is an anonymous closure unless the enqueue API forces a name/provenance — the rich-diagnostics invariant (§2.9) requires the ledger to capture declaration-site provenance for every item, adding API ceremony.; Risks encouraging MISUSE: a flexible 'enqueue arbitrary deferred work' primitive invites beans to push real runtime work (not just post-init aggregation) into the boot-time drain, blurring the 'build graph → finalize → run' phase discipline the feature is meant to instill.

**Mechanisms considered:** a per-container POST-INIT LEDGER of deferred async work items the engine drains once after the eager pass + background join, lock-free, against a read-only complete-graph `ReadyCx`; enqueue API on the creation context: `cx.defer_until_ready(|ready_cx| async move { ... })`; macro turns `#[after_singletons_ready] fn` into an enqueue at the bean's init point; ordering/cleanup/provenance metadata per work item (priority/topo/declaration-site name) — drained in data-resolved order, never enqueue order alone; enqueue-time scoping: only an eager singleton's init precedes the drain, so only it can enqueue work that joins (lazy/prototype/scoped enqueue is no-op/error); ledger as a unification point: readiness instrumentation, @EventListener-discovery sweeps, and user warmup are all drained items (no coupling to the events subsystem); per-item async-cleanup attachable to ease the no-async-Drop cancel/abort unwind

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The ENQUEUE happens at runtime during a bean's init, so the cross-crate story is lighter on the descriptor side than R1/R2: the macro for `#[after_singletons_ready]` emits an `impl`/registration that, when the bean is constructed, enqueues — but the PARTICIPATION is still ultimately DATA (the macro must emit something on the descriptor so the engine knows this bean's init will enqueue, both for the anti-DCE self-check and so a never-constructed (DCE'd) bean's missing enqueue is detectable). leaf-core defines the ledger, the `defer_until_ready` API on the creation context, the `ReadyCx`, and the drain boundary; the thin macro emits the enqueue glue via absolute paths (§99-102). Silent-DCE hazard is the same and arguably WORSE-to-diagnose: a dropped crate's beans never construct, so they never enqueue, so cross-bean aggregation silently doesn't happen — and because the work is anonymous closures, the self-check must rely on expected-enqueue counts/provenance flags on descriptors (§152), making the descriptor flag still necessary despite the runtime-enqueue framing. Generics-cannot-participate inherited (a generic bean's `#[after_singletons_ready]` can't register a concrete enqueuer until instantiated; macro hard-errors §153). Origin-agnostic: a WASM-module bean's init enqueues through the same `defer_until_ready` API, identical to native. Two-source duality applies to the descriptor flags that drive the self-check (static + dynamic source), CANDIDATES not a decision. The closure-capture model raises a T1-adjacent constraint: captured state crossing into the later drain must be `Send`/`'static` enough for whatever executor drains the ledger — narrowing the bean-handle/Send-Sync keystone (Phase 2) more than R1's trait call does.

**T2 — execution context:** Two execution moments. (1) ENQUEUE runs DURING a bean's init phase on the bootstrap task — it must be cheap and non-blocking (just pushing a work item), preserving the 'init = validate wiring' contract; it does NOT do the cross-bean work, only schedules it. (2) DRAIN runs on the bootstrap task at finish-factory-init, after the eager pass + background join, OUTSIDE any creation guard — each work item is an explicit `.await` point against the read-only complete graph, lock-free (the @PostConstruct-deadlock dissolution, §2.5/§2.3); blocking items use spawn_blocking. Because items are owned closures capturing what they need, captured state must be safely shareable into the drain (likely Arc/clone of the needed handles — a §2.5 minimal-Arc tension flagged for Phase 2) and `Send`/`'static` for the draining executor. CANCELLATION is cleaner per-item than R1/R2: a work item can carry its own async-cleanup, so cancelling the drain (or aborting refresh) can run each in-flight item's cleanup explicitly (no async Drop), and un-drained items simply never run (nothing to clean). ERRORS: a failing drained item aborts refresh all-or-nothing with a rich diagnostic — but only if the enqueue API captured declaration-site provenance, since the item is otherwise an anonymous closure (§2.9 requires forcing a name/origin per item). Ordering is data-resolved (priority/topo/declaration) for deterministic drain; sequential-by-default for diagnosability, optional concurrent fan-out (complete read-only graph) deferred to Phase 2. The drain happens exactly once per refresh; a lazy bean built AFTER the drain (on first get) has missed it — its enqueue is a documented no-op/error, the faithful 'not fired for lazy-on-demand' behavior but losing Spring's lazy-force-include nuance. Prototypes/scoped beans never enqueue ready-work (no eager init in the pass).

### Rust clashes
- The whole REASON this hook exists is JVM-shaped and gets WORSE in async Rust, which sharpens (not weakens) the case for it. Spring's @PostConstruct runs UNDER the singleton creation lock against a PARTIAL graph; cross-bean/blocking work there risks an init-time deadlock, and the deliberate escape is this lock-free, complete-graph callback. In async Rust the under-guard hazard is worse — an init future that `.await`s while a creation guard is held can tie up an executor worker and deadlock the whole pool — so leaf MUST keep per-bean init lock-free-of-user-await (bean-instantiation's job) AND provide this post-all-singletons phase as the sanctioned home for heavy cross-bean async work. The clash is that the hazard's NATURE changes (lock-ordering deadlock → executor starvation), but the hook's intent and lock-free-after-complete-graph placement carry over directly and become load-bearing for correctness, not just ergonomics.
- Spring FINDS participants by instanceof over LIVE beans (iterate every singleton instance, check `instanceof SmartInitializingSingleton`); Rust has no runtime type-walk over erased beans. Participation must be DATA: the macro emits a descriptor flag (R1/R3) or a subscriber descriptor (R2) so the engine reads a flag rather than inspecting live types. This is the same instanceof→descriptor-flag clash that recurs across the catalog, and it means a WASM-module/test-double bean that wants the hook is discovered identically (origin-agnostic) — but it also means a generic bean cannot participate (no `'static` symbol/TypeId until concretely instantiated; macro hard-errors with a per-instantiation escape hatch).
- The async hook MUST be invoked over an erased/dyn-stored bean handle, but AFIT (async fn in trait) is not dyn-safe → a boxed future at the dyn boundary (R1's marker-trait call, R2's subscriber dispatch). This is cold and once-per-refresh over few participants, so the alloc is negligible — but it commits the hook's signature to leaf's deferred async-across-dyn boxing standard (a Phase-2 keystone shared with bean-instantiation/bean-registry), and it's the reason R3 explores a closure-ledger framing that avoids calling back into the erased bean at all (at the cost of closure-capture/Send/'static constraints instead).
- No async Drop makes a FAILED or CANCELLED ready-hook a partial-cleanup hazard, even though this phase runs after the all-or-nothing eager pass. A ready-hook that has already kicked off async resources (opened a DB connection for cross-bean prep, spawned an index-build task) and then fails or is cancelled cannot self-clean via Drop — the refresh cancel-path must run the hook's registered async cleanup explicitly, and the all-or-nothing boot guarantee is only as strong as that explicit unwind. R3's per-work-item attachable cleanup is a partial mitigation; R1/R2 inherit bean-instantiation/context-close's reverse-order teardown ledger discipline.
- Spring's lazy-SmartInitializingSingleton FORCE-INCLUDE nuance has no clean Rust analogue under a strictly-eager firing model. Spring Boot's global-lazy flag deliberately EXCLUDES SmartInitializingSingleton beans (and ROLE_INFRASTRUCTURE beans) from being made lazy, so a bean whose whole point is the ready-hook still gets built eagerly and fired (SPR-7014 declined). In leaf, if the hook fires strictly over the eager-singleton ledger, a bean the user marked @Lazy that ALSO wants the ready-hook is contradictory — leaf must either force-include such beans into the eager pass (matching Spring, requires the participation flag to also gate laziness, coupling to lazy-initialization) or document that @Lazy + ready-hook is rejected. R3 handles this worst (enqueue-during-init means a lazy bean simply misses the drain).
- 'A complete graph the hook can query safely' assumes a registry-query API (collection-injection / autowiring-resolution) usable from a read-only complete-graph view — but leaf's registry/handle substrate is undecided. Spring's guarantee is that `getBeansOfType()` here can't trigger half-built peers because all eager singletons exist; replicating it requires the `ReadyCx` to expose lock-free reads over whatever handle type Phase 2 picks (Arc<dyn> / arena slot / borrow), and to define what happens when the hook queries a genuinely-@Lazy bean (Spring builds it on demand — leaf must decide if a ready-hook may trigger lazy creation, and on which task). The query-safety guarantee is real intent; its mechanism is blocked on the deferred registry keystone.
- Spring conflates 'the callback' and 'the discovery of who implements it' in one runtime instanceof sweep; leaf must split them, and the SWEEP ITSELF is a clash. Spring's EventListenerMethodProcessor (a SmartInitializingSingleton) iterates EVERY singleton at ready-time to reflectively find @EventListener methods. leaf has no reflective method scan — @EventListener discovery must be compile-time (macro-emitted listener descriptors), so leaf's analogue does NOT need a runtime all-beans sweep to find listeners. This means the dogfooding motivation for the hook (R2's 'the listener processor is just a subscriber') is partly vestigial in Rust: leaf might discover listeners at build/link time and use the ready-hook only for genuine cross-bean AGGREGATION (building indexes from the now-complete set), not for method discovery — a place where intent parity does NOT imply mechanism parity.
- Firing 'exactly once, after EVERY eager singleton exists' interacts with background-bootstrap's concurrent init and any mid-bootstrap definition registration. Spring v7 joins the @Bean(BACKGROUND) CompletableFutures before the Phase-2 smart-init loop; leaf must define the precise barrier — the hook fires only after ALL eager singletons (including background-built ones, and including any registered mid-bootstrap if leaf keeps that dynamism) are published. If a background-built bean itself participates in the hook, the barrier (join all background → then fire ready-hooks, including the background bean's own) must be exact, and if the registry can mutate mid-bootstrap (bean-registry's deferred mutable-vs-frozen question), 'every eager singleton' is a moving target the firing point must pin down.

### Interactions
- **bean-instantiation** — PRIMARY UPSTREAM DRIVER. bean-instantiation OWNS the eager pre-instantiation pass whose SECOND loop fires this hook; it provides the 'all eager singletons now exist and are published' boundary (after pass-1 + background-bootstrap join) at which the hook runs, explicitly OUTSIDE any creation guard. This feature contributes only the callback CONTRACT and the participation/scoping rules; bean-instantiation invokes it at the structurally-correct moment. The dissolution of Spring's @PostConstruct-under-lock trap is shared: bean-instantiation keeps per-bean init lock-free-of-user-await, and routes heavy cross-bean work here.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — UPSTREAM ORCHESTRATOR. refresh-lifecycle's finish-factory-init phase drives the eager pass and thus this hook as its penultimate sub-step (before finish-refresh starts the runtime lifecycle and publishes the refreshed milestone). A failing ready-hook aborts refresh all-or-nothing via refresh-lifecycle's cancel path (Failed-not-Closed), and the no-async-Drop partial-cleanup unwind on a failed/cancelled ready-hook rides refresh-lifecycle's reverse-order teardown ledger. The hook fires strictly inside finish-factory-init, BEFORE finish-refresh.
- **lifecycle-callbacks** — SIBLING / CONTRAST. lifecycle-callbacks owns the per-bean ordered init chain (@PostConstruct-equivalent), which runs DURING each bean's creation against a PARTIAL graph and (in Spring) under the lock. This feature is its deliberate COUNTERPART: the registry-wide, complete-graph, lock-free phase for the work that must NOT go in per-bean init. The mental model split — per-bean setup in init, cross-bean aggregation in the ready-hook — is the precise division of labor between the two features.
- **lifecycle-events** — NEIGHBOUR / ALTERNATIVE (and R2's substrate). Spring offers `@EventListener(ContextRefreshedEvent.class)` as the documented equivalent of SmartInitializingSingleton; lifecycle-events owns the context-refreshed/started/availability milestones. R2 explicitly unifies this hook WITH lifecycle-events as one 'context milestone + subscribers' primitive. Even under R1/R3, the timing relationship matters: the ready-hook fires inside finish-factory-init, whereas the ContextRefreshed milestone fires later at finish-refresh — a distinction Spring preserves and leaf must decide whether to keep or collapse.
- **events** — DOGFOODING NEIGHBOUR. In Spring, EventListenerMethodProcessor (which discovers @EventListener methods across all beans) IS a SmartInitializingSingleton — the marquee internal user of this hook. R2 makes leaf's @EventListener-discovery a subscriber to the readiness milestone, dogfooding the events substrate. But a rustClash notes leaf likely discovers @EventListener methods at COMPILE/LINK time (macro-emitted descriptors), so leaf does NOT need a runtime all-beans sweep — meaning the dogfooding motivation is partly vestigial and the ready-hook serves genuine cross-bean AGGREGATION more than method discovery.
- **event-multicaster** — R2 SUBSTRATE. Under R2, the SingletonsReady milestone is dispatched once by the event-multicaster to its subscribers. The tension: the multicaster's default error model (listeners independent, errors collected, dispatch continues) conflicts with this hook's needed all-or-nothing fail-fast (a failed readiness hook aborts refresh), so the readiness dispatch needs a distinct fail-fast mode. Not relevant under R1/R3 (direct second loop / ledger drain).
- **lazy-initialization** — SCOPING CONSTRAINT + CLASH. This hook fires ONLY for eager singletons, never lazy-on-demand ones — so lazy-initialization's @Lazy/global-lazy flag GATES participation. The clash (Spring's SPR-7014 force-include of SmartInitializingSingleton beans from global-lazy): a bean that is @Lazy but wants the ready-hook is contradictory; leaf must either force-include such beans into the eager pass (coupling the participation flag to laziness) or reject @Lazy + ready-hook. R3 handles this worst (a lazy bean built after the drain simply misses it).
- **background-bootstrap** — BARRIER COUPLING. The hook fires only after ALL eager singletons exist, INCLUDING background-built ones — so the firing point must come after background-bootstrap's concurrent-init futures have JOINED (Spring v7's CompletableFuture.allOf(...).join() before the Phase-2 loop). If a background-built bean itself participates, the join→fire barrier must be exact. background-bootstrap supplies the concurrency; this feature defines the post-join barrier at which the complete-graph guarantee holds.
- **collection-injection** — DOWNSTREAM CONSUMER (the canonical use). The ready-hook's whole value is safely querying the registry to AGGREGATE collaborators — Spring's `getBeansOfType()` (build a cross-bean index from all matching beans). collection-injection provides the List/Map/Set-of-all-matching resolution the `ReadyCx` exposes; this feature guarantees it is safe HERE (every eager singleton exists, so no half-built peer is triggered) in a way it would not be inside a per-bean @PostConstruct.
- **autowiring-resolution** — DOWNSTREAM CONSUMER. The read-only complete-graph `ReadyCx` exposes single-valued lookups (`get::<T>()`) backed by autowiring-resolution's selection policy. The complete-graph guarantee means such a lookup cannot trigger creation of a half-built eager peer; the open question (a rustClash) is whether a ready-hook querying a genuinely-@Lazy bean may trigger its on-demand creation (Spring does), and on which task.
- **startup-instrumentation** — INSTRUMENTED. Spring v7 wraps each afterSingletonsInstantiated in an ApplicationStartup step `spring.beans.smart-initialize`; leaf mirrors this with a named step (`leaf.beans.smart-initialize`) per participant/work-item. startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default timed-step tree; this feature emits the ready-phase steps and exposes per-participant introspection so both instrumentation and the anti-DCE self-check (expected-vs-found participant count) are writable.
- **concurrency-contract** — RELIES ON. The hook runs lock-free AFTER safe publication of every eager singleton (concurrency-contract guarantees fully-built singletons are visible across threads). This is what makes the complete-graph read-only queries valid: the hook observes finished, safely-published instances, not partially-constructed ones — concurrency-contract frames the publication guarantee this phase depends on.
- **context-close** — CLEANUP COUPLING. Because there is no async Drop, a ready-hook (or R3 work-item) that acquired async resources and then failed/was-cancelled must have its cleanup run explicitly — riding the same reverse-order teardown ledger context-close owns for clean shutdown and refresh-lifecycle reuses for the cancel path. A ready-hook that successfully built cross-bean state may also need that state torn down at close, so its cleanup registration interacts with context-close's ordering.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — ORDERING NEIGHBOUR. The ready-hook (build graph → finalize) runs BEFORE runtime-lifecycle's SmartLifecycle auto-start (→ run): Spring's clean phased model is build → finalize → run. The hook is the 'finalize against a complete static graph' phase; runtime-lifecycle is the subsequent 'start the running components' phase. If leaf keeps two milestones (R2), SingletonsReady precedes auto-start and ContextRefreshed follows it.
- **scopes** — SCOPING CONSTRAINT. The hook is fired ONLY for SINGLETON scope (and only eager ones) — never prototype/request/session/custom scopes. scopes fixes per-scope multiplicity; this feature's participation is structurally limited to the eager-singleton set, so a prototype or web-scoped bean implementing the marker is never fired (in R3, such beans have no eager init in the pass to enqueue from).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the all-singletons-ready hook a BESPOKE marker-trait callback (R1), a UNIFIED post-refresh lifecycle MILESTONE on the shared event/lifecycle bus of which it is one subscriber (R2, matching Spring's own SmartInitializingSingleton≈ContextRefreshedEvent duality), or a post-init WORK-LEDGER the engine drains (R3)? This is the §2.11 unification call viewed through one hook — and it cross-cuts events, event-multicaster, lifecycle-events, and whether the framework has ONE 'context is ready' primitive or several. Choosing here would pre-judge the events-vs-lifecycle meta-architecture, so it must stay a CANDIDATE.
- Does leaf preserve Spring's distinction between the ready-hook (fires inside finish-factory-init, before SmartLifecycle auto-start) and the ContextRefreshed milestone (fires at finish-refresh, after auto-start), or collapse them into one milestone? Some beans rely on 'graph complete but components not yet running' vs 'everything running'. R2's unification may need TWO ordered milestones anyway. Shared with lifecycle-events and runtime-lifecycle.
- How is the async hook invoked over an erased/dyn-stored bean handle given AFIT is not dyn-safe — boxed-future-over-dyn-bean (R1/R2) or a closure work-ledger that avoids calling back into the erased bean entirely (R3, at the cost of closure-capture Send/'static constraints)? This is leaf's deferred async-across-dyn boxing standard viewed through this hook. Shared with bean-instantiation, bean-registry, scopes.
- What is the complete-graph `ReadyCx` query contract, given the deferred registry/handle keystone? Spring guarantees getBeansOfType here can't trigger half-built peers because all eager singletons exist — but may a ready-hook trigger on-demand creation of a genuinely-@Lazy bean (Spring does), and on which task? The query-safety guarantee is real intent; its realization is blocked on the registry/handle substrate (Arc<dyn> vs arena slot vs borrow) and on collection-injection/autowiring-resolution's read-only view shape.
- How is the @Lazy + ready-hook contradiction resolved — force-include participating beans into the eager pass (matching Spring's SPR-7014 EXCLUDE-from-global-lazy behavior, coupling the participation flag to laziness) or reject the combination? R3's enqueue-during-init makes a lazy bean miss the drain entirely, a parity gap. Shared with lazy-initialization and bean-instantiation.
- What is the EXACT firing barrier relative to background-bootstrap and any mid-bootstrap definition registration? The hook must fire only after ALL eager singletons (including background-built and any mid-bootstrap-registered) are published — so the join-all-background→fire-ready-hooks barrier must be pinned, especially if a background-built bean itself participates, and if bean-registry stays mutable mid-bootstrap 'every eager singleton' is a moving target. Shared with background-bootstrap and bean-registry.
- What is the cancellation + partial-cleanup contract for a failed/cancelled ready-hook (or R3 work-item) given no async Drop, and does its error abort refresh all-or-nothing? A hook that already acquired async resources must run explicit cleanup on the cancel/abort path; R2's event-multicaster default error model (collect-and-continue) conflicts with the needed fail-fast. Shared with refresh-lifecycle's cancel ledger and context-close.
- Does the dogfooding rationale survive in Rust — should leaf's @EventListener discovery use this hook (R2) or is it compile/link-time (macro-emitted descriptors), making the ready-hook serve genuine cross-bean AGGREGATION rather than runtime method-discovery? If @EventListener is discovered statically, leaf needs no runtime all-beans sweep, and the hook's role narrows. Shared with events and the metadata/codegen boundary.
- Do multiple participants' ready-hooks run SEQUENTIALLY (deterministic diagnostics) or CONCURRENTLY (the graph is complete and read-only, so independence is plausible)? And how is cross-participant ordering expressed (priority/@Order/topo as data, never registration order)? This is the async-execution-model viewed through the ready phase. Shared with the deferred async-execution model.
- How does the anti-DCE self-check cover this hook specifically — what is the expected-vs-found participant/subscriber/enqueuer count manifest, and where does it live (facade vs generated app-root)? A silently-DCE'd contributing crate's ready-hooks never fire, so cross-bean aggregation silently doesn't happen (the worst silent-failure symptom); the cross-crate doc mandates an expected-count self-check, and R3's anonymous-closure framing makes provenance capture mandatory. Shared with container-core, startup-instrumentation, and the registry substrate.
- Can the same bean register MULTIPLE ready-time work items (R3 lifts Spring's one-method-per-bean limit) and is that desirable, or does leaf hold to Spring's single-callback-per-bean for predictability? Richer expression vs simpler mental model — a DX call. Shared with the unification stance (R1/R2 vs R3).

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated feature section twice — the `SmartInitializingSingleton and Lifecycle` section (902-910: the single run-once callback after all eager singletons exist, outside the per-bean creation lock, against a complete graph, the build→finalize→run mental model, and the ContextRefreshedEvent alternative) — plus the surrounding mechanics that define the hook's body and its motivation: the preInstantiateSingletons two-pass loop with Phase-2 firing afterSingletonsInstantiated and the eager-singletons-only / not-lazy-not-prototype scoping and the v7 background-join + spring.beans.smart-initialize instrumentation (2466-2477); the @PostConstruct-under-the-singleton-lock deadlock trap that this hook exists to escape and the explicit lock-free escape hatches (374-376, 882, 1956-1974, 2502-2504); the dogfooding fact that EventListenerMethodProcessor IS a SmartInitializingSingleton (1814); and the Boot global-lazy EXCLUDE of SmartInitializingSingleton/ROLE_INFRASTRUCTURE beans (2591, the SPR-7014 force-include nuance). I grounded T1 against the cross-crate doc (no-runtime-instanceof / participation-as-data, the silent-DCE #1-failure-mode + mandatory expected-count self-check §152, thin-macro/absolute-path ABI §99-102, linkme-default §166, generics-cannot-register §153, ordering-as-data-via-topo-sort §170, scope-discovery-not-implicitly-global §36, Architectures A/B/C/D composable §335) and T2 against the charter invariants (§2.3 async-first/explicit-execution-context, §2.5 no-global-lock/minimal-Arc, §2.9 rich-early diagnostics, §2.10 thin-macros, §2.11 unification) and the stable envelope (AFIT/RPITIT-not-dyn-safe forcing boxed-future hook calls §12-13/§78, Arc<dyn Any> registry §17/81, trait upcasting 1.86 §11/80, const TypeId 1.91 §16/81, no async Drop as a first-class constraint §86).

CRITICAL SCOPING NOTE for Phase 2/3: this feature is DELIBERATELY THIN — it overlaps bean-instantiation and refresh-lifecycle, which are its provided upstream dependencies (their designs are given and I built on them concretely, not reinvented). bean-instantiation OWNS the eager pass and the per-bean creation sequence/guard; refresh-lifecycle OWNS the fixed-order template whose finish-factory-init step drives the eager pass. smart-initializing contributes ONLY three things on top: (1) the run-once, lock-free, complete-graph callback CONTRACT; (2) the complete-graph QUERY-SAFETY guarantee that makes registry aggregation safe here (vs the half-built graph + held guard inside @PostConstruct); (3) the SCOPING (eager-singletons-only).
