My three realizations therefore diverge on the SHAPE OF THE HOOK ITSELF (not the eager pass, which is bean-instantiation's): R1 = a literal `AfterSingletonsReady` marker trait fired as the eager pass's second loop over a participation-flagged descriptor set (highest Spring fidelity, most teachable, thin macro = `impl` + flag, but a per-feature mechanism that risks duplicating ContextRefreshedEvent); R2 = a UNIFIED post-refresh lifecycle MILESTONE on the shared event/lifecycle bus of which the per-bean hook and the ContextRefreshed listener are two subscriber shapes (maximal §2.11 unification, dogfoods events exactly as Spring's EventListenerMethodProcessor does, but couples a core bring-up guarantee to the unsettled events subsystem and may need two milestones to preserve Spring's timing distinction); R3 = a post-init WORK-LEDGER beans enqueue into during init and the engine drains once lock-free (most rust-native via closures + an async queue, lifts Spring's one-callback-per-bean limit, eases per-item cancel-cleanup, but diverges most from Spring's mental model, strains the thin-macro invariant, and handles the @Lazy nuance worst). R1↔R2 differ on bespoke-callback vs unified-event-subscription; R3 differs on callback-into-bean vs work-the-bean-schedules. R2 can host R1's hook as one subscriber shape; R3's ledger can host R1's participants as enqueuers — genuinely divergent yet Phase-2-composable.

Three things to carry forward: (1) The hook's RAISON D'ÊTRE is sharper in async Rust, not weaker — Spring's @PostConstruct-under-lock deadlock becomes EXECUTOR STARVATION (an init `.await` under a held creation guard can deadlock the pool), so the lock-free, complete-graph, post-all-singletons placement is load-bearing for CORRECTNESS, and this feature is the sanctioned home that lets bean-instantiation keep per-bean init free of user awaits-under-guard. (2) Participation MUST be DATA (a descriptor flag / subscriber descriptor), because Rust has no instanceof-over-live-beans to find who implements the marker — this is origin-agnostic (WASM-module/test-double beans participate identically) but blocks generics (no 'static symbol until concrete; macro hard-errors) and makes the anti-DCE self-check essential (a dropped crate's ready-hooks silently never fire = cross-bean aggregation silently doesn't happen, the worst 'plain BeanFactory' symptom). (3) The @Lazy + ready-hook contradiction and the background-join firing barrier are the two subtlest parity points: Spring force-includes SmartInitializingSingleton beans from global-lazy (SPR-7014) and joins background futures before the Phase-2 loop — leaf must pin both, and R3's enqueue-during-init handles lazy worst (a lazy bean built after the drain misses it entirely).

I refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps participation as DATA (substrate-neutral across linkme/codegen/explicit and Arch A/B/C/D), keeps the hook invocation/handle abstract (the async-across-dyn boxing and registry/handle keystones stay deferred), keeps the ReadyCx query contract dependent on the undecided registry substrate, and keeps the unify-with-lifecycle-events question open (R2 is a CANDIDATE, not a decision). The two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source) is captured as multiple producers of the SAME participating-descriptor set the one second-loop/dispatch/drain consumes. Naming ('AfterSingletonsReady', 'after_singletons_ready', 'ReadyCx', 'ReadyError', 'SingletonsReady', 'fire_after_singletons_ready', 'defer_until_ready', 'on_ready', 'after_singletons_ready', 'leaf.beans.smart-initialize') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (902-910 the SmartInitializingSingleton+Lifecycle section; 374-376 init-under-lock + heavy-work-belongs-after; 882 publishable-only-after-PostConstruct-under-lock; 1814 EventListenerMethodProcessor-is-a-SmartInitializingSingleton; 1956-1974 the @PostConstruct deadlock trap + lock-free escape hatches + design intent; 2466-2477 the preInstantiateSingletons two-pass loop + scoping + v7 background-join/instrumentation; 2502-2504 init-under-lock heavy-work-belongs-after; 2591 Boot global-lazy excludes SmartInitializingSingleton/infrastructure beans, SPR-7014); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. 2.3/2.5/2.9/2.10/2.11, deferred keystones §3, topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (§36 scope-discovery-not-global, §99-102 thin-macro/absolute-path ABI, §152 silent-DCE + mandatory self-check, §153 generics-cannot-register, §166 linkme-default, §170 ordering-as-data-via-topo-sort, §277-335 Architectures A/B/C/D composable); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (§11 trait upcasting 1.86, §12-13 RPITIT/AFIT stable-but-not-dyn-safe, §16-17 const TypeId 1.91 / Arc<dyn Any>, §86 no async Drop first-class constraint).

## Runtime Lifecycle (Lifecycle / SmartLifecycle)  `[runtime-lifecycle]`

Spring's `Lifecycle`/`SmartLifecycle` layer adds a SECOND, RUNTIME state machine ON TOP OF the static singleton graph that refresh-lifecycle builds — deliberately separating bean CREATION (the DI graph; init/destroy) from runtime ACTIVITY (servers listening, schedulers ticking, consumers polling), because "start serving traffic" has different ordering needs than "construct & wire", and overloading init/destroy for it would be wrong. THIS feature is the running-state machine (the phase-grouped start/stop choreography, auto-start, graceful drain) — DISTINCT from refresh-lifecycle (which only TRIGGERS auto-start at finishRefresh and the handoff back), from context-close (which TRIGGERS reverse-phase stop before destruction), from lifecycle-events (which PUBLISHES Started/Stopped facts), and from lifecycle-callbacks (init/destroy, a different axis entirely). The precise intent to replicate: (1) TWO CONTRACTS — `Lifecycle` = {start(), stop(), isRunning()}, NOT auto-started, treated as phase 0; `SmartLifecycle` extends it with isAutoStartup() (default TRUE), getPhase() (default `DEFAULT_PHASE` = Integer.MAX_VALUE, NOT 0), an ASYNC graceful stop(callback) that the processor calls INSTEAD OF the no-arg stop(), and (7.0) isPauseable() (default true). (2) PHASE ORDERING — lower phase STARTS FIRST and STOPS LAST; higher phase starts last and stops first. The MAX_VALUE default means ordinary SmartLifecycle beans start late / stop early — the safe default for a component depending on everything else already running (a network listener starts last, stops first: it stops accepting work before its collaborators vanish). depends-on overrides phase. (3) AUTO-START at end of bring-up — the lifecycle processor starts all auto-start SmartLifecycle beans by ASCENDING phase, each phase group as a unit, after the singleton graph is complete (the refresh-lifecycle handoff). A lazy SmartLifecycle with autoStartup is still started (lazy barely affects it). (4) GRACEFUL PHASED STOP — stop runs DESCENDING phase; within each phase group, stop(callback) is invoked on every member and the processor WAITS until all callbacks fire OR a PER-PHASE timeout elapses (default 10s, per-phase NOT global, tunable) before touching the next-lower phase — so a web server can DRAIN in-flight work before its collaborators stop. Within-phase stop is ordered-and-potentially-CONCURRENT (the original 3.0 semantics; a per-phase latch coordinates async completion). (5) PAUSE/RESTART (7.0) — context-wide pause()/restart() over the same phase machinery (CRaC checkpoint/restore builds on this); isPauseable()==false opts a component out of pause (it gets stop only on real close), as Boot's web-server lifecycles do. (6) THE IMMEDIATE-DESTROY-WITHOUT-STOP HAZARD — a Lifecycle bean MUST tolerate destroy WITHOUT a preceding stop (cancelled bootstrap, stop timeout, hot refresh); drain logic placed only in stop() can be silently bypassed; runtime-mutable lifecycle state must be safely published. (7) WEB-SERVER PHASE BAND — graceful-drain and start/stop sit at MAX-1024 / MAX-2048 so app components quiesce, THEN requests drain, THEN the socket closes. leaf must replicate this INTENT — a phase-grouped, auto-started, gracefully-async-stoppable RUNNING-state machine layered above the singleton graph, with the lower-starts-first/stops-last ordering, per-phase concurrent drain with per-phase timeout, the autoStart/phase/pauseable knobs, and the tolerate-destroy-without-stop guarantee — WITHOUT Spring's JVM lineage: the CountDownLatch + blocking thread-park drain (in async leaf a phase group is a structured-concurrency JoinSet awaited with a per-phase timeout, never a parked OS thread), the synchronized monitor (no global lock), the assumption stop()/start() are sync (they are async in leaf), and the volatile-field discipline (replaced by safe-publication via the ownership/concurrency model). It must drive an ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC population of lifecycle participants (a WASM-module-provided lifecycle component must be started/stopped at the same phase), discovered via a SCOPED registry (static link-time PLUS optional dynamic source), never implicitly global. It must NOT pick the registry substrate, the async-execution/cancellation model, whether phases are a fixed integer axis or a richer dependency-ordered structure, how pause/restart is realized, or the teardown substrate it shares with context-close — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Async-trait running-state machine over an integer phase axis: a LifecycleProcessor groups participants by phase and start-ascends / stop-descends each group as a structured-concurrency unit
Mirror Spring's two-contract model directly in idiomatic async Rust. Two object-safe traits: `Lifecycle { async fn start(&self); async fn stop(&self); fn is_running(&self) -> bool; }` and `SmartLifecycle: Lifecycle + Phased` adding `fn is_auto_startup(&self) -> bool { true }`, `fn phase(&self) -> Phase { Phase::DEFAULT }`, `fn is_pauseable(&self) -> bool { true }`, and a graceful `async fn stop_graceful(&self) -> Result<(), StopError>` that REPLACES the plain stop for smart beans (the rust-native form of Spring's stop(Runnable): the future RESOLVING IS the callback firing — no Runnable, no latch-per-bean; completion is the await). `Phase` is a newtype over i32 with `DEFAULT = i32::MAX` (the documented MAX_VALUE default, so ordinary smart beans start last / stop first) and named bands for the web-server tier (DEFAULT-1024 graceful-drain, DEFAULT-2048 start/stop) shipped as constants. Because AFIT/async-fn-in-dyn-trait is not dyn-safe on the public surface (nightly doc), the trait surface uses the trait_variant Send-variant + a dynosaur-style dyn bridge, OR leaf's thin macro hand-emits `Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>` — so a `dyn SmartLifecycle` participant works regardless of origin (native OR a WASM-module-backed shim). A `LifecycleProcessor` (itself an infrastructure component, the dogfooding pattern) holds the participant set as ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC descriptors (each: phase, autoStart, pauseable flags, a handle to the dyn participant), buckets them by phase into ordered groups, and exposes `async fn start_all()`, `async fn stop_all()`, `async fn pause()`, `async fn restart()`. start_all iterates phase groups ASCENDING; for each group it spawns every member's start() into a structured-concurrency scope (a JoinSet) and awaits the whole group before advancing — so a phase fully starts before the next. stop_all iterates DESCENDING; for each group it spawns every member's stop_graceful() into a scope and awaits with `select!`-over-a-per-phase `timeout` (the per-phase-not-global drain timeout, default 10s, tunable per phase) — when the timeout fires, the still-pending members are recorded (a diagnostic), the group is abandoned, and the next-lower phase proceeds (Spring's exact 'proceed after timeout' semantics, but via tokio::time::timeout over a JoinSet, NOT a parked thread + CountDownLatch). is_running() is read straight from each participant (it owns its own atomic/cell — safe-published per the ownership model, NOT a volatile field). The processor NEVER holds a lock across a participant's start/stop await (the §2.5 / under-lock-await discipline); it only guards its own group-iteration cursor. The IMMEDIATE-DESTROY-WITHOUT-STOP guarantee is honored at the seam with context-close: context-close calls stop_all() THEN destroys; but a participant's destroy/disposable path must be written to tolerate not having been stopped (cancelled bring-up, drain timeout) — leaf surfaces this as a documented contract on the participant trait, exactly Spring's hazard made explicit. pause()/restart() (7.0) reuse start_all/stop_all but SKIP participants whose is_pauseable()==false (they keep running across a pause and only stop on real close).

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity at the lowest conceptual cost: the two traits, the autoStart/phase/pauseable knobs, the MAX-default ordering, the per-phase concurrent drain with per-phase timeout, and the start-ascend/stop-descend choreography map 1:1 to Spring — maximally teachable, `cargo expand` of the thin participant macro reads like a hand-written impl (DX #1, §2.10).; stop_graceful as `async fn -> Result` is the honest rust-native replacement for Spring's stop(Runnable): the future resolving IS the callback firing, so there is no Runnable plumbing, no per-bean latch, and a drain that errors propagates a typed StopError instead of silently completing — strictly richer diagnostics than Spring's fire-and-forget callback (§2.9).; Per-phase drain becomes a structured-concurrency JoinSet awaited under tokio::time::timeout: within-phase concurrency (Spring's 'potentially concurrent shutdown of common-phase components') falls out for free, the per-phase-not-global timeout is a literal `timeout()` per group, and a stuck participant cancels cleanly at the group boundary rather than parking an OS thread (§2.3 async-first, §2.4 the drain wait is a cold-path await on a notification, never a poll).; No global lock and no across-await lock (§2.5): the processor guards only its own phase cursor, never wraps a participant's start/stop await — dissolving the Spring under-lock-callback deadlock risk by execution context, not by a lock; is_running is the participant's own safe-published cell, not a volatile field the framework reaches into.; Origin-agnostic by construction: a participant is a `dyn SmartLifecycle` behind a descriptor, so a native bean, a test double, or a WASM-module-backed shim are all started/stopped at the same phase with no special case — the trait surface (via trait_variant + dyn bridge) keeps the dyn-async dispatch working across origins.; Composes cleanly with the provided neighbour designs: refresh-lifecycle's finish_refresh calls start_all() (the auto-start handoff); context-close calls stop_all() before reverse-order destroy; lifecycle-events gets Started/Stopped facts emitted at the group boundaries — each is a single call into this processor, keeping the orchestration ownership where the upstream designs already put it.

**Cons:** The integer phase axis is exactly Spring's model and carries its sharpest footgun: phases are an opaque global i32 ordering with no declared dependency, so a participant at the wrong phase starts/stops in a surprising order with NO diagnostic — leaf inherits the 'DEFAULT is MAX_VALUE not 0' confusion and the depends-on-overrides-phase special case, neither of which the type system can catch (a real DX cost vs R2's explicit dependencies).; stop_graceful returning before its work is truly drained is unverifiable — the contract is 'the future resolves when drained', but a buggy participant that resolves early defeats the drain just as Spring's early-fired callback does; leaf can time it and warn on suspiciously-fast drains but cannot enforce true quiescence (the same trust boundary Spring has).; The dyn-async trait surface forces the trait_variant + dynosaur (or hand-emitted BoxFuture) layer onto every participant — a per-participant boxing/Pin cost on start/stop (cold path, so acceptable) plus the ergonomic friction of the Send-variant bound, and it pins leaf to that stable workaround until RTN stabilizes (nightly doc).; Pause/restart layered as 'start_all/stop_all minus non-pauseable' is a thin reuse but the SEMANTICS of a paused-but-not-stopped participant (its is_running, its in-flight work, what a second pause does) are subtle and Spring-7-new — under-specifying them here risks an incoherent pause model that R3's explicit state machine would have made total.; Two teardown triggers (context-close's stop_all-before-destroy vs a cancelled-bring-up that destroys WITHOUT stop) must stay coherent with the immediate-destroy-without-stop contract; getting the participant trait's 'tolerate destroy without stop' guarantee wrong leaks the drain that lived only in stop() — Spring's documented hazard carried over and now load-bearing under no-async-Drop.; An i32 phase axis prejudges nothing about the registry substrate but DOES bake in 'ordering is a scalar' — if Phase 2's unification wants one ordering primitive shared with collection-injection (@Order) and auto-config-ordering (topological before/after), a bare integer is less than that primitive and may need rework (a unification tension, §2.11).

**Mechanisms considered:** two object-safe traits `Lifecycle` and `SmartLifecycle: Lifecycle + Phased` with async start/stop and a graceful `async fn stop_graceful() -> Result<(), StopError>` (the future resolving = Spring's stop(Runnable) callback firing); `Phase` newtype over i32 with `DEFAULT = i32::MAX` and shipped web-server-band constants (DEFAULT-1024 / DEFAULT-2048); a `LifecycleProcessor` infrastructure component holding origin-agnostic participant descriptors, bucketing by phase, exposing async start_all / stop_all / pause / restart; per-phase structured-concurrency JoinSet awaited under tokio::time::timeout for the per-phase (not global) concurrent drain — replacing the CountDownLatch + parked thread; dyn-async surface via trait_variant Send-variant + dynosaur dyn bridge (or thin-macro-emitted Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>) so `dyn SmartLifecycle` works across native and WASM-module origins; is_running as the participant's own safe-published cell (ownership model), not a framework-reached volatile field; processor never holds a lock across a participant await

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on HOW participants are collected: leaf-core defines the `Lifecycle`/`SmartLifecycle` traits, the `Phase` type + band constants, the `LifecycleProcessor` contract, and the `StopError`/`LifecycleError` types. Contributing crates depend only on leaf-core and emit a role-tagged `LifecycleParticipantDescriptor` (phase, autoStart, pauseable, a const fn/handle yielding the dyn participant) via the SAME catalog substrate the rest of the framework uses (linkme distributed-slice / build.rs codegen / explicit — Phase 2 decides, Architectures A/B/C/D all fit since the runtime API is a flat `&[Descriptor]` + a runtime topo/bucket). The processor reads descriptors at the auto-start point — it NEVER walks live types (Rust has no instanceof; Spring's iterate-beans-and-instanceof-Lifecycle becomes a descriptor scan). The auto-start handoff is the natural home for the anti-DCE self-check (cross-crate doc §152): if a contributing integration crate (e.g. leaf-tokio's web-server lifecycle) is silently DCE'd, its descriptor is absent and the server never starts — so start_all should assert expected-vs-found participants by role/source and surface a LOUD startup diagnostic naming the missing source, rather than a quietly-non-serving app (the worst silent-failure symptom). Generics can't be registered (no 'static symbol until concrete, cross-crate doc §153) → the descriptor table holds only concrete participant types; the thin macro hard-errors on a generic SmartLifecycle with a register-concrete escape hatch. The two-source duality applies cleanly because lifecycle participants are NOT subject to the definition-freeze boundary (they are runtime activity, discovered AFTER the graph is built): the static link-time registry contributes native participant descriptors, and an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source contributes descriptors whose start/stop bridge into the module — both feed the SAME phase-bucketed processor (CANDIDATES, not a decision); a dynamic source can register LATER than a definition source since participants only need to exist by the auto-start point, which is a softer timing constraint than the freeze (noted, not resolved). The dyn-async trait surface (trait_variant + dynosaur) is the ABI a cross-origin participant must satisfy; keeping it thin keeps the cross-crate surface small and stable.

**T2 — execution context:** Auto-start runs on the bootstrap task at the END of bring-up (COLD PATH) right after refresh-lifecycle's finish_refresh hands off; each phase group's members start CONCURRENTLY inside a structured-concurrency scope (JoinSet) and the group is fully awaited before the next-higher phase — a start() may await (bind a socket, connect a pool) and a blocking start uses spawn_blocking (§2.3 execution-context discipline). Steady-state RUNNING is the participants' own concern (servers/schedulers run on their own tasks); the processor is idle between start and stop — it does NOT poll is_running (§2.4: the running state is event-driven inside each participant, the processor only waits on explicit start/stop triggers, never busy-waits). STOP runs on whatever task drives context-close OR an explicit context stop()/pause(): phase groups DESCENDING, each group's stop_graceful() futures spawned into a scope and awaited under a PER-PHASE tokio::time::timeout (default 10s, tunable per phase) — the drain wait is a cold-path await on the JoinSet's completion notification, never a poll; on timeout the pending members are recorded (a rich diagnostic: which participants in which phase failed to drain) and the group is abandoned so the next-lower phase proceeds (Spring's proceed-after-timeout semantics). CANCELLATION: cancelling the stop task cancels the per-phase scope → in-flight stop futures drop; because there is NO async Drop (nightly doc), a participant holding async-cleanup resources must run that cleanup inside its stop_graceful()/disposable path EXPLICITLY (the container-driven teardown pattern), and the IMMEDIATE-DESTROY-WITHOUT-STOP contract means a participant's destroy must also tolerate stop never having run (cancelled bring-up, timeout). NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the processor guards only its phase cursor; it NEVER holds a lock across a participant's start/stop await, so a slow participant cannot deadlock the executor (dissolving Spring's under-callback-lock risk by execution context). ERRORS: a start() error during auto-start aborts bring-up (propagated to refresh-lifecycle as a typed LifecycleError carrying the failing participant + phase, all-or-nothing); a stop_graceful() error is aggregated per phase group (one participant's drain failure does not abort the others — Spring's best-effort stop) and reported with full per-participant/per-phase context. pause()/restart() run on the explicit caller's task, same phase machinery, skipping non-pauseable participants.

#### R2 — Dependency-ordered participant DAG (no integer phase): participants declare before/after + an optional priority band, the processor topo-sorts into ordered waves and start-forward / stop-reverse them
Question whether the running-state ordering must be an opaque integer at all. Spring uses i32 phases because the JVM gives it no better structuring tool, and the 'DEFAULT is MAX_VALUE not 0' confusion is a direct symptom. leaf's cross-crate doc says to do ALL ordering at runtime via topo-sort over descriptor dependencies/priority, NEVER by a raw scalar — and §2.11 wants ONE ordering primitive shared across the framework. So model the running-state ordering as a DAG: each participant descriptor declares `start_after: &[ParticipantRef]`, `start_before: &[ParticipantRef]` (stop is the reverse), an optional coarse `band: Band` (an enum of named tiers — App, GracefulDrain, Server — that resolves to default edges so the COMMON case needs zero explicit edges), plus autoStart/pauseable. The `LifecycleProcessor` topo-sorts the participants into ordered WAVES (a wave = a maximal set with no ordering edges between members → they start/stop concurrently, recovering Spring's within-phase concurrency as a structural property rather than a phase-equality coincidence), with cycle detection as a rich startup diagnostic (the earliest, §2.9). The two traits are as in R1 (`Lifecycle`, `SmartLifecycle` with async start/stop_graceful, autoStart, pauseable) MINUS getPhase — phase is replaced by the declared edges + band. Spring's exact semantics are recovered as DEFAULT band edges: the App band has no edges (concurrent), the Server band declares start_after(App)/stop the reverse (so the server starts after app components and stops before them — Spring's MAX-vs-default relationship), the GracefulDrain band sits between (drain after the server stops accepting, before app components quiesce). A migration shim accepts a raw phase integer and converts it to synthetic edges so a Spring-shaped participant still works. start_all walks waves FORWARD spawning each wave's members into a structured-concurrency scope and awaiting the wave; stop_all walks waves REVERSE with the same per-wave (now per-wave, the topo analogue of per-phase) timeout + JoinSet drain as R1. pause/restart reuse the wave walk, skipping non-pauseable participants. Because ordering is DATA resolved by topo-sort, this is the SAME primitive collection-injection (@Order/@Priority) and auto-config-ordering (before/after) want — the unification dividend.

**Pros:** Eliminates Spring's sharpest footgun: there is no opaque MAX_VALUE-vs-0 scalar to misread — a participant declares 'I start after the database, before the web server' as edges, which is what the author actually means, and a wrong/missing edge is caught structurally (cycle → sort-time diagnostic) far earlier and more legibly than an integer collision (§2.9, DX #1).; Maximal unification (§2.11): ordering-as-data resolved by topo-sort is exactly the cross-crate doc's guidance (§170: do all ordering at runtime via topo-sort, never scalar/link order) and the SAME primitive collection-injection and auto-config-ordering need — so the whole framework shares one ordering engine instead of three bespoke axes, the explicit reason Phases 2-4 exist.; Within-phase concurrency becomes a STRUCTURAL property (a wave = nodes with no edges between them, started concurrently) rather than Spring's accidental 'same integer phase' coincidence — clearer, and a participant that genuinely must be concurrent with another says so by NOT declaring an edge, which is more honest than picking a matching magic integer.; Bands give the common case Spring's ergonomics for free (App / GracefulDrain / Server as named tiers with default edges) so a typical participant declares only autoStart + a band — strictly more readable than 'phase = DEFAULT - 1024', and the web-server quiesce→drain→close choreography is expressed as named tiers, not arithmetic on MAX_VALUE.; Same async/structured-concurrency execution model as R1 (JoinSet per wave, per-wave timeout, no across-await lock) so it inherits all of R1's §2.3/§2.4/§2.5 wins, while the topo-sort is a cold-path one-time computation whose result is an inspectable wave schedule (good debuggability — 'what order will my components start' is a dumped DAG, not emergent integer ordering).; Origin-agnostic and cross-origin-friendly identically to R1, AND a dynamic WASM-module participant can declare edges relative to named bands/participant-refs it knows by stable id — so a dynamically-discovered participant slots into the ordering without a magic integer it would have to guess.

**Cons:** Diverges from Spring's deliberate integer-phase model, which is a WIDELY-KNOWN mental model; a Spring user reaching for getPhase()/DEFAULT_PHASE finds edges/bands instead — the migration shim helps but the parity is now intent-not-mechanism at a level that may surprise (a real fidelity cost vs R1's 1:1 mapping), and authors must learn leaf's band vocabulary.; Reference-by-participant edges (start_after a SPECIFIC participant) reintroduce a naming/identity problem: a participant must be referrable by a stable id across crates, which couples to bean-naming and raises 'what if the referenced participant isn't present/conditionally absent' — an absent edge target is a new failure class (dangling reference) that the integer axis simply doesn't have.; More machinery for the common case: every start/stop pays a topo-sort + cycle-detection even when everyone just uses default bands and the result is always the same wave order — overhead (cold-path, so tolerable) and conceptual surface Spring's scalar bucket-sort avoids, and it pre-judges that re-orderable/declared ordering is wanted (a §2.11 stance that is partly Phase 2's call).; The band enum is a closed set leaf must get right (App/GracefulDrain/Server) or integration crates need to invent edges anyway; if a new tier emerges (e.g. a message-broker drain tier between server and app) it is either a new band (core edit) or ad-hoc edges (back to per-participant references) — the same closed-vocabulary tension R1's web-server constants have, just relocated.; An under-constrained participant (no edges, no band) lands in the first wave and starts concurrently with everything — which may be wrong but is not a cycle, so the topo-sort can't catch it; 'forgot to declare an edge' is a silent mis-ordering footgun the integer axis at least makes visible as a default phase value.; Pushing ordering to a runtime DAG partly pre-decides the metadata/registry and the ordering-primitive unification keystones (§3) — presenting a DAG as THE shape risks biasing Phase 2; must stay a CANDIDATE, especially since auto-config-ordering and collection-injection have their own Phase-1 designs that might want a different ordering shape.

**Mechanisms considered:** participant descriptors declaring `start_after`/`start_before` edges + an optional named `Band` (App/GracefulDrain/Server) that expands to default edges; stop = reverse; topo-sort over the participant DAG into ordered WAVES (cycle detection as a sort-time startup diagnostic); a wave = no-edge-between members → concurrent start/stop; `Lifecycle`/`SmartLifecycle` traits as R1 but WITHOUT getPhase — ordering is the declared edges/band, not a scalar; a phase-integer migration shim that converts a raw phase to synthetic edges so Spring-shaped participants still work; per-wave structured-concurrency JoinSet + per-wave timeout drain (the topo analogue of R1's per-phase drain); the same topo-sort engine reused for collection-injection @Order and auto-config-ordering — one ordering primitive (the §2.11 unification bet)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same substrate-neutral collection story as R1 (leaf-core defines traits/processor/error types; contributing crates emit `LifecycleParticipantDescriptor`s via the Phase-2 catalog substrate; flat `&[Descriptor]` runtime API fits Architectures A/B/C/D), but the descriptor carries ordering EDGES + a band instead of a scalar phase. The topo-sort is the SAME ordering primitive auto-config-ordering and collection-injection want, so the cross-crate ordering machinery is SHARED (the unification dividend) — a strong T1 argument, but it WIDENS the stable surface: the band vocabulary and the participant-ref id scheme become cross-crate ABI (every crate must agree on Band::Server's meaning and on how a participant is referenced across crates). The topo-sort step is the natural place to run BOTH the anti-DCE self-check AND edge validation (cross-crate doc §152): a silently-DCE'd contributing crate shows up as a missing participant AND as a DANGLING EDGE (some other participant's start_after points at the absent one) → a loud sort-time error naming which participant/source is absent and which edge is unsatisfiable — strictly richer than R1's count-only check because it pinpoints the broken relationship. The flip side is the new dangling-edge failure class itself (an edge target conditionally absent must degrade gracefully, not hard-fail, when the absence is legitimate — coupling to conditions). Generics still can't register a participant (§153) → macro hard-errors. The two-source duality is as clean as R1 (participants escape the definition-freeze), and a dynamic WASM-module participant referencing a band-by-stable-id is actually EASIER than R1's guess-an-integer, because bands are named — but the cross-crate id scheme for participant-refs is the cost (a phase2Question). CANDIDATES not decisions throughout; whether the running-state ordering shares the framework's one ordering primitive is exactly a §2.11 Phase-2 call this realization surfaces.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical execution model to R1 with waves substituted for phase groups: topo-sort + cycle/dangling-edge detection is SYNCHRONOUS cold-path (one-time, before any start), producing the wave schedule and the earliest diagnostics. start_all runs on the bootstrap task at end of bring-up: waves FORWARD, each wave's members spawned concurrently into a structured-concurrency scope and awaited before the next wave (a start() may await; blocking start → spawn_blocking; §2.3). Steady-state running is the participants' own tasks; the processor does not poll (§2.4 — waits on explicit triggers only). stop_all runs REVERSE on the context-close/explicit-stop task: each wave's stop_graceful() futures in a scope awaited under a PER-WAVE tokio::time::timeout (the topo analogue of per-phase; default 10s, tunable), the drain wait an await on the JoinSet completion notification (never a poll), pending-on-timeout members recorded as a rich per-wave diagnostic, then the next-reverse wave proceeds. CANCELLATION + no-async-Drop: exactly as R1 — cancelling the stop task drops in-flight stop futures; explicit cleanup lives in stop_graceful()/disposable; the immediate-destroy-without-stop contract holds (destroy must tolerate no preceding stop). NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): processor guards only its wave cursor, never across a participant await. ERRORS: start error during auto-start aborts bring-up (typed LifecycleError + failing participant + the wave context); stop errors aggregated per wave (best-effort, one drain failure does not abort the wave); a dangling-edge or cycle aborts at SORT time before any participant runs (earliest, richest). pause()/restart() walk the waves on the caller's task skipping non-pauseable participants. The added cold-path cost over R1 is the topo-sort itself — negligible at startup, and its output is an inspectable schedule.

#### R3 — Explicit total running-state machine: the processor models the WHOLE context's run-state (Stopped→Starting→Running→Pausing→Paused→Stopping) as a typed state machine driven by reactive signals, with participants as phase-grouped sub-machines
Push the 'running-state machine' framing to its logical end: instead of a processor that merely iterates participants (R1/R2), model the CONTEXT'S OWN run-state as an explicit, total, typed state machine — `RunState = Stopped | Starting | Running | Pausing | Paused | Stopping | Stopped` — and make pause/restart, partial start, re-entrant stop, and the immediate-destroy-without-stop hazard all PROVABLE properties of that machine rather than ad-hoc reuse (R1/R2's 'pause = start minus non-pauseable' is exactly the under-specified corner). Participants still implement `Lifecycle`/`SmartLifecycle` (async start/stop_graceful, autoStart, pauseable) and are grouped by phase (R1's integer axis OR R2's DAG — orthogonal; this realization is about the CONTROLLER, not the ordering). The controller holds the current RunState behind a reactive primitive (a tokio::sync::watch::Sender<RunState> or an actor-style mailbox — REACTIVE, §2.4: state TRANSITIONS notify watchers via wakers, never a polled flag) and exposes `async fn start()`, `async fn stop()`, `async fn pause()`, `async fn restart()`, `fn run_state(&self) -> RunState`, and `fn watch_state(&self) -> Receiver<RunState>` so app code / readiness probes (lifecycle-events) SUBSCRIBE to run-state changes instead of polling isRunning. Each transition is a guarded async operation: start() is legal only from Stopped/Paused, runs the phase-ascending group starts (R1/R2 mechanics), and on success CAS-transitions to Running publishing the new state to watchers; a concurrent second start() AWAITS the in-flight transition (the watch channel coordinates) rather than racing — dissolving Spring's startup-monitor need WITHOUT a lock (the transition is serialized by the single controller task/actor, §2.5, no global mutex). stop() transitions Running→Stopping→Stopped via the phase-descending per-phase-timeout drain; pause() transitions Running→Pausing→Paused stopping only pauseable participants (non-pauseable stay Running-within-Paused — a state the machine models EXPLICITLY, the CRaC checkpoint case); restart() Paused→Starting→Running re-starts the paused set. The machine is TOTAL: every (state, event) pair has a defined outcome (e.g. stop() while Stopping = await the in-flight stop; destroy-while-Stopping = the immediate-destroy-without-stop path, modeled as a transition not an accident), so the Spring hazards (stop-skipped-on-cancel, double-stop, pause-during-start) become enumerated transitions with defined semantics rather than emergent bugs. lifecycle-events are PUBLISHED as state-transition side effects (Started on →Running, Stopped on →Stopped) directly off the watch channel — the events ARE the state machine's observable output, unifying with lifecycle-events' liveness/readiness intent.

**Pros:** Makes pause/restart and the hazard corners FIRST-CLASS and total: every (state,event) pair is defined, so double-stop, stop-on-cancel, pause-during-start, destroy-without-stop are ENUMERATED transitions with specified semantics — directly fixing Spring's 'stop not guaranteed before destroy' / 'tolerate destroy without stop' footguns by construction rather than by a documented warning (the deepest §2.9 win: the failure mode is designed out, not diagnosed).; Reactive run-state via a watch channel is the textbook §2.4 hot-path answer: readiness/liveness probes and app code SUBSCRIBE to RunState transitions (woken by the watcher) instead of polling isRunning in a loop — and it unifies cleanly with lifecycle-events (the lifecycle facts ARE the state machine's published transitions), so one primitive serves both the running-state and the event/availability intent (§2.11).; Serializing transitions through a single controller task/actor dissolves Spring's startupShutdownLock/synchronized-monitor entirely WITHOUT a lock (§2.5): a concurrent start/stop/pause AWAITS the in-flight transition via the channel rather than contending a mutex, and there is no across-await lock to deadlock the executor — the cleanest no-global-lock posture of the three for the concurrent-lifecycle-operation case.; Strongest pause/restart + CRaC story: 'non-pauseable participants keep running across a pause' is an explicit state (Running-within-Paused) the machine represents, so Boot's WebServer-opts-out-of-pause behavior and CRaC checkpoint/restore are modeled outcomes, not special-cased reuse — exactly the 7.0 surface R1/R2 leave under-specified.; Orthogonal to the ordering choice: the controller composes with EITHER R1's integer phases or R2's DAG for the actual group start/stop, so it does not prejudge the ordering keystone — it adds the run-state-machine layer ABOVE whichever ordering Phase 2 picks, maximizing reuse.; The explicit RunState is a superb diagnostic and test surface: at any instant 'what is the context doing' is a single typed value with a defined transition history, far richer than Spring's scattered active/running booleans — and a misbehaving participant (a start that never completes) leaves the machine legibly in Starting with the offending participant identified, rather than a hung opaque boot (§2.9).

**Cons:** Heaviest conceptual surface and the largest divergence from Spring's model: Spring has NO explicit context-run-state enum (just active/closed flags + per-bean isRunning); modeling a 6-state machine is genuinely MORE than Spring, risking over-engineering the common case (an app that only ever start-then-stops pays for pause/restart/total-transition machinery it never uses) — tension with DX-simplicity for the 90% case.; A single controller task/actor serializing all transitions is a potential CONTENDED chokepoint if lifecycle operations are frequent (they shouldn't be — start/stop/pause are cold-path), but it also means run-state queries that want the latest value go through the channel; mis-tuned, the watch channel's latest-value-wins semantics can drop intermediate states a subscriber cared about (a known watch pitfall) — needs careful design vs a broadcast/event-log.; The actor/controller pattern leans toward an owned-task model that may pre-judge the async-execution & context-propagation keystone (§3): whether leaf even has a controller-actor primitive is a Phase 2 call, and building the run-state machine on one biases that signal — must stay a CANDIDATE (the charter explicitly defers the async/actor model, and kameo is named as optional/pluggable, §6).; Totality is a specification BURDEN: enumerating and defining EVERY (state,event) pair (stop-during-pause, restart-during-stop, start-during-stopping, …) is a large correctness surface to get right and keep coherent; an incompletely-specified machine is worse than R1's simpler model because it LOOKS total but has gaps — and some pairs (restart while Stopping) have genuinely debatable 'right' semantics.; Publishing lifecycle-events as state-transition side effects couples this feature tightly to lifecycle-events/event-multicaster's realization; if that event model lands differently (typed channels vs a bus), the 'events are the machine's output' unification may not hold and the watch channel + the event bus could become two overlapping notification mechanisms (redundancy, §2.11 working against itself).; Still inherits R1's or R2's ordering footguns underneath (it is orthogonal, not a replacement) — so the run-state machine adds a layer of correctness but does not by itself solve 'which phase/edge does my participant belong to'; the hardest user-facing ordering question is unaddressed by this realization alone.

**Mechanisms considered:** an explicit typed context `RunState = Stopped|Starting|Running|Pausing|Paused|Stopping` modeled as a TOTAL state machine (every (state,event) pair defined); the current state held behind a reactive tokio::sync::watch (or actor mailbox) — transitions notify watchers via wakers (§2.4), never a polled flag; `watch_state()` for probes to subscribe; a single controller task/actor serializing all transitions (start/stop/pause/restart) so concurrent operations AWAIT the in-flight transition — no startup lock, no across-await mutex (§2.5); participants still `Lifecycle`/`SmartLifecycle`, grouped by EITHER R1 integer phases OR R2 DAG (orthogonal — this realization is the controller, not the ordering); the immediate-destroy-without-stop hazard + double-stop + pause-during-start modeled as ENUMERATED transitions with defined semantics (designed out, not documented around); lifecycle-events published as state-transition side effects off the watch channel — the events ARE the machine's observable output (unifying run-state with the event/availability intent)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The participant-collection T1 is identical to R1/R2 (leaf-core defines traits/controller/RunState/error types; contributing crates emit participant descriptors via the Phase-2 catalog substrate; flat runtime API fits Architectures A/B/C/D; anti-DCE self-check at the start() transition asserts expected-vs-found participants and surfaces a loud diagnostic if an integration crate is DCE'd so its lifecycle never runs; generics can't register, §153). The DISTINCTIVE T1 surface is the run-state OBSERVATION channel: `watch_state() -> Receiver<RunState>` is a cross-crate API that readiness probes, integration crates, and app code consume — keeping it a thin, stable, origin-agnostic surface (a typed enum + a watch receiver) means a WASM-module participant or an external probe observes context run-state identically to a native one. Because the controller serializes transitions through one owned task/actor, the cross-crate concern is that NO contributing crate may assume it can drive transitions out-of-band — only the controller does; participants are passive (they implement start/stop and are CALLED), which keeps the cross-crate contract clean (descriptors + a passive trait, never a back-channel). The two-source duality is as clean as R1/R2 (participants escape the freeze). The notable cross-crate RISK is that building on a controller-actor primitive assumes Phase 2 provides one — if leaf's async model is task-based-without-actors, the controller becomes a single owned task + channel instead, which is still cross-crate-clean but changes the internal substrate (flagged: this realization's controller substrate is itself downstream of the deferred async/actor keystone, a phase2Question). The lifecycle-events-as-transition-output coupling means this feature and lifecycle-events SHARE the watch/notification substrate across the crate boundary — a unification opportunity but also a coupling that must be coordinated in Phase 3. CANDIDATES not decisions.

**T2 — execution context:** The CONTROLLER is a single owned task/actor (cold-path, idle between transitions) that OWNS the RunState and serializes all transitions — start/stop/pause/restart are messages/calls it processes one-at-a-time, so concurrent callers AWAIT the in-flight transition via the watch/mailbox rather than racing (no lock, §2.5; the transition serialization is by single-ownership of the controller task, not a mutex). Each transition runs the underlying phase/wave group start/stop (R1/R2 mechanics) on the controller's task (or fans group members into a structured-concurrency scope as R1/R2 do), awaiting each group/wave; the same per-phase/per-wave tokio::time::timeout drain applies on stop. State TRANSITIONS publish the new RunState to the watch channel, WAKING subscribers (§2.4 reactive — probes/app code/lifecycle-events are notified, never poll). Steady-state Running is idle for the controller (participants run on their own tasks); a subscriber wanting 'tell me when Running' awaits the watch receiver. CANCELLATION: cancelling a transition (e.g. a hung start) is handled INSIDE the controller — the structured-concurrency scope for that group is cancelled, in-flight start/stop futures drop (no async Drop → explicit cleanup in stop_graceful/disposable), and the machine transitions to a defined state (a cancelled start → back toward Stopped with the partially-started participants explicitly stopped; a cancelled bring-up → the immediate-destroy-without-stop transition); a hung transition is bounded by the same timeouts, surfacing a rich diagnostic (which participant in which phase stalled which transition) rather than an opaque hang — mapping Spring's stuck-startup-thread-interrupt to async cancellation + a state-machine-defined recovery. ERRORS: a start failure transitions to a defined Failed/Stopped state and propagates a typed LifecycleError (failing participant, phase/wave, the transition attempted); stop errors aggregated best-effort per group. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5); the only serialization is single-controller-ownership. The watch channel is the §2.4 notification primitive throughout — the run-state is never busy-waited.

### Rust clashes
- No async Drop means graceful stop and the immediate-destroy-without-stop guarantee must be EXPLICIT container-driven teardown, not Drop. Spring's stop(Runnable) + @PreDestroy/destroy rely on synchronous callbacks; a Lifecycle bean's drain logic lives in stop() and its release in destroy, both sync. In async leaf there is NO async Drop (nightly doc: experimental-incomplete, crashes on tokio shutdown, no dyn story — leaf's participants are dyn-erased), so a participant holding async-acquired resources (open sockets, in-flight requests, spawned tasks) cannot release them in Drop. The graceful stop must be an explicit `async fn stop_graceful()` the processor AWAITS, and the immediate-destroy-without-stop hazard (Spring line 2085: a Lifecycle bean must tolerate destroy without a preceding stop, and runtime-mutable state must be safely published) becomes load-bearing: leaf's disposable/destroy path must run explicit async cleanup AND tolerate stop() never having run (cancelled bring-up, drain timeout, hot refresh) — the all-or-nothing guarantee is only as strong as this explicit-teardown discipline, shared with context-close.
- Spring's stop(Runnable) callback + per-phase CountDownLatch + parked thread maps to `async fn stop_graceful()` + a structured-concurrency JoinSet + tokio::time::timeout. Spring's DefaultLifecycleProcessor invokes stop(callback) on every member of a phase, then BLOCKS a thread on a per-phase CountDownLatch until all callbacks fire or the per-phase timeout (default 10s) elapses. In async leaf, parking an OS thread on a latch is forbidden (§2.3): the within-phase concurrent drain is a JoinSet of stop_graceful() futures awaited under a per-phase tokio::time::timeout, where the future RESOLVING is the callback firing (no Runnable needed) and the timeout firing abandons the still-pending members (recording them as a diagnostic) so the next-lower phase proceeds. The per-phase-NOT-global timeout (Spring's timeoutPerShutdownPhase) is a literal timeout() per group; the drain wait is a cold-path await on a completion notification, never a poll (§2.4).
- The integer phase axis (DEFAULT = Integer.MAX_VALUE, not 0) is a JVM-shaped opaque scalar that clashes with leaf's ordering-as-data guidance. Spring orders by an i32 phase because the JVM offers no better structuring tool, producing the documented 'DEFAULT_PHASE is MAX_VALUE not 0' confusion and the depends-on-overrides-phase special case. leaf's cross-crate doc (§170) says do ALL ordering at runtime via topo-sort over descriptor dependencies/priority, never a scalar, and §2.11 wants ONE shared ordering primitive. So the realizations split: R1 keeps the integer axis for max fidelity (and inherits the footgun); R2 replaces it with a declared dependency DAG resolved by topo-sort (eliminating the magic integer but diverging from a well-known model and reintroducing a participant-identity/dangling-edge concern). Either way, Spring's iterate-beans-and-instanceof-Lifecycle discovery is impossible (no runtime type-walk) — participants are role-tagged descriptors collected from a scoped registry, never discovered by inspecting live instances.
- Spring's startupShutdownLock (ReentrantLock, ex-synchronized monitor) + stuck-startup-thread interrupt collides with §2.5's no-global-lock and async cancellation. Spring v6.1+ guards start/stop/refresh with a ReentrantLock plus an isStartupShutdownThreadStuck() interrupt for a thread stuck WAITING (e.g. a user System.exit during startup). In leaf this cannot be a global lock: concurrent start/stop/pause on the SAME context is serialized by execution context (R3 makes this explicit via a single controller task/actor whose channel makes concurrent operations AWAIT the in-flight transition; R1/R2 by guarding only the phase/wave cursor, never across a participant await). The stuck-thread-interrupt maps to async cancellation + per-phase timeouts + a state-machine-defined recovery — a hung start/stop is bounded by timeouts and surfaces a rich diagnostic (which participant in which phase stalled), never an interrupted OS thread or a deadlocked hook.
- Spring's volatile-field discipline for runtime-mutable Lifecycle state is replaced by safe publication via the ownership/concurrency model. Spring (line 2085) requires a Lifecycle bean's runtime-mutable state (e.g. a `runnable` field) to be `volatile` and robust to out-of-order shutdown, because the JVM lets any thread touch the field. In leaf, is_running() and any mutable run-state is the PARTICIPANT'S OWN safely-published cell (per the deferred scope/ownership model) — the framework never reaches into a participant's field, it only calls start/stop_graceful/is_running. This dissolves the volatile requirement into the type system: the participant owns its state with appropriate interior mutability (an atomic or a properly-synchronized cell), and the processor's no-across-await-lock discipline means no torn reads of framework state either.
- Spring's isRunning() polling for readiness clashes with §2.4's reactive-hot-path rule. Spring exposes isRunning() as a poll-able boolean and readiness probes commonly loop on it. On leaf's hot path (readiness/liveness, app code waiting for 'Running'), polling a flag is forbidden — the running-state must be reactive: R3 makes this explicit with a tokio::sync::watch<RunState> subscribers are woken on, and even R1/R2 expose a notification (the running state change is signalled, not busy-waited). isRunning() remains as a cold-path point query, but the HOT path (wait-until-running, wait-until-stopped) is event-driven via wakers.
- Spring's pause()/restart() (7.0) + isPauseable() + CRaC has no Rust precedent and risks an under-specified partial-stop model. Spring 7.0 adds context pause()/restart() over the phase machinery, with isPauseable()==false opting a component out (it keeps running across a pause, gets stop only on real close — Boot's web server does this), and CRaC checkpoint/restore builds on it. Naively layering pause as 'start/stop minus non-pauseable' (R1/R2) leaves the semantics of a paused-but-not-stopped participant (its is_running, in-flight work, double-pause) ambiguous. The rust-native clash is that without an explicit run-state model these corners are emergent; R3 resolves it by modeling Running-within-Paused as a first-class state — but whether leaf needs the full pause/restart/CRaC surface at all (vs deferring it) is itself a scoping question, since it is Spring-7-new and not core to the start/stop intent.

### Interactions
- **refresh-lifecycle** — UPSTREAM TRIGGER (provided design). refresh-lifecycle's finish_refresh phase STARTS this running-state machine — it calls the processor's start_all()/auto-start (ascending phase) as the penultimate bring-up step, then publishes the RefreshedEvent, and refresh RETURNS once auto-start completes. This feature owns the running-state machine (the phase grouping, the start choreography, the graceful drain); refresh-lifecycle owns only the handoff point and the all-or-nothing semantics around it (an auto-start failure aborts refresh as a typed LifecycleError). The boundary: refresh builds the static graph and hands off; ongoing runtime activity is this feature's.
- **context-close** — DOWNSTREAM TRIGGER + SHARED HAZARD (provided design). context-close STOPS this running-state machine FIRST (reverse/descending phase, graceful per-phase-timeout drain) to quiesce runtime activity BEFORE destroying singletons in reverse dependency order — it calls this feature's stop_all(). This feature owns the phased-drain choreography; context-close owns the subsequent reverse-order destruction. The SHARED hazard is immediate-destroy-without-stop: context-close's clean path stops then destroys, but a cancelled bring-up or a drain timeout destroys WITHOUT a preceding stop, so participants' destroy/disposable must tolerate that — the two teardown entry points (clean close vs cancelled/timed-out) must stay coherent, and this is the precise spot Spring's 'drain logic in stop() can be bypassed' footgun lives.
- **lifecycle-events** — PUBLISHER. This feature publishes the running-state facts lifecycle-events routes: Started (on auto-start/→Running) and Stopped (on stop/→Stopped), plus the readiness/liveness availability state lifecycle-events models. lifecycle-events defines the event payloads and the liveness/readiness contract; this feature fires them at the precise start/stop transitions (R3 makes them literal state-transition side effects off the run-state watch channel, unifying the two). A failed/cancelled start does NOT publish Started — mirroring the refresh cancel-vs-clean distinction.
- **smart-initializing** — SIBLING / SEQUENCING (same Events & Lifecycle area). smart-initializing's afterSingletonsInstantiated callback fires ONCE after all eager singletons exist but BEFORE this feature's auto-start — the documented 'build graph → finalize → run' sequence (Spring line 908). smart-initializing is the lock-free 'all singletons ready' finalize hook; this feature is the subsequent 'run' phase. They are distinct axes: smart-initializing is a one-shot post-construction callback, runtime-lifecycle is an ongoing start/stop state machine. Heavy cross-bean work belongs in smart-initializing (before run), not in a participant's start().
- **lifecycle-callbacks** — ORTHOGONAL AXIS (must not be conflated — the core design rationale). lifecycle-callbacks is init/destroy (construction-time @PostConstruct/afterPropertiesSet and destruction-time @PreDestroy/destroy) — the bean CREATION axis. This feature is start/stop — the runtime ACTIVITY axis. Spring's whole rationale (line 380) is that overloading init/destroy for 'start serving traffic' would be wrong, so they are separate. The interaction is sequencing: a participant is fully constructed+init'd (lifecycle-callbacks) BEFORE it is started (this feature), and stopped (this feature) before it is destroyed (lifecycle-callbacks) — and the immediate-destroy-without-stop hazard is precisely where these two axes' teardowns interleave.
- **scheduling** — DOWNSTREAM PARTICIPANT. @Scheduled tasks (cron/fixedRate/fixedDelay) are a canonical SmartLifecycle participant — the scheduler starts ticking on auto-start and must stop ticking (and drain in-flight scheduled work) on graceful stop. scheduling defines the periodic-work registration against a TaskScheduler; this feature drives WHEN the scheduler starts/stops via its phase placement. The scheduler is exactly the kind of 'runtime activity' (line 380) this feature exists to choreograph, and its polling/interval nature is explicitly a cold-path-permitted poll (§2.4).
- **task-execution** — DOWNSTREAM PARTICIPANT + SUBSTRATE. A TaskExecutor / thread-or-task pool is both a Lifecycle participant (it starts and must drain+shut down gracefully on stop) AND the substrate the graceful-drain JoinSet/structured-concurrency runs on. task-execution provides the executor abstraction (pool/virtual-thread/task selection, graceful shutdown); this feature places it in the phase order so it drains in-flight tasks before its consumers stop, and uses its structured-concurrency primitives for the per-phase concurrent drain.
- **events** — DOWNSTREAM PARTICIPANT (the event-multicaster's async dispatch path). An async event-multicaster with a backing executor is a Lifecycle participant that must drain in-flight async event dispatch on stop. events/event-multicaster define the pub/sub and dispatch; this feature ensures the async-dispatch machinery starts and gracefully stops at the right phase — and in R3, the run-state watch channel and the event bus are candidate-overlapping notification mechanisms that Phase 3 must reconcile.
- **context-propagation** — CROSS-CUTTING CONSTRAINT. Because graceful stop spawns per-phase drain futures into a structured-concurrency scope (a thread/task hop), any ambient context (request/locale/transaction/tracing) a participant's stop_graceful() relies on must be CAPTURED and RESTORED across that hop. context-propagation provides the capture/restore SPI; this feature is one of the boundaries (the start/stop fan-out) that context must survive — exactly the thread-hop problem context-propagation exists to solve, here on the cold-path drain.
- **startup-instrumentation** — EMITTER. This feature emits named, timed steps startup-instrumentation records: the auto-start phase, per-phase-group start steps, and (symmetrically) the graceful-stop per-phase drain durations — the stable taxonomy that diagnoses a slow start or a slow/timed-out drain. startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default opt-in-recording tree; this feature's phase-group boundaries are the steps it records, and the per-phase drain-timeout events are prime diagnostic material.
- **background-bootstrap** — SEQUENCING NEIGHBOUR. background-bootstrap's concurrently-initialized slow singletons must be fully constructed (their futures joined) BEFORE this feature's auto-start runs — a SmartLifecycle participant cannot start before it exists. background-bootstrap owns the concurrent-init-and-join (forced complete by refresh's all-or-nothing boundary); this feature runs strictly after, on the now-complete graph. Note Spring's caveat (line 419): a lazy SmartLifecycle with autoStartup is still started at refresh, so laziness barely defers a participant.
- **concurrency-contract** — FOUNDATION CONSTRAINT. The runtime-mutable lifecycle state (is_running, a participant's runnable/drain flags) is exactly the 'state changed AFTER the bean is published' that concurrency-contract says needs explicit safe publication (Spring's volatile-field requirement, line 2085) — NOT the init-phase config that gets safe publication for free. concurrency-contract frames scope choice as the concurrency lever and delegates mutable-runtime-state safety to the owner; this feature's participants own their run-state with appropriate interior mutability, and the processor's no-across-await-lock discipline upholds the contract for framework-side state.
- **bean-naming** — IDENTITY DEPENDENCY (sharper for R2). Participants must be referrable by a stable identity — minimally for diagnostics (naming which participant stalled a phase), and for R2's declared-edge model a participant referenced by another's start_after needs a stable cross-crate name/id. bean-naming provides the deterministic naming/alias scheme; this feature consumes it for participant identity, and R2's dangling-edge failure class (an edge target conditionally absent) is where naming and conditions meet.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the running-state ordering an integer phase axis (R1, faithful to Spring's getPhase/DEFAULT_PHASE=MAX_VALUE, max fidelity but inherits the opaque-scalar + 'MAX not 0' footgun) or a declared dependency DAG resolved by topo-sort (R2, the cross-crate doc §170 ordering-as-data guidance + the §2.11 one-ordering-primitive bet, but diverges from a well-known model and adds a participant-identity/dangling-edge concern)? This cross-cuts collection-injection (@Order/@Priority) and auto-config-ordering (before/after) — whether ALL THREE share one ordering primitive, or runtime-lifecycle keeps a bespoke scalar axis, is a §2.11 meta-architecture call, not Phase 1's.
- Does leaf need the full Spring-7.0 pause()/restart()/isPauseable()/CRaC surface, and if so is it an explicit total run-state machine (R3, where Running-within-Paused and the hazard corners are enumerated transitions) or a thin 'start/stop minus non-pauseable' reuse (R1/R2, leaving partial-stop semantics emergent)? This decides how much running-state machinery leaf builds, whether checkpoint/restore is in scope at all, and whether the context exposes a typed RunState — and building R3's controller pre-judges whether leaf even has a controller-actor primitive (downstream of the deferred async/actor model).
- What is the cancellation + partial-start/partial-stop cleanup contract given NO async Drop, and is it SHARED with context-close's reverse-order destroy? When a per-phase drain times out, or a start is cancelled mid-phase, or a bring-up is cancelled before stop ever ran, what mechanism guarantees explicit reverse-order async cleanup of exactly the started participants — a structured-concurrency scope-guard, an explicit started-participants ledger, or a disposable run on the teardown path? The immediate-destroy-without-stop guarantee (a participant's destroy must tolerate no preceding stop) is only as strong as this; shared with context-close and the bean-instantiation/teardown substrate.
- How is the per-phase (not global) graceful-drain timeout realized and tuned, and what is the rust-native form of Spring's stop(Runnable) async-completion semantics — `async fn stop_graceful()` whose resolution IS the callback (the realizations' bet), a JoinSet awaited under a per-phase tokio::time::timeout, and per-phase-configurable durations? This couples to the async-execution & context-propagation keystone (the drain runs on a task-hop that must propagate ambient context) and to task-execution (the executor that backs the drain and must itself drain).
- Is the running-state observed REACTIVELY (R3's tokio::sync::watch<RunState> subscribers are woken on, satisfying §2.4 for readiness/liveness probes) or via a poll-able isRunning()? If reactive, is the run-state notification channel SHARED with lifecycle-events (the lifecycle facts ARE the state transitions, a §2.11 unification) or a separate mechanism (risking two overlapping notification substrates)? Cross-cuts lifecycle-events, event-multicaster, and the deferred unified diagnostics/observability model.
- How does the no-global-lock serialization of concurrent lifecycle operations (Spring's startupShutdownLock + stuck-thread-interrupt) map — a single controller task/actor whose channel makes concurrent start/stop/pause AWAIT the in-flight transition (R3), or guarding only a phase/wave cursor while never holding a lock across a participant await (R1/R2)? And how does a HUNG start/stop (Spring's stuck-startup-thread) map to async cancellation + per-phase timeouts + a defined recovery state, rather than an interrupted OS thread? Shared with the async-execution keystone and §2.5.
- How does the two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source) feed the participant population, given that participants ESCAPE the definition-freeze boundary (they are runtime activity discovered after the graph is built, a softer timing constraint than definitions)? Can a dynamic WASM-module participant register after bring-up and still be auto-started, and (R2) reference a named band/participant-id it knows by stable id? A CANDIDATE, not a decision; shared with the whole discovery/SPI family and the registry substrate.
- What is the anti-DCE self-check contract specifically for lifecycle participants — where does the 'expected vs found auto-start participants' manifest live, so a silently-DCE'd integration crate (its web-server/scheduler lifecycle never registered) becomes a LOUD startup diagnostic naming the missing source rather than a quietly-non-serving app (the worst silent-failure symptom)? Does it differ across catalog substrates (linkme vs codegen vs explicit)? Cross-cuts container-core, startup-instrumentation, refresh-lifecycle's self-check, and the registry substrate.

The single most load-bearing intent is the DELIBERATE SEPARATION of the running-state machine from the creation graph (line 380) — start/stop is a different axis than init/destroy with different ordering needs — plus the lower-starts-first/stops-last phase ordering with DEFAULT=MAX_VALUE (387), the per-phase (not global) concurrent graceful drain with a per-phase timeout (391, 420), the async stop(Runnable) called instead of no-arg stop on SmartLifecycle (383), the autoStart/phase/pauseable knobs (382-383, 395), and the immediate-destroy-without-stop guarantee (418, 2085). I grounded T1 against the cross-crate doc's DCE-is-silent self-check mandate (135-152, 345), ordering-as-data/topo-sort guidance (170), Architectures A/B/C/D with an identical flat-descriptor runtime API (277-337), linkme-default thin-descriptors (165-171), generics-cannot-register (153), and scope-discovery-not-global (357); T2 against the charter invariants (esp. §2.3 async-first/explicit-execution-context, §2.4 reactive-hot/poll-cold, §2.5 no-global-lock, §2.9 rich-early diagnostics, §2.10 thin-macros, §2.11 unification) and the stable envelope — crucially NO async Drop as a first-class constraint (273-281, with the explicit container-driven reverse-order-teardown-under-structured-concurrency-with-timeouts alternative being the exact pattern graceful stop needs) and AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe forcing trait_variant + dynosaur on the dyn participant surface (260, 266, 271).\n\nThe three realizations diverge on DIFFERENT axes so Phase 3 can mix-and-match: R1 (integer phase axis + async-trait processor) is the ordering-fidelity bet — 1:1 Spring mapping, max teachability, inherits the MAX-vs-0 footgun; R2 (dependency DAG, no integer phase) is the ordering-unification bet — eliminates the magic scalar, shares one ordering primitive with collection-injection/auto-config-ordering per §2.11, but diverges from a known model and adds a dangling-edge concern; R3 (explicit total run-state machine + reactive watch channel) is the CONTROLLER bet, ORTHOGONAL to the ordering choice (it composes with R1's phases OR R2's DAG underneath) — it makes pause/restart/CRaC and the hazard corners first-class enumerated transitions and unifies run-state observation with lifecycle-events, at the cost of the most machinery and a dependency on a deferred async/actor primitive. R1-vs-R2 is the ordering-shape question; R3 is the run-state-controller question layered above either. All three keep the two traits (Lifecycle/SmartLifecycle), the async graceful stop (future-resolution = callback), the per-phase/per-wave structured-concurrency drain with per-phase timeout, and the immediate-destroy-without-stop discipline.\n\nThree things to carry forward: (1) The async stop(Runnable)→`async fn stop_graceful() -> Result` mapping is clean and STRICTLY RICHER than Spring (typed drain errors, the future resolving IS the callback, no Runnable/latch plumbing) — and the per-phase CountDownLatch + parked thread becomes a JoinSet awaited under tokio::time::timeout, which is the single most important rust-native transformation here. (2) No async Drop makes graceful stop + the immediate-destroy-without-stop guarantee a NON-syntactic explicit-teardown discipline shared with context-close: drain must be explicit in stop_graceful, release explicit in disposable, and BOTH must tolerate cancellation/timeout/no-preceding-stop — Spring's documented 'drain in stop() can be bypassed' footgun is now load-bearing for correctness. (3) §2.4 forbids polling isRunning on the hot path (readiness/liveness), so the running-state wants a reactive notification (R3's watch<RunState> being the explicit form) — and whether that channel is SHARED with lifecycle-events is a real §2.11 unification opportunity Phase 3 should weigh.\n\nI refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps participant population ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a WASM-module/test-double participant starts/stops at the same phase via a dyn-erased descriptor), keeps WHICH-participates as scoped DATA (descriptors from a static link-time registry PLUS an optional dynamic source, never runtime instanceof — Rust has no type-walk), is compatible with cross-crate Architectures A/B/C/D via a flat &[Descriptor] runtime API, and inherits the silent-DCE self-check mandate (auto-start is the natural home for an expected-vs-found participant check). The two-source duality is noted as SOFTER here than for definitions because participants escape the freeze boundary (they only need to exist by auto-start), flagged as a phase2Question not resolved. Naming ('Lifecycle', 'SmartLifecycle', 'Phase', 'Band', 'LifecycleProcessor', 'stop_graceful', 'RunState', 'LifecycleParticipantDescriptor', 'StopError', 'watch_state') is Phase-1 placeholder.
