## Context Close & Destruction Ordering  `[context-close]`

Spring's `close()`/`doClose()` is the context TEARDOWN ORCHESTRATOR — the single, idempotent, ordered shutdown DRIVER that tears the live context back down to nothing in the exact mirror of how refresh built it up. This feature owns the SEQUENCING and the CROSS-BEAN ORDER of teardown; it is DISTINCT from its two upstream dependencies it merely INVOKES — runtime-lifecycle (which owns the phase-grouped graceful STOP machinery; context-close just TRIGGERS its stop_all() first) and lifecycle-callbacks (which owns each bean's per-bean DESTROY chain @PreDestroy→DisposableBean→destroy-method; context-close just walks the registered destroyers in the right ORDER) — and from refresh-lifecycle (the bring-up template; context-close is its inverse), from lifecycle-events (which it asks to PUBLISH the Closed fact), and from scopes (whose non-singleton destruction-callbacks fire on scope-end, a different trigger).\n\nThe precise intent to replicate:\n\n(1) THE doClose() SEQUENCE, exactly and in order: (a) publish ContextClosedEvent (the context is end-of-life, cannot be restarted); (b) lifecycleProcessor.onClose() — STOP all Lifecycle beans in reverse phase with the per-phase graceful drain timeout, BEFORE any destruction, to quiesce runtime activity (close listeners, stop schedulers, drain in-flight requests) so no in-flight work touches a bean about to be destroyed; (c) destroyBeans()/destroySingletons() — run every singleton's destroy chain; (d) closeBeanFactory(); (e) the subclass onClose() hook; (f) resetCommonCaches() + reset listeners + null out the multicaster/messageSource. STOP-BEFORE-DESTROY is the load-bearing intent.\n\n(2) SINGLETON DESTRUCTION ORDER = the MIRROR of creation: reverse registration order while HONORING DEPENDENCIES — a bean is destroyed BEFORE the beans it depends on, and dependents are destroyed before their dependencies. This is a dependency-honoring reverse-topological walk, not naive reverse-insertion.\n\n(3) IDEMPOTENT CLOSE-ONCE GATE: doClose() runs only on a successful CAS (active && closed: false→true). A second close is a no-op. Close is triggered by an explicit ctx.close() OR by a registered shutdown hook (registerShutdownHook) — and in a STANDALONE app, destruction fires ONLY if one of those is invoked; many wrongly assume process exit handles it (sharper in Rust: no GC/finalizers AND no async Drop, so teardown MUST be an explicit, awaited, container-driven async pass).\n\n(4) THE CANCEL-VS-CLOSE DISTINCTION: a FAILED/cancelled refresh calls destroyBeans()+cancelRefresh() (destroys the already-created singletons, sets active=false, resets caches) but does NOT run the full doClose() path — NO ContextClosedEvent, NO lifecycleProcessor.onClose(). The context is left non-active rather than 'closed'. So the destroy walk has TWO entry points (clean close vs cancelled-bring-up) that share the reverse-order destruction but differ in stop/event behaviour — and on a hot/cancelled path Lifecycle.stop() may be SKIPPED (drain logic placed only in stop() is silently bypassed).\n\n(5) THE STARTUP/SHUTDOWN SERIALIZATION: concurrent close/refresh is serialized (Spring's ReentrantLock startupShutdownLock, ex-synchronized monitor), and a shutdown hook stuck on a thread WAITING (e.g. user System.exit during startup) is interrupted/abandoned rather than deadlocking the hook.\n\nleaf must replicate this INTENT — an idempotent, ordered teardown driver that stops runtime activity first then destroys singletons in dependency-honoring reverse order, triggered by explicit close OR a process-signal hook, with the cancelled-bring-up partial-teardown as a distinct sibling entry point — WITHOUT Spring's JVM lineage: the GC-reliant 'forget and finalize' fallback (Rust has no GC AND no async Drop, so the reverse-order destroy walk is the ONLY teardown, mandatory and explicit), the ReentrantLock startupShutdownLock + thread-interrupt (no global lock; serialization by execution context; a hung teardown is bounded by per-bean/per-phase timeouts + async cancellation, never an interrupted OS thread), the synchronized close-once monitor (an atomic CAS, no lock), and the assumption that destroy methods are synchronous reflective calls (leaf awaits an explicit async destroyer per bean under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation). It must drive an ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC teardown (a WASM-module-provided singleton's destroyer is awaited in the same reverse-order walk), over destroyers collected into a SCOPED teardown ledger (never a runtime instanceof — Rust has no type-walk). It must NOT pick the teardown/ledger substrate it shares with bean-instantiation and lifecycle-callbacks, the async-cancellation model, whether the dependency-reverse-order is a recorded creation-order ledger or a topo-sort of the live dependency graph, how the process-signal hook is wired, or the registry substrate — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Linear teardown ledger replayed in reverse: bean-instantiation pushes a destroyer onto an append-only ordered ledger at each singleton's publication; close() drains it LIFO, gated by stop_all-then-destroy and an atomic close-once CAS
Mirror Spring's mechanism directly: destruction order is the LITERAL reverse of construction order, recorded as it happens, so no dependency graph need be re-derived at shutdown. As bean-instantiation finishes each SINGLETON (publication gate: after lifecycle-callbacks' init chain returns Ok), it PUSHES a `Destroyer { bean_id, name, destroy: BoxFuture-producing closure capturing the bean handle + its per-bean destroy chain }` onto an append-only ordered `TeardownLedger` held by the context. Because bean-instantiation already creates a depended-on bean BEFORE its dependent (a dependency is constructed first), reverse-ledger order naturally destroys dependents before dependencies — the dependency-honoring reverse order falls out of recording creation order, exactly Spring's 'reverse registration order while honoring dependencies'. PROTOTYPES push NOTHING (never-destroyed is structural — the ledger simply has no entry). `close()` is the orchestrator: (1) an atomic `state: AtomicU8` CAS (Active→Closing, fail-fast no-op if already Closing/Closed — Spring's close-once); (2) publish ContextClosedEvent via lifecycle-events; (3) await runtime-lifecycle's `stop_all()` (reverse-phase graceful drain, per-phase timeout) — STOP-BEFORE-DESTROY; (4) DRAIN the ledger LIFO: pop each Destroyer and await its destroy chain (which lifecycle-callbacks defined) under a per-bean `tokio::time::timeout`, aggregating errors into a `ShutdownReport` (one bad destroyer never aborts the rest — best-effort); (5) drop the registry/caches, reset listeners, mark state=Closed. CANCELLED-BRING-UP is a SIBLING entry point `cancel_bring_up()`: it drains the SAME ledger LIFO (destroying exactly the singletons that got created so far) but SKIPS the ContextClosedEvent and SKIPS stop_all() (Spring's cancelRefresh path: no event, no onClose, leaves the context non-active not 'closed') — so the ledger is the single source of truth for 'what exists and must be torn down', shared between the two paths. The process-signal HOOK (registerShutdownHook analogue) is an opt-in: a task awaiting a ctrl-c/SIGTERM signal future that calls close() once — purely a trigger, no teardown logic of its own.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity at the lowest cost: destruction = literal reverse of construction, recorded as it happens — exactly Spring's 'reverse registration order honoring dependencies', and the dependency-honoring property is FREE (a dependency is on the ledger before its dependent, so LIFO destroys the dependent first) without ever re-deriving a graph at the fragile shutdown moment.; The TeardownLedger is the single source of truth shared by BOTH entry points (clean close drains it after stop_all+event; cancelled bring-up drains it without either) — so the cancel-vs-close distinction (Spring's cancelRefresh-vs-doClose, the no-event/no-stop subtlety) is expressed as 'same ledger drain, different prologue', maximally teachable and impossible to desync (DX #1, §2.9).; Append-on-publish dovetails perfectly with the provided lifecycle-callbacks design (which already 'registers a destroyer into context-close's reverse-dependency teardown ledger' for singletons and 'registers nothing' for prototypes) and bean-instantiation's publication gate — this realization is the concrete ledger those designs hand to, no reinvention.; No graph machinery, no topo-sort at shutdown: the most robust posture for the moment teardown runs (a half-broken or cyclic graph still has a valid linear creation order to reverse), and the ledger is a flat Vec drained LIFO — minimal heap, no Arc churn beyond the destroyer handles the ownership model already requires (§2.5).; Async-first and no-async-Drop-native (§2.3, nightly doc): the destroyer is an explicit awaited future, drained in reverse under per-bean timeouts with error aggregation into a ShutdownReport — the stable-envelope's exact recommendation, giving deterministic order + timeouts + observability that Drop cannot.; Close-once is a single atomic CAS, not a lock (§2.5): concurrent close() calls all observe the CAS, exactly one wins and runs the drain, the rest return the in-flight completion — Spring's startupShutdownLock dissolved into a lock-free state transition.

**Cons:** Recorded creation order honors dependencies ONLY if every real dependency was actually constructed-before (true for eager constructor-wired singletons) — but setter/field circular references (the early-singleton-reference case) and lazy-resolved or ObjectProvider-deferred dependencies can publish a bean BEFORE a bean it logically depends on, so pure ledger-reverse can destroy in a subtly-wrong order that a live dependency graph would catch (a real fidelity gap vs R2, exactly where Spring's 'honoring dependencies' does MORE than naive reverse-registration).; The ledger must hold a destroyer closure capturing each singleton's handle for the WHOLE container lifetime — coupling teardown to the singleton-handle type (Arc<dyn> vs arena slot, the deferred §3 ownership keystone) and keeping every bean reachable until close, which interacts with how the registry already retains singletons (possible double-retention to design away).; Append-on-publish requires a concurrency-safe push during the (possibly concurrent / background-bootstrap) creation phase — a guarded push onto the ledger that must not become a contention point or hold a lock across a creation await (§2.5); ordering of pushes from concurrently-initialized beans (background-bootstrap) must still reflect dependency completion, not raw wall-clock finish.; Two entry points draining one mutable ledger (close vs cancel-bring-up) must be mutually exclusive with each other and with in-flight creation — a cancelled bring-up races with the bootstrap task that is still pushing; the CAS gate must cover 'creation aborted, now draining' as a state, not just 'close after a successful refresh'.; A destroyer that times out leaves its bean's resource possibly-unreleased and the ledger half-drained — the report must capture this richly, but R1 has no structural way to know whether the un-torn-down remainder is safe to abandon (same trust boundary as Spring's per-phase timeout, now at per-bean granularity).; Linear-reverse prejudges that 'creation order is recorded and reversible' — if Phase 2's ownership/registry substrate doesn't retain a global creation order (e.g. per-scope arenas with independent lifetimes), the single global ledger is less natural than a per-scope or graph-derived order (a §2.11 unification tension with scopes).

**Mechanisms considered:** append-only ordered `TeardownLedger` of `Destroyer` closures pushed at each singleton's publication gate by bean-instantiation; prototypes push nothing (never-destroyed structural); `close()` orchestrator: atomic CAS close-once gate → ContextClosedEvent → await runtime-lifecycle stop_all() → drain ledger LIFO awaiting each destroy chain under a per-bean tokio::time::timeout with error aggregation into a ShutdownReport → reset caches/listeners; `cancel_bring_up()` sibling entry point: drain the SAME ledger LIFO but SKIP ContextClosedEvent + SKIP stop_all() (Spring's cancelRefresh: no event, no onClose, context left non-active); `AtomicU8` state machine (Active→Closing→Closed) for close-once, replacing Spring's startupShutdownLock with a lock-free CAS; opt-in process-signal hook: a task awaiting ctrl-c/SIGTERM that calls close() once (registerShutdownHook analogue) — trigger only, no teardown logic; origin-agnostic Destroyer closures so native / WASM-module / test-double singletons are awaited in the identical reverse walk

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Substrate-neutral on HOW destroyers arrive (they are PUSHED by bean-instantiation at publication, not discovered) — so this feature defines in leaf-core only the `TeardownLedger` contract, the `Destroyer`/`ShutdownReport`/`CloseError` types, the close()/cancel_bring_up() orchestrator, and the state machine; it needs NO cross-crate registry of its own because destroyers are a RUNTIME byproduct of construction, not a link-time contribution. This is a key T1 simplification: context-close does not itself face the DCE-silent-failure hazard for ITS data (a destroyer exists iff the bean was constructed), but it INHERITS it transitively — if an integration crate's singleton was silently DCE'd and never registered/constructed (cross-crate doc §149-152), there is no destroyer, and the symptom surfaces upstream (the bean never existed) rather than here. The relevant cross-crate concern is ORIGIN-AGNOSTICISM of the destroyer: a destroyer is an erased `FnOnce -> BoxFuture` (or a handle into lifecycle-callbacks' destroy chain), so a native bean, a WASM-module bean (destroyer bridges into the module), and a test-double are all drained identically — the dynamic 'classpath' needs no special teardown path. Because participants/destroyers are a runtime ledger, the two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM source) is SOFTER here than for definitions: a dynamically-sourced singleton created after the initial bring-up still pushes its destroyer onto the ledger, so it is torn down in the correct relative position automatically (CANDIDATES, not a decision). Generics: irrelevant to teardown directly (the ledger holds concrete erased destroyers); the generic-cannot-register hazard lives upstream at construction (§153). The only cross-crate surface is the thin Destroyer/report types in leaf-core, hard-coded by no one but bean-instantiation and lifecycle-callbacks — minimal and stable.

**T2 — execution context:** close() runs on whatever task invokes it (an explicit caller's task, or the process-signal hook's task) — a COLD-PATH orchestration. The atomic CAS close-once is wait-free; the loser of a concurrent close awaits the in-flight close's completion (a watch/notify on the state, §2.4 — never a busy-wait, never a lock). STEP ORDER on that task: publish ContextClosedEvent (lifecycle-events; synchronous-ordered dispatch may itself await listeners); then AWAIT runtime-lifecycle stop_all() (reverse-phase graceful drain, per-phase timeout — STOP fully completes/abandons before any destroy); then DRAIN the ledger LIFO — each Destroyer's future awaited under a per-bean `tokio::time::timeout`. Destroyers within the SAME dependency level could be drained concurrently (a structured-concurrency JoinSet) but ACROSS levels strictly sequential to honor reverse-dependency order; R1's linear ledger drains strictly LIFO by default (simplest, fully ordered) with concurrent-within-tie as a Phase-2 optimization. A destroy chain may await (close a pool, flush) and a blocking destroy uses spawn_blocking (§2.3). CANCELLATION: cancelling the close task mid-drain drops the in-flight destroyer future — and because there is NO async Drop (nightly doc), a destroyer whose work was interrupted leaves its resource un-released; the ledger records how far it drained so a partial ShutdownReport is still produced (rich diagnostic: which beans torn down, which timed out, which were abandoned), never a silent swallow (§2.9). NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the ledger is drained by the single close-winner task; the only synchronization is the CAS and a guarded pop, never a lock held across a destroyer await — so a slow destroyer cannot deadlock the executor. ERRORS: per-bean destroy failures are AGGREGATED (best-effort, Spring's behaviour) into the ShutdownReport with full per-bean context; a hung destroyer is bounded by its per-bean timeout and surfaced, not interrupted-as-an-OS-thread (the rust-native form of Spring's stuck-shutdown-thread handling). CANCELLED-BRING-UP runs on the bootstrap task that detected the refresh failure: it drains the ledger LIFO (no event, no stop_all) — the partial graph is torn down all-or-nothing, the exact mirror of refresh's all-or-nothing guarantee.

#### R2 — Dependency-graph-derived reverse teardown: close() topo-sorts the LIVE singleton dependency edges and destroys in reverse-topological waves, so 'honoring dependencies' is computed at shutdown rather than approximated by creation order
Question whether recorded creation order is a faithful enough proxy for 'honoring dependencies' (R1's gap: cycles, lazy/deferred edges). Instead, at close() time, derive the destruction order from the LIVE dependency graph the container already knows (each singleton's resolved dependency set, recorded during autowiring). The context holds a `DependencyGraph` (node = live singleton, edge = depends-on, captured at wiring), and close() runs a REVERSE-TOPOLOGICAL sort: destroy a bean only after all its DEPENDENTS are destroyed (a bean is destroyed before the beans it depends on). The sort produces ordered WAVES — a wave = a set of beans with no remaining dependents — that can be destroyed CONCURRENTLY within the wave and strictly sequentially across waves (recovering Spring's 'a bean destroyed before its dependencies, dependents before dependencies' as a structural property, plus within-wave concurrency for free). CYCLES (setter/field circular singletons that R1 silently mis-orders) are detected at sort time as a rich diagnostic and broken by a documented tiebreak (reverse-creation-order within the cycle, the best available). The per-bean destroyer is still lifecycle-callbacks' destroy chain; this realization changes only HOW the cross-bean order is COMPUTED. close() orchestration is identical to R1 in prologue (CAS close-once → ContextClosedEvent → await runtime-lifecycle stop_all()) then runs the reverse-topo destroy-wave walk under per-bean timeouts + error aggregation. CANCELLED-BRING-UP uses the SAME reverse-topo machinery over the PARTIAL graph built so far (only the beans that got wired have edges), skipping event+stop_all. The dependency edges are the SAME data autowiring-resolution and the DependsOn relationship already produce — so this is the ordering primitive shared with the rest of the framework (the §2.11 unification: one dependency graph drives both forward wiring order AND reverse teardown order, and is the same topo-sort the cross-crate doc §170 mandates for all ordering).

**Pros:** Faithfully realizes 'honoring dependencies' as a COMPUTED property, not an approximation: cycles, lazy/deferred/ObjectProvider edges, and circular setter references are ordered correctly (or diagnosed) where R1's recorded-creation-order would silently mis-destroy — closing R1's sharpest fidelity gap exactly where Spring's wording does more than naive reverse-registration.; Maximal unification (§2.11, cross-crate §170): the SAME dependency-edge data autowiring-resolution captures and the SAME topo-sort the cross-crate doc mandates for all ordering drives BOTH forward construction order and reverse teardown — one ordering primitive, not a bespoke teardown ledger, shared with auto-config-ordering and collection-injection's ordering needs.; Within-wave concurrent destruction falls out as a structural property (a wave = no-remaining-dependents nodes → destroyed concurrently in a JoinSet) — faster teardown than R1's strict LIFO, and the concurrency is HONEST (beans with no inter-dependency are torn down together) rather than an unsafe optimization bolted onto a linear ledger.; Cycle detection at sort time is the EARLIEST, richest diagnostic (§2.9): 'these singletons form a destruction cycle, broken by tiebreak X' is computed and reported before any destroy runs, whereas R1 cannot even see the cycle — turning a silent mis-ordering into an explainable event.; The reverse-topo walk is symmetric with refresh-lifecycle's forward dependency-layer build — teardown is literally 'refresh in reverse over the same graph', maximally teachable and inspectable (close() can dump the planned teardown wave schedule, great debuggability).; Same no-async-Drop, per-bean-timeout, error-aggregation, origin-agnostic-destroyer, CAS-close-once posture as R1 — it inherits all those wins and only swaps the order-computation, so it composes with the same lifecycle-callbacks destroy chains and runtime-lifecycle stop_all().

**Cons:** Requires the container to RETAIN the live dependency graph until close — more state than R1's flat ledger, and it depends on autowiring-resolution actually recording resolved edges (including the hard-to-capture lazy/ObjectProvider/dynamic edges that are the very ones R1 misses), so the fidelity win is only as good as the edge-capture completeness (a coupling to the deferred resolution model).; Topo-sorting at the SHUTDOWN moment runs graph computation exactly when the system may be degraded/failing (a cancelled bring-up, an OOM, a panic-in-progress) — more fragile than R1's 'just reverse the list', and a malformed/partial graph (cancelled bring-up) needs careful handling so the sort itself can't fail the teardown.; Cycle tiebreak is a genuine semantic choice with no perfect answer (reverse-creation within the cycle is a heuristic) — it adds a documented-but-debatable behaviour R1 sidesteps by never modeling cycles, and getting it wrong destroys a cyclic pair in an order that breaks one of them.; Pushes ordering onto a runtime DAG, partly pre-deciding the ordering-primitive and registry/resolution keystones (§3): presenting a dependency-graph teardown as THE shape risks biasing Phase 2 toward 'there is one global dependency graph object' — must stay a CANDIDATE, especially as scopes/ownership may fragment the graph per-scope.; More machinery for the COMMON case (an acyclic eager-constructor graph) where R1's reverse-ledger produces the identical order with far less work — the topo-sort is cold-path so tolerable, but it is conceptual+code surface that the 90% case doesn't strictly need (DX-simplicity tension).; Within-wave concurrent destroy reintroduces the question of shared-resource teardown ordering INSIDE a wave (two beans with no dependency edge but a hidden runtime resource interaction) — concurrency the linear ledger never exposes, a subtle new hazard.

**Mechanisms considered:** a retained `DependencyGraph` (nodes = live singletons, edges = resolved depends-on captured at autowiring) reverse-topo-sorted at close() into destroy WAVES; reverse-topological walk: destroy a bean only after all dependents destroyed; within-wave concurrent (JoinSet), across-wave sequential; cycle detection at sort time as a rich diagnostic, broken by a documented tiebreak (reverse-creation-order within the cycle); identical orchestration prologue to R1 (CAS close-once → ContextClosedEvent → await runtime-lifecycle stop_all()) then the reverse-topo destroy-wave walk under per-bean timeouts + error aggregation; cancelled-bring-up reuses the reverse-topo machinery over the PARTIAL graph, skipping event + stop_all; the SAME topo-sort engine reused for forward wiring order and the cross-crate-doc-mandated all-ordering primitive (the §2.11 / §170 unification bet)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same leaf-core-defines-the-contract posture as R1 (close orchestrator, types, state machine) but the destruction-ORDER input is the live dependency graph rather than a creation-order ledger — so this realization couples cross-crate to whatever records resolved dependency edges (autowiring-resolution + the DependsOn relationship), and that edge data must itself be origin-agnostic (a WASM-module bean's dependency edges must be captured in the same graph so it sorts into the correct teardown wave). The topo-sort is the SAME ordering primitive the cross-crate doc §170 mandates ('do all ordering at runtime via topological sort over descriptor dependencies/priority, never link/section order') and that auto-config-ordering and collection-injection want — a strong T1 unification argument: the teardown order, the forward build order, and the ordering of contributions across crates all share ONE topo-sort engine over edges-as-data, which is exactly the cross-crate-safe way to order things (never relying on link/registration order). DCE-silent-failure is inherited the same way as R1 (a DCE'd crate's singleton has no node, surfacing upstream); but R2 adds a richer self-check opportunity — a singleton present as a node but with a DANGLING dependency edge to an absent (DCE'd) bean is detectable at sort time as a loud diagnostic, pinpointing the missing source (cross-crate §149-152), strictly more than R1's count-only inheritance. The two-source duality is as soft as R1 (a dynamically-sourced singleton contributes its node+edges to the same graph and sorts in correctly). Generics-cannot-register lives upstream (§153). The cross-crate surface is the dependency-edge format (shared with the resolution model) plus the thin destroyer types — wider than R1's ledger-only surface, the cost of the unification.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical execution posture to R1 except the order-computation: close() on the caller/hook task, atomic CAS close-once (wait-free, loser awaits via watch/notify §2.4), then ContextClosedEvent, then AWAIT runtime-lifecycle stop_all() (full stop before any destroy). Then the reverse-TOPO walk: the topo-sort itself is SYNCHRONOUS cold-path work (one-time, before any destroy runs — produces the wave schedule + cycle diagnostics, the earliest failure point §2.9); then destroy WAVES — within each wave the destroyers are spawned into a structured-concurrency scope (JoinSet) and awaited CONCURRENTLY under per-bean tokio::time::timeout, and the next wave proceeds only after the current wave is fully drained/abandoned (across-wave sequential preserves reverse-dependency order; within-wave concurrent because those beans have no inter-dependency). A destroy may await; blocking destroy → spawn_blocking (§2.3). CANCELLATION + no-async-Drop: cancelling the close task cancels the in-flight wave's scope → in-flight destroyer futures drop, resources possibly un-released, the ShutdownReport records the partial state per wave (rich §2.9 diagnostic), never silent. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the close-winner task owns the walk; the graph is read-only at this point (no further wiring), so no lock is held across a destroyer await. ERRORS: per-bean destroy failures aggregated best-effort per wave; a hung destroyer bounded by its timeout; a CYCLE surfaces at sort time (before any destroy) with the tiebreak applied and reported. CANCELLED-BRING-UP runs on the failed-bootstrap task over the PARTIAL graph (only wired beans have edges) — the reverse-topo walk over the partial graph tears down exactly the created beans (no event, no stop_all), mirroring refresh's all-or-nothing; the partial-graph sort must tolerate a half-built graph without itself failing.

#### R3 — Teardown as a guarded, observable shutdown STATE MACHINE driven by a structured-concurrency scope-guard: a typed RunDown machine (Active→Stopping→Destroying→Closed | →Cancelled) makes the cancel-vs-close fork, idempotency, and partial-teardown ENUMERATED transitions, with the destroy walk as a scope-guarded async drop-replacement
ORTHOGONAL to R1/R2 on the order-source (it composes with EITHER underneath); this realization is the CONTROLLER + observability layer, mirroring the provided runtime-lifecycle R3 for symmetry. Push the no-async-Drop + cancel-vs-close + idempotency concerns to their logical end: instead of close()/cancel_bring_up() as two ad-hoc procedures over a ledger or graph (R1/R2), model the context's TEARDOWN as an explicit, total, typed state machine — `RunDown = Active | Stopping | Destroying | Closed | Cancelled` — so Spring's doClose-vs-cancelRefresh fork, the close-once CAS, the stop-skipped-on-cancel hazard, and the partial-teardown-on-cancellation corner all become ENUMERATED transitions with defined semantics rather than emergent branches. The machine is driven by a SCOPE-GUARD: the entire singleton set is owned by a structured-concurrency `ShutdownScope` whose teardown is the explicit, awaited stand-in for async Drop (nightly doc: 'awaited in reverse dependency order under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation' — this realization makes that scope a FIRST-CLASS object). The transitions: from Active, a clean `close()` event → Stopping (await runtime-lifecycle stop_all(), publish ContextClosedEvent) → Destroying (run the reverse-order destroy walk — order computed by EITHER R1's ledger OR R2's graph) → Closed. A `cancel_bring_up()` event from Active (or a refresh-failure signal) → Cancelled-Destroying (run the destroy walk over what-exists, but SKIP Stopping and SKIP ContextClosedEvent — the cancelRefresh fork modeled as a distinct transition) → Cancelled (non-active, NOT Closed — Spring's exact 'left non-active rather than closed' state). The machine is TOTAL: close() while Stopping/Destroying = AWAIT the in-flight teardown (idempotency as a defined transition, dissolving the close-once CAS into the state machine — a second close subscribes to the in-flight one via a watch channel, no lock); close() after Closed = no-op; cancel during Destroying, signal-hook-fires-during-close, etc. all have defined outcomes. The RunDown state is held behind a `tokio::sync::watch<RunDown>` so process-signal hooks, readiness probes, and lifecycle-events SUBSCRIBE to teardown progress (REACTIVE, §2.4 — 'is the context closed yet' is an awaited transition, never a polled flag). The ShutdownScope guard guarantees that even on panic/early-return during teardown, the remaining destroyers are still driven (the structured-concurrency drop semantics give the 'all-or-nothing teardown' the explicit-async-Drop-replacement needs). lifecycle-events' ContextClosedEvent is published as the →Stopping/→Closed transition side-effect, unifying teardown observation with the event bus.

**Pros:** Makes the cancel-vs-close fork and idempotency FIRST-CLASS and total: Spring's doClose-vs-cancelRefresh (no-event/no-stop on cancel), the close-once gate, the stop-skipped-on-cancel hazard, and partial-teardown-on-cancellation become ENUMERATED transitions with defined semantics — directly designing OUT the 'drain logic in stop() silently bypassed on cancel' footgun rather than documenting around it (the deepest §2.9 win).; The ShutdownScope guard is the most literal realization of the nightly-doc no-async-Drop alternative ('reverse-order under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation') as a FIRST-CLASS object — it guarantees remaining destroyers are driven even on panic/early-return during teardown, giving the all-or-nothing teardown guarantee that a plain procedure (R1/R2) achieves only by careful coding.; Reactive teardown observation (§2.4): a process-signal hook, a readiness probe ('am I shutting down?'), and lifecycle-events SUBSCRIBE to RunDown transitions via a watch channel — woken on transition, never polling a closed flag — and the ContextClosedEvent becomes a transition side-effect, unifying teardown observation with the event bus (§2.11).; Idempotent close-once is a DEFINED transition (a second close awaits the in-flight one via the watch channel) rather than an atomic CAS bolted on — and concurrent close/cancel/signal-hook races are enumerated outcomes, the cleanest no-global-lock serialization of teardown operations (§2.5 — Spring's startupShutdownLock dissolved into single-controller-serialized transitions).; Orthogonal to the order-source: the controller composes with EITHER R1's reverse-ledger OR R2's reverse-topo graph for the actual destroy walk — so it does not prejudge the ordering keystone; it adds the teardown-state-machine + scope-guard + observability layer ABOVE whichever order Phase 2 picks, maximizing reuse and mirroring the provided runtime-lifecycle R3 (the run-state machine) for symmetry.; The explicit RunDown state is a superb diagnostic/test surface: at any instant 'what is teardown doing, which bean is being destroyed, what timed out' is a single typed value + a transition history — far richer than a procedure's opaque mid-shutdown stack, and a hung destroyer leaves the machine legibly in Destroying with the offending bean named (§2.9).

**Cons:** Heaviest conceptual surface and the largest divergence from Spring (which has only active/closed booleans + a CAS, no explicit teardown state machine) — modeling a 5-state machine + a scope-guard is genuinely MORE than Spring, risking over-engineering the common case (an app that just close()s once pays for the total-transition + observability machinery it never needs; DX-simplicity tension with §2.1).; Totality is a specification BURDEN: enumerating EVERY (state, event) pair (signal-hook-fires-during-cancel, close-during-Destroying, cancel-during-Stopping, panic-during-a-destroy-wave) is a large correctness surface that LOOKS total but can have gaps — an incompletely-specified machine is worse than R1's simple CAS+procedure because it implies guarantees it may not keep.; Building on a structured-concurrency ShutdownScope guard + a controller-serialized transition model leans toward primitives (scope guards, a single controller task, watch channels) that may PRE-JUDGE the deferred async-execution/structured-concurrency keystone (§3) — whether leaf even has a first-class scope-guard or controller is a Phase-2 call, and building teardown on one biases that signal (must stay a CANDIDATE).; Publishing ContextClosedEvent as a transition side-effect couples teardown tightly to lifecycle-events/event-multicaster's realization; if that event model lands differently (typed channels vs a bus) the unification may not hold and the watch channel + event bus become two overlapping notification mechanisms (redundancy, §2.11 working against itself).; Still inherits R1's or R2's order-computation underneath (it is orthogonal, not a replacement) — so the state machine adds correctness/observability but does NOT by itself answer 'recorded-reverse vs graph-reverse' (the hardest ordering question), which a reviewer might expect a 'state machine' realization to subsume but it deliberately does not.; The watch<RunDown> latest-value-wins semantics can drop intermediate transitions a subscriber cared about (a known watch pitfall) — fine for 'are we closed' but lossy for 'observe every per-bean destroy step', possibly needing a broadcast/event-log alongside, more machinery.

**Mechanisms considered:** explicit typed `RunDown = Active|Stopping|Destroying|Closed|Cancelled` modeled as a TOTAL teardown state machine (every (state,event) pair defined; cancel-vs-close is the Stopping+event vs skip-both fork); a first-class structured-concurrency `ShutdownScope` guard owning the singleton set, whose awaited teardown is the explicit no-async-Drop stand-in (reverse-order, per-bean timeouts, error aggregation, drives remaining destroyers even on panic); the current state behind `tokio::sync::watch<RunDown>` so signal-hooks / readiness probes / lifecycle-events SUBSCRIBE to teardown progress (§2.4 reactive), with idempotent close-once as a defined await-the-in-flight transition (no CAS, no lock); ContextClosedEvent published as the →Stopping/→Closed transition side-effect, unifying teardown observation with the event bus; order-source ORTHOGONAL — composes with EITHER R1's reverse-ledger OR R2's reverse-topo graph for the actual destroy walk (this realization is the controller, not the order); single-controller-serialized transitions dissolving Spring's startupShutdownLock; a stuck destroyer bounded by per-bean timeout + a state-machine-defined recovery, never an interrupted OS thread

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The order-source / destroyer-collection T1 is identical to R1 or R2 (whichever it composes with) — leaf-core defines the RunDown machine, the ShutdownScope contract, the CloseError/ShutdownReport types; destroyers arrive as a runtime byproduct of construction (R1 ledger) or order is derived from the live graph (R2). The DISTINCTIVE cross-crate surface is the teardown OBSERVATION channel: `watch_state() -> Receiver<RunDown>` is a thin, stable, origin-agnostic API that process-signal hooks, integration crates ('flush my buffers when the context starts Stopping'), readiness probes, and app code consume — keeping it a typed enum + a watch receiver means a WASM-module bean or an external probe observes teardown progress identically to a native one. Because the controller serializes transitions, no contributing crate may drive teardown out-of-band — only the controller does; beans/destroyers are PASSIVE (called by the scope-guard), keeping the cross-crate contract clean (passive destroyers + an observation channel, never a back-channel). The notable cross-crate RISK is that the ShutdownScope guard + controller assume Phase 2 provides a structured-concurrency-scope and/or controller primitive — if leaf's async model is task-based-without-scope-guards, the ShutdownScope becomes an explicit ledger + a cancellation-safe drain loop instead (still cross-crate-clean, but a different internal substrate; flagged: this realization's controller/scope substrate is downstream of the deferred async/structured-concurrency keystone, a phase2Question). The lifecycle-events-as-transition-output coupling means context-close and lifecycle-events SHARE the watch/notification substrate across the crate boundary — a unification opportunity that must be coordinated in Phase 3. DCE-silent-failure and the two-source duality are inherited from R1/R2 unchanged. CANDIDATES not decisions.

**T2 — execution context:** A single controller (an owned task or the close-caller's task acting as the serialization point) OWNS the RunDown state and processes teardown EVENTS one-at-a-time, so concurrent close()/cancel/signal-hook callers AWAIT the in-flight transition via the watch/mailbox rather than racing (no lock, §2.5 — serialization by single-ownership, not a mutex). The transition sequence on a clean close: Active→Stopping (await runtime-lifecycle stop_all() — reverse-phase graceful drain; publish ContextClosedEvent, waking watch subscribers §2.4)→Destroying (run the reverse-order destroy walk inside the ShutdownScope: destroyers spawned into the structured-concurrency scope, awaited under per-bean tokio::time::timeout, in R1-LIFO or R2-wave order)→Closed (publish the closed transition). CANCELLATION + no-async-Drop: the ShutdownScope guard is the crux — if the close task is cancelled or panics mid-Destroying, the scope's own teardown DRIVES the remaining destroyers to completion-or-timeout before the scope drops (the structured-concurrency guarantee the explicit-async-Drop-replacement needs), and a destroyer whose work was interrupted leaves its resource un-released with the ShutdownReport recording it (rich §2.9 diagnostic, never silent). A hung destroyer is bounded by its per-bean timeout and surfaces a state-machine-defined recovery (the machine moves on, records the stall) — the rust-native form of Spring's stuck-shutdown-thread, NOT an interrupted OS thread. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the only serialization is single-controller-ownership; the watch channel is the §2.4 notification primitive (teardown progress is never busy-waited). ERRORS: per-bean destroy failures aggregated best-effort into the ShutdownReport; a start/stop error from stop_all() during →Stopping transitions to a defined state and is reported. CANCELLED-BRING-UP is the Active→Cancelled-Destroying→Cancelled transition: the destroy walk runs over what-exists but SKIPS the Stopping transition and the ContextClosedEvent, landing in Cancelled (non-active, not Closed) — Spring's cancelRefresh fork as an enumerated transition, on the failed-bootstrap task.

### Rust clashes
- No async Drop makes the reverse-order destroy walk the ONLY teardown, mandatory and explicit — Rust has neither GC nor finalizers nor async Drop. Spring's standalone teardown is already opt-in (close()/registerShutdownHook, not process exit), but its fallback is the JVM eventually GC-ing/finalizing forgotten beans. leaf has no such fallback: a singleton holding async-acquired resources (open sockets, pools, in-flight tasks) cannot release them in Drop (nightly doc 273-281: async Drop is experimental-incomplete, crashes on tokio shutdown, no dyn story — leaf's beans are dyn-erased), so the container-driven async destroy walk this feature DRIVES is the sole teardown mechanism. This makes context-close LOAD-BEARING for correctness in a way Spring's close() is not: skip it (forget to call close() / wire no signal hook) and async resources leak with no GC safety net — sharpening Spring's 'developers wrongly assume JVM exit handles it' from a footgun into a hard requirement. The destroy walk is awaited in reverse dependency order under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts + error aggregation — the nightly doc's exact prescribed stable alternative.
- Spring's destroySingletons() under a synchronized/ReentrantLock + GC-reliant 'forget' maps to an explicit awaited reverse-order async drain with per-bean timeouts. Spring iterates the disposable beans in reverse registration order honoring dependencies, calling each bean's destroy chain synchronously, and a forgotten/unreachable bean is GC'd. In async leaf, each destroyer is an `async fn shutdown()` the orchestrator AWAITS (no Runnable, no sync method), drained in reverse order under a per-bean tokio::time::timeout, with failures AGGREGATED into a ShutdownReport rather than the first failure aborting (Spring's best-effort destroy). The per-bean timeout (the destroy analogue of runtime-lifecycle's per-phase drain timeout) bounds a hung destroyer; the drain wait is a cold-path await on completion, never a poll (§2.4).
- 'Reverse registration order WHILE HONORING DEPENDENCIES' is ambiguous between recorded-creation-order (R1) and a live-dependency-graph reverse-topo (R2) — and Rust forces the choice into the open. On the JVM Spring records a disposable-bean order and a dependent-bean map and reverses both; in Rust, recorded creation order is a faithful proxy ONLY when every dependency was constructed-before (true for eager constructor wiring, FALSE for setter/field circular references and lazy/ObjectProvider-deferred edges — circular-references and deferral-primitives). R1 keeps the recorded ledger (max robustness, inherits the mis-order-on-cycle gap); R2 reverse-topo-sorts the live graph (correct on cycles via a tiebreak + cycle diagnostic, but couples to edge-capture completeness). Either way, Spring's iterate-and-instanceof-DisposableBean discovery is impossible (no runtime type-walk) — destroyers are a recorded ledger / graph of erased async closures, never discovered by inspecting live instances.
- Spring's startupShutdownLock (ReentrantLock, ex-synchronized monitor) + stuck-shutdown-thread interrupt collides with §2.5's no-global-lock and async cancellation. Spring guards close()/refresh with a lock and, in registerShutdownHook, interrupts/abandons a thread stuck WAITING (e.g. a user System.exit during startup) to avoid deadlocking the hook. In leaf this cannot be a global lock: concurrent close()/cancel/signal-hook on the SAME context is serialized by execution context — an atomic CAS close-once (R1/R2) or single-controller-serialized transitions (R3) where a second close AWAITS the in-flight one via a watch/notify, never contending a mutex and never holding a lock across a destroyer await. The stuck-shutdown-thread maps to async cancellation + per-bean timeouts + a defined recovery: a hung destroy is bounded by its timeout and surfaces a rich diagnostic (which bean stalled teardown), never an interrupted OS thread or a deadlocked hook.
- The cancelRefresh-vs-doClose fork (no ContextClosedEvent, no lifecycleProcessor.onClose() on a failed/cancelled bring-up) becomes a no-async-Drop partial-teardown obligation. Spring's failed refresh calls destroyBeans()+cancelRefresh(): it destroys the already-created singletons but skips the event and the stop pass, leaving the context non-active (not 'closed'); on the JVM the half-built graph is otherwise GC-collectable. In leaf, with no async Drop, the partial graph MUST be explicitly torn down in reverse order (the same destroy walk, minus stop_all and minus the Closed event) or its async resources leak — so refresh's all-or-nothing guarantee depends on this feature's cancelled-bring-up entry point actually running the reverse-order destroy over exactly the beans that got created. The 'stop may be skipped on cancel, so drain-logic-only-in-stop is bypassed' hazard (Spring 418) is inherited and sharpened: a bean's destroy chain must tolerate stop() never having run (shared with runtime-lifecycle's immediate-destroy-without-stop contract).
- Spring's process-exit shutdown hook (a JVM Runtime.addShutdownHook thread) maps to an async signal-listener task, not a thread-parking hook. registerShutdownHook spawns a JVM thread that runs doClose() on SIGTERM/exit. In leaf the analogue is an opt-in task awaiting a ctrl-c/SIGTERM signal future that calls close() once — a cold-path await, not a parked OS thread, and the signal-handler-safety constraints differ (an async signal future on tokio vs a JVM hook thread). The hook is a TRIGGER only (no teardown logic); and because close() is idempotent (CAS / state machine), an explicit close() followed by a signal-fired close() is a defined no-op, dissolving the Spring race where both the hook and an explicit close could contend the lock. The signal-listener is runtime-specific (tokio::signal) so likely lives in an integration crate (leaf-tokio), not the runtime-agnostic core (§6).
- Spring's 'a bean is destroyed before the beans it depends on, dependents before dependencies' relies on a stable retained dependent-graph; Rust's ownership model may not retain a single global graph. The deferred scope/lifetime/ownership keystone (§3) may store singletons in per-scope arenas with independent lifetimes rather than one global registry — in which case neither a single global creation-order ledger (R1) nor one global dependency graph (R2) is the natural carrier, and teardown order may need to be per-scope-then-cross-scope. Spring's single GC'd heap of shared singletons has no such fragmentation; leaf's teardown ordering is downstream of how singletons are owned/retained (a clash flagged as a phase2Question, shared with scopes and concurrency-contract).
- Within-phase/within-level concurrent destruction (Spring's per-phase concurrent stop, and R2's within-wave concurrent destroy) has no async-Drop safety net for a partially-completed concurrent wave. If a destroy wave runs N destroyers concurrently in a JoinSet and the close task is cancelled or one panics, the in-flight futures drop with resources possibly un-released — Spring's synchronous per-phase latch at least completes each callback or times out as a unit. leaf must define what a cancelled/partially-failed concurrent wave leaves behind (recorded per-bean in the ShutdownReport) and ensure the structured-concurrency scope drives the remaining wave members to completion-or-timeout before dropping (R3's ShutdownScope guard makes this explicit; R1/R2 must code it carefully).

### Interactions
- **runtime-lifecycle** — UPSTREAM DEPENDENCY THIS FEATURE TRIGGERS (provided design). context-close calls runtime-lifecycle's stop_all() as the FIRST teardown step (reverse/descending phase, per-phase graceful drain timeout) to quiesce runtime activity BEFORE destroying any singleton — Spring's lifecycleProcessor.onClose()-before-destroyBeans(). runtime-lifecycle OWNS the phase-grouped stop choreography and the per-phase concurrent drain; context-close OWNS only the decision to invoke it first and the subsequent reverse-order destruction. SHARED HAZARD: the immediate-destroy-without-stop contract — context-close's clean path stops-then-destroys, but its cancelled-bring-up path (and a drain timeout) destroys WITHOUT a preceding stop, so every participant/bean's destroy must tolerate stop never having run. This is the exact seam where Spring's 'drain logic in stop() can be bypassed' footgun lives; the two designs must keep their two teardown entry points coherent.
- **lifecycle-callbacks** — UPSTREAM DEPENDENCY THIS FEATURE DRIVES (provided design). lifecycle-callbacks DEFINES each bean's per-bean destroy chain (@PreDestroy → DisposableBean.destroy → destroy-method/inferred close/shutdown) and its internal 3-step order; context-close OWNS the cross-bean reverse-dependency WALK that decides WHEN and IN-WHAT-ORDER those per-bean destroyers run. The provided lifecycle-callbacks design explicitly 'registers a destroyer into context-close's reverse-dependency teardown ledger' for singletons and 'registers nothing' for prototypes — R1's TeardownLedger IS that ledger; R2's graph orders the same destroyers; R3's ShutdownScope drives them. lifecycle-callbacks' partial-destroy-on-init-failure compensation also runs its destroy chain; context-close's cancelled-bring-up path runs the cross-bean version of the same.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — INVERSE SIBLING (provided design context). refresh-lifecycle is the fixed-order BRING-UP template (load definitions → BFPPs → BPPs → init message source/multicaster → eager-instantiate singletons → finishRefresh auto-start); context-close is its INVERSE — teardown is 'refresh in reverse'. The boundary: refresh's all-or-nothing guarantee means a FAILED refresh hands off to context-close's CANCELLED-BRING-UP entry point (Spring's destroyBeans()+cancelRefresh: destroy created singletons, NO ContextClosedEvent, NO stop pass, leave non-active). A SUCCESSFUL refresh hands off to runtime-lifecycle's auto-start; the subsequent clean close() is context-close's primary path. context-close must consume refresh's record of what-got-created (the ledger/graph) so the cancelled path tears down exactly the partial graph.
- **lifecycle-events** — PUBLISHER. context-close asks lifecycle-events to PUBLISH the ContextClosedEvent at the START of the clean close path (Spring publishes it FIRST in doClose, before stop) — the 'context is end-of-life, cannot be restarted' fact — and may publish the closed availability/readiness state. lifecycle-events defines the event payload + the liveness/readiness contract; context-close fires it at the precise transition. CRUCIALLY the cancelled-bring-up path does NOT publish ContextClosedEvent (Spring's cancelRefresh distinction). In R3 the event is literally the →Stopping/→Closed state-transition side-effect, unifying teardown observation with the event bus.
- **scopes** — POLICY SIBLING. scopes fixes per-scope teardown: singleton = destroyed at context close (this feature's primary domain); prototype = NEVER destroyed (the container retains no destroyer — structural, the ledger/graph has no entry); web/session/custom = destruction-callback fires on SCOPE-END (a different trigger than context close — Spring's Scope.registerDestructionCallback). So context-close owns ONLY the singleton (and context-application) scope teardown; shorter-lived scopes are torn down by their own scope-end events, not by close(). The never-destroyed prototype contract is enforced here by there being no destroyer to walk. If the ownership model fragments singletons per-scope-arena, context-close's order may become per-scope-then-cross-scope (a phase2Question shared with scopes).
- **bean-instantiation** — UPSTREAM SUPPLIER of the teardown ledger entries. bean-instantiation's per-bean creation sequence, at the publication gate (after lifecycle-callbacks' init returns Ok), PUSHES the singleton's destroyer onto context-close's ledger (R1) / records its node+edges in the dependency graph (R2). It pushes nothing for prototypes. bean-instantiation also owns the creation-order that R1 reverses and the partial-construction unwind that context-close's cancelled-bring-up path mirrors at cross-bean scope. The publication-then-register-destroyer discipline and the no-lock-held-across-await discipline are shared contracts; context-close consumes the resulting ledger/graph.
- **concurrency-contract** — FOUNDATION CONSTRAINT. The teardown state (which beans torn down, the RunDown/close-once state, the ShutdownReport) is runtime-mutable state changed AFTER beans are published — exactly what concurrency-contract says needs explicit safe publication, not the init-phase config that gets it for free. concurrency-contract frames safe-publication-of-fully-built-singletons and delegates mutable-runtime-state safety to the owner; context-close's close-once is a lock-free atomic CAS (R1/R2) or single-controller-serialized state (R3), and the destroy-walk holds no lock across a destroyer await — upholding the contract for framework-side teardown state without a global lock (§2.5).
- **factory-bean** — ORPHANED-PRODUCT HAZARD (shared with lifecycle-callbacks). A FactoryBean is a singleton with its own destroyer on the ledger; its PRODUCT may also hold resources. Spring ORPHANS the product (does NOT auto-call product.close(); the factory must implement DisposableBean and delegate). With no async Drop + container-driven reverse-order teardown, an orphaned product holding async-cleanup resources is a guaranteed leak (no GC fallback). Whether the product's destroyer also rides the ledger (disciplined, no leak) or stays orphaned (Spring-faithful) is a shared phase2Question; context-close walks whatever destroyers were registered, so the decision is upstream (factory-bean / lifecycle-callbacks) but the leak consequence lands here.
- **events** — DOWNSTREAM PARTICIPANT + ORDERING. The event-multicaster (and any async-dispatch executor backing it) is itself a singleton whose destroyer is on the ledger AND a Lifecycle participant that must drain in-flight async dispatch on stop. context-close stops it (via stop_all) before destroying it, and must destroy it LATE in the reverse walk (it has few dependencies, many dependents) so that ContextClosedEvent — published FIRST — can still be dispatched before the multicaster is torn down. In R3 the run-down watch channel and the event bus are candidate-overlapping notification mechanisms Phase 3 must reconcile.
- **startup-instrumentation** — EMITTER. context-close emits named, timed steps startup-instrumentation records — symmetric to the bring-up steps: the stop pass duration, the per-bean (or per-wave) destroy durations, and timed-out/abandoned destroyers — the stable taxonomy for diagnosing a slow or hung SHUTDOWN (the mirror of a slow boot). startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default opt-in tree; context-close's teardown phase boundaries and per-bean destroy timings are prime diagnostic material, especially a per-bean timeout firing.
- **context-propagation** — CROSS-CUTTING CONSTRAINT. Because the destroy walk spawns per-bean (or per-wave) destroyer futures into a structured-concurrency scope (a task hop), any ambient context (request/locale/transaction/tracing) a destroyer relies on must be CAPTURED and RESTORED across that hop. context-propagation provides the capture/restore SPI; context-close's destroy fan-out is one of the cold-path boundaries that ambient context must survive — the same thread-hop problem context-propagation exists to solve, here on teardown.
- **container-hierarchy** — ORDERING NEIGHBOUR. In a parent/child context hierarchy (child sees parent, not vice versa), a CHILD context must be closed BEFORE its parent (the parent's singletons may be depended on by the child's). context-close in a child triggers only the child's teardown; the parent's close is a separate, later event. The one-directional visibility means a child's destroyers may capture handles to parent singletons, so the parent must not be torn down until all children are closed — a cross-context ordering constraint layered on top of this feature's intra-context reverse order (a phase2Question if hierarchy auto-cascades close).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the dependency-honoring reverse destruction order computed from a RECORDED creation-order ledger (R1 — max robustness at the fragile shutdown moment, but silently mis-orders setter/field circular references and lazy/ObjectProvider-deferred edges where 'honoring dependencies' does more than naive reverse-registration) or a REVERSE-TOPOLOGICAL sort of the LIVE dependency graph (R2 — correct on cycles via a documented tiebreak + cycle diagnostic, within-wave concurrency for free, and the §2.11/§170 one-ordering-primitive shared with autowiring/auto-config-ordering/collection-injection, but couples to edge-capture completeness and runs graph computation exactly when the system may be degraded)? This cross-cuts autowiring-resolution (which records edges), circular-references, deferral-primitives, and the ordering-primitive unification — a meta-architecture call, not Phase 1's.
- Does context-close model teardown as a procedure + an atomic close-once CAS (R1/R2 — simple, Spring-faithful, common-case-cheap) or as an explicit total RunDown state machine + a structured-concurrency ShutdownScope guard (R3 — makes the cancel-vs-close fork, idempotency, partial-teardown, and the stop-skipped-on-cancel hazard ENUMERATED transitions designed-out rather than coded-around, reactive teardown observation via a watch channel, but the most machinery and a dependency on a deferred scope-guard/controller primitive)? This decides how much teardown machinery leaf builds and whether the context exposes a typed RunDown observable — and building R3 pre-judges whether leaf has a first-class structured-concurrency scope-guard / controller, downstream of the deferred async/structured-concurrency model.
- What is the cancellation + partial-teardown contract given NO async Drop, and how is it SHARED across the cancelled-bring-up path, the clean close path, lifecycle-callbacks' partial-destroy-on-init-failure, and runtime-lifecycle's drain-timeout? When a per-bean destroy times out, or the close task is cancelled mid-walk, or a concurrent destroy wave partially fails — what mechanism guarantees the remaining destroyers are still driven to completion-or-timeout (a structured-concurrency scope-guard? an explicit drain loop?), and what does a timed-out/abandoned destroyer leave behind (possibly-unreleased resource) that the ShutdownReport must record? The all-or-nothing teardown guarantee (and refresh's all-or-nothing) is only as strong as this; shared with bean-instantiation, lifecycle-callbacks, runtime-lifecycle, and the async-execution keystone.
- How is the per-bean graceful-destroy TIMEOUT realized and tuned (the destroy analogue of runtime-lifecycle's per-phase drain timeout), and is it per-bean, per-wave, or global? What is the rust-native completion semantics — an `async fn shutdown()` whose resolution IS Spring's destroy-method returning, awaited under a per-bean tokio::time::timeout, with errors aggregated into a ShutdownReport? Couples to task-execution (the executor that backs the drain and must itself drain), context-propagation (ambient context across the destroy task-hop), and the async-execution keystone.
- Where does the singleton-handle the destroyer captures LIVE, given the deferred scope/lifetime/ownership keystone? If singletons are one global Arc-retained registry, R1's single global ledger and R2's single global graph are natural; if they are per-scope arenas with independent lifetimes, teardown order may need to be per-scope-then-cross-scope and there may be no single global creation order to reverse. The destroyer's capture of the bean handle (Arc<dyn> vs arena slot) and the avoidance of double-retention (ledger + registry both holding the bean) are downstream of this keystone. Shared with scopes, bean-instantiation, and concurrency-contract.
- Is teardown progress observed REACTIVELY (R3's tokio::sync::watch<RunDown> that signal-hooks/readiness-probes/lifecycle-events subscribe to, satisfying §2.4 for 'am I shutting down / is it closed yet') or via a poll-able is_closed() flag? If reactive, is the run-down notification channel SHARED with lifecycle-events (the ContextClosedEvent IS the →Stopping/→Closed transition, a §2.11 unification) or a separate mechanism (risking two overlapping notification substrates)? Cross-cuts lifecycle-events, event-multicaster, and the deferred unified diagnostics/observability model.
- How is the process-signal shutdown hook (registerShutdownHook analogue) wired and is it opt-in — an async ctrl-c/SIGTERM listener task that calls close() once — and is it runtime-specific (tokio::signal) and therefore in an integration crate (leaf-tokio) rather than the runtime-agnostic core (§6 pluggable runtimes)? How does it interact with idempotent close-once when both an explicit close() and a signal-fired close() race? Shared with the bootstrap layer (the SpringApplication-equivalent that enables the hook) and the runtime-agnosticism goal.
- How does cross-context hierarchy teardown ordering compose — must a child context be fully closed before its parent (the parent's singletons may be depended on by the child's, and child destroyers may capture parent handles), and does closing a parent auto-cascade-close its children, or are they independent close() calls? This layers a cross-context reverse order on top of the intra-context reverse order. Shared with container-hierarchy and the ownership model (who retains the child→parent reference).
- How does the two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source) feed teardown, given that destroyers are a RUNTIME byproduct of construction (softer than the definition-freeze): a dynamically-sourced singleton created after the initial bring-up pushes its destroyer onto the ledger / contributes its node+edges, so it is torn down in the correct relative position automatically — but can it register a destroyer that bridges into a WASM module, and is it awaited identically? A CANDIDATE, not a decision; shared with the discovery/SPI family and the registry substrate.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated context-close section (407-420: the doClose() step order, stop-Lifecycle-before-destroy intent, reverse-registration-order-honoring-dependencies, destroy-method inference hazard, stop-may-be-skipped-on-hot/cancelled-refresh, per-phase-not-global timeout), the cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction (372: failed refresh destroys beans + sets non-active but skips the event and onClose — the load-bearing cancel-vs-close fork), the startupShutdownLock/stuck-shutdown-thread provenance (401, 2400), the standalone-shutdown-hook requirement (890), prototype-never-destroyed (820), the FactoryBean orphaned-product hazard (2551), and the two upstream PROVIDED designs I build on concretely: runtime-lifecycle (whose stop_all() I trigger first, sharing the immediate-destroy-without-stop hazard) and lifecycle-callbacks (whose per-bean destroy chain I walk in reverse, and which the provided design says explicitly 'registers a destroyer into context-close's reverse-dependency teardown ledger'). I grounded T1 against the cross-crate doc (DCE-is-silent + self-check mandate §149-152/345, ordering-as-data topo-sort §170, Architectures A/B/C/D with an identical flat-descriptor runtime API §277-337, generics-cannot-register §153, scope-discovery-not-global) and T2/clashes against the charter invariants (§2.3 async-first/explicit-context, §2.4 reactive-hot/poll-cold, §2.5 no-global-lock, §2.9 rich-early diagnostics, §2.11 unification) and the capability envelope — most critically NO async Drop as the first-class constraint (nightly doc 273-281) with its EXACT prescribed stable alternative being 'awaited in reverse dependency order under a structured-concurrency scope with per-bean timeouts and error aggregation', which IS this feature.\n\nSCOPING NOTE: this feature is the teardown ORCHESTRATOR/DRIVER — deliberately narrow against its two strong upstream dependencies. runtime-lifecycle owns the STOP machinery (I only trigger it first); lifecycle-callbacks owns each bean's DESTROY chain (I only order the walk). What is UNIQUELY context-close's: the doClose() SEQUENCE (stop-before-destroy), the CROSS-BEAN reverse-dependency-honoring ORDER, the idempotent close-once gate, the explicit-close-OR-signal-hook trigger, and the CANCEL-VS-CLOSE fork (cancelled bring-up destroys the partial graph but skips event+stop, leaving non-active). My three realizations diverge on DIFFERENT axes so Phase 3 can mix-and-match (mirroring how the provided runtime-lifecycle realizations were structured): R1 (recorded reverse-order ledger) is the ORDER-SOURCE = creation-order bet — highest fidelity to Spring's reverse-registration, simplest, robust at the fragile shutdown moment, dovetails with the provided lifecycle-callbacks 'ledger' phrasing, inherits the cycle/lazy mis-order gap; R2 (reverse-topo over the live dependency graph) is the ORDER-SOURCE = dependency-graph bet — faithfully computes 'honoring dependencies' (correct on cycles + within-wave concurrency), unifies with the framework's one ordering primitive per §170/§2.11, but couples to edge-capture and runs computation when degraded; R3 (RunDown state machine + structured-concurrency ShutdownScope guard) is the CONTROLLER/OBSERVABILITY bet, ORTHOGONAL to the order-source (composes with R1's ledger OR R2's graph underneath) — it makes the cancel-vs-close fork, idempotency, and partial-teardown ENUMERATED transitions and is the most literal realization of the no-async-Drop scope-guarded-teardown alternative, at the cost of the most machinery and a dependency on a deferred scope-guard/controller primitive. R1-vs-R2 is the ORDER-COMPUTATION question; R3 is the CONTROLLER question layered above either. All three keep stop-before-destroy, the cancelled-bring-up partial-teardown, per-bean-timeout + error-aggregation reverse-order destroy (the no-async-Drop alternative), idempotent close-once without a global lock, and origin-agnostic destroyers.\n\nThree things to carry forward: (1) NO async Drop makes context-close the SOLE and MANDATORY teardown (no GC fallback) — the reverse-order destroy walk is not a convenience, it is the only thing that releases async resources, and 'developers wrongly assume process exit handles it' goes from footgun to hard requirement. (2) The cancel-vs-close fork is sharpened by no-async-Drop: a failed refresh MUST explicitly tear down the partial graph (same reverse walk, minus stop_all + minus the Closed event) or leak — refresh's all-or-nothing depends on this feature's cancelled-bring-up entry point, and the 'stop skipped on cancel' hazard means destroyers must tolerate stop never having run (shared with runtime-lifecycle). (3) 'Reverse registration order WHILE honoring dependencies' is the crux ambiguity Rust forces open — recorded-creation-order (R1) is a faithful proxy ONLY for eager constructor wiring and silently mis-orders cycles/lazy edges, where reverse-topo (R2) is correct but heavier; this is the central phase2Question, downstream of the ownership/registry/resolution keystones.\n\nI refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps destroyers ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a WASM-module/test-double singleton's destroyer is awaited in the identical reverse walk via an erased async closure), keeps the teardown ledger/graph as a RUNTIME byproduct of construction (so this feature itself faces no DCE-registry hazard — it inherits the upstream one), is compatible with cross-crate Architectures A/B/C/D via the same flat runtime API, keeps the singleton-handle the destroyer captures abstract (the §3 ownership keystone), keeps the timeout/aggregation substrate and async-cancellation model deferred, and keeps the process-signal hook as an opt-in runtime-specific trigger (likely an integration-crate concern per §6). The two-source duality is SOFTER here than for definitions (destroyers escape the freeze — a dynamically-created singleton just pushes its destroyer), flagged as a phase2Question not resolved. Naming ('TeardownLedger', 'Destroyer', 'ShutdownReport', 'CloseError', 'ShutdownScope', 'RunDown', 'close', 'cancel_bring_up', 'watch_state') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. ## Startup Instrumentation (ApplicationStartup / StartupStep)  `[startup-instrumentation]`

Spring's `ApplicationStartup`/`StartupStep` (Framework 7.0, design ref §403-405) is a CONTAINER-WIDE STARTUP TRACER: `ApplicationStartup.start(name)` opens a `StartupStep` carrying an `id` and a `parentId` (so steps nest into a TREE mirroring the bring-up call structure); `tag(k, v)` attaches structured key/value metadata to a step; `end()` records its duration. The marquee DESIGN CHOICE to replicate is the THREE-VALUE OVERHEAD POSTURE: `ApplicationStartup.DEFAULT` is a NO-OP that records nothing (start() returns a step whose tag/end are empty — so the instrumented bring-up costs only an unconditional method call and a moved field on the steady, un-instrumented path), and you OPT IN to recording by swapping in a recording implementation (`BufferingApplicationStartup` keeps a bounded in-memory buffer queryable as a tree; `FlightRecorderApplicationStartup` emits JFR events) — recording is the exception, not the rule. The second load-bearing intent is the STABLE, TOOL-FRIENDLY TAXONOMY: step names are `.`-namespaced, reusable identifiers (`spring.context.refresh` is the root; `spring.context.beans.post-process`, `spring.beans.instantiate`, `spring.beans.smart-initialize`, BFPP-invocation steps are children) so external tooling/JFR can key off a STABLE vocabulary of container phases rather than ad-hoc strings, and tags carry the variable detail (which bean, which post-processor, which BFPP). The whole point is DIAGNOSING SLOW BOOTS: it answers "where did my 4-second startup go?" by giving a timed, nested, named breakdown of exactly the refresh-lifecycle phases (and the per-bean instantiation work) WITHOUT permanently taxing the boot path. leaf must replicate this INTENT — a no-op-by-default, opt-in, hierarchical, named+tagged, timed startup trace over the container bring-up — WITHOUT Spring's JVM lineage: there is no JFR (the recording sink must be a rust-native, pluggable observer, likely `tracing`-shaped or a `Recorder` trait, NOT a hard JFR dependency), the `id`/`parentId` integer-tree-with-mutable-current-pointer model assumes a single synchronous call stack that does NOT hold once bring-up fans out into concurrent eager-singleton waves / background-bootstrap futures (parent linkage must be carried explicitly through async, not read off an ambient thread-local current-step), and `start()` returning a heap `StartupStep` object you mutate is a JVM allocation pattern leaf should not blindly copy (a no-op step should ideally be a zero-sized/inlined nothing, not an allocated object). It is the OBSERVER of refresh-lifecycle (its upstream): refresh-lifecycle OWNS the fixed-order phase sequence and per-phase introspection surface; startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default recorder abstraction, the step tree/tagging/timing contract, and the stable name taxonomy that refresh-lifecycle (and bean-instantiation, smart-initializing, post-processor-spi, background-bootstrap) emit INTO.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — `tracing`-native spans: model every startup step as a `tracing` span under a leaf-owned target, with a NO-OP default subscriber and stable span names/fields as the taxonomy
Reject inventing a bespoke StartupStep object graph; recognize that Rust's ecosystem ALREADY has the exact primitive — `tracing` spans form a parent/child tree (a span entered while another is current becomes its child), carry structured fields (`tag(k,v)` == span fields / `record`), measure duration (span enter→close), and — critically — are NO-OP BY DEFAULT when no subscriber is installed (the `Dispatch::none` path: creating a disabled span is a cheap check + a `Span::none()`). So leaf's startup steps ARE `tracing` spans on a reserved target (e.g. `target: "leaf::startup"`) with the STABLE `.`-namespaced names carried as the span `name` (`leaf.context.refresh`, `leaf.beans.instantiate`, `leaf.beans.smart-initialize`, `leaf.context.beans.post-process`) and bean/processor identity as fields. refresh-lifecycle opens the root span around the whole bring-up and a child span per phase; bean-instantiation opens a `leaf.beans.instantiate` span per bean with a `bean.name`/`bean.type` field; smart-initializing wraps each callback in `leaf.beans.smart-initialize`. The OPT-IN recording is just: the application installs a `tracing` subscriber that subscribes to the `leaf::startup` target (a `Layer` filtering on that target) — a `BufferingApplicationStartup` equivalent is a small custom `Layer` that records closed spans into a bounded in-memory tree; a JFR-equivalent is whatever sink the user wires (OTel, a flamegraph layer, `tracing-chrome`). For the cross-async parent-linkage problem, use `tracing`'s explicit `Span` handle + `Instrument`/`.in_scope()` / `span.enter()` discipline: when a phase fans out into concurrent eager-singleton futures, each future is `.instrument(bean_span)` with the phase span captured as parent EXPLICITLY (not read off a thread-local at poll time) — `tracing` already solves async span propagation this way (`#[instrument]`/`Instrumented`). leaf ships a thin `leaf-startup` facade: `startup::step(name)` returns a `tracing::Span` (or `Span::none()` when the target is disabled), `step.tag(k,v)` forwards to `record`, and the step ends on drop — so the API READS like Spring's StartupStep but is hand-writable `tracing` underneath (thin-macro / no-hidden-logic, §2.10).

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11) and zero NIH: leaf gets the entire no-op-by-default-vs-opt-in-recording posture, the parent/child tree, structured tags, duration, AND a vast existing sink ecosystem (OTel, chrome-trace, flamegraph, journald) FOR FREE from `tracing` — the BufferingApplicationStartup and FlightRecorderApplicationStartup equivalents are both just `tracing` Layers, and leaf never owns a buffer/JFR-bridge it must maintain.; Genuinely no-op when off (low-overhead #2, and the FEATURE'S central intent): with no subscriber interested in `leaf::startup`, `tracing` short-circuits span creation to a cheap enabled-check + `Span::none()` — no allocation, no tree-building — matching `ApplicationStartup.DEFAULT`'s 'records nothing, minimal overhead' exactly, and the per-callsite max-level-filter can compile the check to near-nothing.; Async-first parent linkage is a SOLVED problem here (§2.3): `tracing`'s `Instrument`/`#[instrument]` already carry the parent span explicitly across `.await` and across spawned tasks, so when bring-up fans out into concurrent eager-singleton/background-bootstrap futures, each child step nests under the correct phase WITHOUT an ambient thread-local 'current step' pointer (the precise place Spring's id/parentId model breaks in async — this dissolves it by using explicit Span handles).; DX #1: Rust developers already know `tracing`; 'install a subscriber on the leaf::startup target to see your boot breakdown' is idiomatic and discoverable, RUST_LOG-style filtering gives free per-phase granularity, and the stable `.`-namespaced span names are exactly the tool-friendly taxonomy Spring prizes — now also greppable and OTel-exportable.; Thin facade keeps it hand-writable (§2.10): the `startup::step(name)`/`.tag()` API is sugar over public `tracing` calls; `cargo expand` of any macro shows plain `tracing::span!`/`record` calls, nothing hidden, escape hatch = just use `tracing` directly.

**Cons:** Couples leaf's startup-instrumentation INTENT to the `tracing` crate as a substrate — a real dependency-direction commitment the charter says is Phase 2's to make (is `tracing` part of leaf's opinionated core, or pluggable?); if Phase 2 picks a different diagnostics/observability spine, this realization's whole shape moves, so it pre-judges the unified-diagnostics keystone (§3) unless held as a candidate.; `tracing`'s tree is reconstructed by the SUBSCRIBER from enter/exit events, not handed to it as a first-class `id`/`parentId` pair — a Buffering-equivalent Layer must rebuild the parent/child tree from span lifecycle callbacks (doable via `span.parent()`/extensions, but more work than reading Spring's explicit parentId), and querying 'the startup tree' programmatically (BufferingApplicationStartup.getBufferedTimeline()) means leaf still ships that reconstruction Layer.; Span timing measures enter→close wall-clock, which for an async phase that `.await`s (yields the thread) includes time the task was PARKED, not just CPU — for 'why is boot slow' that's arguably the RIGHT number (wall-clock to-ready), but it differs from Spring's synchronous StartupStep duration and can mislead if a phase awaits I/O concurrently with other phases (overlapping spans, double-counted wall time) — a real interpretation hazard the docs must address.; Field-based tags are stringly/`Value`-typed in `tracing` and a fixed set must be declared at the span macro callsite (dynamic tag keys are awkward) — Spring's `tag(k,v)` is freely dynamic; matching that fully may need `record` with pre-declared `Empty` fields or a `valuable` escape, slightly less ergonomic than an open map.; If leaf does NOT otherwise mandate `tracing`, this makes startup-instrumentation the feature that drags `tracing` into the core dependency set — possibly heavier than a tiny bespoke recorder for users who want only the boot breakdown and nothing else.

**Mechanisms considered:** startup steps = `tracing` spans on a reserved `leaf::startup` target; stable `.`-namespaced names as span `name`, bean/processor identity as span fields; no-op default = no subscriber interested in the target → `tracing` returns `Span::none()` (the DEFAULT-records-nothing analogue); opt-in = install a `Layer` filtering that target; BufferingApplicationStartup equivalent = a custom `tracing` `Layer` that reconstructs+stores the closed-span tree in a bounded buffer; JFR equivalent = any existing sink (OTel/chrome-trace/flamegraph); cross-async parent linkage via `tracing`'s explicit `Instrument`/`#[instrument]`/captured-`Span` (parent carried through `.await` and `tokio::spawn`, not via ambient thread-local); thin `leaf-startup` facade: `step(name) -> Span` (`Span::none()` when disabled), `.tag(k,v)` -> `record`, ends on drop — Spring-shaped API over plain `tracing`; per-callsite `tracing` max-level static filter to compile the enabled-check toward zero on the off path
