**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strong cross-crate story BECAUSE `tracing` is the de-facto cross-crate diagnostics bus: a downstream/integration crate (or a WASM-module-backed processor) emits its OWN startup spans on the SAME `leaf::startup` target with `.`-namespaced names in its own namespace (e.g. `leaf.tokio.executor-init`), and they nest under leaf-core's phase spans AUTOMATICALLY via `tracing`'s ambient/contextual parent — NO link-time registration is needed for instrumentation itself (spans are emitted at call sites, not registered into a slice), so this feature largely SIDESTEPS the DCE hazard (§152) that plagues discovery features: a DCE'd crate simply emits no spans (its work also didn't run), rather than silently breaking a registry. The ONLY cross-crate contract is the STABLE NAME TAXONOMY (the `.`-namespaced vocabulary) and the reserved target — leaf-core publishes the canonical root/phase names as consts so contributing crates key off a stable vocabulary (the tool-friendly-taxonomy intent becomes a published const surface, an ABI-ish commitment). Origin-agnostic by construction: a WASM-module processor's bridge emits spans through the same facade, so dynamically-sourced steps nest identically to native ones — no assumption of native construction. The recording SINK (Buffering/JFR-equivalent Layer) is owned by the application binary (tracing's role-separation, §221: the binary installs the subscriber, libraries only emit) — matching 'who owns the container'. Generics are a non-issue here (spans are emitted at monomorphized call sites, nothing to register), so the generics-cannot-register hazard does not bite this feature. Open: whether leaf re-exports `tracing` or wraps it determines how tightly contributing crates couple to the version.

**T2 — execution context:** Entirely a COLD-PATH concern (startup only) — but it threads through code that BECOMES async-concurrent, so execution context matters (§2.3). The root span is opened on the bootstrap task; each refresh phase's span is a child opened on that task. When bring-up fans out (eager-singleton waves / background-bootstrap futures on the bootstrapExecutor), each spawned/awaited construction future is `.instrument(child_span)` with the parent phase span CAPTURED EXPLICITLY before the hop — so parent linkage survives the thread move (the rust-native fix for Spring's thread-local current-step assumption). NO blocking, NO global lock (§2.5): emitting a span is lock-free (`tracing` uses thread-local scoped state + the subscriber's own sync); the no-op path touches no shared state at all. A blocking recording sink would be the user's Layer problem, not leaf's (and a heavy sink should buffer + offload, documented). CANCELLATION: a span ends on Drop, so if a phase future is cancelled mid-bring-up (the cancelRefresh/Failed path from refresh-lifecycle), its span closes with whatever duration elapsed and the subscriber sees an un-`end()`ed-but-closed span — leaf should TAG the root/phase span with the failure (an `error`/`outcome` field) on the abort path so the recorded trace shows WHERE boot died (rich diagnostics §2.9: the startup trace becomes a boot-failure forensic). No async Drop needed — span close is sync and cheap. ERRORS in instrumentation itself are impossible-by-design (a span never fails); a failing sink is isolated in the subscriber.

#### R2 — Bespoke `StartupRecorder` trait + explicit `StartupStep` handle threaded through the bring-up (the faithful Spring port, rust-native)
Port Spring's model directly but rust-natively, owning the abstraction rather than borrowing `tracing`'s. Define a `trait StartupRecorder: Send + Sync` with `fn record(&self, name: &'static str, parent: Option<StepId>) -> StepId` / `fn tag(&self, id: StepId, key: &'static str, value: TagValue)` / `fn end(&self, id: StepId)` — and a `StartupStep` RAII HANDLE (`{ id: StepId, recorder: &dyn StartupRecorder }`) whose `Drop` calls `end()` so steps close deterministically. The NO-OP default is a unit `NoopRecorder` whose methods are empty and `#[inline]` (so `start()` returns a `StartupStep` with a sentinel id and the calls vanish under optimization — the `ApplicationStartup.DEFAULT` analogue, ideally a ZST so there is no allocation, beating Spring's allocated default step). The recorder lives as ONE field on the container/bring-up context (`RefreshCx`), so refresh-lifecycle threads it through every phase: `let step = cx.startup.step("leaf.context.refresh"); ...; let phase = step.child("leaf.context.beans.post-process");`. The KEY rust-native divergence from Spring's id/parentId-with-ambient-current-pointer: parent linkage is EXPLICIT — `step.child(name)` captures the parent id in the returned handle, so when a phase fans out into concurrent futures, each future is handed (a clone of) its parent `StartupStep` handle and calls `.child()` on it — NO thread-local 'current step', so the tree stays correct across `.await`/spawn (this is the §2.3 fix). Opt-in recording = construct the container with a `BufferingRecorder` (bounded in-memory `Vec<RecordedStep{ id, parent, name, start, end, tags }>`, queryable as a tree after refresh) or a user `Recorder` impl that forwards to whatever sink (a `tracing`-bridge recorder, an OTel recorder, a metrics recorder) — the sink is a TRAIT IMPL, not a hard dependency, so leaf-core stays sink-agnostic. The stable `.`-namespaced names are published as consts in leaf-core. The whole thing is a tiny, self-contained crate with no external observability dependency.

**Pros:** Highest INTENT fidelity and zero substrate commitment: it's the literal Spring model (id/parentId tree, tag, end, DEFAULT-noop-vs-recording) but rust-native, and it pre-judges NOTHING about leaf's broader diagnostics/observability spine (§3) — leaf-core owns a tiny trait and a noop; whether to ALSO bridge to `tracing`/OTel is a separate, additive Recorder impl decided later. Cleanest 'candidate, not a decision' posture.; No external dependency in the core (§ memory/dependency discipline): a unit `NoopRecorder` ZST means the off path is provably free (empty inlined methods, no allocation — strictly leaner than Spring's allocated DEFAULT step), and a user who wants the boot breakdown gets it from a ~200-line BufferingRecorder, not by pulling in `tracing` + a subscriber stack.; Explicit `StartupStep` handle threading makes parent linkage a TYPE-LEVEL fact (you cannot open a child without naming its parent handle), which (a) fixes the async-fan-out parent-linkage problem structurally — no ambient state to lose across `.await` (§2.3) — and (b) makes the instrumentation calls hand-writable and visible (§2.10), no macro magic, the recorder field is just passed down.; Programmatically queryable tree out of the box: BufferingRecorder yields a real `RecordedStep` tree (the BufferingApplicationStartup.getBufferedTimeline analogue) that leaf's OWN tooling (a `leaf doctor`/startup-report) and the refresh-lifecycle anti-DCE self-check can read directly — richer than reconstructing from span events, and feeds §2.9 boot-failure forensics directly.; Full control of the timing/tag semantics: leaf can decide duration = wall-clock-to-ready and ALSO record a 'self time excluding awaited children' if useful, can make tags a freely-dynamic open map (matching Spring's `tag(k,v)` exactly, unconstrained by `tracing`'s pre-declared-field model), and can stamp the abort/Failed outcome precisely.

**Cons:** Reinvents a tree/buffer/tagging primitive `tracing` already provides, and CUTS leaf off from the existing sink ecosystem (OTel, chrome-trace, flamegraph) unless it ALSO writes bridge Recorders — so the unification dividend (§2.11) is forfeited unless a `tracing`-bridge Recorder is shipped anyway, at which point R1 was arguably the simpler path.; Threading the recorder/`StartupStep` handle through EVERY phase and into every fanned-out future is invasive plumbing: refresh-lifecycle, bean-instantiation, smart-initializing, post-processor-spi all gain a `cx.startup`/handle parameter — more API surface and ceremony than R1's ambient-but-explicitly-instrumented spans, and a missed hand-off silently drops a subtree (no compiler help that a child was orphaned).; `&dyn StartupRecorder` in the handle is a dyn-dispatch + the handle holds a borrow/`Arc` to the recorder — to clone a handle across a spawned future the recorder must be `Arc<dyn StartupRecorder>` (minimal-Arc tension §2.5: one Arc shared across all steps, acceptable but present), and the dyn calls on the recording path are non-inlinable (fine — recording is opt-in/cold).; Owns code Spring got from the JVM/JFR for free: the bounded buffer, the tree reconstruction/query API, the overflow policy, and (if wanted) every sink bridge are now leaf's to build, test, and maintain — more surface than R1 for the same user-visible feature.; The published stable name consts + the `StartupRecorder` trait shape become a leaf ABI commitment (tool-friendly taxonomy intent), same as R1, but WITHOUT the payoff of plugging into an existing tool ecosystem that already understands a standard wire format.

**Mechanisms considered:** `trait StartupRecorder { record(name, parent) -> StepId; tag(id,k,v); end(id) }` + RAII `StartupStep` handle whose Drop calls end(); unit ZST `NoopRecorder` with empty `#[inline]` methods as the DEFAULT (records-nothing, no allocation — beats Spring's allocated default step); recorder as ONE field on the bring-up `RefreshCx`, threaded through phases; `step.child(name)` captures parent id EXPLICITLY in the returned handle (no thread-local current-step); across async fan-out, hand each future a cloned parent `StartupStep` handle so `.child()` nests correctly past `.await`/spawn; opt-in recording = `BufferingRecorder` (bounded `Vec<RecordedStep>` queryable as a tree) or a user `Recorder` impl (tracing-bridge / OTel / metrics) — sink is a trait impl, not a core dependency; freely-dynamic tag map + stable `.`-namespaced name consts published by leaf-core

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Cross-crate emission works by SHARING the recorder handle, not by a registry — a downstream/integration crate's processor receives the `StartupStep`/recorder via the bring-up context (origin-agnostic: a WASM-module processor's bridge gets the same handle and emits children identically), so there is NO link-time registration of instrumentation and thus NO DCE hazard for the steps themselves (a DCE'd crate just never gets handed a handle / never runs). The cross-crate CONTRACTS are (a) the published stable `.`-namespaced name consts (the tool-friendly taxonomy as an ABI), and (b) the `StartupRecorder`/`StartupStep` types in leaf-core that contributing crates depend on to emit. The recording sink is owned by the application binary (tracing-style role separation §221): the binary chooses/installs the Recorder; libraries only emit through the handed-down handle — so there is exactly ONE recorder per container, no global singleton. This realization is the natural HOME for the refresh-lifecycle anti-DCE self-check's OUTPUT surface (§152): because BufferingRecorder produces a queryable tree of what actually ran with timings, the 'expected-vs-found infrastructure' check and the startup report read the SAME data structure — instrumentation and the self-check share one surface (unification within the lifecycle family). The two-source duality (static link-time registry + dynamic WASM-module source) is irrelevant to instrumentation's own wiring (it's not a discovered registry) but RELEVANT as a consumer: steps emitted by dynamically-sourced processors must carry a provenance tag (which source) so the trace attributes time correctly — a tag convention, not a registration problem. Generics: non-issue (steps are emitted at call sites; nothing registered).

**T2 — execution context:** COLD-PATH only (bring-up). The recorder is one shared `Arc<dyn StartupRecorder>` (the single minimal-Arc, §2.5) read by every phase on the bootstrap task; `record`/`tag`/`end` on the NoopRecorder are inlined no-ops (off path is free); on the recording path they push into the BufferingRecorder's buffer under a SINGLE fine-grained lock or a lock-free queue (justified, documented, cold-path — not a global lock, §2.5). Parent linkage is carried in the `StartupStep` handle VALUE, so across the eager-singleton/background-bootstrap fan-out each spawned future holds its own parent handle — correct nesting past `.await`/spawn with NO ambient thread-local (the §2.3 fix; this is the explicit-context-discipline answer to Spring's thread-local current-step). Timing is taken with a monotonic clock at child()/end(); leaf controls whether duration is wall-clock-to-ready (default) and may additionally record self-time. CANCELLATION: the `StartupStep` RAII handle's Drop calls `end()`, so a phase future cancelled on the cancelRefresh/Failed path closes its step with elapsed time and an `outcome=cancelled`/`error` tag (leaf stamps it on the abort path) — the recorded tree becomes a boot-FAILURE forensic showing exactly which step was in flight when bring-up died (§2.9). No async Drop required (end() is sync, cheap). ERRORS: instrumentation cannot fail (the recorder's methods are infallible); a misbehaving user Recorder is isolated and must not block the bootstrap task (documented: heavy sinks buffer+offload). Steady-state/hot paths NEVER touch this — it is structurally startup-scoped.

#### R3 — Phase-event stream from refresh-lifecycle's introspection surface: instrumentation is a pure OBSERVER subscribing to lifecycle phase-transition events, recorder reconstructs the tree
Invert the coupling: instead of refresh-lifecycle (and bean-instantiation, etc.) calling INTO an instrumentation API, make refresh-lifecycle EMIT a typed stream of phase-lifecycle events on its already-mandated per-phase introspection surface, and make startup-instrumentation a PURE downstream OBSERVER that subscribes and reconstructs the timed tree. refresh-lifecycle's design already commits to 'a per-phase introspection surface (what was found, what ran) so the check and startup-instrumentation are writable'. R3 takes that literally: define a `StartupEvent` enum — `PhaseEntered { id: StepId, parent: Option<StepId>, name: &'static str, at: Instant }`, `StepTagged { id, key, value }`, `PhaseExited { id, at, outcome }`, plus bean-grain variants (`BeanInstantiateStarted/Finished`, `SmartInitializeStarted/Finished`) — and have the bring-up emit these onto a lightweight in-process notification channel that is NO-OP when there is no observer (a `Option<&dyn StartupObserver>` / an empty broadcast with zero receivers short-circuits emission). startup-instrumentation ships the default observer that builds the `RecordedStep` tree from the event stream (the parent ids arrive IN the events, so the observer just indexes them — no thread-local, no ambient state). The no-op default is 'no observer registered' → the emit sites are `if let Some(obs) = &self.observer { obs.on(ev) }` or a check that compiles away. This makes instrumentation 100% an OBSERVER (it never reaches into bring-up internals), so it composes with the SAME event surface that the anti-DCE self-check, a future 'startup report' tool, an OTel exporter, and a test harness ('assert phase X ran before Y') all consume — one emission surface, many observers. Opt-in 'recording' is just 'attach the buffering observer'; a JFR/OTel-equivalent is 'attach an exporting observer'.

**Pros:** Cleanest separation of concerns and strongest unification (§2.11): bring-up emits ONE typed event stream; instrumentation, the anti-DCE self-check, test assertions about phase order, and any exporter are all just OBSERVERS of the same stream — leaf builds the emission surface once and every diagnostic/observability consumer reuses it (the refresh-lifecycle design explicitly wanted this surface to serve both the self-check AND instrumentation).; Parent linkage is DATA IN THE EVENT (`parent: Option<StepId>`), so the observer reconstructs the tree by indexing ids — no ambient current-step, no thread-local, immune to async fan-out reordering: even if concurrent eager-singleton waves interleave their events, the parent ids reassemble the correct tree (the most robust §2.3 answer — ordering of arrival is irrelevant because structure is explicit in the data).; No-op default is structural: with no observer attached the emit sites short-circuit (an `Option` check / a zero-receiver channel), so the un-instrumented boot path costs at most a predictable-branch per event — matching the DEFAULT-records-nothing intent, and like R2 it needs no external dependency in core.; Decouples the TAXONOMY from the recorder entirely: the stable `.`-namespaced names live in the event payloads (consts in leaf-core), and ANY observer interprets them — so the same stream feeds a human startup report, a CI 'boot budget' assertion, and an OTel exporter without instrumentation owning any sink (sink-agnostic like R2, observer-pluggable like R1).; Testability/DX (§ DX #1): because phases announce themselves as typed events, a test can attach an observer and assert 'leaf.context.beans.post-process entered before any leaf.beans.instantiate' — turning the startup taxonomy into a verifiable contract, and the event enum is a self-documenting catalog of the bring-up's observable milestones.

**Cons:** Risks DUPLICATING the events/event-multicaster feature's job and even refresh-lifecycle's own lifecycle-events: leaf already has an event bus and lifecycle-events (ContextRefreshed etc.) — a SECOND, separate startup-event channel needs strong justification (these events fire DURING bring-up before the multicaster/many beans exist, and must be ultra-cheap/no-op, which the general event bus may not guarantee — but the overlap is a real tension to resolve, not ignore).; Highest indirection for what is fundamentally 'time these phases': every step becomes an entered/tagged/exited event triple the observer must pair up and the recorder must reassemble — more moving parts and more allocation potential than R2's direct handle, and a malformed/missing PhaseExited (e.g. on a cancelled future) leaves a dangling open step the observer must time-out or mark, a reconstruction edge case R2's RAII handle handles by construction.; The emission surface's COST when an observer IS attached depends on the channel substrate (Phase 2's reactive-notification choice §2.4): a naive broadcast or per-event boxed-dyn call may allocate per event during the hottest part of boot (per-bean instantiate events for a 500-bean graph) — must be a lightweight synchronous `&dyn StartupObserver::on(&Event)` call, NOT a heavyweight async channel, or it taxes the recording path noticeably (and choosing that substrate edges toward a Phase 2 decision).; Pre-decides part of the introspection-surface SHAPE that refresh-lifecycle owns: making it an EVENT STREAM (vs R2's pull/handle model) is a coupling-direction stance that belongs to the lifecycle/diagnostics meta-design — must stay a candidate, since the self-check might prefer a queryable end-state snapshot over a live event stream.; Bean-grain events (per-bean instantiate start/finish for the whole graph) make the stream VOLUMINOUS exactly when recording is on — the observer's buffer + the emission cost scale with bean count, so the 'minimal overhead even when recording' bar is harder to hit than R2's targeted spans, and bounding/sampling policy becomes the observer's burden.

**Mechanisms considered:** refresh-lifecycle emits a typed `StartupEvent` stream (`PhaseEntered{id,parent,name,at}` / `StepTagged` / `PhaseExited{id,at,outcome}` + bean-grain variants) on its per-phase introspection surface; instrumentation = a pure `StartupObserver` that reconstructs the `RecordedStep` tree by indexing parent ids from events (no thread-local, immune to async reordering); no-op default = no observer attached → emit sites short-circuit (`Option<&dyn StartupObserver>` check / zero-receiver channel compiles away); ONE emission surface, many observers: instrumentation, anti-DCE self-check, phase-order test assertions, OTel exporter all subscribe to the same stream (unification); stable `.`-namespaced names carried IN the event payloads (consts in leaf-core); taxonomy decoupled from any recorder/sink; lightweight synchronous `&dyn StartupObserver::on(&Event)` dispatch (NOT an async channel) to keep the recording path cheap on the cold path

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Cross-crate behaviour mirrors R2 (no link-time registration of instrumentation, no DCE hazard for the steps themselves) but routes everything through the OBSERVER surface: a downstream/integration/WASM-module processor emits `StartupEvent`s through the same bring-up-provided emit handle (origin-agnostic — the event carries the step name + parent id regardless of who produced it; a provenance tag identifies dynamic sources), and the application binary attaches the observer(s) (tracing-style role separation §221 — exactly one observer-set per container, owned by the binary). The cross-crate CONTRACTS are the `StartupEvent` enum + the stable `.`-namespaced name consts in leaf-core (an ABI commitment, same as R1/R2). This realization makes instrumentation share its emission surface with refresh-lifecycle's MANDATED anti-DCE self-check and with phase-order test harnesses — the strongest unification of the three, since 'what ran, in what order, with what timing, from which source' is ONE stream consumed by many tools (the self-check asserts expected-vs-found over it; instrumentation times it; a test asserts ordering over it). Generics: non-issue (events emitted at call sites). The two-source duality affects instrumentation only as a provenance TAG on events (which source emitted the step), not as a registration concern. Risk to flag: if Phase 2 unifies this with the general events bus / lifecycle-events, the cross-crate event-delivery semantics (buffering before listeners exist — exactly refresh-lifecycle's early-event-buffering concern) must be reconciled so startup events emitted before any observer is attached are either buffered or defined as lost.

**T2 — execution context:** COLD-PATH only. Emission is a lightweight SYNCHRONOUS call on the bootstrap task: `if let Some(obs) = observer { obs.on(&StartupEvent::…) }` — no async, no channel await, NO global lock (§2.5; the observer's own buffer uses one fine-grained lock or a single-threaded cold-path Vec since bring-up is largely sequential and fan-out events carry explicit parent ids so the observer can index without ordering guarantees). The reactive-vs-poll invariant (§2.4) is honored trivially: this is push/notification (events), never polling, and it's cold-path so even a synchronous observer call is fine. Across the eager-singleton/background-bootstrap fan-out, each future emits PhaseEntered/Exited with parent ids it was handed — so concurrent emission INTERLEAVES on the stream but the observer reassembles by id (arrival order irrelevant; the most cancellation/reorder-robust of the three). The observer must be cheap and MUST NOT block the bootstrap task (a heavy/exporting observer buffers + offloads to its own task — documented execution-context discipline §2.3). CANCELLATION: on the cancelRefresh/Failed path, a cancelled phase future SHOULD emit `PhaseExited{outcome: Cancelled/Error}` — but unlike R2's RAII handle this is not automatic, so refresh-lifecycle must guarantee a terminal event per open step (via its own scope-guard on the abort path) or the observer marks un-exited steps as 'incomplete' with last-known time; either way the recorded tree shows where boot died (§2.9). ERRORS: emission is infallible; a panicking observer must be caught/isolated so a buggy diagnostic can't abort an otherwise-healthy boot (a documented isolation boundary). No async Drop involved.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's `id`/`parentId` step tree assumes a SINGLE SYNCHRONOUS CALL STACK with an ambient 'current step' pointer — which breaks the moment bring-up fans out into concurrent eager-singleton waves / background-bootstrap futures. `ApplicationStartup` implementations track the parent by reading the in-flight step off the calling thread (the recorder knows 'the current step' because refresh runs synchronously on one thread). In async leaf, refresh-lifecycle's eager phase fans out construction onto a bootstrapExecutor and `.await`s, so there is NO single current step and no reliable thread-local to read it from (the value could resume on a different worker). Every realization therefore makes PARENT LINKAGE EXPLICIT — carried in a `Span`/`StartupStep` handle (R1/R2) or as `parent: StepId` data in the event (R3) — NEVER read off ambient thread-local state at emit time. This is the central rust-native divergence for THIS feature.
- `StartupStep` as an ALLOCATED, MUTABLE OBJECT returned by `start()` is a JVM allocation pattern; the no-op DEFAULT should be a ZERO-COST nothing in Rust, not an allocated empty object. Spring's `ApplicationStartup.DEFAULT.start(name)` still returns a (trivial) `StartupStep` instance you call `.tag()`/`.end()` on; the JVM's escape analysis/GC makes that cheap. In Rust, leaf can do BETTER: the no-op recorder is a ZST with `#[inline]` empty methods (R2), or `tracing::Span::none()` (R1), or an `Option`-check that compiles away (R3) — so the off path allocates NOTHING and ideally compiles to near-zero. Blindly porting 'start() returns a heap StartupStep' would tax the steady un-instrumented boot path against the feature's own central intent (no-op-by-default, minimal overhead, §2.2-#2).
- No JFR / no built-in flight recorder — the recording SINK must be a rust-native pluggable observer, not a hard dependency. Spring ships `FlightRecorderApplicationStartup` because JFR is in the JVM. Rust has no JFR; the equivalent is `tracing` subscribers, OTel exporters, chrome-trace, flamegraph, or a bespoke buffer. So leaf must NOT bake any one sink into the abstraction: the recorder/observer is a TRAIT the application binary implements/installs (tracing-role-separation §221), and leaf at most ships a tiny in-memory buffering default + optionally a `tracing` bridge. Picking the sink substrate (is `tracing` core or pluggable?) is a Phase 2 diagnostics-spine decision this feature must not pre-empt — hence three realizations spanning 'lean on tracing' (R1) to 'own a tiny trait, bridge later' (R2/R3).
- Span/step DURATION over an async phase measures WALL-CLOCK-INCLUDING-PARKED-TIME, not synchronous CPU time as Spring's StartupStep does. Spring's refresh runs synchronously, so a StartupStep's duration is real work time on one thread. In async leaf a phase may `.await` (yield the worker) and concurrent phases/waves may OVERLAP, so naive enter→close timing includes time the task was parked and can DOUBLE-COUNT overlapping wall time across concurrent steps. For 'why is my boot slow (time-to-ready)' wall-clock is arguably the RIGHT metric, but it differs from Spring's semantics and can mislead — leaf must DEFINE and document the timing semantics (wall-clock-to-ready, possibly plus self-time excluding awaited children) rather than assume Spring's synchronous-duration meaning carries over.
- No async Drop means a step opened with RAII-close semantics closes SYNCHRONOUSLY even when its scope is an async future. Spring ends steps explicitly in `finally`. leaf wants RAII (`StartupStep` ends on Drop — R1's span, R2's handle) so a cancelled/early-returning phase still closes its step — but with no async Drop, that close MUST be a cheap synchronous operation (record an end timestamp), never anything that needs to await. This is fine for instrumentation (ending a step is just stamping a monotonic clock + maybe an outcome tag) — but it forbids any design where 'ending a step' flushes asynchronously to a sink inline; flushing must be the sink/observer's deferred concern, off the bring-up task.
- The stable `.`-namespaced NAME TAXONOMY becomes a cross-crate ABI commitment with NO classpath to anchor it. Spring's reusable step names are just strings agreed by convention within one JVM. In leaf, downstream/integration crates emit steps that must nest under and key off the SAME vocabulary, but there is no global classpath/registry to define it — so leaf-core must PUBLISH the canonical root/phase names as `const`s (and a namespacing convention for third-party steps) that contributing crates depend on. This turns 'a tool-friendly taxonomy' into a versioned public surface (an ABI-ish commitment), and is the only real cross-crate contract this feature introduces — but unlike discovery features it carries NO DCE hazard (steps are emitted at call sites, not registered into a link-time slice).
- Spring auto-wires `ApplicationStartup` onto the context (`context.setApplicationStartup(...)`) and injects it into infrastructure via `ApplicationStartupAware` — leaf has no such ambient injection of the recorder. In Spring the recorder is set on the context and infrastructure beans receive it via the Aware mechanism (ApplicationContextAwareProcessor fires `ApplicationStartupAware`, §2494). leaf's aware-callbacks equivalent could do similarly, but the recorder is needed DURING bring-up by code that runs BEFORE most beans exist (the phases themselves), so it cannot rely on bean-injection — it must be a field on the bring-up context (`RefreshCx`) threaded explicitly (R2) or an ambient-but-explicitly-instrumented surface (R1/R3), not delivered via the bean Aware path. The Aware delivery is at most a convenience for application beans that want to emit their OWN init steps.

### Interactions
- **refresh-lifecycle** — PRIMARY UPSTREAM / the thing being instrumented. refresh-lifecycle OWNS the fixed-order phase sequence and (per its own design) a per-phase introspection surface explicitly intended so 'the [anti-DCE] check and startup-instrumentation are writable'. startup-instrumentation defines the no-op-by-default recorder/observer abstraction, the step-tree/tag/timing contract, and the stable `.`-namespaced taxonomy; refresh-lifecycle's phase structure IS the taxonomy (root `leaf.context.refresh`, child per phase) and is where steps are opened/closed. The cancelRefresh/Failed abort path is where the recorder must capture WHERE boot died (an outcome tag on the in-flight step). Coupling direction (push-into-recorder R1/R2 vs emit-events-observed-by R3) is the key divergence.
- **bean-instantiation** — INSTRUMENTED. bean-instantiation's eager pre-instantiation drives per-bean construction — the `spring.beans.instantiate`/`leaf.beans.instantiate` step per bean (tagged with bean name/type) is the finest-grained and most VOLUMINOUS instrumentation, and the per-bean timing is the main payload for diagnosing a slow boot (which bean took 2s). bean-instantiation opens/emits the instantiate step around each bean's construct→populate→init; instrumentation records it. The volume (one step per bean for a large graph) is the principal overhead concern even on the recording path.
- **smart-initializing** — INSTRUMENTED. Each `afterSingletonsInstantiated` callback is wrapped in a `spring.beans.smart-initialize`/`leaf.beans.smart-initialize` step (Spring does exactly this, §2477). smart-initializing fires its callbacks AFTER all eager singletons exist and OUTSIDE any creation lock; instrumentation times each callback as a child of the eager-instantiate phase, so expensive cross-bean post-init aggregation shows up distinctly in the boot breakdown.
- **post-processor-spi** — INSTRUMENTED. The `spring.context.beans.post-process`/`leaf.context.beans.post-process` step wraps the run-definition-processors + register-instance-processors phases, and individual BFPP invocations get their own steps (Spring emits BFPP-invocation steps, §405). post-processor-spi defines the processor contracts; instrumentation times each processor's run as a child step (tagged with processor identity) so a slow ConfigurationClass-equivalent BFPP is visible. Origin-agnostic: a WASM-module-provided processor's step nests identically.
- **background-bootstrap** — INSTRUMENTED + the async-fan-out stress case. background-bootstrap runs slow independent singletons concurrently on the bootstrapExecutor; their instantiate steps run on DIFFERENT tasks/threads, which is exactly where Spring's thread-local current-step model breaks (rustClashes). Each background future must carry its parent step handle/id explicitly so its instantiate step nests under the eager-instantiate phase despite the thread hop — the concrete reason every realization makes parent linkage explicit. Background steps' wall-clock durations also OVERLAP (the timing-semantics clash).
- **events** — POTENTIAL UNIFICATION TENSION (esp. R3). leaf already has an event bus, a multicaster (event-multicaster), and lifecycle-events (ContextRefreshed-equivalent). R3 proposes a SEPARATE startup-event stream; this overlaps and must be reconciled — startup events fire DURING bring-up, often before the multicaster/observers exist (exactly refresh-lifecycle's early-event-buffering concern), and must be ultra-cheap/no-op, which the general bus may not guarantee. Whether startup instrumentation reuses the event substrate or is a distinct lightweight channel is a phase2Question. (lifecycle-events publishing ContextRefreshed at finish-refresh is a SEPARATE, coarser milestone than the per-phase startup steps.)
- **aware-callbacks** — DELIVERY MECHANISM for application beans (minor). Spring injects the recorder via `ApplicationStartupAware` (fired by the ApplicationContextAwareProcessor, §2494) so infrastructure/application beans can emit their OWN startup steps. leaf's aware-callbacks equivalent could deliver the recorder/observer handle to beans that want to instrument their init — but the bring-up phases themselves CANNOT rely on this (they run before beans exist), so Aware delivery is at most a convenience layer atop the context-threaded recorder, not the primary path (rustClashes).
- **container-core** — HOSTS the recorder. container-core's engine-vs-facade split determines where the recorder/observer lives: the facade installs/owns the configured recorder (default = noop) as a container-level capability, the bare engine defaults to noop — mirroring Spring's `context.setApplicationStartup()` being a context-level concern. The recorder is a field on the bring-up context the facade configures; a bare-engine user gets the zero-cost noop.
- **task-execution** — EXECUTION-CONTEXT SOURCE (T2). The bootstrapExecutor that background-bootstrap and the eager-singleton fan-out run on is task-execution's concern; instrumentation must propagate parent step context ACROSS the thread/task hops that executor introduces (R1 via `.instrument()`, R2 via handed-down handle, R3 via parent-id-in-event). A heavy recording sink must itself offload to a task rather than block the bootstrap task — explicit execution-context discipline the sink (not leaf-core) owns.
- **condition-report** — SIBLING DIAGNOSTIC, shared spirit. condition-report records every match/no-match with a human-readable reason so convention-over-configuration stays accountable; startup-instrumentation records WHERE TIME WENT during the SAME bring-up. Both are opt-in accountability surfaces over the refresh sequence; they may share the introspection-surface plumbing (R3's event stream could carry condition verdicts too) and a unified 'leaf doctor'/startup-report tool would consume both — a unification opportunity to flag, not resolve.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- What is leaf's unified DIAGNOSTICS/OBSERVABILITY SPINE, and is `tracing` part of the opinionated core or a pluggable bridge? This decides whether startup-instrumentation IS `tracing` spans (R1, free sink ecosystem + free no-op + free async propagation, but a hard `tracing` core dependency) or a bespoke recorder/observer that OPTIONALLY bridges to `tracing`/OTel (R2/R3, zero substrate commitment but reinvents tree/buffer/sink). This is a §3-keystone-adjacent call (the unified error/diagnostics model, §2.9/§5) and must not be pre-judged by this feature — held as a candidate across all three realizations. Cross-cuts condition-report, the error model, and every feature that wants to emit diagnostics.
- Is the recorder a PULL/HANDLE model that bring-up calls into (R1/R2) or a PUSH event stream that instrumentation OBSERVES (R3)? This is the coupling-direction between refresh-lifecycle and its diagnostics consumers, and it determines the shape of refresh-lifecycle's mandated per-phase introspection surface (a queryable end-state snapshot, a handed-down handle, or a live typed event stream). It also decides whether the anti-DCE self-check, phase-order test assertions, and instrumentation share ONE surface (R3's strongest unification claim) or several. Belongs to the lifecycle/diagnostics meta-design, not this feature.
- Does the startup-event/step surface REUSE leaf's general events/event-multicaster/lifecycle-events substrate or is it a DISTINCT lightweight channel? Unifying (§2.11) is attractive but startup steps fire DURING bring-up before the multicaster/observers/most beans exist (the early-event-buffering problem refresh-lifecycle already faces) and must be near-zero-cost when un-recorded — constraints the general bus may not meet. If unified, the early-emission semantics (buffer vs lose steps emitted before any observer attaches) must be defined. Cross-cuts events, event-multicaster, lifecycle-events, refresh-lifecycle.
- What are the precise TIMING SEMANTICS for a step over an async/concurrent phase — wall-clock-to-ready (includes parked time, allows overlapping/double-counted concurrent steps) vs self-time (excludes awaited children) vs both? Spring's synchronous StartupStep duration does not carry over once phases `.await` and eager-singleton/background-bootstrap waves overlap. This needs a defined, documented contract (and possibly both numbers) so a slow-boot diagnosis is not misled by overlapping concurrent waves. Cross-cuts the async-execution model and background-bootstrap.
- How is the stable `.`-namespaced NAME TAXONOMY governed as a cross-crate ABI, and where do the canonical name consts live? Downstream/integration/WASM-module crates must nest steps under and key off the same vocabulary with no classpath to anchor it; leaf-core must publish the canonical root/phase names + a third-party namespacing convention as a versioned surface. What is the versioning/compat policy, and does a dynamically-sourced (WASM-module) step carry a provenance tag so time is attributed to the right source? Cross-cuts the cross-crate composition model and the discovery/SPI family.
- What is the bounding/overflow/sampling policy for the recording path given per-bean instantiate steps make the stream VOLUMINOUS (one step per bean for a 500-bean graph) exactly when recording is on? BufferingApplicationStartup is bounded in Spring; leaf must decide the bounded-buffer/sampling/level-filter policy and whose concern it is (the recorder/observer's, per the binary-owns-the-sink role separation), so 'minimal overhead even when recording' stays achievable. Cross-cuts bean-instantiation (the volume source) and the diagnostics spine.
- On the cancelRefresh/Failed abort path, what GUARANTEES that every open step gets a terminal close+outcome so the recorded tree is a usable boot-FAILURE forensic (§2.9)? R2's RAII handle gives this by construction; R1's span-close-on-drop too; R3's event model needs refresh-lifecycle to emit a terminal PhaseExited per open step on abort. The contract (who stamps the failure outcome, how dangling open steps are handled, no async Drop available) is shared with refresh-lifecycle's cancel-vs-close teardown and the unified error model — must be decided where those are.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. The dedicated Spring section is short and unambiguous (§403-405): the four load-bearing intents are (1) the no-op-DEFAULT-vs-opt-in-recording overhead posture, (2) the id/parentId step TREE with tag(k,v) + end()-duration, (3) the stable `.`-namespaced tool-friendly NAME TAXONOMY, (4) the purpose = diagnosing slow boots. I cross-read the refresh-lifecycle section (§325-405) for WHERE steps are emitted (the exact v7 sequence + `spring.context.refresh` root, `spring.context.beans.post-process`, and §2477 confirming `spring.beans.smart-initialize` wraps each afterSingletonsInstantiated and §405 confirming `spring.beans.instantiate` + BFPP-invocation steps), §2494 for ApplicationStartupAware delivery, and §2466-2477 for the fan-out (background-bootstrap on bootstrapExecutor) that breaks the synchronous-current-step assumption. I grounded T2 against the charter (§2.2 DX#1/overhead#2, §2.3 async-first/explicit-context, §2.4 reactive-not-poll [trivially satisfied: cold-path, push-not-poll], §2.5 no-global-lock/minimal-Arc, §2.9 rich diagnostics, §2.10 thin-macros, §2.11 unification) and the nightly envelope (no async Drop → step-close must be synchronous; RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe is not pressing here since the recorder trait's methods are sync-infallible; tracing's Span::none() and ZST-noop are stable). T1 against the cross-crate doc: crucially, this feature is UNUSUAL in the catalog because it carries NO DCE hazard — steps are emitted at monomorphized call sites, not registered into a link-time slice, so §149-152's silent-DCE problem does not bite the instrumentation itself (only the published name-taxonomy consts are a cross-crate contract); §221's tracing-role-separation (binary owns the sink, libraries only emit) maps perfectly to 'who installs the recorder'; §153's generics-cannot-register is a non-issue here.\n\nThe three realizations are genuinely DIVERGENT on the substrate/coupling axis, not cosmetic: R1 LEANS ON `tracing` (gets no-op-default, async parent-propagation, tree, tags, and the entire sink ecosystem for free — but commits a core dependency and pre-judges the diagnostics spine); R2 OWNS a tiny bespoke `StartupRecorder` trait + RAII `StartupStep` handle threaded explicitly through bring-up (literal Spring port, zero substrate commitment, ZST-noop beats Spring's allocated default — but reinvents the buffer/tree/sinks and is invasive plumbing); R3 INVERTS the coupling to a typed phase-event STREAM that instrumentation purely OBSERVES (strongest unification — one emission surface shared by instrumentation + anti-DCE self-check + phase-order tests + exporters; parent-id-in-data is the most async-reorder-robust — but risks duplicating the events bus and is the highest indirection/volume). R1 vs R2 differ on borrow-tracing vs own-a-trait; R3 differs on push-events vs pull-handle. They compose: R3's event stream could be IMPLEMENTED by emitting `tracing` events (R1∩R3), and R2's recorder could be ONE observer of R3's stream — so Phase 3 can merge, e.g. 'bespoke event stream in core (R3) with a shipped `tracing`-bridge observer (R1) and an in-memory buffering observer (R2)'.\n\nThe single most load-bearing intent to preserve is the NO-OP-BY-DEFAULT, OPT-IN-RECORDING posture — it is literally why the feature exists (instrument the boot WITHOUT permanently taxing it), and Rust can do BETTER than Spring's allocated default step (ZST/Span::none/Option-check that compiles toward zero). The sharpest rust-native divergence is that Spring's id/parentId tree rides an ambient synchronous current-step pointer that DOES NOT survive leaf's async eager-singleton/background-bootstrap fan-out — so ALL three realizations make parent linkage EXPLICIT (handle or event data), never thread-local. I refused to pick the diagnostics substrate (is `tracing` core?) — that is the §5 unified-diagnostics keystone and the dominant phase2Question; every realization stays a candidate and keeps the recorder/observer SINK pluggable and the step emission ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a WASM-module processor's steps nest identically via the same handle/event, carrying a provenance tag). I deliberately surfaced the R3-vs-events-bus unification tension and the timing-semantics-over-async-phases question as UNRESOLVED phase2Questions rather than deciding them. Naming ('StartupRecorder', 'StartupStep', 'StartupEvent', 'StartupObserver', 'RecordedStep', 'NoopRecorder', 'leaf.context.refresh', 'leaf::startup' target, 'BufferingRecorder') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (403-405 the dedicated ApplicationStartup/StartupStep section — the precise intent; 325-372 refresh fixed-order sequence + where steps are emitted; 2466-2477 preInstantiateSingletons fan-out + smart-initialize step + background-bootstrap; 2494 ApplicationStartupAware delivery); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. 2.2/2.3/2.4/2.5/2.9/2.10/2.11, deferred keystones §3, candidate meta-concepts §5, identity/topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (135-155 DCE layers — noted as NOT biting this feature, 165-171 linkme-default/thin-descriptor, 218-235 ecosystem lessons incl. tracing role-separation §221 and build-time-codegen, 230-235 leaf implications); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (160-182 RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe, 273-281 no async Drop as a first-class constraint shaping synchronous step-close).

## Application Event System  `[events]`

Spring's event system is an in-process observer pub/sub built into ApplicationContext (deliberately NOT the bare BeanFactory/engine): DI answers "who do I depend on?", events answer "who reacts when something happens?" WITHOUT the publisher knowing the reactors. The payoff is open/closed wiring — a producer emits a FACT and is oblivious to consumers; new reactions are added by adding beans, never by editing the emitter — which keeps high-fan-out cross-cutting reactions (audit, cache eviction, notifications) from turning the emitter into a dependency hub. THIS feature ('events') owns the publish/subscribe MODEL and listener wiring; the dispatch engine itself (threading, error isolation, the single replaceable seam) is the neighbour event-multicaster, lifecycle facts are lifecycle-events, and transaction-phase deferral is transactional-events. The concrete intents leaf must replicate (intent, not mechanism): (1) PUBLISH path — a publisher handle (ApplicationEventPublisher analogue) emits an event with no listener knowledge; injectable like an infrastructure dependency. (2) TWO listener authoring models — the classic typed-interface listener (ApplicationListener<E> / single onApplicationEvent), and the PREFERRED POJO model (@EventListener on any managed-bean method, the param type selecting the event), where one bean hosts MANY listeners, can target a specific generic instantiation, filter via a condition, order via @Order, and chain via return value. The intent for preferring annotated methods: the interface forces one-event-per-class and ties the class to the framework; annotated methods are POJO-friendly and consistent with @Transactional/@Scheduled. (3) PAYLOAD events — publishing ANY object (no mandatory framework base type) so domain events stay plain records; a non-event payload is auto-wrapped (PayloadApplicationEvent analogue) and listeners declare just the payload type. (4) GENERIC event targeting — @EventListener on EntityCreatedEvent<Person> matches only that instantiation; under erasure this needs the concrete type to advertise its full type (ResolvableTypeProvider analogue). (5) Per-event CONDITION filtering at dispatch (delegates to expression-language). (6) @Order ordering, meaningful only synchronously within one event type. (7) RETURN-VALUE-AS-NEW-EVENT chaining (a non-void return is re-published; a collection/array publishes each element; null/void publishes nothing) — explicitly DISABLED on async listeners (the result would arrive after the publisher returned). (8) SYNC-BY-DEFAULT dispatch on the caller's thread/context (preserving the caller's transaction/security/exception semantics), with async an opt-in that changes those semantics — and the dispatch funneled through ONE replaceable seam (the event-multicaster neighbour). (9) Context-HIERARCHY double-delivery (an event in a child is also delivered to ancestor listeners). (10) EARLY-event buffering (events fired before the dispatcher exists are queued, not lost). (11) Gotcha: a LAZY bean's @EventListener methods are silently never registered. The deep Rust tension: Spring's whole model leans on runtime reflection (scan beans for listener methods, type-erased Object events, per-event SpEL, reflective return-value re-publish) and on a flat in-process bus keyed by runtime Class — none of which Rust has; leaf must synthesize listener discovery from compile/link-time metadata, key dispatch by TypeId/typed channels, and make 'sync on the caller's context' an explicit async-execution discipline rather than an emergent thread property.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Typed event channels: monomorphized typed listener sets keyed by event type, publish is a direct typed dispatch
Lean into Rust's type system: the bus is NOT one erased Object channel but a collection of TYPED channels, one per event type E, each holding an ordered Vec of typed listener handles `Fn(&E) -> ListenerOutcome`. Publishing is `ctx.publish(e: E)` — generic over E, monomorphized, resolving E's channel by `TypeId::of::<E>()` from a `HashMap<TypeId, ErasedChannel>` where each ErasedChannel internally stores `Vec<TypedListener<E>>` recovered by one downcast at the channel boundary (the only erasure, amortized once per publish, not per listener). The classic listener is a trait `EventListener<E> { async fn on(&self, e: &E) -> Result<ListenerOutcome, ListenerError>; }`. The preferred POJO model is the thin `#[event_listener]` attribute on a bean method: the macro reads the single param type E and EMITS (a) the hand-writable adapter that downcasts the bean Arc and calls the method, and (b) ONE const Descriptor (bean TypeId, event TypeId, order:i32, an optional condition closure fn-pointer, an async factory fn-pointer that yields the boxed listener future) — the heavy 'register this into channel E' logic lives in leaf-core, not the macro. Payload events: a plain user type IS the event type E directly — no wrapper needed; `publish(my_dto)` dispatches on `TypeId::of::<MyDto>()`. The PayloadApplicationEvent 'source + payload' envelope becomes an optional generic `Event<P>{source, payload}` a publisher MAY use, but the rust-native default is that the domain type itself is the channel key. Generics: `EntityCreated<Person>` and `EntityCreated<Order>` are DISTINCT TypeIds (monomorphization gives leaf for free exactly the type-safe targeting Spring fakes with ResolvableTypeProvider) — the erasure problem simply doesn't exist. Ordering: each channel's Vec is sorted by the descriptor's order field at registration (cold path). Condition: an optional `Fn(&E)->bool` closure on the listener handle, checked before invocation (delegates shape to expression-language's EvalCtx). Return-value chaining: a listener returning `ListenerOutcome::Emit(vec_of_events)` is re-published by the dispatcher loop; on async listeners chaining is a compile-or-startup error (the macro hard-errors if an async listener declares a non-unit return used for chaining). Hierarchy double-delivery: a child context's publish walks `self` then `parent` channels (parent link from container-hierarchy).

**Pros:** Maximal rust-native fit + best low-overhead (invariant 2): publish is a TypeId hashmap lookup + one channel downcast + a tight typed loop — no per-listener erasure, no per-event reflective scan; the hottest path (event dispatch, invariant 4) is a direct monomorphized call, not a tree-walk.; Generic event targeting (EntityCreated<Person> vs <Order>) is SOLVED FOR FREE by monomorphization — no ResolvableTypeProvider analogue, no runtime type-arg reconstruction, no erasure trap; the type system gives the exact type-safety Spring works hard to approximate.; Payload events are trivially native: the domain type IS the event; no mandatory framework base type leaks into the domain model (this realizes Spring's payload intent more cleanly than Spring, which still wraps in PayloadApplicationEvent internally).; Strong, early diagnostics (invariant 9): a listener for an event type that is never published, or a return-type/event-type mismatch, is a compile-time or startup-pass error pointing at the attribute span — far better than Spring's runtime 'listener never fires' gotchas.; Thin macro (invariant 10): emits an adapter + one const Descriptor only; cargo expand shows a plain typed closure registered against a typed channel — fully hand-writable, escape hatch is to register a closure listener directly.

**Cons:** Heterogeneous fan-out where a listener wants 'any event' or 'a common supertrait of several events' is awkward: there is no single Object channel, so a cross-type listener must subscribe to each concrete TypeId, or events must implement a marker trait and the bus keep a parallel `dyn` channel — reintroducing erasure for that case (Spring's `@EventListener` with a common supertype param is a first-class case this realization makes second-class).; Late/dynamic publishing of a type the bus has no channel for is a no-op-or-error decision: a publisher emitting an event type with zero registered listeners must be cheap and silent (Spring tolerates it), but a TYPO in the event type can't be distinguished from a legitimately-unlistened event without a registry self-check.; Return-value chaining must re-enter the (generic) publish path with a possibly-different event type, which the dispatcher only knows as an erased ListenerOutcome carrying `Box<dyn Any>` events — so the chaining path DOES pay erasure (one downcast per chained event) even though the primary path doesn't; the type-purity is partial.; The channel store is a `HashMap<TypeId, ErasedChannel>` that must be frozen-after-registration to stay lock-free on the hot path (invariant 5); supporting runtime listener add/remove (a Spring-ish ability) needs interior mutability that fights the lock-free goal.; A condition that needs cross-event or ambient context still wants the expression-language EvalCtx; wiring that typed closure to carry `&E` plus an env handle adds a small per-listener capture cost and couples the two features' context shapes.

**Mechanisms considered:** proc-macro emits one const Descriptor (bean TypeId, event TypeId, order, optional condition fn-ptr, async factory fn-ptr) + a hand-writable adapter; heavy registration logic in leaf-core; HashMap<TypeId, ErasedChannel> where ErasedChannel downcasts to Vec<TypedListener<E>> (one erasure per publish, not per listener); TypeId::of::<E>() as the channel key — monomorphization gives generic-instantiation distinctness for free (const TypeId stable 1.91); Listeners as async trait objects boxed at the dyn boundary (AFIT not dyn-safe → Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>> per the nightly doc) — boxing acceptable on dispatch cold-ish paths, concrete/static for hot; linkme distributed-slice OR build-time codegen OR explicit register() for collecting listener descriptors across crates (candidate, deferred); optional Event<P>{source,payload} envelope type for the rare case a listener needs the source handle

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Listener descriptors are exactly the cross-crate discovery problem the composition doc analyzes: a `#[event_listener]` in a downstream/sibling crate must reach the context. Candidates (NOT decided here): (A/B) a `#[distributed_slice(leaf_core::EVENT_LISTENERS)] static __LEAF_x: ListenerDescriptor` per listener, collected at link time — zero runtime cost but subject to Layer-0 (an unreferenced listener-only crate contributes nothing), --gc-sections emptying, and silent-empty failure; (C) build-time codegen enumerating opted-in crates; (D) explicit `ctx.register_listener(...)`/`scan!{crate_a}` as the universal fallback. The dual-classpath duality applies: the static link-time listener set is the default 'classpath', and an optional dynamic/WASM-module source contributing listener descriptors (whose factory fn-pointers call into the module) is a SECOND source feeding the SAME typed-channel registration API — origin-agnostic because the channel only ever sees an erased factory producing a typed listener. Critical T1 hazard specific to events: the silent-DCE 'listener never fires' failure is INDISTINGUISHABLE from Spring's lazy-bean gotcha and from a legitimately-unlistened event — so a startup self-check (expected-vs-found listener descriptors per contributing crate, failing/warning loudly) is non-negotiable, mirroring the doc's expected-count assertion. The macro emits absolute `::leaf_core::` paths; the Descriptor/EventListener trait/ListenerOutcome types live in leaf-core for ABI stability; macros hold zero logic and hard-error on generic event types that can't be const-keyed.

**T2 — execution context:** Publish is async-first: `async fn publish<E>(&self, e: E)`. SYNC-BY-DEFAULT intent is realized as 'dispatch runs inline on the caller's task by default' — i.e. publish awaits each listener sequentially on the SAME task/execution-context the publisher is on, preserving the caller's ambient context (the rust-native analogue of Spring's 'caller's thread/transaction'), with ordering honored. This is NOT blocking-the-executor: listeners are awaited, not spawned, so the publisher's `.await` point drives them; a listener that needs to block must use the runtime's blocking facility (documented, execution-context discipline §2.3). Opt-in ASYNC dispatch (spawn each listener onto an executor) is delegated to the event-multicaster seam — that neighbour owns the executor/threading choice; THIS feature only guarantees the default is inline-on-caller and that async changes the contract (chaining off, errors routed to a handler not the caller). Error propagation (§2.9): default sync dispatch aborts the chain on the first listener error and returns it to the publisher (Spring's no-ErrorHandler behavior), UNLESS the multicaster has an error handler that isolates listeners — a per-dispatch policy, surfaced as a phase2 question. Cancellation: because dispatch is inline awaits, cancelling the publisher's future cancels in-flight listeners at their await points; a structured-concurrency scope around the dispatch loop bounds this. Hot-path discipline (invariant 4): no polling — dispatch is direct typed calls/awaits driven by the publish future. Listener futures are boxed at the dyn boundary (Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>); Send is required so async listeners survive a thread hop if the multicaster later spawns them.

#### R2 — Erased in-process bus faithful to Spring: one dynamic event channel keyed by runtime type, listeners as dyn adapters
Replicate Spring's shape directly: ONE multicaster holds all listeners as erased `Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>` and dispatch is `publish(event: impl Into<ErasedEvent>)` where ErasedEvent carries `Box<dyn Any + Send + Sync>` plus a runtime TypeKey (TypeId, OR for generics a richer type token a la ResolvableType). The multicaster, on publish, computes the event's TypeKey, looks up matching listener adapters (cached by TypeKey like Spring caches by event class), and invokes each. A `ListenerAdapter` trait erases over the concrete event: `async fn supports(&self, key: &TypeKey) -> bool; async fn invoke(&self, event: &dyn Any) -> Result<ListenerOutcome, ListenerError>;` — each adapter downcasts the `&dyn Any` to its concrete event type internally. The classic listener and the `#[event_listener]` POJO both compile down to a ListenerAdapter (the macro emits an adapter that downcasts the bean + downcasts the event + calls the method). Payload events: `publish(my_dto)` wraps a non-adapter type in an erased envelope exactly like PayloadApplicationEvent; listeners declaring the payload type get it unwrapped by their adapter. Generics: because TypeId erases type args, this realization must carry a richer TypeKey (a leaf-owned type token capturing the full generic shape, the ResolvableTypeProvider analogue) for events that opt into generic targeting — events implement a `ResolvableEventType` trait returning their full key; without it, `EntityCreated<Person>`/<Order> collide (the SAME tradeoff Spring documents, faithfully reproduced). Ordering, condition, chaining, hierarchy: all as Spring — adapters sorted by order, condition checked in the adapter, non-unit return re-published through the same erased publish, child-then-parent delivery.

**Pros:** Highest Spring-parity and the cleanest 'listener for a common supertype / any event' story: a single erased bus makes cross-type and marker-based listeners first-class (the case R1 makes awkward), because matching is a runtime `supports(key)` vote, not a fixed typed channel.; One uniform listener abstraction (ListenerAdapter) for BOTH the classic interface and the POJO model and future listener kinds (transactional-events builds its phase-deferring adapter as just another ListenerAdapter — exactly Spring's 'the adapter layer is the extension point'), maximizing unification (invariant 11).; Dynamic add/remove of listeners at runtime is natural (it's a list of dyn adapters), supporting Spring-ish programmatic listener registration and dynamic/WASM-module-contributed listeners without a frozen-channel constraint.; The erased ErasedEvent + TypeKey is inherently origin-agnostic: a WASM-module-produced event or a test-double listener is indistinguishable from a native one — slots into the same bus.; Return-value chaining is uniform (re-publish through the same erased path) with no special-casing of the primary vs chained path that R1 needs.

**Cons:** Worst overhead on the hot path (directly fights invariant 2 and 4): every publish does an erased lookup, per-listener `supports` votes and a downcast per invocation, and (for generics) a richer-than-TypeId key comparison — a Spring-style tree of dynamic checks on the event-dispatch hot path, the exact reflective-bus cost Rust could avoid.; Re-imports the generics erasure trap leaf could otherwise dodge: needs a hand-rolled ResolvableType-equivalent type token + a ResolvableEventType trait users must implement for generic events, and without it the <Person>/<Order> collision is a silent mis-targeting bug — replicating a Spring weakness rather than fixing it.; Heavy erasure/boxing (Box<dyn Any>, Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>, boxed futures) means Arc/heap churn per publish (invariant 5 tension), and a downcast that can fail at runtime turns a type mismatch into a runtime error rather than a compile error (weaker on §2.9 timing).; Listener matching cache (TypeKey -> Vec<adapter>) is mutable shared state that, if listeners can be added at runtime, needs locking — risk of a hot-path lock unless carefully sharded/frozen (invariant 5).; Diagnostics are runtime-shaped: 'no listener matched' / 'downcast failed' surface at dispatch, later than R1's compile/startup detection.

**Mechanisms considered:** ErasedEvent = Box<dyn Any + Send + Sync> + TypeKey; one multicaster with Vec<Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>>; ListenerAdapter trait (supports/invoke) erasing over concrete event — the single uniform listener kind; transactional/async are adapter subtypes; leaf-owned TypeKey richer than TypeId (ResolvableType analogue) + ResolvableEventType trait for opt-in generic targeting; Arc<dyn Any> downcast (stable 1.0/1.29) + trait upcasting (1.86) to coerce dyn-listener supertraits; TypeKey->listeners match cache (mirrors Spring's per-event-class cache); frozen-after-refresh or sharded to avoid hot-path lock; inventory life-before-main OR linkme for collecting dyn adapters across crates (typetag-style self-registration; candidate, deferred)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Because adapters are dyn and can be runtime-computed (not necessarily const), this realization fits the inventory (life-before-main constructor) substrate as well as linkme — the typetag pattern of registering `Box<dyn Trait>` across the dependency graph maps directly onto registering `Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>`. Candidates (deferred): inventory submit! per listener (flexible, non-const, but pre-main hazards + weaker wasm), linkme const descriptors (zero-cost but const-only, so a non-const adapter needs a const factory fn-ptr), build-time codegen (C), or explicit registration (D). The dual-classpath duality is especially clean here: static link-time adapters PLUS a dynamically-loaded/WASM-module adapter source both just push `Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>` onto the same bus — the erased bus is the natural meeting point for both 'classpaths'. Same Layer-0 / silent-empty hazard and mandatory self-check as R1; same leaf-core ABI home for ListenerAdapter/ErasedEvent/TypeKey; same absolute-path thin-macro discipline. One extra T1 wrinkle: the richer TypeKey must be constructible cross-crate without a global type registry, so it likely embeds TypeId plus const type-arg tokens emitted by the macro at each event's declaration site.

**T2 — execution context:** Same async-first publish and same sync-by-default-on-caller's-task contract as R1 (inline awaits, ordering preserved, ambient context preserved). The difference is purely internal: dispatch threads through erased `supports`/`invoke` calls and downcasts rather than a typed channel — each adapter `invoke` returns a boxed future awaited inline by default. Opt-in async (spawn) again delegates to the event-multicaster seam. Error propagation, cancellation, and hot-path no-polling discipline are identical to R1, with the caveat that the per-listener `supports` vote and downcast add CPU work on the dispatch hot path that R1 avoids — directly relevant to invariant 4. Listener add/remove at runtime (a capability this realization enables) must use fine-grained interior mutability, never a global lock; the match cache invalidation on add/remove is a cold-path operation. Boxed Send futures at the dyn boundary as in R1.

#### R3 — Hybrid: typed channels as the zero-cost default + an erased fallback channel for cross-type/marker/dynamic listeners, behind one EventBus interface
Unify R1 and R2 behind ONE `EventBus`/`EventPublisher` interface so consumers (publishers, lifecycle-events, transactional-events) program against a single surface, while the SUBSTRATE is two cooperating stores: (a) R1's typed channels (`HashMap<TypeId, TypedChannel<E>>`) as the zero-cost default for the overwhelmingly common 'listener for a concrete event type' case, and (b) R2's single erased channel (`Vec<Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>>` with a `supports(TypeKey)` vote) for the genuinely-dynamic cases R1 handles poorly: cross-type/'any event' listeners, marker-trait listeners, generic targeting needing a richer key, and runtime/WASM-module-added listeners. On publish<E>, the bus dispatches to E's typed channel (fast path, direct typed loop) AND offers the erased event to the erased channel's adapters whose `supports` vote matches (fallback path) — both contributing to one ordered dispatch sequence. The `#[event_listener]` macro chooses the backend by inspecting the listener: a concrete single-event-type method registers into the typed channel (fast); a method whose param is a marker trait / generic-needing-key / opted into dynamic registration emits an erased ListenerAdapter into the fallback channel. Authoring is uniform (@event_listener everywhere, or the classic typed listener trait); the bus hides which backend a listener landed in. This is the nightly doc's recommended 'keep the engine behind an internal abstraction' applied to events: zero-cost common case, contained dynamism for the rest, one interface so transactional-events/lifecycle-events don't care which path a listener uses. Payload events, ordering, condition, chaining, hierarchy all defined once on the EventBus interface and honored by both backends (the dispatch sequence merges typed + erased listeners and sorts by order across both before invoking).

**Pros:** Best DX/overhead BALANCE (invariants 1+2): the 99% concrete-event case is R1's monomorphized zero-erasure dispatch; the awkward-for-R1 cases (cross-type, marker, generic-key, runtime/WASM listeners) get R2's first-class erased handling — neither weakness forces a global compromise.; Keeps the erased/dynamic machinery OFF the common hot path: a publish whose type has no erased-channel matches pays only the typed-channel cost; the dyn-vote tax is incurred only when erased listeners actually exist for that type.; Maximal unification (invariant 11): ONE EventBus interface serves all publishers and BOTH backends, and transactional-events/lifecycle-events build on the interface without knowing the backend — exactly the Phase-2-friendly 'shared primitive, swappable substrate' shape; future listener kinds slot in as either backend.; Origin-agnostic and dual-classpath-clean: native concrete listeners go to typed channels; dynamic/WASM-module/runtime-added listeners go to the erased channel; both feed one ordered dispatch — the two 'classpaths' meet at the EventBus, not at two code paths consumers must know about.; Generic targeting is free where monomorphization suffices (typed channel) and STILL possible via the richer-key erased channel where a runtime-resolved key is genuinely needed — gets R1's free generics without permanently losing R2's dynamic-generic capability.

**Cons:** Most machinery + the merge-ordering subtlety: a single dispatch must collect typed-channel listeners AND matching erased-channel adapters and order them together by @Order across two stores of different shapes — getting the merged ordering right (and stable) is fiddly and a silent mis-order is a real hazard (invariant 9).; Two backends risk SEMANTIC DRIFT: a listener's condition/chaining/error behavior must be identical whether it landed in the typed or erased channel, or the same @event_listener means subtly different things depending on the macro's backend choice — a correctness-and-trust hazard mirroring the expression-language hybrid's drift risk.; The unifying EventBus interface may have to erase the typed channel's identity at the seam (e.g. dispatch returns through a common path), risking clawing back some of R1's zero-cost unless the typed fast path is specialized to bypass the interface — a real tension with invariant 2.; Larger conceptual + maintenance surface than either pure realization; the macro's 'which backend' decision rule is itself a thing users must understand when reasoning about ordering/overhead (leaky abstraction risk).; Deciding the backend split and the merge-order contract edges toward pre-judging machinery Phase 2 should unify — must stay a candidate SHAPE (one interface, two cooperating channels), not a settled architecture.

**Mechanisms considered:** Single EventBus/EventPublisher trait over two stores: HashMap<TypeId,TypedChannel<E>> (fast) + Vec<Arc<dyn ListenerAdapter>> erased fallback; Macro backend selection: concrete-single-type listener -> typed channel; marker/generic-key/dynamic-opt-in listener -> erased adapter; Merged dispatch sequence: gather typed listeners + erased adapters whose supports() matches, sort by @Order across both, invoke in one inline-await loop; enum dispatch vs Box<dyn> at the EventBus seam to preserve zero-cost on the typed fast path (the nightly 'internal abstraction' pattern); TypeId fast key + richer leaf-owned TypeKey only on the erased channel (pay the ResolvableType cost only where needed); substrate-neutral descriptor collection (linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit) feeding whichever channel a listener targets — deferred

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Inherits both R1's and R2's T1 profiles, partitioned by backend: const concrete listener descriptors suit linkme/codegen (typed channel); non-const/dynamic adapters suit inventory or explicit registration (erased channel) — so the hybrid can use the BEST substrate per listener kind, which is a genuine T1 advantage but also more substrate surface to keep coherent. The dual-classpath duality is its natural home: the static link-time set populates mostly typed channels, the optional dynamic/WASM-module source populates the erased channel, and both merge at the EventBus — the cleanest expression of 'two sources, one registration surface'. Same non-negotiable startup self-check (expected-vs-found across BOTH channels), same Layer-0/--gc-sections/silent-empty hazards, same leaf-core ABI home (EventBus, TypedChannel, ListenerAdapter, ErasedEvent, TypeKey, ListenerOutcome) and thin absolute-path macros. Extra T1 burden: the self-check and the `leaf doctor`-style discovery listing must report which channel each listener landed in, so a 'listener never fires' diagnosis can tell typed-channel-DCE-drop from erased-channel-no-match.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical async-first, sync-by-default-on-caller's-task contract as R1/R2 at the EventBus interface — consumers see ONE dispatch semantics regardless of backend, which is itself the benefit: a publisher/transactional-listener writes against a sync-default inline-await interface that is reactive/non-polling (invariant 4) whichever backend serves it. The only T2 subtlety is the merged dispatch: typed-channel listeners (direct typed calls) and erased-channel adapters (downcast + dyn invoke) are awaited in one ordered inline loop, so the per-listener cost differs by backend but the ordering/error/cancellation contract is uniform. Opt-in async (spawn) delegated to the event-multicaster seam as in R1/R2; error propagation, structured-concurrency cancellation of the inline dispatch loop, and Send-bounded boxed futures at the erased dyn boundary are as in R1/R2. The merge-order computation runs on the cold registration path (sort once after refresh), keeping the hot publish path free of sorting.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's listener DISCOVERY is runtime reflection: EventListenerMethodProcessor (a SmartInitializingSingleton) walks every managed bean, finds @EventListener methods reflectively, and wraps them in adapters. Rust has no runtime bean/method reflection and no classpath — listener discovery must be synthesized from compile/link-time metadata (descriptors emitted by the macro, collected via linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit), inheriting the entire cross-crate composition fragility (Layer-0 unreferenced-crate drop, --gc-sections emptying, silent-empty registries). The 'my listener never fires' failure mode becomes structural in Rust, not just the lazy-bean gotcha it is in Spring.
- The event itself is an erased Object on a single bus keyed by runtime Class; Rust prefers typed channels keyed by TypeId. This is the core fork between R1 (typed, zero-erasure, but cross-type/'any event' listeners are awkward) and R2 (erased, faithful, but reflective-cost on the hot path). There is no single representation that is both zero-cost-typed AND erased-uniform — the hybrid (R3) pays merge-ordering complexity to get both. Phase 2's type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone decides this.
- Generic event targeting depends on JVM type erasure + ResolvableTypeProvider: EntityCreated<Person> vs <Order> are indistinguishable unless the event advertises its full ResolvableType at runtime. In Rust this INVERTS — monomorphization makes the two DISTINCT TypeIds automatically (R1 gets type-safe targeting for free), but only for statically-known instantiations; a genuinely runtime-resolved generic key (R2) must hand-roll a ResolvableType-equivalent token because TypeId erases type args, so the clash flips from 'Rust is worse' to 'Rust is better except in the dynamic case'.
- Return-value-as-new-event chaining is reflective in Spring: a non-void return is inspected at runtime and re-published; a Collection/array is unrolled. In Rust the listener's return type is statically known, so chaining must be modeled as a typed ListenerOutcome (Emit(events)/None), and re-publishing a heterogeneous chained event re-introduces erasure (the chained event's type may differ from the listener's input) — and Spring's 'disabled on @Async' caveat becomes a hard compile/startup error in leaf since an async listener's result arrives after the publisher's future resolves.
- Sync-by-default 'on the caller's thread, preserving the caller's transaction/security/exception semantics' relies on JVM ThreadLocals carrying ambient context. leaf is async-first with explicit execution-context discipline (no implicit thread-locals across .await); 'sync on the caller's context' must become 'dispatch inline on the caller's TASK via awaits' and ambient context (transaction, locale, tracing) must be explicitly propagated (context-propagation neighbour), NOT smuggled through thread-locals — a deliberate divergence from Spring's mechanism that preserves its intent.
- Async dispatch in Spring uses a TaskExecutor and loses ThreadLocal/MDC/Observation context unless a ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator is added. In leaf, spawning a listener onto an executor is the explicit async-execution decision (delegated to event-multicaster + task-execution neighbours), and context propagation across the spawn is the context-propagation SPI's job — but the same async caveats (chaining off, errors to a handler not the caller, ordering not guaranteed) hold and must be enforced by leaf's contract, not discovered at runtime.
- Spring listeners are invoked via a dyn-dispatched adapter and may be async; AFIT/RPITIT traits are NOT dyn-compatible on stable, so an `async fn on(&self, e: &E)` listener trait stored as `dyn` MUST box its future (Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>) at the dyn boundary — an alloc per listener invocation. Zero-cost async + dyn dispatch cannot both hold; leaf must keep a monomorphized typed fast path (R1/R3 typed channel) and accept boxing on the erased/dyn path (R2/R3 fallback).
- Context-hierarchy double-delivery (a child's event reaches ancestor listeners) and early-event buffering (events before the dispatcher exists are queued) both assume a mutable, restartable bus with a parent link and a buffer flushed at a known refresh phase. In Rust these need an explicit parent reference (from container-hierarchy) walked at publish, and an explicit early-buffer drained by the refresh-lifecycle phase that initializes the multicaster — there is no implicit container-managed restart/reset, so refresh/close listener-state reset must be an explicit container-driven step.
- Spring tolerates publishing an event with zero listeners as a cheap no-op, and a lazy bean silently loses its @EventListener registration. In a typed-channel design (R1/R3) a publish to a type with no channel must be cheap-and-silent, but that makes a publisher-side TYPO (or a DCE-dropped listener) indistinguishable from a legitimately-unlistened event — so leaf must add a startup self-check (expected-vs-found listeners) to convert Spring's silent gotchas into loud, early diagnostics (§2.9), a capability Spring lacks.

### Interactions
- **event-multicaster** — THE dispatch engine this feature publishes through. 'events' owns the publish/subscribe MODEL (publisher handle, listener authoring, payload/generic/condition/chaining/ordering semantics); event-multicaster owns the single replaceable dispatch SEAM — synchronous-by-default ordered dispatch on the caller's task, opt-in async (executor/spawn choice), per-dispatch error handling/isolation, and return-value-as-new-event re-publishing. The boundary: this feature defines WHAT a listener is and HOW it's selected/ordered; the multicaster defines WHERE/HOW dispatch runs and how errors/threads are handled. The 'one named replaceable multicaster' intent lives there; this feature merely routes publish through it.
- **lifecycle-events** — Primary first-party PUBLISHER on this bus.
