Context lifecycle facts (refreshed/started/stopped/closed) and availability (liveness/readiness) are published as ordinary events through this same model — dogfooding that proves the bus is general. lifecycle-events depends on this feature's publish path and on the early-event buffering this feature defines (the first lifecycle facts fire around refresh, before app listeners may exist). The refresh-lifecycle phase that initializes the dispatcher and drains the early buffer is where these two meet.
- **transactional-events** — Built ON TOP of this feature's listener-adapter layer — the canonical illustration that the model is extensible. A transaction-phase-deferring listener is just another listener kind (an adapter that defers invocation to a commit/rollback phase). This feature must expose a listener-adapter/extension seam (uniform in R2/R3, special-cased in R1) so transactional-events can add phase-aware deferral without modifying the bus. Defaults-execution/fallback-execution coupling (a listener that no-ops when no transaction is active) rides this seam.
- **expression-language** — Supplies the per-event @EventListener CONDITION filtering, evaluated at dispatch (the hot path). This feature provides the listener's condition slot and the event/args as the EvalCtx root (#root.event / event, #root.args, arg-by-name/index); expression-language provides the evaluator. The strongest argument for expression-language's monomorphized-closure realization is exactly this hot-path per-event condition — the two features must agree the condition is a sync, non-blocking, Send predicate over &E plus an env handle.
- **container-core** — Decides whether the event PUBLISHER is an always-present façade service (the ApplicationContext-only intent — events are NOT on the bare engine) or an attachable capability; this feature is one of the context services container-core layers on. The publisher handle is injectable like ApplicationContext/ResourceLoader (a resolvable infrastructure dependency, not a normal bean), and the bus's existence-only-on-the-context split mirrors container-core's BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext (R1) / capability (R2/R3) decision.
- **container-hierarchy** — Provides the parent link that makes context-hierarchy double-delivery work: an event published in a child context must also reach ancestor-context listeners. This feature's publish path walks self-then-parent channels using container-hierarchy's one-directional parent reference; the 'listener fires multiple times across contexts' behavior and the disambiguation (compare originating context) are defined against container-hierarchy's visibility rules.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Owns the fixed-order bring-up phase that INITIALIZES the dispatcher and REGISTERS listeners (the initApplicationEventMulticaster + registerListeners + early-event-flush analogue). This feature's early-event buffering is drained at that phase; the listener-state reset on refresh/close (no implicit container restart in Rust) is an explicit step refresh-lifecycle drives. ContextRefreshedEvent (a lifecycle-events fact) publishes at the end of this phase.
- **smart-initializing** — In Spring, @EventListener method discovery is performed by EventListenerMethodProcessor, itself a SmartInitializingSingleton (runs once after all eager singletons exist, outside the creation lock). leaf replaces the reflective scan with compile/link-time descriptors, but the TIMING analogue remains: listener registration finalization is the single lock-free post-all-singletons-exist step — the rust-native home for binding collected listener descriptors to their (now-constructed) bean instances.
- **task-execution** — Backs the opt-in ASYNC dispatch path (via event-multicaster): 'run this listener somehow' delegates executor/pool/virtual-thread choice to task-execution. This feature's contract that async changes semantics (chaining disabled, errors to a handler, ordering not guaranteed across concurrent listeners) is the consequence of handing dispatch to a task-execution-provided executor; the two must agree on the execution-context handoff.
- **context-propagation** — Required to preserve the 'caller's context' intent across an ASYNC listener thread hop. Sync-default dispatch keeps ambient context by running inline on the caller's task; async dispatch needs context-propagation's capture/restore SPI to carry transaction/locale/tracing across the spawn (the ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator analogue). The EvalCtx for a listener condition that needs ambient context also rides this — context enters explicitly, never via a thread-local across .await.
- **async-execution** — Parallels the per-listener @Async path: Spring's per-listener @Async (requires @EnableAsync) runs one listener off-thread via the async-execution proxy. In leaf this is the same machinery as marking any method async-dispatched; this feature must agree with async-execution on the per-listener async opt-in and on the shared caveat set (no return-chaining on async listeners, exception routing to an async handler).
- **lazy-initialization** — Source of the classic 'listener never fires' gotcha: a @Lazy bean's @EventListener methods are silently never registered in Spring. leaf must decide whether a lazy bean's listener descriptors are still registered (diverging from Spring to fix the gotcha) or honored-lazy (matching Spring) — and surface it via the startup self-check. The interaction is the boundary between eager listener registration and lazy bean construction.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Typed channels vs one erased bus vs the hybrid (R1/R2/R3): is the event store keyed by TypeId with typed channels (zero-erasure, free generics, awkward cross-type listeners), a single erased Object bus (Spring-faithful, hot-path reflective cost, generics-erasure trap), or both behind one EventBus interface (best balance, merge-ordering complexity + drift risk)? This is entangled with the deferred type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone and cannot be settled per-feature — it dictates the whole dispatch shape and the cost on the event hot path (invariant 4).
- What is leaf's substrate for collecting listener descriptors across crates (linkme distributed-slice vs inventory life-before-main vs build-time codegen vs explicit register), and how is the Layer-0 unreferenced-crate-drop + --gc-sections silent-empty hazard handled so a 'listener never fires' failure becomes a loud startup self-check rather than a silent miss? Couples directly to the cross-crate composition meta-concept and must reconcile with the SAME substrate chosen for component discovery / auto-config.
- Does the event PUBLISHER (and the bus) exist only on the context façade (Spring's deliberate ApplicationContext-only split — never the bare engine) or is it an attachable capability, and is the publisher handle a resolvable infrastructure dependency? Shares the container-core BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext (two-types vs one-capability) decision; many neighbours assume the bus is always present on the context.
- How is 'sync-by-default on the caller's context' realized in an async-first framework with NO implicit thread-locals across .await — is it 'dispatch inline on the caller's task via sequential awaits', and how does ambient context (transaction/locale/tracing) propagate to inline-sync vs spawned-async listeners? Shares the async-execution & context-propagation meta-concept (T2) with context-propagation, async-execution, and task-execution; the answer fixes what 'preserving the caller's semantics' even means in leaf.
- Default error-propagation/isolation policy for a dispatch: does the first listener error abort the chain and propagate to the publisher (Spring's no-handler default) or is listener isolation the default — and is this a per-dispatch policy on the multicaster? Shares the unified error/diagnostics meta-concept (§2.9) and must be consistent with how the rest of leaf surfaces partial-failure of a fan-out operation.
- Return-value-as-new-event chaining: is it modeled as a typed ListenerOutcome (and therefore re-entering publish with a possibly-erased chained event), and how is the 'disabled on async listeners' caveat enforced — compile-time (macro hard-error), startup pass, or runtime? Cross-cuts the metadata/codegen-boundary (what the macro can know about the listener's return type) and the fail-fast-timing ladder.
- Generic event targeting: where monomorphization gives distinct TypeIds for free (typed channels) this is solved, but does leaf ALSO need a runtime-resolved richer type key (a ResolvableType analogue) for dynamically-typed or WASM-module-contributed generic events — and if so, how is that key constructed cross-crate without a global type registry? Couples to type-erasure substrate and the dual-classpath candidate.
- Do dynamic/WASM-module-contributed listeners and runtime listener add/remove need to be first-class (favoring an erased channel / inventory substrate) or is the static link-time listener set sufficient for the core (favoring typed channels / linkme), with dynamic sources as an optional second 'classpath'? This is the dual-classpath candidate (static link-time registry PLUS optional dynamic directory-scanned source) applied to listeners — a candidate, not a decision, that interacts with the origin-agnostic-provider guardrail.
- Listener registration TIMING and the lazy-bean interaction: is listener registration finalized in the single post-all-singletons-exist lock-free step (the SmartInitializingSingleton analogue), and does a lazy bean still register its listeners (diverging from Spring to fix the silent gotcha) or honor laziness (matching Spring)? Cross-cuts refresh-lifecycle, smart-initializing, and lazy-initialization.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent — I read the full Spring events section (lines 1788-1913) plus the boot event sequence (264-293), refresh phases (344-372), the SmartInitializingSingleton timing (904), and the concurrency contract (1918-1944); the API is explicitly stable/unchanged in v7/Boot4 so the parity target is solid. Scope discipline: I kept THIS feature to the publish/subscribe MODEL + listener wiring, and deliberately pushed the dispatch engine (threading, error isolation, the single replaceable seam, async/return-chaining mechanics) to the event-multicaster neighbour, lifecycle FACTS to lifecycle-events, and transaction-phase deferral to transactional-events — those neighbours own machinery this feature only routes through. The three realizations are genuinely divergent on the axis that matters for events: the SHAPE of the bus (typed channels / erased Object bus / hybrid), which is the event-specific facet of Phase 2's type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone — I refused to pick it and surfaced it as the first phase2 question. Three things to carry forward: (1) The single most important rust-native opportunity is that monomorphization SOLVES generic event targeting for free in the typed-channel realizations (R1/R3) — Spring's ResolvableTypeProvider exists only to fake what Rust's type system gives natively; the only place a runtime type-key is needed is the genuinely-dynamic case, which inverts Spring's clash. (2) The 'listener never fires' failure mode is FAR more dangerous in Rust than Spring: Spring's version is just the lazy-bean gotcha, but Rust adds silent link-time DCE drop of listener descriptors — so a startup self-check (expected-vs-found listeners per contributing crate, failing/warning loudly) is non-negotiable across ALL realizations, and the doctor/discovery listing must say which channel a listener landed in. (3) 'Sync on the caller's thread/transaction' is a JVM-thread-local mechanism that does NOT survive .await; I treated 'dispatch inline on the caller's TASK via sequential awaits, with explicit context-propagation for async' as the intent-preserving rust-native realization across all three, but the concrete model is Phase 2's (T2). Two clashes dominate downstream and belong to Phase 2: the bus-shape/erasure decision and the async-execution/context-propagation model — I did not settle either. The dual-classpath duality the brief asked me to flag (static link-time listener set as the default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic/WASM-module listener source) is captured as two descriptor sources feeding ONE typed-or-erased registration API — most natural in R2's erased bus and R3's erased fallback channel, and kept as a candidate, not a decision. I used 'EventBus'/'EventPublisher'/'ListenerAdapter'/'channel' as Phase-1 placeholder names only. I wrote no files. Grounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (events 1788-1913, boot sequence 264-293, refresh 344-372, smart-init 904, concurrency contract 1918-1944), /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2, deferred §3), /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0/A/B DCE, Architectures A-D, self-check mandate, dual-classpath/WASM), /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (AFIT/RPITIT NOT dyn-safe -> box at dyn boundary, trait upcasting 1.86, Arc<dyn Any>/TypeId registry, const TypeId 1.91, no async Drop -> explicit shutdown).

## Event Multicaster & Dispatch  `[event-multicaster]`

Spring's multicaster (doc lines 1840-1909, plus the early-multicaster note 283, refresh phases 344-371, and the concurrency-philosophy framing 1920) is the ONE named, replaceable seam through which the context funnels ALL event publishing: `AbstractApplicationContext.publishEvent` delegates to a single bean named exactly `applicationEventMulticaster` (magic-name; absent => a `SimpleApplicationEventMulticaster` default is installed), which resolves matching listeners (caching by event type), orders them, and invokes them. The whole reason it exists is "sensible global default, targeted local override": rather than scatter executor/error-handling config across every listener, Spring concentrates the dispatch POLICY (threading + error isolation) in one swappable place — override the bean, call `setTaskExecutor(...)` and `setErrorHandler(...)`, and you've changed dispatch semantics for every event at once. Intents this feature (NOT the `events` neighbour) must replicate, intent-not-mechanism: (1) ONE replaceable dispatch seam that all publishing routes through; (2) SYNCHRONOUS-BY-DEFAULT, sequential, on-the-caller's-thread dispatch — `publishEvent` blocks until every listener returns, deliberately preserving the caller's transaction/security/exception semantics so an event behaves like an ordinary method call you didn't have to wire; (3) OPT-IN ASYNC at two granularities (a global TaskExecutor on the multicaster => ALL events off-thread; a per-listener `@Async` => just that listener), where async deliberately CHANGES the contract (return-value chaining disabled, exceptions routed to a handler not the caller, context not propagated unless decorated, ordering not guaranteed across concurrent listeners); (4) ERROR HANDLING / isolation — with no ErrorHandler the first listener that throws aborts the remaining chain and propagates to the publisher; setting an ErrorHandler decouples listeners from each other (each error caught/routed, chain continues); checked exceptions wrap as UndeclaredThrowableException; (5) @Order ordering, meaningful only synchronously within one event type; (6) RETURN-VALUE-AS-NEW-EVENT chaining — a non-void listener return is re-published through the SAME publish path (collection/array unrolled), explicitly disabled on async; (7) it is the natural extension point for new listener KINDS (transactional-events' phase-deferring listener-adapter is built ON the same dispatch layer); (8) early-event buffering and context-hierarchy double-delivery flow through this seam. The rust-native reframing: "one replaceable bean" becomes "one injectable dispatch strategy/engine the container owns and can swap"; "sync on the caller's thread preserving transaction/context" becomes "dispatch inline on the caller's TASK via sequential awaits, ambient context explicit (no thread-locals across .await)"; "opt-in async via setTaskExecutor" becomes "the multicaster delegates spawning to the injected task-execution executor seam" (the already-designed neighbour); "setErrorHandler" becomes an injectable error-policy on the engine. I deliberately do NOT own the bus SHAPE (typed channels vs erased bus vs hybrid — that is the `events` neighbour's R1/R2/R3 axis) nor listener authoring/discovery; I own WHERE/HOW dispatch runs, the error/isolation policy, the async opt-in handoff, and the return-chaining re-publish mechanics, all behind one swappable seam.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Multicaster as an injectable dyn-trait engine bean: the literal replaceable seam (port of `applicationEventMulticaster`)
Mirror Spring most directly: there is ONE object-safe `Multicaster` trait, registered as a role-marked infrastructure bean (`applicationEventMulticaster` analogue), injected wherever publish happens, and the container installs a `DefaultMulticaster` if none is user-provided (the magic-name-default analogue). The publisher handle (`events` neighbour) holds `Arc<dyn Multicaster>` and calls `multicaster.dispatch(event_ref, &listener_set).await`. The trait surface is small and policy-bearing, NOT model-bearing: `async fn dispatch(&self, event: &ErasedEvent, listeners: ListenerSeq<'_>) -> DispatchOutcome` plus the swappable knobs as constructor-injected collaborators — an `error_policy: Arc<dyn DispatchErrorPolicy>` (the `setErrorHandler` analogue: AbortOnFirst | IsolateEach{sink}) and an optional `async_executor: Option<Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>>` (the `setTaskExecutor` analogue, pulled straight from the task-execution neighbour). `DefaultMulticaster::dispatch` is the engine loop: walk the already-ordered listener sequence the `events` layer handed it, for each listener either (sync default) `.await` it inline on the caller's task, honoring @Order, applying the error policy per listener, and collecting any `ListenerOutcome::Emit(events)` to re-publish through the same publish path after the current pass; OR (global-async mode, executor present) hand each listener future to `async_executor.spawn(...)` and DON'T collect return values (chaining off, errors routed to error_policy's sink not the caller, ordering abandoned). The seam is the trait object: swap the bean for a `MeteredMulticaster` (wraps another + emits per-dispatch metrics), a `TracingMulticaster`, or a wholly custom engine — zero call-site change because everyone holds `Arc<dyn Multicaster>`. Because the trait must be dyn (so the engine is swappable and an alternate-origin/WASM or test-double engine can stand in), its async `dispatch` is boxed at the dyn boundary (the unavoidable AFIT-not-dyn-safe wall). The ErasedEvent + ListenerSeq are origin-agnostic handles the `events` layer produces, so the engine never assumes how listeners were discovered or whether the event is native or module-sourced. Per-listener async (`@Async` on one listener) is NOT this engine's concern — that listener arrives already wrapped as an async-dispatching listener kind by the events+async-execution layers; the engine just awaits whatever future the listener hands back (which, if it self-spawned, resolves immediately to a detached handle).

**Pros:** Tightest intent parity: 'one replaceable bean you override to set executor + error handler' is literally a dyn-trait engine bean with two injected policy collaborators — the Spring `applicationEventMulticaster` mental model ports 1:1 and the override story (swap the bean, or swap a policy) is teachable and obvious.; Swap-by-config falls out for free (invariant 1 DX): publishers hold `Arc<dyn Multicaster>`, so installing a metered/traced/async-default/custom engine needs zero call-site edits — the 'sensible global default, targeted local override' rationale realized exactly.; Clean separation of concerns vs the `events` neighbour: this trait carries dispatch POLICY (where it runs, error isolation, async handoff, chaining re-publish) and is oblivious to bus SHAPE — `events` can pick typed-channels / erased-bus / hybrid and hand this engine a uniform ordered ListenerSeq, so the two features compose without either dictating the other's substrate.; The error policy as an injected `Arc<dyn DispatchErrorPolicy>` makes Spring's AbortOnFirst-vs-isolate a first-class, swappable, testable object rather than a buried flag — and the IsolateEach{sink} variant gives the per-listener decoupling Spring's setErrorHandler provides, with a typed error sink instead of swallowing.; Async opt-in is honestly delegated to the task-execution neighbour's `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>` rather than reinvented: the engine doesn't choose pools/virtual-threads, it just hands futures to the injected executor — the global-TaskExecutor intent realized through the already-designed shared execution seam, with the async contract-change (no chaining, errors-to-sink) enforced structurally.

**Cons:** The dyn boundary boxes the `dispatch` future per publish (AFIT-not-dyn-safe; nightly doc lines 162, 442) and the ListenerSeq erases listener types — so even the sync fast path pays an alloc per dispatch and the engine can't statically specialize per event type; on hot fan-out this is the zero-cost-monomorphized-dispatch loss (invariant 2/5 tension), mitigated only if `events`' typed-channel path is allowed to bypass the dyn engine for the common case (which couples the two features).; Return-value chaining re-enters publish with a possibly-different (erased) event type, so the engine must carry chained events as `Box<dyn Any + Send>` and re-dispatch them through the erased path — the chaining path pays erasure regardless of whether the primary path is typed (the same partial-purity wrinkle the `events` R1 flagged, now living in the engine).; Two policy collaborators (error_policy + async_executor) plus the engine is more wiring than a single fat object; a misconfiguration (executor set but error policy still AbortOnFirst) yields surprising semantics (async errors hit a sink, sync errors abort) that must be documented as a coherent matrix, not discovered.; 'Sync on the caller's context' is only as faithful as the inline-await discipline: it preserves the caller's TASK and explicit ambient context, but NOT thread-locals (a deliberate divergence) — a porter expecting ThreadLocal/transaction-on-the-thread will be surprised, so the engine must document that ambient context is carried explicitly (context-propagation neighbour), not smuggled.; A single replaceable engine bean is mutable-shared infrastructure; supporting runtime swap or per-context engines (hierarchy) needs care to stay lock-free on the dispatch hot path (invariant 5) — the engine's own state (error_policy, executor handles) must be frozen-after-refresh.

**Mechanisms considered:** object-safe `dyn Multicaster` engine trait stored as `Arc<dyn Multicaster>` (the replaceable seam = the trait object), container installs a `DefaultMulticaster` if none provided (magic-name-default analogue); boxed `dispatch` future + erased ListenerSeq at the dyn boundary (trait_variant Send-variant / async-trait box; AFIT not dyn-safe per nightly doc lines 162/180/442); `Arc<dyn DispatchErrorPolicy>` injected collaborator (AbortOnFirst | IsolateEach{sink}) — the setErrorHandler analogue as a swappable object; `Option<Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>>` injected from the task-execution neighbour — the setTaskExecutor analogue; global-async = spawn each listener, chaining off; engine-owned re-publish loop collecting `ListenerOutcome::Emit(Box<dyn Any + Send>)` and re-dispatching through the same publish path (return-chaining mechanics); wrapping engines (MeteredMulticaster / TracingMulticaster) as decorator beans over an inner `Arc<dyn Multicaster>` — the override-by-composition story

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The `Multicaster` trait, `DispatchErrorPolicy` trait, `ErasedEvent`/`ListenerSeq`/`ListenerOutcome`/`DispatchOutcome` types live in leaf-core for ABI stability (emitted code and the `events` layer hard-code `::leaf_core::` paths). The DEFAULT engine bean is itself a registration that crosses crates the same way every infrastructure bean does — so it inherits the Layer-0 / `--gc-sections` / silent-empty hazard the composition doc analyzes (lines 28-36, 149-152): if the default-engine-providing crate is declared but never path-referenced, the binary could silently have NO multicaster and every publish becomes a hard error — so the container's expected-vs-found self-check (composition doc line 152) MUST assert the multicaster exists at refresh, exactly like the task-execution neighbour's mandatory-executor self-check. Crucially this feature does NOT itself collect listeners across crates — that is the `events` neighbour's descriptor-collection problem (linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit, all CANDIDATES, deferred); this feature only RECEIVES an already-assembled ListenerSeq, so it is substrate-neutral on the discovery mechanism. The dual-classpath duality (static link-time listener set as the default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic/WASM-module listener source) is invisible to the engine BY DESIGN: both sources feed the `events` layer's registration API and surface to this engine only as listeners in the ListenerSeq, with the engine's ErasedEvent/erased-listener handles making a module-sourced listener indistinguishable from a native one (origin-agnostic guardrail honored). A custom engine contributed by an integration crate (e.g. leaf-observability's MeteredMulticaster) registers via the same bean substrate and is selected by primary/qualifier — same force-link + self-check obligations as any swappable infrastructure bean.

**T2 — execution context:** This feature IS the dispatch execution-context decision, so its T2 is load-bearing. SYNC-BY-DEFAULT is realized as: `dispatch` runs inline on the caller's TASK, awaiting each listener sequentially in @Order, so the publisher's `.await` drives the listeners and the caller's ambient context (transaction/locale/tracing, carried explicitly via context-propagation — NOT thread-locals, which don't survive .await) is in scope for each listener; `publish().await` does not resolve until every sync listener has returned (the 'blocks until every listener returns' intent). This is NOT blocking the executor — listeners are awaited, not run on a blocked thread; a listener that must do blocking work routes through the task-execution neighbour's `spawn_blocking` seam (execution-context discipline, charter §2.3). OPT-IN GLOBAL ASYNC: when the injected `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>` is present, each listener future is handed to `executor.spawn(...)`; the engine does NOT await completion, so `publish().await` returns after dispatch is scheduled — and the contract CHANGES: return-value chaining is disabled (the result would arrive after the publisher returned — enforced by the engine simply not collecting outcomes in async mode), errors route to the error_policy's sink (the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler analogue) not the caller, ordering across concurrent listeners is abandoned (@Order meaningless), and ambient context crosses the spawn ONLY via a task-execution TaskDecorator (context-propagation neighbour). ERROR PROPAGATION (§2.9): default sync + AbortOnFirst policy => first listener error stops the chain and propagates the rich error to the publisher (Spring's no-handler default); IsolateEach{sink} => each listener error is caught, attributed (which listener, which event), routed to the sink, chain continues. CANCELLATION: because sync dispatch is inline awaits, cancelling the publisher's future cancels in-flight listeners at their await points; a structured-concurrency scope around the dispatch loop bounds this (no detached tasks in sync mode). Async-mode spawned listeners follow the task-execution neighbour's cancellation/drain contract (cooperative, drain-on-shutdown). HOT-PATH discipline (invariant 4): dispatch is direct awaits driven by the publish future — no polling. Boxed Send futures at the dyn boundary; Send required so async-spawned listeners survive a thread hop on a multi-thread executor.

#### R2 — Dispatch policy as data + a thin engine: a `DispatchPolicy` value (sync | async-via-named-executor | isolate) resolved per event-type, engine is one monomorphized loop
Question whether the seam needs to be a dyn bean at all. Spring's multicaster conflates 'the engine' and 'the policy'; in leaf the POLICY (run inline vs spawn on executor X, abort-on-first vs isolate-to-sink, chaining on/off, drain timeout) is the part users actually override, and it is pure DATA. So model dispatch policy as a small descriptor — `DispatchPolicy { mode: Inline | Spawn{executor: ExecutorHandle} , on_error: Abort | Isolate{sink: ErrorSinkHandle}, chaining: bool }` — bound from config (dogfooding config-properties/binder, mirroring the task-execution neighbour's R3 profiles-as-data), with a default 'application' policy (Inline + Abort + chaining-on) and OPTIONAL per-event-type overrides (e.g. `leaf.events.dispatch.<EventTypeName>.mode=spawn`). There is essentially ONE thin engine — a monomorphized `dispatch(event, listeners, policy)` free function / small generic struct — that consults the resolved policy and runs the loop accordingly: Inline => sequential inline awaits honoring @Order, collect+re-publish outcomes if chaining; Spawn => hand each listener to the named executor (resolved from the task-execution neighbour by name), no await, no chaining, errors to sink; Abort/Isolate decided per the policy's on_error. The publisher handle carries a cheap `DispatchHandle` (a clonable token naming a policy, like task-execution R3's ExecutorHandle) rather than a fat dyn engine; `handle.dispatch(event, listeners)` looks up the policy and runs the thin engine. 'Replaceable seam' survives as 'change the policy data' (Spring's setTaskExecutor/setErrorHandler become two config keys or a programmatic policy override) — the override is a data edit, not a bean swap, so virtual-thread-style 'flip async on for all events' is one property; and per-event-type granularity (which Spring lacks — it's all-or-per-listener) is a config-native bonus. A genuinely custom engine (rare) is still possible as an alternate engine impl behind the handle (the deeper seam), preserving origin-agnosticism for a WASM/alternate-runtime dispatch backend.

**Pros:** Most literal realization of 'sensible global default, targeted local override' as TUNING not code: dispatch behavior is data bound from the Environment, so 'all events async' / 'isolate listeners for event X' / 'chaining off' are config keys, uniform with all other leaf config (config-properties/binder/config-metadata reuse — strong unification, invariant 11), and finer-grained than Spring's global-or-per-listener split.; Keeps the engine MONOMORPHIZED and off the dyn surface for the common Inline case (invariant 2/5): no boxed dispatch future, no erased engine vtable — the thin engine can be a generic loop the compiler specializes, paying erasure only where the policy actually says Spawn (which already implies a boxed future at the executor seam anyway).; Policies are inspectable/diagnosable data (invariant 9): a `leaf doctor`-style report can list every event type, its resolved dispatch mode/error-policy/chaining, and which executor it spawns to — far easier than introspecting a graph of wrapper engine beans, and ties to startup-instrumentation.; Composes cleanly with the task-execution neighbour's own profiles-as-data realization: a Spawn policy just names an ExecutorHandle, so 'which executor backs async event dispatch' and 'how that executor is tuned' are one consistent config surface, not two abstractions to reconcile.; A typo'd/missing policy or executor name is caught by a startup validation pass over the policy table (the task-execution neighbour's same pattern), converting Spring's silent magic-name fallback ('named it wrong => silently got the sync default') into a loud early diagnostic.

**Cons:** 'Behavior chosen by a config-resolved policy table' trades compile-time legibility for flexibility — a missing/misnamed policy or executor is a startup-or-runtime error, not a compile error (charter §2.9 prefers the earliest LEGIBLE diagnostic), mitigated only by the validation pass; and per-event-type policy keys are stringly-typed against event type names, which is fragile across refactors.; Diverges hardest from Spring's 'the multicaster is a BEAN you override' mental model — there is no `applicationEventMulticaster` object to `@Primary`/qualify; the seam moved from DI into a config table, which a porter must relearn (teaching cost; intent-parity says acceptable but it's real friction, same critique as task-execution R3).; A truly custom dispatch ENGINE (not just custom policy) — e.g. one that batches, dedups, or reorders events — is now 'replace the engine impl behind the handle,' a deeper and rarer seam than R1's swap-the-bean; the 99% override (executor + error handling) is easy but the long-tail override is more invasive.; Per-event-type policy resolution adds a lookup + branch per publish vs R1's already-resolved engine; cheap, but on the hottest fan-out paths the policy lookup (even if a frozen map) is non-zero, and if policies can vary by event type the engine can't be fully specialized at one site.; Two granularities of async (global default policy vs per-event-type override) don't cover Spring's PER-LISTENER `@Async` cleanly — per-listener async must still be handled by the listener arriving pre-wrapped from events+async-execution, so the policy table and the per-listener path coexist and must be explained as complementary, not competing.

**Mechanisms considered:** `DispatchPolicy` descriptor (mode Inline|Spawn{executor} , on_error Abort|Isolate{sink}, chaining bool, drain_timeout) bound from the Environment via config-properties/binder — the replaceable seam = policy data; cheap clonable `DispatchHandle` token at the publisher (names a policy), like task-execution R3's ExecutorHandle — low inject-path overhead; one thin monomorphized engine loop `dispatch(event, listeners, policy)` — no fat dyn engine for the Inline common case; per-event-type policy overrides keyed by event TypeName (resolved at refresh into a frozen map); default 'application' policy otherwise; Spawn policies name an ExecutorHandle resolved from the task-execution neighbour — one consistent execution config surface; startup validation pass over the policy table (fail-fast on missing/misnamed policy or executor) — converts Spring's silent magic-name fallback into a loud diagnostic; alternate custom engine impl behind the handle as the deep origin-agnostic seam

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The thin engine + `DispatchPolicy` schema + `DispatchHandle` live in leaf-core; policy VALUES come from the Environment (config files/env vars) resolved at runtime, so adding/retuning dispatch behavior needs NO recompile and NO cross-crate registration — this side-steps the link-time DCE hazard for the tuning surface entirely (a genuine advantage: config is a real runtime 'classpath' for dispatch policy, mirroring the task-execution neighbour's R3 T1). What still crosses crates: any Spawn policy names an executor that must be provided by the (mandatory, force-linked, self-checked) task-execution runtime crate — so a policy naming an unlinked executor is a startup-validation failure (loud, not silent). An error SINK that is CODE (a metrics/observation sink) registers as a bean/descriptor via whatever substrate Phase 2 picks and is referenced BY NAME from policy data — a policy naming an unlinked sink fails validation. Listener discovery remains the `events` neighbour's problem (substrate-neutral here). The dual-classpath duality is again invisible to the engine: dynamic/WASM-module listeners arrive in the ListenerSeq from the events layer; a dynamic source could even contribute a policy override at runtime (config is the natural meeting point), and the engine treats it identically. A custom engine impl from an integration crate registers at the handle's deep seam and is force-linked + self-checked like any mandatory infrastructure. Net: less link-time fragility for the tuning surface, same fragility (with the same self-check obligation) for the named executor/sink dependencies.

**T2 — execution context:** The engine + policy own ALL dispatch execution-context decisions in one auditable, config-described place (charter §2.3). Inline mode: identical sync-by-default contract to R1 — sequential inline awaits on the caller's task, @Order honored, ambient context explicit (no thread-locals across .await), `publish().await` blocks until all sync listeners return, structured-concurrency scope bounds cancellation, chaining collected and re-published if `chaining:true`. Spawn mode: each listener handed to the named `ExecutorHandle` (task-execution neighbour owns the pool/virtual-thread/blocking-offload decision); engine does not await; chaining forced off (policy `chaining` ignored in Spawn — enforced at validation, a policy with mode=Spawn+chaining=true is a config error); errors route to the policy's named sink not the caller; ordering abandoned; ambient context crosses the spawn only via a task-execution TaskDecorator (context-propagation neighbour). on_error=Abort vs Isolate decided per policy as in R1. spawn_blocking for genuinely blocking listeners routes through the task-execution seam (never block an async worker). Cancellation/drain: Inline cancels at await points within the publisher's scope; Spawn follows the named executor's cooperative-cancellation + drain-timeout contract (the policy's drain_timeout feeds the executor's drain). HOT-PATH (invariant 4): the only added cost over R1 is the per-publish policy lookup (a frozen-map read after refresh) — no polling, dispatch is await-driven. Policy binding happens during the Environment-ready/refresh phase before any publish; the policy table is frozen-after-refresh so the hot path is lock-free (invariant 5).

#### R3 — Pipeline multicaster: dispatch as an ordered, composable interceptor chain over one inner sink (decorators are the override mechanism)
Lean hardest into unification (invariant 11) by recognizing that Spring's multicaster overrides (setTaskExecutor, setErrorHandler, ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator, observation/metrics) are all CROSS-CUTTING WRAPPERS around a core dispatch act — which is exactly the shape of the task-execution neighbour's TaskDecorator chain and of leaf's AOP interceptor substrate. So model the multicaster as a thin core sink (`CoreDispatch`: the sequential inline-await loop over an ordered ListenerSeq, honoring @Order, collecting chaining outcomes) wrapped by an ORDERED chain of `DispatchInterceptor`s, composed once at refresh. Each concern Spring bakes into the multicaster becomes one composable interceptor: an `AsyncDispatchInterceptor` (intercepts and hands the inner dispatch to a task-execution executor — the setTaskExecutor analogue), an `ErrorIsolationInterceptor` (wraps each listener invocation in catch + route-to-sink — the setErrorHandler analogue), a `ContextPropagationInterceptor` (capture/restore ambient context around the dispatch — the ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator analogue, delegating to context-propagation), a `MetricsInterceptor`, a `TracingInterceptor`. The interceptor trait is `async fn around(&self, ctx: DispatchCtx<'_>, next: Next<'_>) -> DispatchOutcome` — the same around-advice shape as leaf's AOP interceptors, so the two features SHARE the interceptor primitive rather than each inventing a wrapper mechanism. 'One replaceable seam' becomes 'one composable pipeline you add/remove/reorder interceptors on' — and 'replace the whole multicaster' is the degenerate case of replacing the CoreDispatch sink. Interceptors are contributed as beans (ordered by @Order, runtime-resolved per the composition doc's ordering-at-runtime rule), so an integration crate's observation interceptor just registers itself into the chain — no central edit. Async/error/context-propagation are no longer special trait methods or policy fields; they are PEERS in one ordered chain, which is the maximal-unification answer to 'the multicaster funnels all dispatch through one place.'

**Pros:** Maximal unification (invariant 11): the multicaster's threading/error/context-propagation/observation overrides are ONE composable interceptor chain sharing the SAME around-advice primitive as leaf's AOP interceptors and conceptually the task-execution TaskDecorator chain — three features (AOP, task-execution decoration, event dispatch) converge on one wrapper mechanism instead of three bespoke ones, exactly the Phase-2-friendly 'shared primitive' shape.; Open/closed extensibility for dispatch concerns (DX, invariant 1): adding metrics, tracing, rate-limiting, dedup, or transactional-phase deferral to dispatch is 'register another interceptor bean,' no edit to the engine or to existing interceptors — and an integration crate (leaf-observability) contributes its interceptor the same way, mirroring Spring's 'wire concerns by adding beans' open/closed payoff that the whole event system is built to deliver.; The transactional-events neighbour's phase-deferring listener becomes naturally expressible as a dispatch interceptor (defer the inner invocation to a commit phase) OR as a listener kind — giving Phase 2 a clean choice, and proving the 'listener-adapter layer is the extension point' Spring intent (line 1893) is realized as a first-class composable seam.; Ordering of cross-cutting concerns is explicit and uniform: @Order across interceptors decides whether metrics wrap error-isolation wraps async (a real correctness question — do you want to time the spawn or the completion?) — Spring hides this ordering, leaf surfaces it as the same ordering model used everywhere (invariant 9 legibility).; The core sink stays thin and monomorphizable; only the interceptors that exist for a given context are composed, so an app with no async/metrics interceptors pays nearly the bare-loop cost (overhead scales with concerns actually configured, invariant 2).

**Cons:** Most machinery and the heaviest conceptual surface of the three: an interceptor chain + a core sink + per-interceptor ordering is more concepts than 'a bean with two setters' (R1) or 'a policy value' (R2); over-engineering risk for what is, in the common case, 'just run the listeners inline' — and it edges toward pre-judging the AOP-interceptor-sharing decision that is Phase 2/3's to make (must stay a candidate SHAPE).; Per-dispatch the chain is walked via `next.run().await` hops, each an async indirection (and boxed at any dyn interceptor boundary — AFIT-not-dyn-safe), so a deep chain adds layered boxing/await overhead on the dispatch hot path (invariant 2/4 tension) — worse than R1's single dyn hop or R2's monomorphized loop when many interceptors are present.; Interceptor ordering is a new correctness hazard: get the order of async-vs-error-isolation-vs-context-propagation wrong and semantics shift subtly (e.g. error isolation INSIDE async means errors hit the sink; OUTSIDE means they can't, because async already returned) — a powerful but sharp tool that demands the dispatch contract be specified as 'which interceptor must be innermost/outermost,' not left free.; Async dispatch as an interceptor blurs the clean 'sync-default vs opt-in-async changes the contract' line that R1/R2 keep crisp: whether chaining/error/ordering semantics hold now depends on whether an AsyncDispatchInterceptor is present AND where it sits — harder to reason about than a single mode flag.; Sharing the AOP interceptor primitive is a unification BET: if Phase 2 decides AOP interception and event dispatch want different around-advice shapes (e.g. AOP needs join-point metadata events don't), this realization's central selling point partly collapses — so its value is contingent on a cross-feature decision not yet made.

**Mechanisms considered:** thin `CoreDispatch` sink (inline-await loop, @Order, chaining-collection) wrapped by an ordered `Vec<Arc<dyn DispatchInterceptor>>` composed once at refresh — the replaceable seam = the pipeline; `async fn around(&self, ctx: DispatchCtx, next: Next) -> DispatchOutcome` interceptor trait sharing leaf's AOP around-advice shape (candidate unification); AsyncDispatchInterceptor (hands inner dispatch to a task-execution executor), ErrorIsolationInterceptor (catch+route-to-sink), ContextPropagationInterceptor (capture/restore via context-propagation), Metrics/Tracing interceptors — concerns as peers; interceptors as @Order-ordered beans, ordering resolved at runtime (composition-doc ordering-at-runtime); integration crates contribute interceptors via the same bean substrate; boxed futures at each dyn interceptor boundary (AFIT not dyn-safe); enum-dispatch or monomorphized chain for a fixed core set to limit boxing; transactional-events' phase-deferral expressible as a DispatchInterceptor OR a listener kind — leaving Phase 2 the choice

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Core sink + `DispatchInterceptor` trait + `DispatchCtx`/`Next`/`DispatchOutcome` live in leaf-core for ABI stability. Interceptors are beans contributed across crates via whatever substrate Phase 2 picks — so this realization has MORE cross-crate registration surface than R1/R2 (each interceptor is a contribution subject to the Layer-0/--gc-sections/silent-empty hazard, composition doc 28-36/149-152), and the self-check must verify not just 'a multicaster exists' but that EXPECTED interceptors (e.g. the context-propagation interceptor if async is configured) are actually present — a missing interceptor silently changes dispatch semantics (async listeners silently lose context if the ContextPropagationInterceptor was DCE'd), a worse-than-R1 silent-failure variant demanding a richer self-check (which interceptors landed in the chain, in what order). Interceptor ORDER is resolved at runtime by @Order (never link/registration order — composition doc 154), so the unspecified-registration-order hazard is defused by design. The shared-with-AOP interceptor primitive means this feature's T1 is entangled with the AOP substrate's T1 (same trait, same registration story) — a coupling that is a unification win IF Phase 2 keeps them shared and a coordination cost if it splits them. Listener discovery stays the events neighbour's concern. Dual-classpath duality: a dynamic/WASM-module source could contribute an interceptor (origin-agnostic — the chain only sees `Arc<dyn DispatchInterceptor>`), feeding the same pipeline as native interceptors; the task-execution executor an AsyncDispatchInterceptor uses is still the mandatory force-linked runtime crate. Thin absolute-path macros, leaf-core ABI home, expected-vs-found self-check over the interceptor chain are all non-negotiable as in R1/R2.

**T2 — execution context:** The chain composition IS the execution-context decision, made explicit by interceptor presence/order (charter §2.3). With NO interceptors: pure sync-by-default — CoreDispatch runs listeners inline on the caller's task, @Order honored, ambient context explicit (no thread-locals across .await), `publish().await` blocks until all return, chaining collected+re-published, cancellation bounded by the publisher's structured-concurrency scope (identical sync contract to R1/R2). With an AsyncDispatchInterceptor present: it intercepts and hands the inner `next` (the remaining chain + core dispatch for that listener/event) to a task-execution executor — so async-ness is localized to where the interceptor sits, and the contract-change (chaining off, errors to sink, ordering abandoned, context only via the ContextPropagationInterceptor) holds for dispatch passing through it; this is more flexible than R1/R2's single async mode but MORE subtle (the contract now depends on chain composition). The ContextPropagationInterceptor captures ambient context on the caller's task before an async hop and restores it inside the spawned task (the ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator analogue), delegating WHAT-is-captured to context-propagation — so context survival across the spawn is a composed interceptor, not a special case. Error semantics: ErrorIsolationInterceptor wrapping each listener gives Spring's setErrorHandler isolation; its ABSENCE gives abort-on-first-and-propagate (the default) — and its ORDER relative to AsyncDispatchInterceptor decides whether async errors are catchable (innermost = catches sync+async listener body; outside async = can't catch the spawned failure, which routes to the executor's uncaught-failure handler). spawn_blocking for blocking listeners via the task-execution seam. Cancellation/drain follow the task-execution contract for spawned dispatch and the structured scope for inline. HOT-PATH (invariant 4): no polling; cost is the chain-walk (next.run().await hops + boxing at dyn boundaries) scaling with the number of interceptors configured — bare-loop cost when none are present. Chain composition runs once on the cold refresh path; the hot publish path only walks the pre-composed chain.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's 'one replaceable multicaster BEAN' is a runtime, GC'd, mutable singleton you reassign via a magic bean name; Rust has no magic-name bean lookup and no implicit default-install — the 'replaceable seam' must be an explicitly-typed injectable (dyn engine in R1, policy data + handle in R2, interceptor pipeline in R3) that the container deliberately installs a default for and that the expected-vs-found self-check guarantees exists. Spring's magic-name silent fallback ('name it wrong => silently get the sync default') becomes a loud startup error in leaf — a deliberate divergence that fixes a Spring gotcha.
- 'Synchronous on the caller's THREAD, preserving the caller's transaction/security/exception semantics' relies on JVM ThreadLocals carrying ambient context down the call stack. leaf is async-first with NO implicit thread-locals across .await — so 'sync on the caller's context' must become 'dispatch inline on the caller's TASK via sequential awaits,' and ambient context (transaction/locale/tracing) must be EXPLICITLY propagated (context-propagation neighbour), never smuggled through thread-locals. The intent (an event behaves like an ordinary method call you didn't wire) is preserved; the mechanism inverts.
- async fn in traits is NOT dyn-compatible on stable (nightly doc lines 162, 442): a multicaster that is a swappable trait object (R1) AND whose core operation is async cannot be zero-cost — the dispatch future is boxed at the dyn boundary (Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>), one alloc per dispatch. The same wall hits R3's dyn interceptor boundaries. Only R2's monomorphized thin engine (no dyn engine for the inline case) and R1/R3's typed fast paths escape it. Zero-cost-async and dyn-swappable-seam cannot both hold.
- Spring's setErrorHandler ABORTS-vs-ISOLATES by mutating a field on a live bean, and an absent handler means the first throw aborts the chain and propagates. In Rust this is cleaner as a typed error POLICY (an injected object in R1, a policy-data field in R2, an interceptor's presence/order in R3) — but 'propagate to the publisher' means returning a rich Result up the publish().await chain (good), while 'isolate' means catching at each listener and routing to a typed sink. The async case has nowhere to propagate to (the publisher already returned), so async errors MUST go to a sink/handler — Spring's AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler intent, but enforced structurally because there is no caller to throw back to.
- Return-value-as-new-event chaining is reflective in Spring (inspect a non-void return at runtime, re-publish, unroll collections). In Rust the listener's return type is statically known and modeled as a typed ListenerOutcome (Emit/None) by the events layer — but the engine re-publishing a chained event of a DIFFERENT type than the input re-introduces erasure (the chained event arrives as Box<dyn Any + Send>), so the chaining re-publish path pays erasure even when the primary path is typed. And Spring's 'chaining disabled on async' becomes structural: the engine in async mode simply doesn't collect outcomes, because the result would arrive after the publisher's future resolved — a compile/startup-enforced invariant, not a runtime check.
- Spring's two async granularities (global TaskExecutor on the multicaster; per-listener @Async) both lean on a thread pool and ThreadLocal-preserving decorators. In leaf, GLOBAL async = the multicaster delegates spawning to the injected task-execution executor seam (already designed), and PER-LISTENER async = the listener arrives pre-wrapped as an async-dispatching kind by events+async-execution — so this feature owns the global handoff but per-listener async is a composition with neighbours, and BOTH inherit the task-execution Send + 'static bounds and the context-propagation-across-spawn requirement, which Spring gets implicitly from threads.
- No async Drop on stable (nightly doc line 86, 280): Spring's multicaster is a context bean torn down implicitly; leaf's dispatch engine, if it holds spawned-listener handles or a drain buffer, must be torn down via an explicit container-driven shutdown().await (drain in-flight async dispatches, then abort) — there is no Drop-based cleanup. Ties to context-close / runtime-lifecycle: the engine must quiesce (stop accepting publishes, drain in-flight dispatch) before the beans whose listeners it invokes are destroyed.
- Early-event buffering (events fired before the multicaster exists are queued and flushed at the registerListeners phase — doc lines 361, 371) assumes a mutable, restartable bus with a buffer drained at a known refresh phase. In Rust there is no implicit container-managed restart: the early buffer must be an explicit structure drained by the refresh-lifecycle phase that installs the engine, and the engine's state-reset on refresh/close must be an explicit container-driven step, not emergent.
- Spring caches matching listeners BY EVENT CLASS inside the multicaster and walks the parent context for hierarchy double-delivery. In leaf, listener resolution/caching and the typed-channel-vs-erased-bus shape belong to the events neighbour (the engine RECEIVES an already-resolved ordered ListenerSeq), and hierarchy double-delivery is the events layer walking self-then-parent via container-hierarchy's parent link — so the engine stays shape-agnostic, but the self-vs-parent dispatch ordering and the originating-context disambiguation must be defined against container-hierarchy's one-directional visibility, with no implicit ambient parent reference.

### Interactions
- **events** — The OWNING model this feature is the engine for. `events` owns the publish/subscribe MODEL — the publisher handle, listener authoring (classic trait + #[event_listener] POJO), payload/generic targeting, per-event condition filtering, @Order ordering, return-value ListenerOutcome typing, and (critically) the BUS SHAPE (typed channels / erased bus / hybrid — its R1/R2/R3 axis) and listener DISCOVERY/resolution. THIS feature receives an already-resolved, already-ordered ListenerSeq plus an ErasedEvent and owns only WHERE/HOW dispatch runs: sync-default inline-await, opt-in async handoff, error policy/isolation, and the return-chaining re-publish. The boundary is sharp: events decides WHAT a listener is and WHICH fire in what order; the multicaster decides the execution context, error handling, and threading of the dispatch. The 'one replaceable seam' lives here; events routes all publish through it.
- **task-execution** — Provides the executor seam this feature's opt-in async dispatch delegates to. The 'global TaskExecutor on the multicaster' intent (setTaskExecutor) is realized by injecting the task-execution neighbour's `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>` (R1) / naming an ExecutorHandle in a Spawn policy (R2) / an AsyncDispatchInterceptor handing dispatch to it (R3) — the multicaster NEVER chooses pools/virtual-threads/blocking-offload itself, it hands futures to the executor. The async contract-change (chaining off, errors-to-sink, ordering abandoned, Send + 'static bounds, drain-timeout) is the consequence of this handoff; the two must agree on the execution-context handoff and on spawn_blocking for genuinely-blocking listeners.
- **context-propagation** — Required to preserve the 'caller's context' intent across an ASYNC dispatch hop (the ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator analogue, doc line 1909). Sync-default inline dispatch keeps ambient context by running on the caller's task; async dispatch needs context-propagation's capture/restore SPI to carry transaction/locale/tracing across the spawn (a task-execution TaskDecorator in R1/R2, a ContextPropagationInterceptor in R3). Hard dependency: the rust-native ambient-context-across-.await model is Phase-2-deferred, so this feature can only DECLARE the propagation seam for async dispatch, not implement it — thread-local-shaped propagation would be actively wrong across task hops.
- **transactional-events** — Built ON this feature's dispatch-extension seam — the canonical proof the engine is extensible (Spring line 1893: the listener-adapter layer is the extension point). A transaction-phase-deferring listener is either another listener KIND that arrives in the ListenerSeq or a DispatchInterceptor (R3) that defers the inner invocation to a commit/rollback phase. This feature must expose enough of a seam (a swappable engine in R1, a policy/interceptor hook in R3) that transactional-events can add phase-aware deferral without modifying the core dispatch. The defaultExecution/fallbackExecution coupling (no-op when no transaction active) rides this seam.
- **lifecycle-events** — Primary first-party PUBLISHER routed through this engine (dogfooding). Context lifecycle facts (refreshed/started/stopped/closed) and availability (liveness/readiness, the AvailabilityChangeEvent that rides the same bus, doc line 1908) dispatch through this seam — proving the engine is general. The early-event buffering this feature drains at the refresh phase is exactly what the first lifecycle facts (fired around refresh, before app listeners exist) depend on.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Owns the fixed-order bring-up phase that INSTALLS this engine and drains its early-event buffer (the initApplicationEventMulticaster + registerListeners + early-event-flush analogue, doc lines 344-371). This feature's engine must be initialized at that phase, before eager-singleton instantiation publishes anything; the engine's state-reset on refresh/close (no implicit container restart in Rust) is an explicit step refresh-lifecycle drives. The expected-vs-found self-check (that the engine exists) fires in this phase.
- **async-execution** — Parallels the per-listener @Async dispatch path (doc line 1869). Spring's per-listener @Async (requires @EnableAsync) runs ONE listener off-thread via the async-execution proxy; in leaf that listener arrives pre-wrapped as an async-dispatching kind, and this engine simply awaits whatever future it returns. This feature and async-execution must agree on the shared async caveat set (no return-chaining on async listeners, exceptions routed to an async handler not the caller) so per-listener async and global-async dispatch present one coherent contract.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — The engine participates in the running-state machine: if it holds in-flight async-dispatch handles or an early/pending buffer, it must quiesce on stop() (stop accepting publishes, drain in-flight dispatch up to a timeout) before destruction — the graceful-drain intent. Because there is no async Drop, this is an explicit container-driven step ordered by runtime-lifecycle's phases, mirroring the task-execution executor's lifecycle participation.
- **context-close** — Symmetric teardown: context-close must drive this engine's explicit shutdown().await — quiesce dispatch (drain in-flight async listeners) BEFORE the singletons whose listeners it invokes are destroyed in reverse dependency order, so an in-flight dispatch never references a half-destroyed bean. No async Drop, so the container owns this ordering explicitly.
- **container-hierarchy** — Provides the parent link enabling context-hierarchy double-delivery (doc line 1903): an event published in a child context is also dispatched to ancestor-context listeners. The events layer walks self-then-parent using container-hierarchy's one-directional parent reference and hands the merged ListenerSeq to this engine; the engine stays hierarchy-agnostic but the self-vs-parent dispatch ordering and the originating-context disambiguation are defined against container-hierarchy's visibility rules.
- **container-core** — Decides whether the dispatch engine (and publisher) exist only on the context FAÇADE (Spring's deliberate ApplicationContext-only split — events are NOT on the bare engine) or as an attachable capability, and registers/selects the engine through the façade: role-marked Infrastructure, the default installed if no user engine is provided, and its mandatory presence enforced by the façade's expected-vs-found self-check (the silent-DCE defense). Shares the BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext (two-types vs one-capability) decision; many neighbours assume the engine is always present on the context.
- **expression-language** — Indirect, via the events neighbour: per-event @EventListener condition filtering is owned by events (it provides the condition slot and the event/args EvalCtx root; expression-language provides the evaluator) and is applied during listener RESOLUTION before the ListenerSeq reaches this engine. This feature does not evaluate conditions itself, but the dispatch contract must agree the condition is a sync, non-blocking, Send predicate so it can be checked on the dispatch hot path without forcing a spawn.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the dispatch seam a swappable dyn-trait engine bean (R1), config-resolved dispatch-policy data + a thin monomorphized engine (R2), or a composable interceptor pipeline over a thin core sink (R3)? This is the event-multicaster-specific facet of the type-erasure/registry-substrate AND the AOP-interceptor-sharing keystones: R3 bets that event dispatch and AOP interception want the SAME around-advice primitive, R2 bets dispatch policy is config data like the task-execution profiles, R1 bets it is a literal bean. Cannot be settled per-feature — it dictates the cost on the event dispatch hot path (invariant 4) and the override DX.
- What is leaf's DEFAULT error-propagation/isolation policy for a dispatch, and is it per-engine, per-policy, or per-interceptor? Spring's no-handler default ABORTS the chain on first error and propagates to the publisher; setErrorHandler ISOLATES. Does leaf default to abort-and-propagate (faithful, fail-loud) or isolate (resilient fan-out)? And how does the async case (no caller to propagate to => errors must go to a sink) reconcile with the sync case? Shares the unified error/diagnostics meta-concept (§2.9) and must be consistent with how the rest of leaf surfaces partial failure of a fan-out operation.
- How is 'sync-by-default on the caller's context' realized given NO implicit thread-locals across .await — confirmed as 'dispatch inline on the caller's TASK via sequential awaits,' and how does ambient context (transaction/locale/tracing) propagate to inline-sync vs spawned-async listeners? Shares the async-execution & context-propagation meta-concept (T2) with task-execution, async-execution, and context-propagation; this feature can only DECLARE the async-propagation seam until that model is settled — a thread-local-shaped answer would be actively wrong.
- Return-value-as-new-event chaining: the events layer models it as a typed ListenerOutcome, but the engine re-publishing a chained event of a DIFFERENT type than the input re-enters publish with an erased Box<dyn Any + Send> — where does that erasure live and how is the 'disabled on async' caveat enforced (the engine simply not collecting outcomes in async mode, validated at startup, vs a macro hard-error at the listener)? Cross-cuts the metadata/codegen boundary and the bus-shape decision owned by events.
- Does the dispatch engine (and publisher) exist only on the context FAÇADE (Spring's ApplicationContext-only split) or as an attachable capability, and is the engine a resolvable infrastructure dependency installed-by-default? Shares the container-core BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext decision; the engine's mandatory presence (a publish to a missing engine is catastrophic) makes the expected-vs-found self-check non-negotiable wherever it lives.
- What is the cross-crate substrate and force-link + self-check policy that GUARANTEES the binary never silently lacks its dispatch engine (and, in R3, its expected dispatch interceptors)? A DCE-dropped engine or a dropped context-propagation interceptor silently changes dispatch semantics (async listeners silently lose context) — a worse-than-ordinary Layer-0 hazard. Binds to the deferred cross-crate composition model and must reconcile with the SAME substrate chosen for the task-execution mandatory-executor self-check.
- Is per-listener async (@Async on one listener) owned here, by async-execution, or by the events listener-wrapping layer — and how do the two async granularities (global-via-executor and per-listener) present ONE coherent contract (chaining off, errors-to-sink, ordering abandoned)? Cross-cuts async-execution and the task-execution handoff; Spring keeps them separate paths that share caveats, and leaf must decide where the per-listener wrapping happens.
- Where is the dispatch-extension seam that transactional-events (and future listener kinds) build on — is phase-deferral a listener KIND in the ListenerSeq, a dispatch POLICY, or a dispatch INTERCEPTOR (R3)? This decides whether the listener-adapter extensibility Spring demonstrates is realized as a composable interceptor (favoring R3 and AOP-primitive sharing) or as a listener-kind in the events layer — a unification candidate, not a decision.
- Early-event buffering and engine state-reset on refresh/close: is the early buffer an explicit structure drained at the refresh-lifecycle phase that installs the engine, and is the engine torn down via an explicit container-driven shutdown().await that quiesces in-flight async dispatch before bean destruction? Cross-cuts refresh-lifecycle, runtime-lifecycle, and context-close, and depends on the no-async-Drop constraint being handled framework-wide consistently.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent — I read the full multicaster section (doc lines 1840-1909) plus the synchronous-by-default + @Order + async-events subsections, the early-multicaster note (283), the refresh phases that init/register/flush (344-371), the unifying-philosophy framing (1911-1913, 1920), and the AvailabilityChangeEvent-rides-the-bus dogfooding note (1908); the API is explicitly stable/unchanged in v7/Boot4 (line 1796), so the parity target is solid.

Scope discipline — THE key framing: the already-produced `events` neighbour design draws the boundary explicitly, and I held to it. `events` owns the publish/subscribe MODEL, listener authoring/discovery, payload/generic/condition/ordering/chaining SEMANTICS, and — most importantly — the BUS SHAPE (its R1 typed-channels / R2 erased-bus / R3 hybrid axis). THIS feature (event-multicaster) owns the single replaceable dispatch SEAM: WHERE/HOW dispatch runs (sync-default inline-await vs opt-in async), the error/isolation policy, the async handoff, and the return-chaining re-publish mechanics. I deliberately did NOT re-pick the bus shape — I made the engine SHAPE-AGNOSTIC (it receives an already-resolved, already-ordered ListenerSeq + an ErasedEvent from the events layer), so my three realizations diverge on a DIFFERENT axis than events' three: events diverges on how listeners are STORED/KEYED; I diverge on how the dispatch POLICY seam is REALIZED — R1 a swappable dyn-trait engine bean (literal Spring port), R2 dispatch-policy-as-data + a thin monomorphized engine (mirrors the task-execution neighbour's R3 profiles-as-data, config-native), R3 a composable interceptor pipeline (maximal unification, bets on sharing leaf's AOP around-advice primitive). They span most-Spring-faithful (R1) to most-rust-native-unifying (R2/R3). This keeps Phase 1 signal unbiased and gives Phase 2 a clean fork that is the event-multicaster facet of the type-erasure/registry-substrate AND the AOP-interceptor-sharing keystones.

Three things to carry forward: (1) This feature is the natural meeting point of TWO already-designed neighbours — it routes the `events` model through the `task-execution` executor seam. I built ON both concretely: opt-in async = delegate spawning to task-execution's injected executor (NOT reinventing pools/virtual-threads); 'sync on the caller's context' = inline-await on the caller's task with context-propagation for the async hop (NOT thread-locals, which don't survive .await). (2) The deepest divergence from Spring's mechanism is that 'one replaceable BEAN with setTaskExecutor/setErrorHandler' becomes an explicitly-typed injectable seam with a container-installed default and a MANDATORY expected-vs-found self-check — turning Spring's silent magic-name fallback ('named it wrong => silently sync default') into a loud startup error. The engine being missing is catastrophic (every publish fails), so the self-check is non-negotiable across all three realizations, same severity as the task-execution mandatory-executor self-check. (3) The async contract-change (chaining off, errors-to-sink, ordering abandoned) is enforced STRUCTURALLY in leaf, not discovered at runtime: in async mode the engine simply doesn't collect ListenerOutcomes (chaining off because the result arrives after publish() returned) and there is literally no caller to propagate to (errors must go to a sink) — Spring's intent, made structural by async-first semantics.

Two clashes dominate downstream and belong to Phase 2: the seam-shape decision (R1/R2/R3, entangled with both the registry-substrate and AOP-interceptor-sharing keystones) and the async-execution/context-propagation model (shared with task-execution, async-execution, context-propagation) — I settled neither. The dual-classpath duality (static link-time listener set PLUS optional dynamic/WASM-module listener source) is INVISIBLE to this engine by design: both sources feed the events layer's registration API and surface here only as listeners in the ListenerSeq, with ErasedEvent/erased-listener handles making module-sourced listeners indistinguishable from native (origin-agnostic guardrail honored); I captured it as a candidate at the events-layer level, not a decision here.

I used 'Multicaster'/'DispatchPolicy'/'DispatchInterceptor'/'CoreDispatch'/'ListenerSeq'/'ErasedEvent'/'DispatchErrorPolicy' as Phase-1 placeholder names only. I wrote no files. Grounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (multicaster 1840-1909, sync-default/Order/async 1856-1871, early multicaster 283, refresh phases 344-371, concurrency philosophy 1911-1920, AvailabilityChangeEvent 1908, TaskExecutor cluster 2016-2071 for the async handoff, ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator 1909/2089-2153); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2.1-§2.11, deferred §3); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0 28-36/149, --gc-sections 143-146, silent-empty + self-check mandate 152, linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit candidates 108-168, ordering-at-runtime 154, dual-classpath/WASM 171/225); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (AFIT/RPITIT NOT dyn-safe => box at dyn boundary 162/180/442, trait_variant Send-variant 135/260, no async Drop => explicit shutdown 86/280, Arc<dyn Any>/TypeId/trait-upcasting 481/393). The neighbour Phase-1 designs handed to me (events, task-execution) are the concrete dependencies I built on; lifecycle-events, transactional-events, async-execution, context-propagation, runtime-lifecycle, context-close, container-core, container-hierarchy, refresh-lifecycle, expression-language are referenced by catalog intent only (their Phase-1 designs not yet in docs/design).

## Built-in Lifecycle / Availability Events  `[lifecycle-events]`

Spring's lifecycle-events feature does TWO distinct things on ONE bus, and the duality is the whole design tension to replicate. (HALF 1 — lifecycle FACTS) The container publishes immutable, point-in-time facts about its own milestones as ordinary events on the SAME multicaster app code uses: ContextRefreshedEvent (after refresh(), all eager singletons exist — and notably CAN FIRE MORE THAN ONCE for refreshable contexts, so one-time listeners must guard themselves or prefer the started/ready facts), ContextStartedEvent / ContextStoppedEvent (on explicit runtime start()/stop() of the Lifecycle layer), ContextClosedEvent (on close()/shutdown-hook — end-of-life, cannot restart). Boot layers a richer ORDERED run sequence on top: ApplicationStarting -> EnvironmentPrepared -> ContextInitialized -> ContextPrepared -> (WebServerInitialized) -> ContextRefreshed -> ApplicationStarted -> AvailabilityChange(LivenessState.CORRECT) -> [runners execute here] -> ApplicationReady -> AvailabilityChange(ReadinessState.ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC); ApplicationFailed on startup error. The design INTENT: startup is a state machine with well-defined, STABLE observation points so extension authors get precise hooks ('after env ready but before context exists') and the framework has ONE uniform place to layer cross-cutting concerns (logging, metrics, availability) without scattering callbacks. Two gotchas leaf must convert into structure: events firing BEFORE the bus/context exists (Starting, EnvironmentPrepared) cannot be hosted beans — they need a pre-context registration path + early buffering; and an ApplicationReady-listener failure can FAIL startup (it is routed through run-failure handling, not merely logged). (HALF 2 — availability STATE) This is the load-bearing, easy-to-miss part: liveness (LivenessState: CORRECT/BROKEN — 'the internals are sound; restart me if broken') and readiness (ReadinessState: ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC/REFUSING_TRAFFIC — 'route traffic to me or not') are CORE, not Actuator: the core run listener itself publishes them. Crucially availability is BOTH delivered as an ordinary AvailabilityChangeEvent on the bus (dogfooding — probes/health subscribe like any listener) AND a RETAINED, last-write-wins, queryable CURRENT STATE: a k8s liveness/readiness probe at any later instant must read 'what is the state NOW', and ANY component can PUBLISH a state change at runtime (a broken DB pool flips readiness to REFUSING_TRAFFIC mid-flight; recovery flips it back) — so availability is a small reactive state-holder with an event projection, not a one-shot fact. The Kubernetes integration window is precise and intentional: runners/startup-data-loading run AFTER 'started'+LivenessState.CORRECT but BEFORE 'ready'+ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC, so migrations finish before readiness gates open. The rust tension: Spring's facts ride the type-erased reflective bus keyed by runtime Class; leaf must publish them as typed events through whatever the events/event-multicaster neighbours land on (typed channels / erased bus / hybrid) — but the STATE half is genuinely different machinery: a retained, transition-validated, subscribable value with current-value reads (probe cold-path polling is legitimate per charter §2.4) layered with an event projection — and the pre-context early-fact buffering and the cancel-vs-close fact distinction (a failed refresh publishes NO refreshed/closed fact) must be explicit, not emergent. This feature OWNS the catalog of facts + the availability state model + WHO publishes WHICH fact at WHICH milestone; it does NOT own the bus engine (events/event-multicaster), the phase boundaries that trigger facts (refresh-lifecycle/runtime-lifecycle/context-close fire them), or the per-listener authoring model (events).

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Facts-as-typed-events + availability as a separate reactive state-cell with an event projection (two-mechanism split)
Treat the two halves with their two natural rust-native mechanisms and connect them by ONE projection. (A) LIFECYCLE FACTS are plain typed event structs published through the events/event-multicaster bus: `struct Refreshed{ container: ContainerId, generation: u32 }`, `struct Started`, `struct Stopped`, `struct Closed{ reason: CloseReason }`, `struct StartupFailed{ phase, error: Arc<RefreshError> }` — each a distinct type, so listeners select by type exactly as the events neighbour prescribes (monomorphized typed channel in events-R1, or an erased adapter in events-R2; this feature is agnostic). The container itself is the sole legitimate PUBLISHER of facts; it calls `bus.publish(Refreshed{..})` at the precise milestone (refresh-lifecycle fires Refreshed at end-of-finish-refresh; runtime-lifecycle fires Started/Stopped around the phased start/stop; context-close fires Closed; the cancel path fires StartupFailed and deliberately NOT Refreshed/Closed). `generation` makes the 'Refreshed can fire more than once' reality explicit and lets one-time listeners guard. (B) AVAILABILITY is NOT a fact — it is a small reactive STATE-CELL owned by the container: `struct Availability{ liveness: watch::Sender<LivenessState>, readiness: watch::Sender<ReadinessState> }` (a single-producer-multi-consumer reactive cell — tokio::watch-shaped but runtime-agnostic via a leaf reactive-cell primitive, NOT a poll loop on the hot path, satisfying §2.4: subscribers are woken on change). Reads are `availability.liveness()` / `.readiness()` returning the CURRENT value (a k8s probe handler reads this — a cold-path point read, no polling). Writes are `availability.set_readiness(REFUSING_TRAFFIC, source)` — ANY component (a DB-pool guardian, an app service) holds an `AvailabilityHandle` (injectable like the publisher) and flips state at runtime; transitions are validated (an enum state-machine, not free Strings). The PROJECTION wires the two halves: a single internal bridge subscribes to the availability cell and re-publishes each transition as an `AvailabilityChanged{ kind, old, new, source }` event on the bus — so the dogfooding intent ('availability rides the same bus') holds WITHOUT making the bus the source of truth for current state. The container drives the standard transitions at the standard milestones: at Started it sets liveness=CORRECT; after runners complete it sets readiness=ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC; on close it sets readiness=REFUSING_TRAFFIC first (quiesce intent). Probes get a clean two-surface API: subscribe (reactive, for event-driven health export) OR point-read (for a synchronous probe handler). Pre-context EARLY FACTS (Starting, EnvironmentPrepared analogues — facts that fire before the bus exists) are handled by an early-fact buffer the container drains when the bus is initialized (the registerListeners-flush analogue), or published through a pre-context standalone mini-bus the events neighbour provides.
