**Pros:** Cleanest intent fidelity to the ACTUAL Spring design, which itself treats availability as a retained state object (ApplicationAvailability bean) DISTINCT from the events it emits — leaf's split mirrors Spring's own two-object reality (AvailabilityState + AvailabilityChangeEvent) rather than collapsing them, so the 'probe reads current state' need is first-class, not reconstructed by replaying events.; Probe reads are correct-by-construction and reactive (§2.4): a readiness probe reads the current cell value (cold-path point read) or subscribes for change-driven export — never replays an event log or polls a hot path. Last-write-wins, transition-validated state is exactly what k8s liveness/readiness semantics demand.; Runtime state changes by arbitrary components are natural and decoupled: a broken-DB-pool guardian flips readiness without knowing who probes — the open/closed wiring the events charter prizes, but for STATE not just facts.; Memory-disciplined (§2.5): the state-cell is one small struct with two reactive senders (no per-event heap log, no Arc-per-fact churn); facts are stack-built typed structs published and dropped. The projection bridge is a single long-lived subscriber, not N.; Origin-agnostic and dual-source ready: a WASM-module component can hold an AvailabilityHandle and flip readiness, and a WASM-contributed lifecycle listener subscribes to facts — both go through the same handle/bus API the events neighbour already makes origin-agnostic.

**Cons:** TWO mechanisms (event bus + reactive state-cell) is more surface than a single bus, and the projection bridge is a piece of always-on machinery that must stay coherent (every state transition must produce exactly one event, no double-fire, no drop) — a correctness seam Spring gets 'for free' by publishing the event AS the state change.; The reactive-cell primitive is itself a Phase-2 decision (which runtime-agnostic watch/notify abstraction) — this realization leans on a primitive leaf has not chosen, coupling lifecycle-events to the async-execution/context-propagation meta-concept more than a pure-event design would.; Possible state/event ordering skew: a subscriber that BOTH reads the cell AND listens for the projected event could observe them in either order under concurrency; defining 'the event is published strictly after the cell is updated' is a contract that must be specified and is easy to get subtly wrong.; Doubles the 'who can write' surface — a publisher handle (facts, container-only) AND an availability handle (state, app-wide) — two injectable capabilities to document and to keep from being confused (a component must not 'publish a Refreshed fact', only the container may).; The early-fact pre-context buffering still has to exist for the Starting/EnvironmentPrepared analogues, so this realization does not eliminate the early-buffer machinery the bus needs anyway — it inherits it.

**Mechanisms considered:** typed fact structs (Refreshed/Started/Stopped/Closed/StartupFailed) published through the events/event-multicaster bus — agnostic to that bus's typed-vs-erased shape; availability as a reactive state-cell (watch/notify-shaped, runtime-agnostic leaf primitive) with current-value point reads (cold-path probe) + change subscription (reactive, §2.4) — NOT a poll loop; enum state machines LivenessState{CORRECT,BROKEN} / ReadinessState{ACCEPTING,REFUSING} with validated transitions, not free strings; a single internal projection bridge subscribing to the cell and re-publishing AvailabilityChanged events (dogfooding intent without making the bus the source of truth); two injectable capabilities: a container-only fact PublisherHandle and an app-wide AvailabilityHandle; early-fact buffer drained at bus-init for pre-context facts (the registerListeners-flush / standalone-initialMulticaster analogue); generation counter on Refreshed to make multi-fire explicit and guardable

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Facts and the availability state model live in leaf-core (stable ABI): the fact struct types, the Liveness/Readiness enums, the AvailabilityHandle, and the PublisherHandle trait. A listener for a fact in a downstream/sibling crate (e.g. a health-export crate listening for AvailabilityChanged, or an app crate listening for Refreshed) is exactly the events neighbour's cross-crate listener-descriptor problem — it inherits whatever discovery substrate events lands on (linkme distributed-slice / inventory / build-time codegen / explicit), and inherits the SAME silent-DCE hazard: a health-export crate whose subscriber is DCE-dropped becomes a probe that never updates — INDISTINGUISHABLE from a legitimately-quiet probe — so the mandatory startup self-check (cross-crate doc §152/§345) must cover lifecycle-listener descriptors too. A component that WRITES availability (holds an AvailabilityHandle) is NOT a discovery problem — it acquires the handle by injection, so no link-time registration, no DCE risk for the write path (an asymmetry worth noting: writers are safe, subscribers inherit the DCE risk). Origin-agnostic: leaf-core's AvailabilityHandle and PublisherHandle are construction-agnostic, so a WASM-module-hosted guardian flipping readiness or a dynamically-loaded health exporter slot in via the same APIs. Dual-classpath duality applies only to the SUBSCRIBER side (static link-time listeners for facts PLUS an optional dynamic/WASM listener source feeding the same bus), as a CANDIDATE — the state-cell itself is a single owned object, not a registry, so it has no classpath duality. Thin macros: any `#[lifecycle_listener]` sugar emits only an events-style descriptor against absolute `::leaf_core::` paths; the fact types and handles are hand-constructible.

**T2 — execution context:** All standard fact publication happens on COLD paths at phase boundaries (refresh-lifecycle's finish-refresh, runtime-lifecycle's start/stop, context-close) on the bootstrap/shutdown task — async-first, each an `.await` on the bus's publish (which the events neighbour defines as inline-on-caller's-task by default). The availability state-cell is the one HOT-PATH-adjacent piece: a point READ (probe handler) is a lock-free current-value snapshot (watch::borrow-shaped) — no await needed, no poll; a WRITE wakes subscribers reactively (§2.4 — wakers, not interval polling), and is a cheap atomic-pointer/seqlock-shaped store under the no-global-lock discipline (§2.5). A probe SERVER that exposes readiness over HTTP polls k8s-style on its OWN cold schedule, but internally each poll is a point read — no busy-wait. The projection bridge runs as one long-lived task awaiting cell changes and publishing the projected event inline; its lifetime is container-scoped and it is shut down explicitly (no async Drop — §nightly) when the container closes. CANCELLATION/ERRORS: a fact-listener that errors follows the events/event-multicaster error policy (abort-chain-or-isolate — a phase2 question owned there); BUT the Spring-specific 'an ApplicationReady listener failure can FAIL startup' intent means the container must treat the Ready/started fact dispatch specially — its result is observed by the bring-up task and a failure aborts startup (routed into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path), unlike ordinary facts whose listener failures are isolated. State transitions never block; a broken-resource guardian flipping readiness does so from its own task and the change propagates by waker. On close, readiness is flipped to REFUSING_TRAFFIC FIRST (before runtime-lifecycle stop and destruction) to quiesce — an explicit ordered step, mirroring Spring's stop-before-destroy intent.

#### R2 — Unified retained-event log: every milestone (fact AND availability) is a retained typed event; 'current availability' is the last retained event of its kind
Collapse the duality the OTHER way: make EVERYTHING a typed event, and give the bus (or a thin lifecycle-scoped layer over it) RETENTION so 'current state' is just 'the most recent retained event of a kind'. Lifecycle facts and availability changes are all typed events: `Refreshed`, `Started`, `Stopped`, `Closed`, `LivenessChanged{ state, source }`, `ReadinessChanged{ state, source }`. The events/event-multicaster bus gains (for a marked set of types) a RETAINED-LAST semantics: when a `ReadinessChanged` is published, the bus stores it as the last value for that type; a NEW subscriber is immediately delivered the retained last value (replay-latest), and a probe queries `bus.latest::<ReadinessChanged>()` to read current readiness. This is the MQTT-retained-message / BehaviorSubject pattern: state is the latest event, not a separate cell. Availability writes are publishes: any component with the publisher handle calls `publish(ReadinessChanged{ REFUSING_TRAFFIC, source })`. Probes either subscribe (and get the retained latest immediately, then live updates — fully reactive) or call `latest::<ReadinessChanged>()` (cold point read of the retained slot). Lifecycle facts that are NATURALLY one-shot (Closed) are retained too, so 'is this container closed?' is `latest::<Closed>().is_some()`. The standard milestone transitions are the container publishing the right retained events at the right phases. Early facts before the bus exists are buffered and replayed, and because retention is built in, late subscribers naturally catch up — the early-buffer and retained-latest are the SAME mechanism. One conceptual model ('lifecycle is a stream of retained typed events'), one publish API, one subscribe API.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11) and minimal conceptual surface: ONE mechanism (typed events with retained-last) serves facts, availability state, probe reads, late-subscriber catch-up, and early-event buffering — the events charter's open/closed wiring extends to state with zero new primitive, and there is exactly ONE publish path and ONE subscribe path to learn.; Most faithful to the literal Spring statement 'AvailabilityChange rides the same bus' and to the dogfooding intent — here it LITERALLY is the same bus, no projection bridge to keep coherent, no second mechanism, so the state/event ordering-skew bug of R1 cannot occur (the event IS the state).; Late subscribers and probes are handled by the SAME retained-latest mechanism that solves early-event buffering — a new health exporter that starts after readiness already changed immediately sees current readiness; refresh-lifecycle's early-fact flush becomes a special case of retention rather than bespoke buffering machinery.; Querying any milestone uniformly: 'has it refreshed / started / closed / what is readiness' are all `latest::<T>()` — a single tidy introspection surface that startup-instrumentation and a `leaf doctor` can dump as 'the retained lifecycle state vector'.; Strong escape-hatch/legibility (§2.10): the retained-event store is inspectable data; the whole lifecycle/availability state of a container is one dumpable map of TypeId -> last event.

**Cons:** Retention is a STATEFUL bus feature that the events/event-multicaster neighbours may NOT want — it pushes per-type mutable storage and replay-on-subscribe INTO the dispatch engine, growing it and adding interior-mutable per-type slots (a hot-path / §2.5 tension and a possible lock on the retained-slot) that a stateless bus avoids; this realization effectively DECIDES a bus capability that belongs to the neighbour, risking the guardrail against declaring shared machinery.; 'State as the latest event' is a weaker model than a validated state machine: there is no natural place to enforce legal transitions (CORRECT->BROKEN ok, but is BROKEN->ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC a category error?) — validation has to be bolted onto the publish path per-type, re-introducing the very state-machine logic R1 makes first-class, but now scattered.; Replay-latest-on-subscribe has subtle semantics: does a brand-new fact subscriber get the retained Refreshed from a PRIOR generation? For multi-fire ContextRefreshed this can mis-fire one-time listeners (the exact Spring gotcha) — retention can AMPLIFY the multi-fire footgun rather than tame it.; Memory: a retained-last slot per lifecycle/availability type is small, but generalizing retention invites retaining MORE event types, and the bus now holds Arcs to last-values indefinitely (until overwritten) — a slow-creep heap/retention concern versus R1's single fixed-size cell.; Probe point-read becomes a typed lookup into the bus's retained store (a TypeId-keyed map read) rather than a direct cell borrow — slightly more indirection on the (cold) probe path, and it couples the probe handler to the bus's internal retained representation.

**Mechanisms considered:** retained-last (MQTT-retained / BehaviorSubject) semantics on the bus for a marked set of lifecycle/availability event types; replay-latest-on-subscribe so new subscribers (and late probes) immediately receive current state — unifying early-event buffering with state retention; `bus.latest::<ReadinessChanged>()` typed point-read for synchronous probe handlers (cold path); publish-as-write: availability changes are ordinary publishes carrying source + new state; per-type retained slot keyed by TypeId in the bus (interior-mutable, frozen-vs-locked tension); optional per-type transition validator on the publish path to approximate state-machine legality

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Heaviest coupling to the events/event-multicaster registry substrate because it asks that substrate to also own RETAINED-LAST storage — so its T1 is the events neighbour's T1 PLUS a retained-store ABI in leaf-core (the marked-retained type set, the latest() lookup). Listener/subscriber discovery across crates is identical to events' problem and inherits the same linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit candidates and the same silent-DCE self-check mandate (§152). One ADVANTAGE for T1: because state is replayed to late subscribers, a cross-crate health-export subscriber that registers late (or is discovered through a dynamic/WASM source after bring-up) still gets current state immediately — retention smooths over discovery-timing differences between the static link-time set and a dynamic directory-scanned source, which is a genuine plus for the dual-classpath duality. DISADVANTAGE: the retained store is a single mutable structure that, if a dynamic source can publish into it, must be writable post-bring-up — constraining any 'freeze after refresh' optimization the events neighbour might want. Origin-agnostic holds: a WASM component publishes a ReadinessChanged like any other publisher. Generics: retained types must be concrete (no static slot for a generic type) — macro hard-errors, consistent with the rest of leaf. Thin macros: emit only typed-event publish/subscribe descriptors; the retention marking is a const attribute on the type in leaf-core, hand-writable.

**T2 — execution context:** Publish/subscribe execution is exactly the events/event-multicaster contract (inline-on-caller's-task by default, async opt-in delegated to the multicaster). The retained-slot WRITE adds a per-type store update on the publish path: this must be a fine-grained, lock-light update (an atomic swap of an Arc<LastEvent> per type, or a sharded slot) under §2.5 — NOT a global bus lock, or every availability flip would contend the whole dispatch path (a real hot-path hazard this realization must engineer around). Replay-latest-on-subscribe runs on the subscribing task at registration (cold path) — a single delivery of the retained value before live updates begin. Probe point-read (`latest::<T>()`) is a cold-path lock-free read of the retained Arc (clone-the-Arc / borrow), no await. CANCELLATION/ERRORS follow the events/event-multicaster policy; the Spring 'Ready-listener failure fails startup' special case still requires the bring-up task to OBSERVE the result of dispatching the readiness/started milestone events and route a failure into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path — same as R1, but here it is observing an event dispatch rather than a fact dispatch. No async Drop: the retained store is dropped with the container on explicit close; there is no background task (unlike R1's projection bridge), which is a T2 simplification — no extra long-lived task to shut down. Reactive on hot paths (§2.4): subscribers are woken on publish; nothing polls.

#### R3 — Lifecycle as a first-class observable STATE MACHINE; facts and availability are derived projections (state-source-of-truth, events are views)
Invert R2: make the SINGLE SOURCE OF TRUTH an explicit, typed container LIFECYCLE STATE MACHINE, and treat BOTH the discrete facts AND the availability states as PROJECTIONS/views of that machine. The container owns one `ContainerLifecycle` value: a phase enum `Phase{ Created, Refreshing, Refreshed, Running, Stopping, Stopped, Closing, Closed, Failed{phase,err} }` PLUS the two orthogonal availability sub-states `liveness: LivenessState`, `readiness: ReadinessState`. This whole thing is a reactive observable (one cell holding the composite lifecycle state, change-notified). Transitions are DRIVEN by the lifecycle owners (refresh-lifecycle advances Created->Refreshing->Refreshed; runtime-lifecycle advances ->Running and ->Stopping->Stopped; context-close advances ->Closing->Closed; abort advances ->Failed) and are VALIDATED by the machine (illegal transitions are a loud error, not a silent mis-state — Closed is terminal, etc.). The bus is fed by a single PROJECTOR that watches the state machine and emits the appropriate typed event on each transition: entering Refreshed emits a `Refreshed` event (with generation), entering Running emits `Started`, a liveness change emits `LivenessChanged`, a readiness change emits `ReadinessChanged`, entering Closed emits `Closed`, entering Failed emits `StartupFailed` (and deliberately does NOT emit Refreshed/Closed — the cancel-vs-close distinction falls out of the state machine's transition table for free). Consumers choose their altitude: (a) probes/components SUBSCRIBE to or point-READ the state machine (current phase + liveness + readiness) — the reactive, queryable surface; (b) app code that wants the open/closed event style LISTENS for the projected facts on the bus. Availability writes are state-machine transitions a component requests via a handle (`lifecycle.request_readiness(REFUSING, source)`), validated and then projected. The state machine is the ONE thing; facts are its event-shaped shadow and availability is its queryable face.

**Pros:** Strongest model integrity and diagnostics (§2.9): ONE validated state machine means illegal/contradictory lifecycle states are impossible-by-construction (can't be Closed AND ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC; can't fire Refreshed after Failed) — the cancel-vs-close distinction, the Closed-is-terminal rule, and the started-before-ready ordering are encoded in the transition table, not in scattered publish calls, and a violation is a rich error naming the illegal edge.; Tames the multi-fire ContextRefreshed footgun structurally: the state machine carries a generation/epoch and KNOWS it is re-refreshing, so the projected Refreshed event carries that, and one-time-vs-every-time semantics are a property of HOW you consume the machine (latest phase vs the event stream) — the Spring gotcha becomes a documented, modeled choice.; Best fit for both consumption styles at once: k8s probes get a queryable/subscribable current-state surface (the state machine face), while app code gets the open/closed event-emission style (the projection) — neither is second-class, and they are provably consistent because both derive from one source.; Excellent introspection/legibility (§2.10) and unification (§2.11): 'the lifecycle' is ONE inspectable object a `leaf doctor`/startup-instrumentation can render as a phase+availability snapshot and a transition log; the same state-machine primitive could be reused for other phased state (it generalizes the runtime-lifecycle phase machine and the refresh-lifecycle state, hinting at shared machinery).; Memory-disciplined (§2.5): one composite state cell + one projector task; no per-type retained store (R2) and a single projection seam (cleaner than R1's two independent halves), so the smallest steady-state footprint of the three.

**Cons:** Most machinery and the boldest meta-architecture claim — building a first-class validated state machine as the source of truth, with the events as mere projections, edges toward DECLARING how lifecycle state is modeled framework-wide (it implicitly wants to subsume refresh-lifecycle's and runtime-lifecycle's own state), which risks overstepping the per-feature guardrail; the state-machine-as-keystone is arguably a Phase-2 decision.; Couples lifecycle-events tightly to the EXACT phase models of refresh-lifecycle, runtime-lifecycle, and context-close (it encodes their transitions in one table) — if those neighbours' phase shapes shift in Phase 2/3, this central machine must be re-cut; it is the least independently-stable of the three.; The single composite state cell mixes orthogonal axes (bring-up phase vs liveness vs readiness): readiness can flip REFUSING/ACCEPTING WHILE Running, independently of liveness — modeling these as one machine risks either an explosion of composite states or an awkward 'phase plus two side-channels' that isn't really one clean machine, undermining the 'one state machine' elegance.; The projector (state-change -> event) is the same always-on coherence seam R1 has, plus it now carries MORE responsibility (every transition type -> right event type), so the projection-correctness burden is higher, and a missed projection means a fact silently never fires.; Heaviest to specify and the widest stable ABI (the phase enum, the transition table, the availability sub-states, the projection mapping all become public surface) — and pre-committing the transition table risks baking in Spring-shaped phases (initMessageSource-era orderings) that a rust-native bring-up may not have, the same JVM-lineage hazard refresh-lifecycle flags.

**Mechanisms considered:** one composite reactive lifecycle state cell: Phase enum + LivenessState + ReadinessState as the single source of truth; validated transition table (Closed terminal, Failed excludes Refreshed/Closed, started-before-ready) encoding cancel-vs-close and ordering rules structurally; a single projector task watching the state machine and emitting the matching typed event per transition (facts + availability changes are projections); point-read + subscribe on the state machine for probes/components; listen-for-projected-facts on the bus for app code; generation/epoch in the machine to model multi-refresh and carry it into the projected Refreshed event; handle-driven transition requests (request_readiness/request_liveness) validated by the machine before projection; reuse of one phased-state-machine primitive potentially shared with runtime-lifecycle / refresh-lifecycle (unification candidate)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The state machine is a single owned object in leaf-core (not a registry), so its core has NO cross-crate discovery problem and NO DCE hazard for the state itself — a strength. The cross-crate surface is purely on the CONSUMER side: components that request transitions (DB-pool guardian flipping readiness) acquire a handle by injection (no link-time registration, DCE-safe), and listeners for the PROJECTED facts inherit the events neighbour's discovery substrate and its silent-DCE self-check mandate (§152) exactly as R1/R2. leaf-core hosts the Phase enum, availability enums, the transition table, the handle, and the projection mapping (a wide but stable ABI). Origin-agnostic: a WASM-module guardian requests transitions through the same handle; a WASM-contributed health exporter subscribes to the state machine or the projected events. Dual-classpath duality applies only to projected-fact subscribers (static link-time + optional dynamic source), as a CANDIDATE; the state machine is singular. The strongest T1 caveat is internal-coupling rather than cross-crate: because the transition table encodes the phase models of three neighbour features, the leaf-core that owns it must depend on (or re-declare) their phase vocabularies — a workspace-internal coupling to manage, not a discovery problem. Generics: facts/availability are concrete types; macro hard-errors on generics. Thin macros: any sugar emits only handle calls / event-subscription descriptors against absolute paths; the state machine and transition table are hand-constructible normal code.

**T2 — execution context:** The state machine is advanced on the COLD lifecycle paths (bring-up, runtime start/stop, close) by the owning neighbour tasks; each transition is a validated, in-memory state update on the bootstrap/shutdown task (no await needed for the transition itself; validation is sync and cheap). Availability transition REQUESTS from arbitrary components run on those components' own tasks and are applied to the cell under fine-grained no-global-lock discipline (§2.5 — an atomic/seqlock-shaped composite-state update, or a tiny per-cell lock held only for the swap). The projector runs as ONE long-lived container-scoped task awaiting state changes and publishing the matching event inline (reactive, §2.4 — woken on change, never polling); it is shut down explicitly on close (no async Drop — §nightly). Probe READS are cold-path lock-free snapshots of the composite state (phase+liveness+readiness) — no await, no poll; a probe SERVER polls on its own external cadence but each internal read is a point read. CANCELLATION/ERRORS: an invalid transition request is a synchronous rich error to the requester (does not corrupt the cell); the projector's published events follow the events/event-multicaster error policy. The Spring 'Ready-listener failure fails startup' intent maps to: the bring-up task drives the machine into Running/Ready, the projector publishes Started/ReadinessChanged, and the bring-up task OBSERVES that dispatch's result — a failure routes into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path and transitions the machine to Failed (which by the transition table suppresses any Refreshed/Closed projection). On close the machine is driven to Closing and readiness is transitioned to REFUSING_TRAFFIC FIRST (quiesce) before runtime-lifecycle stop and destruction — the ordering is an explicit edge in the table, not emergent.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's lifecycle facts ride the type-erased reflective bus keyed by runtime Class; leaf must publish them as TYPED events through whatever the events/event-multicaster neighbours land on. Each fact (Refreshed/Started/Stopped/Closed/Failed) becomes a distinct Rust type selected by TypeId/typed channel (events-R1) or an erased adapter (events-R2) — there is no single Object bus and no reflective listener scan. This is not a worsening: typed facts give compile-time-checked listeners, but it means lifecycle-events cannot assume a single erased bus and must be agnostic to the bus shape the events neighbour chooses.
- Availability is a RETAINED, queryable, last-write-wins STATE in Spring (the ApplicationAvailability bean holds current liveness/readiness), but Spring's events are one-shot facts on a fire-and-forget bus. Rust has no implicit retained-event/BehaviorSubject; leaf must MAKE the retention explicit — either a separate reactive state-cell (R1/R3) or a retained-last bus capability (R2). The clash: 'current readiness for a probe at time T' cannot be reconstructed from a fire-and-forget typed-event publish; it needs a deliberately-modeled state holder. This is the central new-machinery question this feature adds beyond plain events.
- Probe reads want CURRENT state synchronously, but leaf is async-first (§2.3) and reactive-on-hot-paths (§2.4). A k8s readiness probe handler must answer 'are you ready NOW' — modeled as a cold-path point READ of a reactive cell (lock-free current-value snapshot), with subscription as the reactive change-driven path. The clash with Spring: Spring reads a ThreadLocal-free shared bean field synchronously; leaf must define this as an explicit cold-path read of a watch/seqlock-shaped cell (the reactive primitive itself being a Phase-2 async-execution decision), and must ensure the probe path neither blocks nor busy-polls internally.
- Spring's 'AvailabilityChange rides the same bus' dogfooding is literally one publish; in leaf, if availability is a separate state-cell (R1/R3) the dogfooding requires an explicit PROJECTION bridge (state-change -> published event) that must stay coherent (exactly one event per transition). If instead state lives ON the bus as retained-last (R2), it pushes stateful retention into a dispatch engine the events/event-multicaster neighbours may want to keep stateless — so 'same bus' has no zero-cost rust-native realization: you pay either a projection seam or a stateful-bus capability.
- ContextRefreshedEvent CAN FIRE MORE THAN ONCE (refreshable contexts), and a 'replay latest' or naive retained-event design AMPLIFIES the one-time-listener footgun rather than taming it. Spring's own guidance is 'guard yourself or prefer ApplicationStarted/Ready'. In leaf this must be modeled explicitly — a generation/epoch on Refreshed (R1/R3) or a careful retention-replay policy (R2) — so a one-time listener can distinguish first-refresh from re-refresh; the JVM gives no help here and Rust gives none either, so it is pure modeling.
- Spring's earliest events (Starting, EnvironmentPrepared) fire BEFORE the context/bus exists, via a standalone initial multicaster, and CANNOT be hosted beans. In leaf there is no implicit pre-context bus; the pre-context facts need either a standalone mini-bus or an early-fact buffer drained when the real bus is initialized (the registerListeners-flush analogue). The clash: leaf cannot rely on listener beans for pre-bring-up facts and must provide a non-bean early registration path — entangled with refresh-lifecycle's early-event-buffering and the events neighbour's early-buffer design.
- 'An ApplicationReady-listener failure can FAIL startup' (routed through run-failure handling, not merely logged) is a DELIBERATE break from the bus's normal isolate-or-log behavior. In leaf this means the bring-up task must OBSERVE the dispatch result of the started/ready milestone specifically and route a failure into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path (-> Failed state, no Refreshed/Closed fact), whereas ordinary lifecycle-fact listener failures follow the multicaster's default error policy. The error-propagation contract is thus NOT uniform across facts — a special case that must be explicit, not emergent.
- No async Drop (a first-class leaf constraint): the close fact and the readiness=REFUSING_TRAFFIC transition that must happen FIRST on shutdown (quiesce before stop/destroy) cannot be triggered by a Drop impl on the container. The ordered close sequence (flip readiness -> publish Closed -> stop runtime-lifecycle -> destroy) must be an explicit container-driven async shutdown step, and any projection bridge / projector task must be explicitly shut down — there is no implicit teardown. Also mirrors the cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction: a failed bring-up must NOT publish Closed (only Failed), and must not run the clean quiesce sequence.
- Spring smuggles the caller's transaction/security context through ThreadLocals on synchronous dispatch; leaf has no implicit thread-locals across .await. Sync-default fact dispatch must be 'inline on the caller's task via awaits' (delegated to events/event-multicaster), and any async health-export listener that hops threads needs explicit context-propagation (the context-propagation neighbour). For lifecycle facts this matters less than for app events (facts carry simple data), but the availability-write path from an arbitrary component still must not assume ambient thread-local context survives to the projector/subscribers.
- The composite of bring-up PHASE (Refreshed/Running/Closed) and the two ORTHOGONAL availability axes (liveness, readiness) does not collapse cleanly into one JVM-style flat event log: readiness flips independently while Running, liveness is about restart-vs-not, phase is about bring-up/teardown. Spring keeps them as separate event types + a separate availability bean. A leaf design that tries to unify them into one state machine (R3) risks a state explosion or an awkward 'phase plus two side-channels'; one that keeps them as separate typed events (R1/R2) must still define their interactions (Closed forces REFUSING; Failed forces BROKEN-or-unknown) explicitly.

### Interactions
- **events** — UPSTREAM — the publish/subscribe MODEL and listener authoring this feature's FACTS use. Lifecycle facts (Refreshed/Started/Stopped/Closed/Failed) and projected availability changes are published via the events publisher handle and consumed by app/probe code via the events listener model (typed listener or POJO #[event_listener]). lifecycle-events defines the CATALOG of fact types and WHO publishes WHICH at WHICH milestone; events defines what a listener is and how it's selected. This feature is the marquee first-party PUBLISHER dogfooding the bus, and inherits events' typed-vs-erased bus shape, early-event buffering, hierarchy double-delivery, and cross-crate listener discovery.
- **event-multicaster** — UPSTREAM — the dispatch SEAM facts flow through. The sync-by-default-on-caller's-task dispatch, opt-in async, per-dispatch error handling/isolation, and ordering all belong to the multicaster; lifecycle-events merely routes fact publication through it. CRITICAL coupling: the Spring 'ApplicationReady-listener failure can fail startup' intent means the milestone dispatch for started/ready needs a NON-default error treatment (observe-and-fail-startup) that the multicaster's error-policy must be able to express — surfaced as a phase2 question shared with event-multicaster.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — PRIMARY TRIGGER for bring-up facts. refresh-lifecycle's finish-refresh phase publishes the Refreshed fact (the ContextRefreshedEvent analogue) and drives the started/liveness transition; its cancel path publishes Failed (StartupFailed) and DELIBERATELY NOT Refreshed/Closed (the cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction). refresh-lifecycle also owns the phase that initializes the bus/multicaster and DRAINS the early-fact buffer (the registerListeners-flush analogue) — so the early pre-context facts this feature defines are flushed at a refresh-lifecycle phase. The 'Ready-listener failure fails startup' path routes back into refresh-lifecycle's abort.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — PRIMARY TRIGGER for runtime facts. runtime-lifecycle's phased auto-start publishes the Started fact (ContextStartedEvent) after SmartLifecycle beans start by ascending phase; an explicit stop publishes Stopped. The readiness=ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC transition is timed AFTER start completes (and after runners, per the k8s window). On graceful stop, runtime-lifecycle quiesces while readiness has already flipped to REFUSING_TRAFFIC. lifecycle-events defines the facts/state; runtime-lifecycle fires them at its start/stop milestones.
- **context-close** — TRIGGER for the Closed fact and the ordered quiesce. context-close's clean shutdown flips readiness to REFUSING_TRAFFIC FIRST, publishes the Closed fact (ContextClosedEvent), then stops Lifecycle beans (runtime-lifecycle), then destroys singletons — the stop-before-destroy / quiesce intent. lifecycle-events defines Closed + the close-time availability transition; context-close fires them in order. A FAILED bring-up (refresh-lifecycle cancel) does NOT enter this clean path — no Closed fact, only Failed — the distinction this feature must model (R3 encodes it in the transition table).
- **smart-initializing** — TIMING SIBLING for the Refreshed fact. Spring's ContextRefreshedEvent and SmartInitializingSingleton.afterSingletonsInstantiated() are the two sanctioned lock-free post-all-singletons-exist hooks for expensive cross-bean work; both run after every eager singleton exists and OUTSIDE any creation lock. lifecycle-events' Refreshed fact fires at the same boundary smart-initializing's callback does (end of eager instantiation) — they are alternative hooks for the same 'graph is complete' moment, and the docs steer heavy work to one of them rather than @PostConstruct.
- **context-propagation** — Required to preserve ambient context (tracing/locale/transaction) across an ASYNC fact listener or async health-export subscriber thread hop. Sync-default fact dispatch keeps context by running inline on the caller's task; async export needs context-propagation's capture/restore SPI. Also relevant to the availability WRITE path: a component flipping readiness from its own task must not assume thread-local context reaches the projector/subscribers — context enters explicitly, never via a thread-local across .await.
- **task-execution** — Backs any OPT-IN ASYNC dispatch of lifecycle facts and async health exporters (via event-multicaster). 'Run this lifecycle/availability listener somehow' delegates pool/virtual-thread choice to task-execution; the projector task (R1/R3) and any async availability subscriber run on a task-execution-provided executor. The shared caveat set (async changes error/ordering semantics) applies.
- **container-hierarchy** — In Spring a child context's event also reaches ancestor listeners (double-delivery); lifecycle facts published in a child are delivered to parent listeners too, and a listener registered in multiple contexts can fire multiple times — disambiguate by comparing the originating container. lifecycle-events' facts carry a ContainerId precisely so a listener can tell which container's milestone it observed; the publish path walks self-then-parent per container-hierarchy's one-directional parent link (a behavior owned by events but exercised heavily by lifecycle facts).
- **container-core** — Decides whether the lifecycle PUBLISHER and the availability state-holder live only on the context FAÇADE (Spring's deliberate ApplicationContext-only split — never the bare engine) or are attachable capabilities, and whether the publisher/AvailabilityHandle are resolvable infrastructure dependencies (injectable like ApplicationContext). lifecycle-events' facts are a context-services concern container-core layers on; a bare engine emits no lifecycle facts (the plain-BeanFactory parity).
- **startup-instrumentation** — COMPLEMENTARY observation surface. startup-instrumentation records a TREE of timed named startup steps (opt-in, no-op by default) for slow-boot diagnosis; lifecycle-events publishes DISCRETE milestone facts on the bus for app/probe reaction. They observe the same bring-up from two angles: instrumentation = internal timed taxonomy; lifecycle facts = external reactive milestones. A leaf doctor / introspection surface can render both (the timed step tree AND the current lifecycle/availability state vector).
- **lazy-initialization** — Source of the classic 'my listener never fires' gotcha: a @Lazy bean's lifecycle/@EventListener methods are silently never registered. For lifecycle facts this is acute — a lazy health-export bean listening for AvailabilityChanged would silently never update probes. lifecycle-events must surface (via the startup self-check) whether a lazy bean's lifecycle listeners are still registered (diverging from Spring to fix the gotcha) or honored-lazy (matching Spring) — shared with the events/refresh-lifecycle/smart-initializing decision.
- **conditions** — Availability/health export and lifecycle reactions are often CONDITIONAL (present only when an actuator/health crate or a k8s integration is on the dependency graph). The availability MODEL itself is core (Spring's deliberate 'availability is core, not Actuator'), but the PROBE EXPORT (HTTP endpoints, k8s adapters) is a conditional integration that backs off when absent — so the core publishes liveness/readiness unconditionally and the export layer attaches via conditions/auto-configuration.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is availability STATE modeled as a separate reactive state-cell (R1/R3) or as retained-last events ON the bus (R2)? This is the core fork for THIS feature and it is entangled with the events/event-multicaster type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone: a retained-last bus pushes stateful per-type storage into a dispatch engine that may want to stay stateless, while a separate cell needs leaf's not-yet-chosen reactive-cell primitive. Cannot be settled per-feature — it depends on whether the bus is typed-channels, erased, or hybrid AND on the async-execution reactive-primitive decision.
- What is leaf's runtime-agnostic reactive STATE primitive (the watch/BehaviorSubject/seqlock-shaped cell) that backs availability current-value reads + change subscription WITHOUT polling on the hot path (§2.4) and WITHOUT a global lock (§2.5)? This belongs to the async-execution & context-propagation meta-concept and is shared with any other feature needing observable shared state (scopes, runtime-lifecycle running-state). The probe-read path and the projector both depend on its shape.
- How is the Spring 'ApplicationReady-listener failure FAILS startup' special case realized — a non-default per-dispatch error policy on the multicaster that the bring-up task observes and routes into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path? This shares the unified error/diagnostics meta-concept (§2.9) and the event-multicaster error-policy question: lifecycle-fact dispatch needs a NON-uniform error contract (some milestone listener failures abort startup; ordinary ones isolate), which must be expressible in whatever error model Phase 2 picks.
- Where do the lifecycle PUBLISHER and the AvailabilityHandle live — context-façade-only (Spring's deliberate ApplicationContext-only split) or attachable capability — and are they resolvable infrastructure dependencies? Shares the container-core BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext decision; many neighbours assume the bus + availability are always present on the context.
- How are PRE-CONTEXT early facts (Starting/EnvironmentPrepared analogues) emitted and buffered before the bus exists — a standalone pre-context mini-bus, an early-fact buffer drained at a refresh-lifecycle phase, or (in R2) the same retained-latest mechanism that handles late subscribers? Shares refresh-lifecycle's early-event-buffering and the events neighbour's early-buffer design; the answer determines whether pre-bring-up extension hooks are first-class.
- Does the multi-fire ContextRefreshed reality get modeled with a generation/epoch on the Refreshed fact, and is the one-time-vs-every-time consumption a documented choice — and how does that interact with any retained-latest replay (R2 risks replaying a stale Refreshed to new subscribers, amplifying the footgun)? Cross-cuts the events listener model and the retention decision.
- Should the THREE axes — bring-up phase, liveness, readiness — be ONE composite state machine (R3, strong integrity but risks state-explosion and over-claiming a Phase-2 keystone) or separate typed events/states with explicitly-defined interactions (R1/R2)? This edges toward whether leaf has a shared phased-state-machine primitive reused by refresh-lifecycle and runtime-lifecycle — a unification (§2.11) question that cannot be decided for lifecycle-events alone.
- Does a lazy bean still register its lifecycle/availability listeners (diverging from Spring to fix the silent 'listener never fires' gotcha, especially dangerous for a health exporter) or honor laziness (matching Spring)? Cross-cuts events, refresh-lifecycle, smart-initializing, and lazy-initialization, and must be reconciled with the cross-crate silent-DCE self-check so a never-updating probe is a loud diagnostic, not a silent miss.
- How do dynamic/WASM-module-contributed availability writers and lifecycle-fact subscribers slot in (the origin-agnostic + dual-classpath guardrail), given that the availability STATE is a single owned cell (no classpath duality) while the SUBSCRIBER side inherits the static-link-time-plus-optional-dynamic-source duality? A candidate, not a decision — interacts with the events discovery substrate and the origin-agnostic-provider guardrail.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated lifecycle-events section (1873-1913: the four context facts incl. the multi-fire ContextRefreshed and Closed-is-terminal, the Boot ordered run sequence 1887, the hidden concepts 1899-1909 incl. 'AvailabilityChange rides the same bus' 1908 and 'Ready-listener failure can fail startup' 1907 and 'lazy beans lose @EventListener' 1901 and 'multicaster magic name' 1902 and 'hierarchy double-delivery' 1903 and 'context-propagation for async listeners' 1909), the Boot run sequence + the 'availability is core not Actuator' hidden concept (264-285), the precise k8s runner window (307: runners after started+LivenessState.CORRECT but before ready+ACCEPTING_TRAFFIC), the refresh sequence that fires ContextRefreshed + the cancelRefresh-vs-doClose distinction (340-372), the runtime Lifecycle/SmartLifecycle layer that fires Started/Stopped + graceful stop-before-destroy (378-413, 2720-2731), and the concurrency contract (1918-1944). The Spring API is explicitly stable/unchanged in v7/Boot4 (1796), so the parity target is solid.\n\nScope discipline: I kept THIS feature to (a) the CATALOG of lifecycle facts, (b) the availability STATE model (liveness/readiness as retained, queryable, transition-validated state with an event projection), and (c) WHO publishes WHICH fact at WHICH milestone. I deliberately pushed the bus engine/dispatch/error-isolation to events + event-multicaster, the phase boundaries that TRIGGER facts to refresh-lifecycle / runtime-lifecycle / context-close, the per-listener authoring model to events, and the timed-step taxonomy to startup-instrumentation. The single most important rust-native insight is that Spring's lifecycle-events is really TWO things on one bus, and the availability half is NOT a fire-and-forget fact — it is a RETAINED, queryable, last-write-wins STATE that probes read at arbitrary later times and that arbitrary components mutate at runtime. That state-vs-fact duality IS my divergence axis: R1 splits them (typed facts on the bus + a separate reactive availability cell + one projection bridge — closest to Spring's own AvailabilityState-bean-plus-AvailabilityChangeEvent reality); R2 unifies via retained-last events on the bus (one mechanism, but pushes stateful retention into a possibly-stateless dispatch engine and amplifies the multi-fire footgun); R3 makes a first-class validated lifecycle STATE MACHINE the source of truth with facts+availability as projections (strongest integrity and diagnostics, structurally encodes cancel-vs-close and Closed-terminal, but the most machinery and edges toward a Phase-2 keystone). They are genuinely divergent on the state-representation axis yet Phase-2-composable.\n\nThree things to carry forward: (1) availability needs a RETAINED queryable state holder + a reactive subscription path (probe point-read = cold-path read per §2.4; never a hot-path poll) — this is the new machinery beyond plain events, and the reactive-cell primitive is a Phase-2 async-execution decision I refused to pick. (2) The error contract for lifecycle facts is NON-UNIFORM: ordinary fact-listener failures isolate, but the started/ready milestone follows Spring's 'a Ready-listener failure FAILS startup' and must route into refresh-lifecycle's cancel path — surfaced as a phase2 question shared with event-multicaster. (3) The cancel-vs-close distinction (a failed bring-up publishes Failed, NOT Refreshed/Closed, and skips the clean quiesce) plus NO ASYNC DROP means the ordered close sequence (flip readiness=REFUSING first -> publish Closed -> stop runtime-lifecycle -> destroy) and the projector/bridge shutdown must be EXPLICIT container-driven steps; R3 encodes the cancel-vs-close suppression in its transition table for free.\n\nI refused to settle the bus shape (inherited from events), the reactive-state primitive, the error model, or whether the three axes are one state machine. The dual-classpath / origin-agnostic guardrail applies asymmetrically here and I flagged it: the availability STATE is a single owned cell (no classpath duality, and writers acquire a handle by injection so the WRITE path is DCE-safe), while the SUBSCRIBER side inherits the events neighbour's static-link-time-plus-optional-dynamic-source duality and its mandatory silent-DCE self-check (a DCE-dropped health-export subscriber = a probe that silently never updates, indistinguishable from a quiet probe — non-negotiable to self-check). Names used (Refreshed/Started/Stopped/Closed/StartupFailed, LivenessState/ReadinessState, AvailabilityHandle, PublisherHandle, ContainerLifecycle/Phase, projector/projection bridge, ContainerId, generation/epoch) are Phase-1 placeholders only. I wrote no files.
