## Transaction-Bound Event Listeners  `[transactional-events]`

This feature is Spring's CANONICAL PROOF that the event model is EXTENSIBLE without modifying it: `@TransactionalEventListener` (Spring §1891-1897, in `spring-tx`) is a meta-specialization of `@EventListener` that DEFERS the listener's invocation to a TRANSACTION PHASE rather than firing it inline at publish. The whole point the brief asks me to replicate is INTENT-as-illustration: a new listener KIND is built ENTIRELY on top of the existing listener-adapter layer (the `events` neighbour's seam) plus the transaction-synchronization hooks (the `transaction-management` neighbour's seam) — I add NO new dispatch engine, NO new bus; I add ONE new adapter behavior that registers a synchronization on the active transaction and fires the listener at the chosen phase.\n\nThe precise mechanics to replicate (intent, not mechanism): (1) FOUR PHASES — BEFORE_COMMIT (run while the tx is still open, before flush/commit; a listener error here can still abort the commit), AFTER_COMMIT (the DEFAULT; run only if the tx committed successfully — the safe place to do externally-visible side effects like sending an email or enqueuing a message, because the data is durably committed), AFTER_ROLLBACK (run only if the tx rolled back), AFTER_COMPLETION (run on either outcome, carrying the outcome). (2) BINDS TO THE CURRENT TRANSACTION'S SYNCHRONIZATION — at publish time, if a transaction is active, the listener invocation is NOT run inline; instead a synchronization callback is registered on the active tx, captured with the event, and the multicaster's actual dispatch is deferred until the tx reaches the chosen phase boundary. (3) NO-ACTIVE-TRANSACTION BEHAVIOR — if there is NO active transaction when the event is published, the listener is SILENTLY SKIPPED (does nothing) UNLESS `fallbackExecution=true`, in which case it runs inline like a normal `@EventListener` (§1897: this coupling, via `@EventListener#defaultExecution`→`fallbackExecution` default-false, is WHY a transactional listener baffingly no-ops outside a tx — leaf must make this LOUD/diagnosable, not silent, per charter §2.9). (4) REACTIVE-TX SUPPORT (Spring 6.1+) — because the reactive transaction manager carries tx context in the Reactor `Context` not `ThreadLocal`, the event must carry the tx context (the `TransactionalEventPublisher` analogue); this maps DIRECTLY to leaf's async-first world where there is no ThreadLocal across `.await` and the tx resource lives on the LOGICAL TASK (the `transaction-management` + `context-propagation` neighbours own that primitive). (5) IT IS STILL AN `@EventListener` — same authoring model (a POJO method on a managed bean whose param selects the event), same condition filtering, same generic targeting, same ordering — it ONLY changes WHEN/WHETHER the listener fires relative to the tx lifecycle.\n\nWhat I OWN (Phase-1, this feature): the `#[transactional_event_listener(phase = ..., fallback = ...)]` attribute DATA model (a meta-specialization of the `events` neighbour's `#[event_listener]`); the PhaseAwareAdapter that wraps the underlying listener and, at dispatch, either registers a tx-synchronization or runs the fallback/skip path; the binding of the deferred (event, listener) pair to the active transaction's synchronization registry (driven by `transaction-management`'s phase hooks); and the no-tx skip-vs-fallback policy with its diagnostic. What I DO NOT own and explicitly BUILD ON: the publish/subscribe model, listener authoring, condition/generic/ordering/chaining semantics, and the multicaster dispatch seam (all `events` + `event-multicaster`); the TransactionManager, the open/commit/rollback lifecycle, the resource-binding-to-execution-context primitive, and the synchronization-hook protocol (BEFORE_COMMIT/AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION registration + firing — all `transaction-management`); the ambient-context-across-.await primitive (`context-propagation`). The deep Rust tension: Spring binds the deferred listener to a `ThreadLocal` `TransactionSynchronizationManager`, fires synchronizations on the committing thread, and re-discovers the listener reflectively — NONE of which leaf has. leaf must (a) bind the deferred listener to the TASK-scoped transaction (not a thread), (b) model the phase as DATA on a const descriptor the `events` macro already emits, (c) re-route the multicaster's would-be-inline dispatch into a tx-synchronization the `transaction-management` neighbour fires at the phase boundary ON THE SAME LOGICAL TASK, and (d) make the silent-no-op-without-tx a loud, diagnosable, intentional choice.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Phase-aware listener adapter: deferral is a property of the listener descriptor; the multicaster re-routes its inline dispatch into a tx-synchronization (faithful TransactionalEventListener port)
The most literal intent-port and the cleanest 'built ON TOP of the event model' illustration. `#[transactional_event_listener(phase = AfterCommit, fallback = false)]` is a THIN meta-specialization of the `events` neighbour's `#[event_listener]`: it emits the SAME const listener Descriptor that `#[event_listener]` emits (bean TypeId, event TypeId, order, optional condition fn-ptr, async factory fn-ptr) PLUS one extra const field `defer: Option<TxDeferral { phase: TxPhase, fallback: bool }>`. A normal `#[event_listener]` leaves `defer = None`; a transactional one sets it. So the listener lands in EXACTLY the same typed-channel-or-erased store the `events` neighbour already built (whichever of events-R1/R2/R3 wins) — NO new registry, NO new bus, which is the whole point. The deferral is realized at DISPATCH: when the `event-multicaster` is about to invoke a listener whose Descriptor has `defer = Some`, instead of awaiting it inline it consults the CURRENT execution context for an active transaction (via `transaction-management`'s 'is a tx active on this task' query). Two paths: (a) ACTIVE TX → it does NOT invoke the listener; it registers a tx-SYNCHRONIZATION on the active transaction (using `transaction-management`'s synchronization-registration hook), capturing the event value (moved/Arc'd into the synchronization) + the listener handle + the phase; when the tx reaches that phase boundary (BEFORE_COMMIT before flush, AFTER_COMMIT only on success, AFTER_ROLLBACK only on rollback, AFTER_COMPLETION always-with-outcome), `transaction-management` fires the registered synchronizations and THAT is when the deferred listener future is finally awaited — on the same logical task that owns the tx. (b) NO ACTIVE TX → policy from the descriptor: `fallback = true` runs the listener inline NOW (degrades to a plain sync listener); `fallback = false` SKIPS it — but unlike Spring's silent no-op, leaf emits a structured diagnostic-grade trace event ('transactional listener X for event E skipped: no active transaction; set fallback=true to run regardless') so the baffling-no-op (§1897) becomes observable (charter §2.9). The phase value (`TxPhase` enum) and `fallback` are pure DATA; the macro carries zero logic. The PhaseAwareAdapter (the rerouting logic) lives in leaf-core, shared by all listeners, parameterized by the descriptor's `defer` field — it is the explicit, hand-writable analogue of Spring's `ApplicationListenerMethodAdapter` + `TransactionalApplicationListenerMethodAdapter` split. BEFORE_COMMIT listener errors propagate into the tx outcome (can abort the commit); AFTER_* listener errors are isolated/routed to the multicaster's error handler (they cannot un-commit a committed tx) — a per-phase error contract.

**Pros:** PUREST illustration of the brief's intent — 'a new phase-aware listener built ENTIRELY on top of @EventListener': it adds ONE data field to the existing Descriptor and ONE rerouting branch in the existing multicaster, reusing the events neighbour's whole authoring/condition/generic/ordering machinery and the transaction-management neighbour's whole synchronization protocol. Maximal unification (charter §2.11); this feature is a thin consumer of two seams, not a parallel subsystem.; Thin macro (charter §2.10): `#[transactional_event_listener]` emits the same const Descriptor as `#[event_listener]` plus a const `TxDeferral`; `cargo expand` shows an ordinary listener descriptor with a phase enum — fully hand-writable, and the programmatic escape hatch is to set `defer` on a descriptor directly or register the synchronization by hand.; Async-first and context-honest: deferral binds the listener to the TASK-scoped transaction (not a thread), so the deferred future fires on the SAME logical task that owns the tx resource — the rust-native realization of Spring's 6.1 reactive-tx 'carry tx context in the event' fix, achieved natively because leaf already binds tx to the task (transaction-management/context-propagation own that primitive).; Converts Spring's silent-no-op footgun (§1897) into a LOUD, diagnosable, intentional behavior (charter §2.9): the skip path emits a structured trace; a startup self-check can warn 'transactional listener exists but no transaction-management capability is enabled', catching the 'my listener never fires' class of bug far earlier than Spring.; Per-phase error contract is explicit (charter §2.3): BEFORE_COMMIT errors feed the tx outcome (can abort), AFTER_* errors are isolated (cannot un-commit), AFTER_COMPLETION carries the outcome — the cancellation/error propagation is designed, not emergent.

**Cons:** Requires the `event-multicaster` dispatch loop to GROW a deferral branch ('if descriptor.defer is Some and a tx is active, register a synchronization instead of awaiting') — so this feature is NOT purely additive at the descriptor level; it needs a cooperating hook IN the multicaster, coupling it to whichever events-R1/R2/R3 bus shape Phase 2 picks (the deferral branch must work identically for typed channels and the erased fallback).; The captured event must be MOVED/cloned into the synchronization that outlives the publish call (the publisher's `publish(e).await` returns BEFORE the deferred listener runs at commit) — so the event must be `Send + 'static` (it rides a synchronization stored on the tx and fired later, possibly after a worker hop), and a non-Clone event consumed by a deferred listener forces an Arc-wrap or a move — a heap/ownership cost the inline path doesn't pay, and a constraint on what events can be transactionally listened to.; Inherits transaction-management's no-async-Drop cancellation hazard at one remove: if the tx is cancelled/dropped between publish and the phase boundary, the registered synchronization's deferred listener must NOT fire for AFTER_COMMIT (correct — no commit happened) and the captured event/listener must be reclaimed without an async destructor — so the synchronization registry's teardown rides transaction-management's explicit (non-Drop) finalization.; Ordering across deferred + non-deferred listeners for the same event is subtle: a plain listener fires inline at publish, a BEFORE_COMMIT listener fires later at commit, an AFTER_COMMIT later still — so @Order is only meaningful WITHIN a phase, not across the publish-vs-commit timeline; this must be documented (a user expecting @Order to interleave a plain and a transactional listener is surprised), and the multicaster must group-by-phase before applying @Order.; Depends on transaction-management exposing a synchronization-registration + phase-firing protocol that itself is Phase-1-sketched-not-decided (its R1/R2/R3 all mention 'synchronization hooks the interceptor drives' but the exact protocol shape is open) — so this realization's correctness is gated on a neighbour's deferred protocol.

**Mechanisms considered:** `#[transactional_event_listener(phase, fallback)]` as a thin meta-specialization of `#[event_listener]`, emitting the SAME const listener Descriptor + one const `TxDeferral { phase: TxPhase, fallback: bool }` field (defer=None for plain listeners); PhaseAwareAdapter in leaf-core: a shared rerouting wrapper parameterized by the descriptor's `defer` field — the explicit analogue of Spring's TransactionalApplicationListenerMethodAdapter, hand-writable; multicaster deferral branch: at dispatch, if `defer = Some` AND a tx is active on the task → register a tx-synchronization capturing (event, listener handle, phase) instead of awaiting inline; tx-synchronization registration + phase-firing protocol BORROWED from transaction-management (BEFORE_COMMIT/AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION boundaries fire registered synchronizations on the same logical task); no-tx policy as DATA: fallback=true → run inline now; fallback=false → skip + emit a structured diagnostic trace (loud, not silent); TxPhase as a plain enum carried as const data; per-phase error contract (BEFORE_COMMIT errors abort the tx; AFTER_* errors isolated to the multicaster error handler); event captured into the synchronization as Send + 'static (Arc-wrap or move) so it outlives the publish call

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** A `#[transactional_event_listener]` in a downstream/sibling crate must reach the context exactly like any `#[event_listener]` — it rides the SAME descriptor-collection substrate the `events` neighbour picks (linkme distributed-slice default / inventory / build-time codegen / explicit), with the SAME Layer-0 unreferenced-crate-drop + --gc-sections silent-empty hazards and the SAME non-negotiable startup self-check (a transactional listener silently DCE-dropped is the worst case — a side effect that was supposed to fire after commit silently never fires, e.g. a confirmation email never sent). This feature adds NO new cross-crate registration vehicle; the `defer` field is just extra const data on the existing Descriptor, so it inherits the events substrate wholesale (a T1 advantage — nothing new to make coherent). The descriptor types (Descriptor, TxDeferral, TxPhase) live in leaf-core/leaf-framework for ABI stability; the macro emits absolute `::leaf_core::` paths and holds zero logic, hard-erroring on generic event types that can't be const-keyed and on a phase value that isn't a known TxPhase variant. CRITICAL feature-specific T1 hazard: a transactional listener is USELESS without the `transaction-management` capability AND a TransactionManager being wired — so the startup self-check must additionally assert 'if any descriptor has defer=Some, the tx-synchronization protocol is available', failing/warning loudly if a transactional listener crate is linked but transaction management was never enabled (the 'baffling no-op' becomes a startup diagnostic, not a runtime mystery). The dual-classpath duality applies cleanly: a static link-time transactional listener is the default 'classpath'; a dynamic/WASM-module-contributed transactional listener feeds the SAME descriptor API with defer=Some — origin-agnostic, BUT a WASM-guest listener deferred to AFTER_COMMIT means the guest-side side effect fires across the WIT boundary at the commit phase, and the captured event must marshal across the boundary into the guest at firing time (a real marshalling question for the dynamic half).

**T2 — execution context:** Async-first, and the execution-context story is the heart of this feature. PUBLISH is unchanged: `publish<E>(e).await` routes through the multicaster as in the events neighbour. The divergence is at DISPATCH of a deferred listener: instead of awaiting the listener inline on the publisher's task, the multicaster registers a tx-synchronization and RETURNS — so the publisher's `publish(e).await` completes WITHOUT the deferred listener having run (a deliberate, documented semantic divergence from a plain listener, mirroring Spring). The deferred listener future is awaited LATER, when transaction-management fires the phase boundary — and CRITICALLY it must be awaited ON THE SAME LOGICAL TASK that owns the transaction (so the tx resource binding, locale, tracing context are still visible; this is why the binding is task-scoped not thread-scoped — charter §2.3). BEFORE_COMMIT listeners run while the tx is still open, BEFORE the manager's commit .await, so their work is part of the transaction and their errors can abort the commit (error propagates into the tx outcome). AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION run AFTER the commit/rollback .await resolves — their errors CANNOT change the already-finalized tx outcome and are routed to the multicaster's error handler (isolated), never back to the original publisher (who is long gone). CANCELLATION: if the tx future is dropped between publish and the phase boundary, the registered synchronization must be reclaimed WITHOUT firing AFTER_COMMIT (no commit happened) and WITHOUT an async destructor (no async Drop on stable — nightly doc) — so reclamation rides transaction-management's explicit (non-Drop) finalization; a BEFORE_COMMIT listener in flight when the tx is cancelled is cancelled at its await point under the tx's structured-concurrency scope. No polling — deferral is event-driven (the phase boundary firing is a notification, charter §2.4). The captured event + listener handle ride a synchronization stored on the tx (minimal heap: one Arc/Box per deferred listener); listener futures are Send-bounded boxed at the dyn boundary (Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>) so they survive the (possible) worker hop between publish and commit.

#### R2 — Deferral as a multicaster dispatch-policy plugin: a pluggable 'dispatch decision' SPI the tx-phase rule slots into (generalize the listener-adapter extension seam)
Take the brief's 'illustrates how the event model is built upon' LITERALLY and generalize: instead of teaching the multicaster a hard-coded tx-deferral branch (R1), make the multicaster's per-listener dispatch DECISION pluggable via a small SPI — a `DispatchPolicy` (or `ListenerInvocationStrategy`) that, given (event, listener descriptor, execution context), decides HOW/WHEN the listener runs: INLINE now, DEFERRED to a callback, SKIPPED, or SPAWNED. The default policy runs everything inline (the events neighbour's sync-by-default). The `transactional-events` feature contributes ONE `DispatchPolicy` — `TransactionPhasePolicy` — that recognizes descriptors carrying a `TxDeferral` and, when a tx is active, returns a DEFERRED decision binding the listener to a tx-synchronization at the chosen phase (else INLINE if fallback, else SKIPPED-with-diagnostic). The `#[transactional_event_listener]` macro still emits a plain listener Descriptor + a `TxDeferral` data field (as R1), but the multicaster has NO tx-specific code — it just consults the registered DispatchPolicies in order and obeys the first that claims the listener. This makes async-per-listener (the `@Async` on `@EventListener` analogue, an events/async-execution concern) ALSO a DispatchPolicy (returns SPAWNED), and makes future listener kinds (rate-limited, retried, batched) slot in the SAME way — the listener-adapter extensibility Spring illustrates becomes a FIRST-CLASS pluggable seam rather than a one-off. The policy's DEFERRED decision returns a 'how to register the deferral' closure that this feature implements against transaction-management's synchronization protocol; the multicaster only knows 'this policy wants me to hand the listener+event to this callback instead of awaiting it'.

**Pros:** Most faithful to the brief's META-point — 'the event model is BUILT UPON / extensible': it turns the listener-adapter extension from a hard-coded special case (R1) into a genuine pluggable SPI, so transactional-events is literally just one DispatchPolicy among several (async-per-listener, future kinds), maximizing unification across the whole Events area (charter §2.11) and making the extension point self-evident rather than implicit.; Decouples the multicaster from tx entirely: the `event-multicaster`/`events` neighbours need NO tx knowledge — only a stable DispatchPolicy SPI — so the tx feature lives entirely in its own crate contributing a policy, keeping dependencies pointing inward (charter §6: core never depends on tx; tx depends on the events SPI).; One uniform mechanism for ALL non-inline dispatch (defer / spawn-async / skip / future kinds) means the per-listener async opt-in (the @Async-on-@EventListener case) and tx-deferral share the SAME seam and the SAME ordering/error reasoning — fewer bespoke paths, easier to reason about which listener runs when (charter §2.11).; Origin-agnostic and dynamic-friendly: a WASM-module or runtime-added DispatchPolicy can change dispatch behavior without touching the multicaster, and a dynamic transactional listener slots in as data the existing TransactionPhasePolicy already handles — the SPI is the natural meeting point for static + dynamic 'classpaths'.; Thin macro preserved (charter §2.10): the macro still only emits descriptor data (TxDeferral); ALL behavior is in the policy, a normal testable crate — the policy is hand-writable and a user could register their own DispatchPolicy as the ultimate escape hatch.

**Cons:** Bigger conceptual surface and a NEW abstraction (DispatchPolicy SPI) that this feature alone might not justify — it edges toward pre-judging the Events area's machinery (whether the multicaster SHOULD have a pluggable dispatch-decision seam is arguably the events/event-multicaster neighbours' call, not this feature's) — risks over-generalizing from a single example, which Phase 2 should decide.; Per-listener dispatch now costs a policy-chain consultation on the (hot-ish) dispatch path even for plain inline listeners (the default policy must claim them) — unless the multicaster short-circuits 'no policies registered → inline', the SPI adds overhead to the common case (charter §2 low-overhead #2), exactly the kind of indirection the events-R1 typed-channel realization tried to avoid.; Two policies could both claim a listener (a transactional AND async listener — the @Async @TransactionalEventListener combination Spring explicitly handles), and the SPI must define composition/ordering of policies (defer-then-spawn? spawn-then-defer?) — a real semantic-composition problem R1 doesn't have because tx-deferral is the only special case it hard-codes.; The DEFERRED decision still ultimately calls transaction-management's synchronization protocol, so it inherits ALL of R1's tx-binding, Send+'static-event-capture, no-async-Drop-cancellation, and gated-on-neighbour-protocol cons — the SPI adds a layer but does not remove the underlying hard problems.; Harder to give rich, early diagnostics: with dispatch behavior hidden behind a pluggable policy chain, 'why did my listener defer/skip/spawn' is a runtime question about which policy claimed it — whereas R1's hard-coded branch is statically obvious; the SPI must expose a 'leaf doctor'-style 'this listener will be handled by policy X' report to stay legible (charter §2.9).

**Mechanisms considered:** DispatchPolicy / ListenerInvocationStrategy SPI on the multicaster: (event, descriptor, ctx) → Decision { Inline | Deferred(register-callback) | Skipped(reason) | Spawned(executor) }; default policy = run inline (events neighbour's sync-by-default); short-circuit when no policies registered to avoid hot-path cost; transactional-events contributes ONE TransactionPhasePolicy claiming descriptors with TxDeferral; returns Deferred(bind-to-tx-synchronization) | Inline(fallback) | Skipped(no-tx diagnostic); async-per-listener (@Async @EventListener analogue) ALSO modeled as a DispatchPolicy returning Spawned — unifying the two non-inline kinds; policy composition/ordering contract for listeners claimed by multiple policies (the async + transactional combination); `#[transactional_event_listener]` still emits only descriptor data (TxDeferral); behavior lives in the policy crate (thin macro); DEFERRED's register-callback implemented against transaction-management's synchronization protocol (same underlying mechanism as R1)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The listener descriptors ride the SAME events-substrate as R1 (linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit) with the same Layer-0/--gc-sections/silent-empty hazards and self-check mandate. The NEW T1 element is the DispatchPolicy contribution: policies are themselves cross-crate contributions (the TransactionPhasePolicy lives in the tx-events crate and must be DISCOVERED and registered into the multicaster) — so this feature adds a SECOND collected thing (policies) on top of listeners, doubling the substrate surface that must stay coherent (both ride whatever Phase 2 picks). A policy is likely NON-const (it holds the binding-to-tx closure + a handle to the synchronization protocol), so it leans toward the inventory/explicit substrate (typetag-style registration of a `Box<dyn DispatchPolicy>`) rather than linkme's const-only path — a divergence from the events neighbour if events used linkme for descriptors, meaning the two collected things might use DIFFERENT substrates (more surface, but the cross-crate doc explicitly allows mixing). Same leaf-core ABI home for the DispatchPolicy trait + Decision enum + TxDeferral; same absolute-path thin macros. Critical self-check: 'a transactional listener descriptor exists' AND 'the TransactionPhasePolicy is registered' must BOTH hold, else the listener silently falls through to the default inline policy (firing at publish, NOT deferred — a subtle correctness bug worse than skipping); the self-check must assert the policy is present whenever transactional descriptors are. Dual-classpath: a dynamic/WASM source can contribute BOTH listeners and policies to the same SPIs — the most flexible for dynamic extension, but the most surface to keep origin-agnostic.

**T2 — execution context:** Same async-first publish and same deferred-on-the-tx-task execution contract as R1 — the DEFERRED decision binds the listener to the tx-synchronization fired at the phase boundary on the same logical task, with identical BEFORE_COMMIT-can-abort / AFTER_*-isolated / cancellation-without-async-Drop semantics. The added T2 element is policy CONSULTATION on the dispatch path: for each listener the multicaster asks the policy chain for a Decision before acting — this runs synchronously (non-blocking, a cheap match over descriptor data + a tx-active check) on the dispatch path, so it must be a fast, Send, non-blocking decision (no .await inside the decision itself; the decision RETURNS a deferral closure, it doesn't perform the deferral). The Spawned decision (async-per-listener) hands the listener to an executor (the events/async-execution path) — and the policy-composition question (a listener that is BOTH deferred AND spawned) has a sharp T2 consequence: does the deferred listener, when fired at AFTER_COMMIT, then SPAWN (losing the tx task context, which is correct since the tx is already committed)? The contract must be 'defer-then-spawn': bind to the phase, and at firing, spawn — with context-propagation carrying tracing across the spawn (charter §2.3). No polling; the policy chain is consulted once per listener per publish (cold-ish), the phase firing is notification-driven. Decision/policy objects are read-only-after-refresh shared state (Arc), no global lock on the dispatch path.

#### R3 — Outbox-style typed deferred-event queue bound to the transaction scope: the transaction OWNS a typed pending-events buffer drained at the phase boundary (rust-native value-passing, minimal multicaster coupling)
Invert the binding direction to minimize multicaster coupling and lean into rust-native value-ownership. Instead of the multicaster reaching into the tx to register a synchronization per deferred listener (R1/R2), the ACTIVE TRANSACTION owns a typed `DeferredEvents` buffer (a small per-phase collection living in the tx's scope value — the transaction-management neighbour's Transaction handle, especially its R2 'Transaction as a context-scope value'). When `publish<E>(e)` is called inside an active tx AND there exist deferred listeners for E, the publish path does NOT dispatch to those listeners; it ENQUEUES (event clone/Arc + the set of matching deferred listener handles, grouped by phase) into the transaction's `DeferredEvents` buffer and returns. The transaction-management interceptor, at each phase boundary (BEFORE_COMMIT before its commit .await, AFTER_COMMIT after a successful commit, AFTER_ROLLBACK after rollback, AFTER_COMPLETION always), DRAINS the buffer's matching phase bucket and re-enters the multicaster's dispatch for those (event, listener) pairs — now with the deferral 'consumed', so they run inline at that boundary. Plain (non-deferred) listeners for E still fire inline at publish as usual. The no-tx case: if there is no active tx, deferred listeners either skip (fallback=false, with diagnostic) or run inline (fallback=true) — decided at publish since there's no buffer to enqueue into. This makes the deferred event a VALUE the transaction OWNS and drains (an in-process transactional-outbox shape) rather than a callback registered on a synchronization registry — the tx's lifecycle naturally bounds the buffer's lifetime (drained-or-discarded at completion), and ownership/teardown is structural (the buffer drops with the tx scope; no async-Drop needed because draining is explicit at the boundary, and discard-on-cancel is just dropping the buffer's owned values).

**Pros:** Cleanest ownership/lifetime story (charter §2.5): the deferred events are a VALUE the transaction OWNS in its scope, so their lifetime is structurally bounded by the tx — drained at the phase boundary or dropped on cancellation, with NO synchronization-registry to reclaim and NO async-Drop hazard (dropping the buffer drops the owned event values; the only async work is the explicit drain-and-dispatch at the boundary).; Minimal multicaster coupling: the multicaster's publish path only needs 'if a tx is active and this event has deferred listeners, enqueue into the tx buffer instead of dispatching them' — and the DRAIN re-enters the NORMAL inline dispatch path, so the multicaster has ONE dispatch routine reused at both publish-time (plain listeners) and phase-boundary-time (drained deferred listeners); no per-listener synchronization registration callback (simpler than R1, far simpler than R2's SPI).; Rust-native value-passing aligns with the transaction-management R2 (Transaction-as-scope-value) and context-propagation R2/R3 (context rides the future as a value): the deferred-events buffer is just another value on the tx scope, so it survives work-stealing migration automatically and needs no thread-local/ambient registry — the most coherent fit if those neighbours choose the value-passing realizations.; Naturally supports a clean transactional-OUTBOX extension (a real-world pattern this shape enables): the buffer of after-commit events is exactly an in-process outbox; persisting it (for at-least-once delivery across crashes) becomes a drop-in variant of the same buffer, a capability Spring's synchronization model doesn't structurally suggest — a rust-native bonus aligned with AFTER_COMMIT's 'durable side effects' intent.; Diagnostics are concrete and inspectable (charter §2.9): 'this transaction has N events buffered for AFTER_COMMIT' is a readable value on the tx; the skip-vs-fallback decision happens at publish with a structured trace, and a drained-but-the-listener-errored event is attributable to its originating publish + phase.

**Cons:** Tightest coupling to the transaction-management Transaction handle's SHAPE: it presumes the tx exposes an attachable typed buffer on its scope value — which pre-judges transaction-management's deferred scope/ownership model (its R2 vs R1) and the charter §3 scope/lifetime keystone harder than R1/R2 do (they only need a synchronization-registration hook, which all tx realizations sketch); R3's correctness is gated on the tx being a scope-VALUE that can carry a buffer, not just a synchronization registry.; Ordering across deferred listeners from DIFFERENT publish calls within one tx is now a buffer-drain-order concern: if a tx publishes E1 then E2 both with AFTER_COMMIT listeners, the drain order (publish order? @Order across the merged bucket?) must be defined, and merging @Order across events buffered at different times is fiddly (R1's per-synchronization model has the same issue but the buffer makes the merge explicit and visible — a wash that becomes a documentation burden).; The enqueue path must CLONE or Arc the event (it outlives the publish call inside the buffer) — same Send+'static-event constraint as R1/R2, but now EVERY deferred publish pays the buffer-insertion cost (a heap push) even before knowing the tx will commit; for a tx that publishes many deferred events then rolls back, all that buffering is wasted work (R1's synchronization registration has a similar but arguably lighter per-listener cost).; Re-entering the multicaster's dispatch from inside the transaction-management interceptor at the phase boundary creates a control-flow dependency tx→events that must avoid a cycle (a drained AFTER_COMMIT listener that itself publishes a new event needing a NEW tx, or chains a return-value event — the chaining-into-a-committed-tx case) — the re-entrancy and the 'can a drained listener start/need a transaction' question is a real hazard R1/R2 share but R3's drain-loop makes more prominent.; Less obviously 'built ON TOP of @EventListener' than R1: the deferral lives in the transaction's buffer-drain rather than in the listener-adapter layer, so it reads more like a tx feature that happens to call events than like 'an extensible event-listener kind' — slightly weaker on the brief's specific illustration intent, even though the user-facing authoring (`#[transactional_event_listener]`) is identical.

**Mechanisms considered:** Transaction owns a typed `DeferredEvents` buffer (per-phase buckets) on its scope value (leans on transaction-management R2 Transaction-as-scope-value); publish path: if tx active AND event has deferred listeners → enqueue (event Arc/clone + matching deferred listener handles, grouped by phase) into the tx buffer; else dispatch/skip/fallback inline; transaction-management interceptor DRAINS the matching phase bucket at each boundary and RE-ENTERS the normal multicaster inline dispatch for those (event, listener) pairs; plain listeners unaffected — fire inline at publish; only deferred listeners are buffered; structural lifetime: buffer drops with the tx scope on cancel/rollback-discard (no async-Drop); explicit drain-and-dispatch at commit/completion boundaries; optional persisted-outbox variant of the same buffer for at-least-once AFTER_COMMIT delivery (rust-native bonus aligned with AFTER_COMMIT's durable-side-effect intent); `#[transactional_event_listener]` emits the same descriptor + TxDeferral data (identical authoring to R1/R2)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Listener descriptors ride the same events-substrate (linkme/inventory/codegen/explicit) with the same Layer-0/--gc-sections/silent-empty hazards and self-check. R3 adds the LEAST new cross-crate machinery of the three — there is NO new SPI (R2) and NO per-listener synchronization-callback registration (R1); the deferred behavior is a buffer the tx owns + a drain the tx interceptor performs, both living in the transaction-management + transactional-events crates with no extra collected thing. The cross-crate coupling is instead a TYPE/API coupling: the transactional-events crate must depend on transaction-management's Transaction-handle API to attach/read the buffer, so the dependency edge tx-events→transaction-management is explicit and inward-pointing (charter §6). Same leaf-core/leaf-framework ABI home for TxPhase, TxDeferral, DeferredEvents. Critical self-check identical to R1: 'transactional listener exists but transaction-management/Transaction-buffer capability absent' must fail/warn loudly. Dual-classpath: a dynamic/WASM transactional listener enqueues into the same tx buffer (origin-agnostic at the buffer API) — but a WASM-guest listener drained at AFTER_COMMIT must marshal the buffered event across the WIT boundary at drain time (same dynamic-half marshalling question as R1), and if the persisted-outbox variant is used, the buffered event must be SERIALIZABLE (a stronger constraint than R1's in-memory Arc, reintroducing a serde-style bound for that variant only).

**T2 — execution context:** Async-first; the execution context centers on the tx interceptor's DRAIN, not the multicaster. ENQUEUE happens synchronously on the publisher's task inside `publish(e).await` (a buffer push — non-blocking, cheap), and publish returns WITHOUT the deferred listeners running (same documented divergence as R1/R2). The DRAIN runs INSIDE the transaction-management interceptor on the tx's logical task at the phase boundary: BEFORE_COMMIT drains-and-awaits its bucket BEFORE the manager's commit .await (so those listeners' work is inside the tx and their errors can abort the commit — the interceptor must await the drained listeners and feed errors into its commit decision); AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION drain-and-await AFTER the commit/rollback .await resolves (errors isolated, routed to the multicaster error handler, cannot un-finalize the tx). All drained listeners run on the SAME logical task that owns the tx (tx resource binding, locale, tracing still visible — charter §2.3). CANCELLATION is the cleanest of the three: if the tx future is dropped before the boundary, the buffer (an owned value on the tx scope) is simply DROPPED with its owned event values — no AFTER_COMMIT fires (correct), no synchronization to reclaim, NO async-Drop needed (the events are plain owned values; dropping them is synchronous) — this is R3's signature T2 advantage over R1/R2's synchronization-reclamation. The re-entrancy hazard is a T2 concern: a drained AFTER_COMMIT listener that publishes a NEW deferred event has NO active tx at that point (the tx is committed), so its deferred listeners hit the no-tx skip/fallback path — the drain must NOT re-buffer into the just-committed tx (a cycle guard). Send+'static on buffered events (they ride the tx future across awaits/migration); listener futures Send-boxed at the dyn boundary. No polling — drain is triggered by the phase-boundary notification (charter §2.4). Minimal heap: one buffer per tx with deferred events, Arc/owned event values, dropped wholesale on cancel.

### Rust clashes
- Spring binds the deferred listener to the THREAD via TransactionSynchronizationManager's NamedThreadLocal synchronizations set (§2114-2118), and fires those synchronizations on the committing thread. In async leaf there is NO ThreadLocal across .await and the tx resource lives on the LOGICAL TASK, not the OS thread (a task migrates workers at every await under work-stealing). So the deferred (event, listener) binding must attach to the TASK-SCOPED transaction (via transaction-management + context-propagation's task-scoped substrate), and the deferred listener must fire on the same logical task at the phase boundary — Spring's whole thread-local synchronization-registry storage strategy must NOT be ported. This is the same charter §3 keystone clash that transaction-management inherits, viewed through the deferred-listener lens; it is also EXACTLY the gap Spring's own 6.1 reactive-tx fix addressed (carrying tx context in the event because Reactor uses Context not ThreadLocal, §1893) — leaf's task-scoped binding realizes that fix natively for ALL listeners, not just reactive ones.
- The deferred event must OUTLIVE the publish call: the publisher's publish(e).await returns BEFORE the deferred listener runs (at commit), so the event value must be captured into a synchronization (R1/R2) or a tx-owned buffer (R3) that lives until the phase boundary. This forces the event to be Send + 'static (it rides a structure stored on the tx and fired later, possibly after a worker hop) — a constraint Spring never has (the JVM GC keeps the Object alive freely). A non-Clone event consumed by a deferred listener must be moved or Arc-wrapped; this makes 'which events can be transactionally listened to' a type-level question with an ownership/heap cost the inline event path doesn't pay.
- NO async Drop on stable (nightly doc 273-281: AsyncDrop crashes tokio shutdown) — so the deferral structure (synchronization registration in R1/R2, or the tx-owned buffer in R3) CANNOT use an async destructor to fire or reclaim deferred listeners on tx cancellation/drop. AFTER_COMMIT listeners must NOT fire if the tx was cancelled before committing (no commit happened), and the captured event/listener must be reclaimed without async cleanup. R1/R2 must ride transaction-management's explicit (non-Drop) finalization to reclaim synchronizations; R3 sidesteps this by making the buffer an OWNED VALUE that drops synchronously with the tx scope (no async work to reclaim — its signature advantage). A genuine correctness constraint with no JVM analogue.
- Spring's silent-no-op-without-a-transaction (§1897: fallbackExecution=false default means a transactional listener baffingly does nothing outside a tx) is a documented footgun. In Rust this footgun gets WORSE structurally: a transactional listener can ALSO be silently DCE-dropped at link time (cross-crate doc Layer-0/--gc-sections), so 'my after-commit email never sent' has TWO indistinguishable silent causes (no active tx vs dropped descriptor). leaf must convert BOTH into loud diagnostics: a structured trace on the no-tx skip path, AND a startup self-check asserting (a) transactional listener descriptors were collected and (b) the transaction-management/synchronization capability is actually wired — turning Spring's baffling no-op into an early, legible diagnostic (charter §2.9).
- Spring re-discovers @TransactionalEventListener methods via reflection (it's a meta-annotated @EventListener processed by EventListenerMethodProcessor) and reads the phase reflectively at runtime. Rust has no runtime reflection — the phase (BEFORE_COMMIT/AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION) and fallback flag must be captured AS CONST DATA at macro-expansion time (a TxDeferral field on the same listener Descriptor the events neighbour already emits), and the macro must hard-error on a phase value that isn't a known variant. The deferral is data the dispatch layer reads, not a reflectively-discovered annotation.
- BEFORE_COMMIT vs AFTER_* listeners have FUNDAMENTALLY different error-propagation semantics that Rust's Result-based async makes explicit (vs Spring's exception-based, where a BEFORE_COMMIT throw rolls back the tx and an AFTER_COMMIT throw is logged). In leaf: a BEFORE_COMMIT listener runs BEFORE the commit .await and its Err must feed into the tx's commit-or-rollback DECISION (it can abort the commit); an AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK listener runs AFTER the tx is finalized and its Err CANNOT change the outcome (it is isolated/routed to the multicaster error handler, never back to the original publisher who is long gone). This per-phase error contract must be designed explicitly (charter §2.3), not emergent — the phase determines WHERE in the tx interceptor's control flow the listener's Result is consumed.
- @Order across deferred and non-deferred listeners for one event is meaningless across the publish-vs-phase TIMELINE: a plain listener fires inline at publish, a BEFORE_COMMIT listener fires later (still pre-commit), an AFTER_COMMIT listener fires later still (post-commit) — so @Order can only order WITHIN a phase, never interleave a plain and a transactional listener. Spring has the same reality but leaf must document it loudly and the dispatch layer must group-by-phase before applying @Order (a user expecting @Order(1) on a plain listener to run before @Order(2) on an after-commit listener is correct only because of the phase, not the order value).
- Return-value-as-new-event CHAINING (the events neighbour's feature) interacts hazardously with deferral: an AFTER_COMMIT listener's returned event would be re-published AFTER the tx is committed — so chaining from a deferred listener re-enters publish with NO active tx (the original tx is finalized), meaning a chained event's own transactional listeners hit the no-tx skip/fallback path. This mirrors the events neighbour's 'chaining disabled on async listeners' caveat (the result arrives after the publisher returned) — leaf must define whether chaining from a deferred listener is allowed at all, and if so that the chained event runs in NO tx context (a compile/startup or documented-runtime contract, cross-cutting the events neighbour's chaining model).

### Interactions
- **events** — DIRECT upstream — this feature IS a specialization of the events neighbour's @EventListener model. `#[transactional_event_listener]` is a thin meta-specialization of `#[event_listener]`, emitting the SAME const listener Descriptor (bean TypeId, event TypeId, order, condition, async factory) PLUS a TxDeferral data field; the listener lands in whatever typed-channel/erased/hybrid store events-R1/R2/R3 builds. This feature reuses events' entire authoring/condition/generic-targeting/ordering machinery and adds ONLY the WHEN/WHETHER deferral — it is the explicit illustration that the events neighbour called out (its transactional-events interaction note: 'a transaction-phase-deferring listener is just another listener kind, an adapter that defers invocation to a commit/rollback phase'). The events neighbour must expose a listener-adapter/extension seam this feature plugs into (uniform in events-R2/R3, special-cased in events-R1).
- **event-multicaster** — The dispatch SEAM whose inline invocation this feature RE-ROUTES into a tx-synchronization (R1), via a pluggable DispatchPolicy (R2), or whose dispatch it RE-ENTERS at the phase boundary after draining a tx-owned buffer (R3). The multicaster owns sync-by-default ordered dispatch, the error-handler seam, and async opt-in; this feature changes, for deferred listeners only, the TIMING of when the multicaster actually awaits the listener (deferred to a tx phase rather than inline at publish). The per-phase error contract (BEFORE_COMMIT errors abort the tx; AFTER_* errors routed to the multicaster's error handler) is a cooperation between this feature and the multicaster's error-handling seam.
- **transaction-management** — DIRECT upstream and co-equal foundation — this feature is built ON its transaction-synchronization hooks. transaction-management exposes the BEFORE_COMMIT/AFTER_COMMIT/AFTER_ROLLBACK/AFTER_COMPLETION phase boundaries and the synchronization-registration protocol (R1/R2) or the Transaction-handle that carries a deferred-events buffer (R3, leaning on transaction-management's R2 Transaction-as-scope-value). The 'is a transaction active on this task' query, the task-scoped tx-resource binding (so deferred listeners fire on the same logical task with tx context visible), and the explicit non-async-Drop finalization on cancellation all come from transaction-management. transaction-management's own design explicitly named this: 'transaction-management must expose the transaction-synchronization registration + phase callbacks that transactional-events consumes — the explicit illustration that the event model is built upon tx.'
- **context-propagation** — Provides the task-scoped ambient substrate that makes 'fire the deferred listener on the same logical task as the tx, with tx/locale/tracing context still visible' work across .await. Because the deferred listener fires LATER than publish (at the phase boundary), the execution context that was current at publish vs at firing differs; context-propagation's capture/restore SPI governs which context the deferred listener sees (it should see the TX's context at the phase boundary, not the publisher's stale context). For R2/R3's defer-then-spawn composition (a deferred listener that also spawns at AFTER_COMMIT), context-propagation carries tracing across the spawn.
- **lifecycle-events** — Sibling listener kind on the same bus, and a contrast case: lifecycle facts (refreshed/started/stopped/closed) are published through the same events model but are NOT transaction-bound — they illustrate the PLAIN listener path this feature specializes. A transactional listener and a lifecycle listener can coexist on one bean; the dispatch layer must route each by its descriptor's deferral data (defer=None → inline at publish for lifecycle facts; defer=Some → tx-phase deferral).
- **expression-language** — Supplies the per-event condition filtering inherited from the @EventListener model — a transactional listener can ALSO carry a condition (evaluated to decide whether the listener applies at all). The interaction subtlety: WHEN is the condition evaluated — at publish (when deciding to defer) or at the phase boundary (when actually firing)? Spring evaluates at the adapter; leaf must decide whether a deferred listener's condition is checked at enqueue/registration time or at drain/fire time (the event value is the same, but ambient context differs between the two moments). Shares the events neighbour's condition-slot contract (a sync, non-blocking, Send predicate over &E).
- **async-execution** — Parallels the per-listener @Async path and composes with deferral (Spring explicitly supports @Async @TransactionalEventListener). In R2 both deferral and async-spawn are DispatchPolicies that may both claim a listener, requiring a composition contract (defer-then-spawn: bind to the phase, then at AFTER_COMMIT firing spawn onto an executor — correct because the tx is already committed so losing the tx task context is fine). The shared caveat set (no return-value chaining on async/deferred-then-spawned listeners, errors to a handler not the original publisher) must be consistent between this feature and async-execution.
- **task-execution** — Backs any spawned execution of a deferred listener (the defer-then-spawn / async transactional listener case) via the multicaster's async opt-in. 'Run this AFTER_COMMIT listener on some executor' delegates pool/virtual-thread choice to task-execution; this feature only guarantees the listener is bound to the AFTER_COMMIT phase and that, once the phase fires, spawning (if requested) follows the task-execution-provided executor with context-propagation carrying context across the hop.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Owns the bring-up phase that finalizes listener registration (the SmartInitializingSingleton analogue) — transactional listener descriptors are bound to their (now-constructed) bean instances at the same single post-all-singletons-exist step as plain listeners. The startup self-check this feature mandates ('transactional listeners exist AND transaction-management is wired') runs as part of this fixed-order, all-or-nothing refresh, so a transactional-listener-without-tx-capability misconfiguration aborts/warns at startup with full context rather than at the first publish.
- **lazy-initialization** — Inherits the classic 'listener never fires' gotcha doubly: Spring silently never registers a @Lazy bean's @EventListener (and thus its @TransactionalEventListener) methods. leaf must decide (consistently with the events neighbour) whether a lazy bean's transactional-listener descriptors are still registered (diverging from Spring to fix the gotcha) or honored-lazy — and surface it via the startup self-check, because a silently-unregistered AFTER_COMMIT listener means a durable side effect silently never happens (a more severe consequence than a plain listener missing).
- **container-hierarchy** — Context-hierarchy double-delivery interacts with deferral: an event published in a child context also reaches ancestor-context listeners (via the events neighbour's parent-walk). A transactional listener in an ancestor context, reached by a child's publish, must still bind to the SAME active transaction (the tx is task-scoped, not context-scoped) — so the deferral binds to the task's tx regardless of which context's listener it is, while the double-delivery disambiguation (compare originating context) rides container-hierarchy's visibility rules unchanged.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Where does the deferral BIND, and what is the binding's lifetime substrate: a synchronization callback registered on the active transaction (R1/R2) vs a typed deferred-events buffer the transaction OWNS as a scope value (R3)? This is entangled with transaction-management's deferred Transaction-handle shape (its R1 synchronization-registry vs R2 Transaction-as-scope-value) AND the charter §3 scope/lifetime/ownership keystone — this feature cannot pick it; its correctness is gated on how transaction-management models the active transaction and its synchronization protocol.
- Should the multicaster's per-listener dispatch DECISION be a hard-coded tx-deferral branch (R1) or a pluggable DispatchPolicy/ListenerInvocationStrategy SPI that tx-deferral, async-per-listener, and future listener kinds all slot into (R2)? Generalizing to an SPI best expresses the brief's 'the event model is extensible' meta-point and maximizes unification (charter §2.11), but edges toward pre-judging the events/event-multicaster neighbours' machinery and adds hot-path consultation cost — a cross-cutting Events-area decision shared with events, event-multicaster, and async-execution, not resolvable per-feature.
- How is the no-active-transaction case made loud rather than silent (fixing Spring's §1897 baffling no-op), and how is it distinguished from a link-time DCE-dropped descriptor? Both produce 'my after-commit listener never fired' but need different remedies (set fallback=true vs force-link the crate). Couples to the unified error/diagnostics model (charter §2.9) and the cross-crate composition self-check — the diagnostic vocabulary must be shared with the events neighbour's 'listener never fires' self-check.
- What is the exact per-phase error-and-cancellation contract: how does a BEFORE_COMMIT listener's Err feed into the tx commit-or-rollback decision (it can abort), how are AFTER_* listener errors isolated from the already-finalized tx, and how is a deferred listener reclaimed when the tx is cancelled before its phase WITHOUT async Drop (nightly doc)? Shares the unified cancellation-contract meta-concept (T2) with transaction-management, context-close, task-execution, and retry-resilience — needs ONE answer for all resource-holding/deferred-callback interceptors, not a per-feature hack.
- When is a deferred listener's CONDITION (the @EventListener condition slot) and its captured event evaluated/snapshotted — at publish/enqueue time or at phase-boundary/fire time — given the ambient execution context differs between those two moments? Cross-cuts the expression-language hot-path-condition decision and the context-propagation 'which context does a later-fired callback see' question.
- Is return-value-as-new-event CHAINING permitted from a deferred listener, and if so, in what tx context does the chained event run (the original tx is finalized by AFTER_COMMIT, so the chained event has no active tx)? This mirrors the events neighbour's 'chaining disabled on async listeners' caveat and must be enforced consistently (compile-time macro hard-error, startup pass, or documented runtime contract) — cross-cuts the events chaining model and the metadata/codegen boundary (what the macro knows about the listener's return type).
- Do dynamic/WASM-module-contributed transactional listeners need to be first-class, and how does a guest-side AFTER_COMMIT listener marshal the buffered/captured event across the WIT boundary at the phase-firing moment (and SERIALIZE it for the persisted-outbox variant of R3)? This is the dual-classpath candidate (static link-time listener set PLUS optional dynamic source) applied to deferred listeners, interacting with the origin-agnostic-provider guardrail and transaction-management's WASM-manager resource-marshalling question.
- Should leaf offer a PERSISTED transactional-outbox variant (R3's buffer persisted for at-least-once AFTER_COMMIT delivery across crashes), and is that this feature's concern or an ecosystem-integration concern layered on top? It is a rust-native opportunity aligned with AFTER_COMMIT's durable-side-effect intent that Spring's synchronization model doesn't structurally suggest, but it pulls in serialization and durability concerns that may belong outside the core (charter §1 scope) — a candidate to flag, not resolve.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the exact Spring section the brief pointed to (§1891-1897, `@TransactionalEventListener` — extensibility, illustrated, incl. the defaultExecution→fallbackExecution coupling that explains the silent-no-op) plus the surrounding events model (§1788-1913), the synchronous-by-default dispatch and async caveats (§1856-1871), the TransactionSynchronizationManager thread-bound/never-inheritable section (§2114-2118), and the context-propagation SPI (§2120-2155). The API is explicitly STABLE/unchanged in v7/Boot4 (§1796), so the parity target is solid. The brief's framing is precise and I honored it: this feature's whole identity is being the CANONICAL ILLUSTRATION that the listener-adapter layer is extensible — a phase-aware listener built ENTIRELY on top of @EventListener — so I kept the design as a thin consumer of two neighbour seams (events' listener-adapter layer + transaction-management's synchronization hooks) rather than inventing any new bus, engine, or registry.\n\nScope discipline: I do NOT own the publish/subscribe model, listener authoring, condition/generic/ordering/chaining semantics, or the dispatch seam (all events + event-multicaster); I do NOT own the TransactionManager, the open/commit/rollback lifecycle, the resource-binding-to-task primitive, or the synchronization-hook protocol (all transaction-management); I do NOT own the ambient-context-across-.await primitive (context-propagation). I OWN: the `#[transactional_event_listener(phase, fallback)]` attribute DATA model (a meta-specialization of `#[event_listener]` adding a const TxDeferral field), the deferral REROUTING (the listener is bound to a tx-synchronization / DispatchPolicy / tx-owned buffer and fired at the phase boundary instead of inline at publish), the no-tx skip-vs-fallback policy WITH its loud diagnostic (fixing Spring's silent no-op), and the per-phase error contract.\n\nThe three realizations are genuinely DIVERGENT on the axis that defines this feature — WHERE the deferral binds and how much the multicaster must cooperate: R1 (faithful port: deferral is a data field on the listener descriptor + a rerouting branch in the multicaster that registers a tx-synchronization — purest 'built on top of @EventListener' illustration, thinnest, most intent-faithful, but needs a cooperating branch in the multicaster and inherits the Send+'static-event-capture + no-async-Drop-reclaim cons); R2 (generalize: a pluggable DispatchPolicy SPI on the multicaster that tx-deferral, async-per-listener, and future kinds all slot into — best expresses the 'event model is extensible' meta-point and maximizes unification, but bigger surface, hot-path consultation cost, policy-composition problem, and arguably over-generalizes from one example, which is Phase 2's call); R3 (invert: the transaction OWNS a typed deferred-events buffer drained at the phase boundary — cleanest ownership/lifetime/cancellation story since dropping the buffer is synchronous with NO async-Drop hazard, enables a rust-native transactional-outbox bonus, but tightest coupling to transaction-management's Transaction-handle shape and slightly weaker on the 'built on @EventListener' illustration since the deferral lives in the tx's drain rather than the listener-adapter layer). All three share the identical authoring surface (`#[transactional_event_listener]`), the four phases, the task-scoped binding, the loud-no-tx-diagnostic, and the no-async-Drop cancellation constraint — so they are composable layers Phase 2/3 can merge, not exclusive.\n\nThe single most important rust-native realization I want to carry forward: Spring's 6.1 reactive-tx fix (carry the tx context INSIDE the event because Reactor uses Context not ThreadLocal, §1893) is EXACTLY what leaf must do for ALL transactional listeners, not just reactive ones — because leaf is async-first with the tx bound to the LOGICAL TASK, not a thread; leaf realizes Spring's special-case reactive fix as the universal, native default. Second: Spring's silent-no-op-without-a-tx footgun (§1897) gets structurally WORSE in Rust (a transactional listener can ALSO be silently DCE-dropped at link time — and a missing AFTER_COMMIT listener means a durable side effect like a confirmation email silently never fires), so a loud diagnostic on the skip path AND a startup self-check ('transactional listeners exist AND transaction-management is wired') is non-negotiable across all three realizations. Third: the per-phase error semantics are NOT uniform — BEFORE_COMMIT errors can abort the commit (consumed before the manager's commit .await), AFTER_* errors are isolated (the tx is already finalized) — this must be designed explicitly, not emergent.\n\nTwo clashes dominate downstream and belong to Phase 2, unresolved: the binding-substrate decision (synchronization-callback vs tx-owned-buffer, entangled with transaction-management's Transaction-handle shape and the scope/ownership keystone) and the whether-to-generalize-to-a-DispatchPolicy-SPI decision (an Events-area machinery question shared with events/event-multicaster/async-execution). The dual-classpath duality the brief asked me to flag (static link-time transactional listeners as the default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic/WASM-module source, both feeding the same descriptor API with defer=Some) is captured as a candidate, with the WASM-guest AFTER_COMMIT marshalling-at-fire-time question surfaced. I used `#[transactional_event_listener]`, `TxDeferral`, `TxPhase`, `PhaseAwareAdapter`, `DispatchPolicy`, `DeferredEvents` as Phase-1 placeholder names only. I wrote no files.\n\nGrounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (transactional events §1891-1897; events model §1788-1913; sync-default + async caveats §1856-1871; TransactionSynchronizationManager never-inheritable §2114-2118; context-propagation SPI §2120-2165); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 — esp. 2.2 intent-not-mechanism, 2.3 execution-context discipline, 2.4 reactive-on-hot-paths, 2.5 memory discipline, 2.9 diagnostics, 2.10 thin macros, 2.11 unification; deferred keystones §3); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0 unreferenced-crate-drop + --gc-sections silent-empty + mandatory self-check, lines 149/152/345; linkme-vs-inventory substrate; Architectures A-D; two-source/dual-classpath line 362; ordering-as-data 355); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (no async Drop on stable, 273-281 — the load-bearing cancellation/reclaim constraint; AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe → Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>> at the dyn boundary; const TypeId 1.91 for const descriptors; Arc<dyn Any>/downcast + trait upcasting 1.86 as the erasure backbone); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/design/explorations/wasm-modules-for-leaf.md (origin-agnostic provider seam; WASI 0.3 async ratified so a guest AFTER_COMMIT listener can fire async-across-the-boundary; directory-scanned dynamic 'classpath' as the second source; resource-handle marshalling). Neighbour Phase-1 designs handed to me directly: events (the listener model + multicaster seam; its transactional-events interaction note explicitly anticipates this feature as 'just another listener kind / phase-deferring adapter') and transaction-management (owns the synchronization hooks; its transactional-events interaction note explicitly commits to exposing the BEFORE/AFTER-commit/rollback phase callbacks this feature consumes). Other neighbours (event-multicaster, lifecycle-events, context-propagation, expression-language, async-execution, task-execution, refresh-lifecycle, lazy-initialization, container-hierarchy) referenced by catalog intent.

## Area coherence notes

- DEFAULT ERROR-ISOLATION POLICY is raised independently by events (phase2 'first listener error abort vs isolation'), event-multicaster (phase2 'abort-and-propagate vs isolate'), AND smart-initializing/lifecycle-events (which both NEED fail-fast: a failed ready-hook aborts refresh; an ApplicationReady-listener failure fails startup). These are the SAME decision but the features state subtly different defaults: event-multicaster leans 'Spring-faithful abort-and-propagate' as the dispatch default, while lifecycle-events explicitly needs a NON-default 'observe-and-fail-startup' for milestone dispatch, and smart-initializing needs all-or-nothing that R2's event-multicaster 'collect-and-continue' default would violate. The area must settle ONE error model with a per-dispatch policy override, or these three features will assume incompatible defaults at the same seam.
- REACTIVE-STATE / NOTIFICATION-CHANNEL DUPLICATION: lifecycle-events (availability state cell), runtime-lifecycle (R3 watch<RunState>), and context-close (R3 watch<RunDown>) each independently propose a runtime-agnostic reactive state primitive, and EACH separately flags 'is this notification channel SHARED with the event bus or a separate mechanism?' as an open question. This is one unresolved unification (do lifecycle/availability/run-state transitions ride the event-multicaster, or a separate watch-shaped cell with an event projection?) re-raised three times. Left unreconciled it risks TWO overlapping notification substrates (the event bus AND a watch channel) carrying the same lifecycle transitions — exactly the duplication §2.11 warns against. The three features must agree whether 'state transition' and 'fact event' are one mechanism or two.
- SMART-INITIALIZING vs CONTEXT-REFRESHED FACT timing is consistently described (ready-hook fires inside finish-factory-init; ContextRefreshed fires later at finish-refresh after auto-start), and BOTH smart-initializing and lifecycle-events correctly note they are alternative hooks for 'graph complete'. But R2 of smart-initializing wants to UNIFY the ready-hook with lifecycle-events as 'one milestone + subscribers', while lifecycle-events keeps Refreshed as a SEPARATE later fact — if both unifications are taken, the single 'context ready' primitive would need TWO ordered milestones (singletons-ready before auto-start, refreshed after), which both features acknowledge but neither fully commits to. The area should decide up front whether there is one milestone or two, because runtime-lifecycle's auto-start sits BETWEEN them and three features straddle that boundary.
- LISTENER DISCOVERY SUBSTRATE + ANTI-DCE SELF-CHECK is independently raised as a phase2Question by events, lifecycle-events, transactional-events, refresh-lifecycle, runtime-lifecycle, smart-initializing, aware-callbacks, AND startup-instrumentation — every feature that contributes a discovered participant. All correctly defer to 'the shared cross-crate composition substrate', but they describe the self-check manifest's HOME inconsistently (events: a 'startup self-check'; refresh-lifecycle: 'facade vs generated app-root'; runtime-lifecycle: 'expected vs found auto-start participants'; container-core: 'the facade's expected-vs-found'). This is coherent in DEFERRING but the manifest's location and shape must be ONE answer; otherwise each feature's 'loud startup diagnostic' is computed against a different manifest. Flag for a single area-wide self-check contract.
- TRANSACTIONAL-EVENTS' adapter seam vs events' listener-adapter seam: transactional-events builds on 'the events listener-adapter extension seam' and event-multicaster's 'dispatch-extension seam' — but it is ambiguous whether tx-deferral is (a) a DATA field on the descriptor that events emits and the multicaster reads (transactional-events R1 + events' descriptor-with-TxDeferral-field), (b) a pluggable DispatchPolicy on the multicaster (transactional-events R2 + event-multicaster R2/R3), or (c) a tx-owned outbox buffer that re-enters publish (transactional-events R3). events, event-multicaster, AND transactional-events each describe the seam from their own side without a shared commitment to WHERE the deferral decision lives (in the resolved descriptor before the ListenerSeq, or as a dispatch interceptor inside the engine). Combined with the unnamed EventListenerFactory seam (see missingFeatures), this is the single most under-specified intra-area boundary — three features point at one seam they describe three different ways.
- CONDITION-EVALUATION TIMING is a genuine cross-feature inconsistency surfaced by transactional-events ('is the @EventListener condition checked at enqueue/publish or at phase-fire?') that events does NOT address — events specifies the condition is a sync non-blocking Send predicate evaluated 'at dispatch (the hot path)', implicitly assuming inline-at-publish, but a deferred (transactional) listener fires LATER with different ambient context, so 'at dispatch' is genuinely ambiguous for deferred listeners. events and transactional-events must agree whether the condition slot is evaluated once at publish-time resolution or re-evaluated at fire-time, because the event value is the same but #root context differs — and async listeners face the identical question. The condition-timing contract is shared by events, transactional-events, async-execution, and expression-language and is not yet consistent.
- CANCELLATION / PARTIAL-CLEANUP CONTRACT (no async Drop) is correctly identified as a SHARED meta-concept by refresh-lifecycle, context-close, lifecycle-callbacks, runtime-lifecycle, smart-initializing, aware-callbacks, AND transactional-events — all defer to 'the shared teardown ledger + structured-concurrency scope'. This is coherent and well-cross-referenced. The only risk: context-close R1 (linear LIFO ledger) vs R2 (reverse-topo of live graph) vs R3 (state-machine scope-guard) must be the SAME substrate that refresh-lifecycle's cancel path and lifecycle-callbacks' partial-init-destroy compensation reuse — all three teardown ENTRY points (clean close, cancelled refresh, partial-init-failure) share one walk, which the designs assert but pin to a substrate that is itself deferred. Consistent, but the resolution must land in one place for all three entry points simultaneously.
- LAZY-BEAN 'LISTENER NEVER FIRES' divergence decision is raised identically by events, lifecycle-events, and transactional-events (does a @Lazy bean still register its @EventListener/lifecycle/transactional listeners, diverging from Spring to fix the gotcha, or honor laziness?). All three correctly say 'surface via the startup self-check' and 'reconcile across the family' — coherent. But smart-initializing raises the SHARP version (the @Lazy + ready-hook contradiction: force-include into the eager pass per SPR-7014, or reject) and runtime-lifecycle notes '@Lazy barely affects SmartLifecycle (auto-start forces init)'. These are the SAME 'lazy participation' question across four features with three different Spring precedents (lazy listeners silently dropped; lazy SmartInitializing force-included; lazy SmartLifecycle force-started). The area should resolve 'does laziness gate participation in cross-cutting registries' as ONE policy with documented per-mechanism exceptions, not four independent decisions.
- STARTUP-INSTRUMENTATION vs lifecycle-events vs the event bus: startup-instrumentation R3 (phase-event stream) explicitly flags it 'overlaps and must be reconciled' with events/event-multicaster/lifecycle-events because startup steps fire DURING bring-up before the multicaster exists (the same early-buffering problem refresh-lifecycle owns). This is the correct flag, but it means THREE early-emission mechanisms are in play — the events early-buffer, the lifecycle-events pre-context facts, and startup-instrumentation's pre-recorder steps — each with its own 'emitted before the consumer exists' story. The area should confirm these share ONE early-buffering primitive (drained at the same refresh phase) rather than three parallel pre-context channels.
